HEADLINE: FLAWS ARE FOUND IN CONTRACT PLAN TO AID MINORITIES

SERIES: Hidden Partners, Phantom Contracts: First of two articles.

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER with THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
New York City's ambitious effort to steer more city contracts to companies owned by women or minorities has been marred by faulty bookkeeping and the use of companies whose real control is unclear, an analysis of scores of contracts shows.
In a three-month examination of 56 companies that received most of the large construction contracts under the program, The New York Times found that 9 companies, or 16 percent, have strong business and personal ties to white businessmen, casting doubt on their claims to be independently owned and controlled by women or minorities. 
 The inquiry also found widespread bookkeeping discrepancies. The Dinkins administration, which created the program two years ago as one of its major initiatives, claimed to have let more than 30 contracts to women- or minority-owned companies that in fact were never awarded, city records show.
The combination of questionable companies and faulty record-keeping inflated the value of the program, making it appear much more successful than it actually was. In all, the Times analysis found, $87 million in contracts that the Dinkins administration said were awarded in the program's first year -- or one-third of the $270 million total -- either were never really awarded, could not be accounted for by city officials or went to companies whose true management control is in doubt.
Although Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani has left the program's future uncertain, the city's experience provides a kind of cautionary tale. It reflects some of the broad problems found in other cities and states when the social intentions of affirmative action collide with the harsh competitive realities of the construction marketplace.
Programs like New York's do benefit many companies that are truly owned by minorities and women. But several critics said the awarding of large contracts to companies with strong ties to white businessmen ultimately hurts the legitimate women- and minority-owned companies because it denies them city contracts.
Not long after he took office in January, Mr. Giuliani dropped one of the initiative's provisions: a measure allowing a minority- or women-owned company to win a contract even if its bid was up to 10 percent higher than the lowest bid.
But Mr. Giuliani has left intact the rest of the program, including its overall goal of allotting 20 percent of municipal contracts to such companies. Whether Mr. Giuliani and his commissioners will pursue the non-binding goals as aggressively as the Dinkins administration remains to be seen. Senior Giuliani administration officials, after being told in interviews about the findings, said they would consider changes in the city's oversight that might improve bookkeeping and help screen out companies that are not really independently owned by women or minorities.
Some of the arrangements seem to resemble the crude fronts set up by white contractors to win government work in earlier affirmative-action efforts by the Federal and state governments, but there are significant differences.
Twenty years ago, a white contractor might have paid a black or Hispanic worker a small bonus to create a new corporation in the employee's name. The white contractor would then do the actual work, with the employee contributing only his name and ethnic background.
Today, white contractors willing to bend the rules have mastered a more sophisticated art, despite the massive paperwork demanded by the government before the companies can be certified as owned by women or minorities.
Alliances of husbands and wives can sometimes obscure who really controls a company, as can interlocking directorships. And agencies under political pressure to increase participation by women and minorities are sometimes less than aggressive in investigating questionable companies.
In the case of New York City, many of the questionable connections can be found in questionnaires filled out by the companies and maintained in computerized city files. Officials of the lead agency in the affirmative-action effort, the Department of Business Services, acknowledged that they did not even look at those files before deciding to certify the companies as independently owned and operated by women or minorities.
 
Owners
White Men In Background
 Of the nine companies found to have links to white businessmen, three were owned by women who lacked previous experience in construction, but whose husbands had long track records in the field. The husbands were described as employees, and the wives were said to control the companies.
Sometimes the women become quite involved with the companies' operations, often performing the office work while the husbands oversee the field work. But whether such companies are really owned and controlled by the women is questionable, many experts say.
One New York company, the Integral Construction Corporation, described itself as minority-owned, but city records showed that it was also a subsidiary of a company owned and controlled by white men.
Similar links between white businessmen and companies claiming to be owned by minorities or women have been found in many cities and states, including Indianapolis, Chicago, New Orleans and Maryland, said Timothy Bates, the chairman of the graduate program in urban policy analysis at The New School, who has studied affirmative-action programs around the country.
"It was often very blatant, with at least 50 percent of the companies turning out to be fronts," Mr. Bates said.
Other New York companies were found to have complex interlocking directorships in which the small women- or minority-owned companies entered into joint ventures with large white-owned companies whose principals also held equity in the smaller firms.
 
Close Connections
 Among those was Associated Construction Trades Inc., a Bronx-based concern whose principal owner and president is Paul Diaz. The company is involved in a joint venture on two city construction contracts with the S.R.A. Contracting Corporation, a Staten Island company whose principal owner and president is Andrew Greco.
City records show that Mr. Greco holds less than 50 percent of the stock of Mr. Diaz's company, but they do not say exactly how much. Mr. Diaz did not respond to two telephone messages seeking comment.
The city encourages joint ventures, saying the larger white-owned companies help nurture the smaller contractors and provide access to large construction jobs.
There is no rule prohibiting the owner of the larger company in a joint venture from holding equity in the small company. But senior members of the Giuliani administration said they might consider such a rule, because in their view the close financial connection could easily compromise the independence of the women- or minority-owned company.
City officials said they were unaware of most of the companies' links to white businessmen until they were informed recently by reporters. But it is those links that challenge both the owners' claims to independence and the effectiveness of the city's oversight.
"As long as the city is certifying and awarding contracts to bogus companies, it means the status of minority-owned business enterprises and woman-owned business enterprises is for sale," said Kathryn Wylde, the president of the New York City Housing Partnership, a nonprofit organization that works with developers and community groups to build housing in poorer neighborhoods.
The Housing Partnership did not take part in the affirmative-action program. But Ms. Wylde has long pushed for efforts that would nurture minority-owned construction companies.
"Anybody who is trying to really increase the participation of minorities in the industry and in the local economy is frustrated because it's very hard to tell who is straight and who isn't," Ms. Wylde said.
 
Supervision
Who Works For Whom?
 A & G Video Survey Inc. appears to be just the kind of small, fledgling enterprise that Mayor David N. Dinkins had in mind two years ago when he created the program. Since its creation in 1989, the company, owned by Annette Selesky of Farmingville, L.I., had won only three small subcontracts from New York City to inspect sewers and water mains. Then, in the spring of 1993, under the Dinkins initiative, it hit the jackpot: three large contracts worth a total of $1.2 million.
But A & G Video Survey is not all it seems. Mrs. Selesky's only prior professional experience was in a jewelry store. It was her husband, George, now her employee, who had two decades of experience in construction. And when her company won the contracts last year, it did so in a joint venture with a large, well-established Queens construction company that employed Mr. Selesky when A & G Video Survey was created.
The rules of the Department of Business Services say that a woman or minority owner must hold a majority of a company's shares and demonstrate that he or she "has the authority to control and operate independently the day-to-day business decisions." The owner must also have experience and technical competence in the business.
"We're looking for independence, that the minority is his own manager, not having to check with someone else," said Mary Colon, the director of the affirmative-action program at the Department of Business Services. "Independence is the key."
 
No Previous Experience
 Yet the department certified A & G Video Survey as a woman-owned enterprise in October 1992, despite the information that Annette Selesky provided to the department about her own work history and her husband's.
The resume she submitted showed that she had no previous experience in her company's specialty: videotaping and photographing sewers and water mains. Before creating her company in 1989, she had worked for 10 years in a Suffolk County jewelry store, becoming a "certified diamondtologist" and rising to assistant manager.
It was her husband, George, who gave her the idea of creating A & G Video Survey, Mrs. Selesky said. "He came home and explained it to me, and it just sounded intriguing," she said in an interview. "It sounded like something I could do."
It was also something that Mr. Selesky could do, and had done. By 1989, he had worked for 17 years as a construction superintendent for 10 different contractors, developing expertise in surveying and sewers, according to his resume. When A & G Video Survey was incorporated, Mr. Selesky was working for A.F.C. Enterprises, a large contractor based in Queens. He was in charge of coordinating all of the company's surveying.
Mr. Selesky has been a full-time employee of A & G Video Survey since its inception, supervising construction in the field, Mrs. Selesky said. She concentrates on bidding, insurance and other paperwork.
"He works for me," she said.
City officials apparently did not look into whether this arrangement entitled Mrs. Selesky to claim that she independently owns and operates A & G Video Survey. Nor, officials acknowledge, did they look at whether the company's current relationship with Mr. Selesky's old employer, A.F.C. Enterprises, affects her contention that she has sole control over her company.
The three large contracts that A & G Video Survey won last year from the city's Department of Environmental Protection, worth a total of $1.2 million, involve rebuilding water mains and sewers in Queens and renovating a water-supply aqueduct in Westchester. The company won the contracts in a joint venture with A.F.C. Enterprises, which was awarded $101 million in city contracts on its own during the last four years.
 
Partners
Intricate Links Raise Questions
 Except for A & G Video Survey, the nine companies found to have close links to white businessmen entered the city's program through a special fast-track procedure.
The Dinkins administration, which had long promised to create an affirmative-action program, was in a great hurry to get it started. The first two years of Mr. Dinkins's tenure had been taken up in meeting requirements set by the United States Supreme Court in a case involving affirmative action in Richmond.
Trying to speed up the start of the program in the last five months of 1992, the Department of Business Services created an expedited process: if a company had been previously certified as being owned by women or minorities by New York State, the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey or other government agencies, it merely had to provide the city with a copy of the previous certification and sign an affidavit.
The Integral Construction Corporation was certified this way. Formed 13 years ago, Integral Construction is a large company that has won many contracts with city and state agencies. It received a $6.5 million contract last year from the Department of General Services to renovate 12 station houses for the Police Department.
Integral qualifies as a minority-owned concern because at least 51 percent of the company is owned and controlled by Charles Uribe, who is of Hispanic origin, according to a city questionnaire filled out by the company. Mr. Uribe is a well-known figure in New York's construction industry and within the Democratic Party; he was a leading fund-raiser for Mr. Dinkins and for Mayor Edward I. Koch.
But in the questionnaire, along with other documents, Integral Construction is described as a subsidiary of another construction company, a large, established company called the A. J. Contracting Company. A. J. Contracting, which says it is owned primarily by white men, controls Integral Construction, according to questionnaires filed by each of the companies. The two organizations share office space and equipment at 470 Park Avenue South.
 
Problem Question of Independence
 They also have the same shareholders, according to the questionnaires. Mr. Uribe's partners in Integral Construction are Jerome A. Gannon, Kenneth D. Smith and John T. Pello. All four men are also listed as the principal owners of A. J. Contracting. Because A. J. Contracting describes itself as controlled by white men, Mr. Uribe holds less than 50 percent of the stock. Mr. Uribe said he is the chairman of both companies, as well as their chief operating officer.
The interlocking relationship suggests that Integral Construction would have a hard time meeting the test of independent ownership and operation.
When Ms. Colon, the director of the affirmative-action program, was told about the relationship of the companies, she said the links raised serious questions. "We'd definitely like to know the connections," Ms. Colon said.
Mr. Uribe contended that the information each of the companies had filed with the Office of Contracts was simply inaccurate. "They are separate corporations," Mr. Uribe said in an interview. "And I'm in the process of becoming a majority owner of A. J."
"It should say that it's not a subsidiary," Mr. Uribe said of the questionnaires, which were compiled by the vice presidents of Integral Construction and A. J. Contracting. "They are two independent companies."
"I have never gone after a job because I'm a minority," he added. "I go after them because I do good work."
 
City Hall
Priority Program Loses Its Patron
 The affirmative-action effort was a centerpiece of Mr. Dinkins's four-year term and a significant issue in the mayoral campaign last fall. Mr. Dinkins frequently cited it as a major achievement, saying that in a single year he had nearly doubled, to 17 percent from 9 percent, the portion of city contracts awarded to companies owned by minorities and women. Mr. Giuliani, his challenger, called the program "bad social policy" and said he would eliminate it, although since taking office he has stopped short of doing so.
After being told of the findings by The Times, Rudy Washington, who took over the program in January after he was appointed Commissioner of Business Services by Mr. Giuliani, said he had asked the Department of Investigation to look into the questionable companies.
 
'Paucity of Contracts'
 "I'd like to see it stopped," he said. "If someone set up a sham operation and they defrauded the city, I have no compunction in seeking indictments."
In an interview, Mr. Dinkins said it was not surprising that the start of such a large, ambitious contracting program would have some problems, and he said better record-keeping and oversight might be needed. But any problems should not obscure the overall value of the program or the long history of discrimination that it was meant to reverse, he said.
"We know historically that there is discrimination against women, against minorities," Mr. Dinkins said. "They got a paucity of the contracts over a long period of time."
"This does not mean that I am in favor of sham corporations or misbehavior of any kind," he said.
NEXT: Discrepancies in official claims about the program's achievements.
 
 
How the Inquiry Was Conducted
 A comprehensive list produced by the Department of Business Services shows that 3,817 contracts, worth $235 million, were awarded to 568 companies owned by women or minorities during the program's first year, the fiscal year that ran from July 1992 through June 1993.
Another $35 million in subcontracts were supposed to have been awarded under the program, but the department said it was unable to identify them.
Most of the contracts that the city was able to identify were small, involving the purchase of supplies and services. The Times studied 135 relatively large contracts awarded by various city agencies to 56 companies for construction, a field long dominated by companies owned by white men.
The Times researched the contracts by requesting data from a dozen city agencies through a legally enforceable request under the Freedom of Information Act. Some agencies took as much as three months to respond to the requests.
The examination found that $34 million in contracts listed by the Department of Business Services were not awarded or have been canceled since they were awarded last year. It also determined, by using other city records, that 9 of the 56 corporations have close connections with white businessmen. The companies received three of the five largest contracts awarded under the program, and they were awarded a total of $18 million in contracts.

HEADLINE: Haitian Dissident Tells Of Rape by Police Agents

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, June 24

 BODY:
Nearly a month after four armed police agents raped her because of her political activities, Rose Margui Lormil's biggest problem is that the scars she carries from the experience are not visible.
Miss Lormil, 24, is a member of Comme Il Faut, or As It Should Be, a grass-roots organization whose loyalists have been hunted by the Haitian military since the army seized power in a coup in September 1991. 
 She shudders as she recounts the rape and the weeks of hiding and terror that followed.
 
'No Other Choice for Me'
 "I must get out of my country now," said Miss Lormil, looking frail in a red polka-dot dress, one of the few pieces of clothing she managed to take with her when she fled. "There is no other choice for me."
But her safe conduct out of the country is anything but certain, like the fate of thousands of her compatriots before her in the 33 months since President Jean-Bertrand Aristide fled into exile.
With no police report of the rape, nor a card showing membership in any of Haiti's hundreds of grass-roots political groups, she must prove to the satisfaction of American immigration officials that she has a credible fear of political persecution.
Though Miss Lormil's case will soon be decided by American officials here in Haiti, the challenge she faces is little different from that of dozens of boat people who flee this country each week only to be intercepted by the United States Coast Guard.
Under a new policy of the Clinton Administration, Haitians who are stopped at sea while fleeing by boat are transferred to an American-leased ship off Jamaica for consideration of their pleas for asylum in the United States.
Whether they are interviewed here or at sea, Haitian asylum seekers must win over heavily burdened immigration agents who have to make snap decisions on the credibility of their claims.
 
Subjective Judgments
 Judgments like these often determine the fate of an individual, but they remain highly subjective in a society where political terror is rampant and documentation is hard to come by.
Miss Lormil's story seems typical to anyone who has spoken with large numbers of Haitian refugees. The abundance of such stories has fueled unending tensions between the Administration, which is seeking to contain a wave of emigration, and advocates for the refugees, who say those who flee have never been treated justly.
In a meeting in the home of foreigners who have helped shelter them, Miss Lormil and her boyfriend, Bouz Bellerice, 24, told how they were followed during a stroll near the downtown area of Port-au-Prince.
Mr. Bellerice is also a member of Comme Il Faut, which sprang up in the coastal town of Grande-Goave in 1986 during the demonstrations that led to the fall of the Duvalier family dictatorship. Fearing arrest, Mr. Bellerice said he tried to hide political documents, some of which bore Father Aristide's picture, before the men shadowing them approached.
When the four men drew near in a public park, Miss Lormil and Mr. Bellerice said, an aggressive round of questioning began, and within moments the men began beating Mr. Bellerice, forcing him to the ground while they hit him with sticks.
With dusk falling, the two said, one of their attackers raped Miss Lormil in the park. Mr. Bellerice said the men warned him that if he protested, the two would be killed.
 
Taken to a Courtyard
 Miss Lormil said she was taken to the ethnology department at the University of Haiti and raped in a courtyard there by each of the men over the next two hours.
"They were carrying .45's," Miss Lormil said. "At that point I hoped they would kill me." When the men finished attacking her, she said, they forced her to give them her address and then left her in the courtyard.
Mr. Bellerice and Miss Lormil met at their house later that evening but have not gone back since, for fear that the men might return. Almost immediately, Miss Lormil applied for political asylum at a downtown office set up by the American Embassy to receive such claims. Her interview with an immigration official is expected in the next few days, but she has been given no indication of when a decision will be made.
Mr. Bellerice, an unemployed auto mechanic apprentice, said he was not seeking asylum abroad because he wanted to remain in Haiti to work for political change.
Miss Lormil said she had sought medical help in the days after she was raped, but had not thought to get a copy of the medical report and could not recall the name of the doctor. Diplomats say details like those are often deciding factors in whether or not a Haitian will be granted refuge in the United States.
What identifies the attack by the four men as political, the two say, are the insults hurled at them as Aristide supporters during the incident, and a string of threatening telephone calls in the days before the rape.
As they shift hiding places, the two find themselves in the same situation as most Haitians who have fled this country. "All we have is our story," Mr. Bellerice said.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Hundreds of Refugees Held
 MIAMI, June 25 -- More than 300 Haitians were intercepted at sea today, bringing the two-day total of refugees found in international waters by the United States Coast Guard to more than 500.
Coast Guard officials in Miami said that the Haitians had been rescued from eight badly overloaded wooden boats and were being taken to Jamaica where immigration officials would determine which of them would be eligible for political asylum in the United States.

HEADLINE: Taiwan Pushes to Rebuild Its Position in Global Community

BYLINE: By EDWARD A. GARGAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TAIPEI, Taiwan, June 25

 BODY:
After years of international isolation, including expulsion from the United Nations and the loss of diplomatic recognition by all but a handful of countries, Taiwan has embarked on an aggressive diplomatic campaign to re-establish itself as a legitimate member of the global community, a campaign that has enraged China.
For the past six months, Taiwan's President, Lee Teng-hui, has engaged in what is called "vacation diplomacy" across southeast Asia, arranging golf outings to coincide with meetings with regional leaders. Mr. Lee has also visited Costa Rica and Nicaragua and appeared in Pretoria last month for the inauguration of Nelson Mandela as President of South Africa, one of the countries that still recognizes this island's sovereignty. 
 Taiwan has begun to sound out countries in Europe, Asia and the Western Hemisphere on whether they would support the country's readmission to the United Nations. Taiwan's Prime Minister, Lien Chan, has proclaimed this as one of Taiwan's central diplomatic goals.
 
'We Have to Break Through'
 "We call on the international community to recognize that the Republic of China exists," said Mr. Lee, upon returning from his trip to South Africa. "We have to break through the mainland's diplomatic blockade and gain a seat in international society."
In promoting its claims for renewed international recognition, Taiwan has trumpeted its democratic politics, its free markets and its growing economic influence, fueled by Taiwan's substantial investments in the region. Mr. Lee and other senior Taiwanese officials have also repeatedly pointed out that this island of 21 million people is the world's 12th largest trader and has the 20th largest economy, with a gross national product of $220 billion.
China has consistently opposed Taiwan's efforts to rejoin the United Nations, or indeed any effort by Taiwan to raise its international stature, since it regards such moves as efforts to assert Taiwan's independence. China formally considers Taiwan to be one of its provinces and requires all countries recognizing Beijing to declare that Taiwan remains an integral part of China.
But Mr. Lee's diplomatic offensive coincides with a growing sense of distance from China among the Taiwanese, not merely because of the political hostility, but because of a progressive cultural alienation from a country that is increasingly seen as brutal, lawless, corrupt and irrelevant to Taiwan's future.
 
'Nothing to Do With Our Lives'
 "People are alienated talking about China," said Parris H. Chang, an opposition member of Taiwan's legislature. "It has nothing to do with our lives. I am very pleased to see Lee Teng-hui going abroad. He's doing exactly what we want to do."
Jason C. Hu, the Government's senior spokesman, said: "Because we are well developed economically and are democratic, we believe we should be received more warmly internationally."
"We are an independent sovereignty at the moment," he said. "There is no one above us. But all this is being done under a one-China policy. We say our reinvolvement in the U.N. -- I don't say readmission -- would provide a better way to interact with the people of the mainland. We are realistic enough to see that this may not happen in the short term. But we have to be pragmatic. We are two political entities."
These moves, however, have infuriated China, which sees Mr. Lee as moving toward promoting Taiwan's formal independence, a status that Beijing has warned it would stop through armed intervention, if necessary. In a recent, harshly worded commentary in the official Beijing newspaper, the People's Daily, China accused Mr. Lee of betraying Taiwan's links to China.
"How can Lee Teng-hui kowtow like this to foreign leaders?" the Beijing paper demanded. "Where has Chinese feeling gone? Lee does not understand Chinese feeling and does not know that problems among the Chinese are solved internally and not with foreigners. The leaders of mainland China are calling for dialogue with the government in Taiwan, but Lee prefers to talk to foreigners."
Taiwan no longer talks about reasserting sovereignty over the mainland. But Mr. Hu, the government spokesman, said that "we don't rule out unification," a possibility he linked to China becoming a free and democratic country. He conceded that "no one is holding their breath" about that, however.
Not only China has been cool to Taiwan's sudden offensive. Last month, when Mr. Lee was flying to Central America, the United States refused to permit him to stay overnight in Hawaii, where his plane had stopped for refueling.
 
Facing 'the Realities'
 But several official foreign observers here contend that 45 years after the Communists seized control of China, banishing the Nationalists under Chiang Kai-shek to Taiwan, the links between China and Taiwan have ruptured forever.
"The whole question of national identity is in many ways a core issue here," said an Asian observer. "It it coming into discussions more and more. The realities are that Taiwan is large, does exist and is a force to be reckoned with in economic terms. Now they feel they should be given some international space. It's an emotional, psychological thing. They feel inferior. They feel they are treated as pariahs."
Taiwan's search for international respectability will not be easy. Entry into the United Nations is regarded as unlikely by foreign diplomats.
The chief obstacle to Taiwan's enhanced stature, many foreign observers as well as Taiwanese contend, is the insistence by the United States that Taiwan is an inseparable part of China, and that any move by Washington to elevate relations with Taipei would endanger relations with Beijing.
Still, both the Government and the opposition are convinced that the island's future can only be assured by establishing the principle of independent sovereignty. "You will find," said Mr. Chang, the opposition legislator, "that this pace of getting Taiwan recognized as a separate political entity will accelerate in the next two years."

GRAPHIC: Photo: President Lee Teng-hui of Taiwan, seated among his bodyguards, smiling in a legislative session last month as lawmakers hurled insults at one another. He is working to raise his country's global status. (Reuters)

HEADLINE: Texas and California: 2 Views of Illegal Aliens

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: EL PASO

 BODY:
In California, where sentiment against illegal aliens runs strong, Senator Barbara Boxer's proposal to use the National Guard to patrol the Mexican border was considered rather modest. But in Texas, when Jim Mattox, a Democratic candidate for the Senate, suggested this spring that the military be used to crack down on illegal immigration, his opponent jumped all over him.
Mr. Mattox had been the early favorite in the Democratic field, but he lost in the April runoff election to Richard Fisher, who said: "South Texans don't want tanks on their lawns." Mr. Fisher carried El Paso County by a margin of nearly 3 to 2, swept practically every other border county and suffered no backlash elsewhere. 
 While the campaign was about more than immigration, it served as a potent reminder that attitudes about immigration and its political ramifications are by no means monolithic in the two most populous states along the Mexican border.
Immigration is much less of a hot-button topic in Texas than in California. The reasons, many immigration and political experts say, include the relative strength of the two states' economies as well as differences in geography and history. And with the Governors of California and Texas up for re-election this fall, there are striking differences in how each is dealing with the immigration issue.
 
'Save Our State' Initiative
 In California, Gov. Pete Wilson, a Republican, has made illegal immigration a centerpiece of his campaign, proposing an amendment to the Constitution to deny citizenship to those born on American soil. He recently announced that he favored an initiative on the November ballot that would deny schooling and nonemergency medical care to illegal aliens.
In Texas, Gov. Ann W. Richards, a Democrat, has made no such proposals and does not talk at length about illegal immigration, focusing more on business ties with Mexico.
Mexico's Deputy Foreign Minister, Andres Rozental, recently praised Ms. Richards for promoting trade. "She comes to Mexico," Mr. Rozental said in an interview with The San Diego Union-Tribune, Mr. Wilson's hometown newspaper. "We have a dialogue with her. We are able to discuss issues. And we have none of that with Mr. Wilson."
Even when Governor Richards decided recently to file a Texas version of California's lawsuit to try to win Federal reimbursement for the costs of illegal immigration, she went out of her way to portray the matter as a budget dispute with Washington, not an attack on illegal aliens.
Governor Richards was notably absent last week when Mr. Wilson and the Governors of Florida and Arizona, which are also suing over the costs of illegal immigration, went to Washington to complain in person. A spokeswoman said she had a prior speaking commitment in Texas.
To be sure, plenty of Texans complain about illegal immigration. And few politicians here would go as far as Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani of New York City or Gov. Mario M. Cuomo of New York, both of whom have recently made highly favorable comments about illegal aliens.
El Paso is the site of perhaps the most draconian effort to deter illegal crossings along the Mexican border. A 20-mile wall of Border Patrol agents along the Rio Grande has reduced the number of attempted illegal crossings by up to 75 percent.
The operation has much support in El Paso, a city that is often said to be joined at the hip with its larger Mexican neighbor, Ciudad Juarez. Some people here blame illegal aliens for rising crime and resent them for showing up destitute.
 
Cutting Into Business
 But many local leaders and business owners openly fret about economic consequences.
Sylvia Soliz, who owns a small store on South El Paso Street that sells shoes and T-shirts, said her business had plummeted by almost 50 percent since the border operation began.
El Paso's Mayor, Larry Francis, said he preferred the border operation to such radical steps as an amendment to the Constitution or a ban on schooling and health care.
Many people in El Paso, which has a population of 515,000 and is 70 percent Hispanic, support a balanced approach to illegal immigration.
"We should deal with it," said Joe Carrizales Jr., a jewelry store manager who moved here legally with his parents from Juarez 20 years ago. "But it's not that drastic a problem. And with a lot of politicians, it comes out in a real prejudicial way. It's like: 'Let's go after the Hispanic community. Let's get the troops after them.' "
Experts say the healthier economy in Texas and the less generous -- and less expensive -- social services here help explain why illegal immigration is seen here as a less serious problem, compared with California.
"It is just part of the American psyche," said Arturo Vargas, vice president of the Mexican American Legal Defense and Educational Fund, "that in poor economic times, there is a scapegoat to be sought after."
Texas also has fewer illegal aliens.. It estimates that 3 percent of its population is illegal, compared with a 7 percent estimate by California. The Federal Immigration and Naturalization Service insists that the actual figures are lower in both cases.
While both states have a roughly equal percentage of Hispanic residents, about 25 percent, Texas has a much lower percentage of foreign-born residents and a long-established Mexican-American political presence in many border cities. Running for a Congressional seat in South Texas in 1902, John Nance Garner, who went on to become Vice President under Franklin D. Roosevelt, assiduously courted voters along the Rio Grande.
"Along a good deal of the border, 8 out of 10 of those spoke only Spanish," Mr. Garner's biographer, Marquis James, wrote in 1939. "John had learned enough Spanish to pass the time of day with his Latin constituents, and he could cuss very satisfactorily in that tongue."
 
Editorial Criticism
 The border cities are hundreds of miles away from Texas's biggest cities, Houston and Dallas. But in California, the main border crossing point is just a few miles from downtown San Diego.
A recent editorial in The Los Angeles Times criticized Governor Wilson for what it said was the "clumsy and hostile way in which his administration has dealt with Mexico and Mexicans." It also unfavorably compared Governor Wilson with Governor Richards: "When Wilson goes to the border, he usually stays on the U.S. side and campaigns for votes. When Richards goes, she crosses over into Mexico and invites constructive engagement."
But Leslie A. Goodman, a Wilson spokeswoman, said the criticism was "dishonest" and unfair because Mr. Wilson was a champion of the North American Free Trade Agreement. Ms. Goodman said the Governor was trying to address a budget crisis with his immigration proposals, not provoke a fight with Mexico, and she pointed to recent favorable comments about Mr. Wilson by Ernesto Ruffo Appel, the Governor of Baja California, California's neighboring Mexican province.
"I know him, and I know he is a positive man," Governor Appel told border governors at a conference a few weeks ago. "I know he likes Mexicans. I understand very much the problems my colleague Pete is suffering in California."

HEADLINE: Japanese Premier's Fall Forces Shift by the U.S. at Trade Talks

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, June 25

 BODY:
Just when it seemed that a year of tortuous trade negotiations with Japan was about to bear fruit, the resignation of Prime Minister Tsutomu Hata today is likely to leave the United States waiting once again in frustration, unable to reach market-opening agreements before a summit meeting next month of the seven leading industrialized nations.
The political turmoil arising from Mr. Hata's resignation could divert Japan's attention from the trade talks just as negotiators are set to enter the final, critical week that will determine whether the two nations can forge agreements before the meeting in Naples to increase sales in Japan of foreign insurance policies, automobiles, telecommunications and medical equipment. 
 The setback is forcing an uncomfortable change in the Clinton's Administration's position. Washington's best hope now for a speedy agreement is to count on Japan's bureaucrats, whom it once derided as being the recalcitrant gatekeepers of Japan's economy.
The Clinton Administration had been counting on political reformers, represented by Mr. Hata and his predecessor, Morihiro Hosokawa, to open Japan's markets by forcing their will on the reluctant bureaucrats.
But Japan is about to get its fourth Prime Minister since the so-called "trade framework" agreement setting ground rules for the negotiations was signed a little less than a year ago. And it now seems that there will not be a strong political leader in Japan for months, if not years, until a stable new political system evolves to replace nearly four decades of one-party rule by the Liberal Democratic Party.
It is possible that the vacancy in the Prime Minister's office can be filled next week and even that Mr. Hata will be re-elected, meaning there will be little loss of momentum in economic policies. But whoever takes the office is likely to be hamstrung by the same coalition politics that weakened Mr. Hata and Mr. Hosokawa. And the new Prime Minister is likely to remain in office only until the next general election, probably at the end of the year.
"There aren't too many viable politicians around," said a United States Government official. He said officials were hopeful that Japan's ministries would have enough authority and leeway to negotiate an agreement on their own and that it was now just a matter of ironing out details.
But that could be wishful thinking. Eventually, politicians are called in to resolve difficult questions -- to make a "political decision," in the parlance of negotiators.
"If there is a political vacuum for some time, I think the talks will be halted," a Japanese trade official said today.
 
Avoiding a 'Political Vacuum'
 After serving only two months as Prime Minister, Mr. Hata announced his resignation today just before the Parliament was scheduled to vote on a no-confidence motion that would almost certainly have ejected him from office. He said resigning would avert the "political vacuum" that would arise if he instead called for new elections -- his other option had he lost the no-confidence vote.
But many political analysts think the struggle among the various parties to form a new governing coalition will leave a vacuum nevertheless, just as Japan is facing economic decisions.
In addition to negotiating agreements on specific trade sectors, Japan is supposed to unveil a new package of measures by the end of the month to deregulate its economy, cut income taxes and increase long-term public spending, all measures sought by the United States.
This package could be left incomplete because of the political confusion. And if a new party comes into power, it could conceivably throw all the plans of the old regime out the window.
 
Parts of Plan Opposed
 The Socialists oppose parts of the tax reform plan that is advocated by Mr. Hata's ruling coalition. The Liberal Democratic Party might ease up on deregulation to protect traditional constituents like farmers, small-shop keepers and construction companies.
By increasing the probability that the trade talks will stall, the political confusion could lead to a rise in the Japanese yen, which is already pressing up against the psychological barrier of 100 to the dollar.
Political confusion could also delay Japan's nascent economic recovery. Growth in the economy would allow Japan to buy more imports and would have the greatest effect in the short run of lessening the country's towering trade surplus.
Still, some experts said that no matter who takes power, Japan will gradually move in the direction of deregulation, opening its markets and increasing spending on public works. This is a consensus society, and the consensus is that such measures are needed, not to appease the United States but to make sure Japan's economy remains as vibrant as it has been in the past.
"They have no choice but to follow the lines that have been laid by the Hata administration," said Takeshi Kondo, general manager of the office of political and economic research at Itochu Corporation, a major trading company. "There are no viable political options."
 
No Deadlines, U.S. Says
 To some extent, Washington has already laid the groundwork for playing down any disappointment at the summit meeting in Naples, which will run from July 8-10. It has said that there are no deadlines for the trade negotiations and that it is willing to accept agreements in individual sectors rather than all or nothing. In February when the trade talks broke down at the summit meeting between President Clinton and Mr. Hosokawa, Washington had rejected piecemeal agreements.
Despite such statements, both nations have a strong interest in producing some agreement at the summit meeting. Both want to stop the battering of the dollar in world currency markets, which threatens to accelerate inflation in the United States and imperil the budding economic rebound in Japan. Japan's huge trade surplus is a major reason the dollar is weak against the yen, so any setback in trade talks could send the yen higher.
Further delays risk undermining the credibility of the trade strategy toward Japan, which was supposed to yield measurable results in opening Japan's markets. So far, it has not even yielded measurable results in forging agreements.
For the next few days, Japanese political involvement in the trade talks will not be needed. The matter will be in the hands of the bureaucrats, who are hammering away at the nitty-gritty of changing Government bidding procedures and measuring access to Japan's auto market.
United States officials say that if agreements are to be reached before the Naples meeting, they should be concluded by next weekend. Otherwise, the talks might be postponed until after the meeting.
The Administration wants to avoid last-minute negotiations at the Naples meeting itself because they could lead to an embarrassing disappointment, like the letdown in February when trade talks collapsed at a meeting between Mr. Hosokawa and President Clinton.
Negotiators say that an agreement on insurance has the best chance of being concluded in time for the summit meeting. Talks on Japanese Government procurement of telecommunications and medical equipment, which once seemed to be moving smoothly, have hit some roadblocks recently, though it is not out of the question that an agreement will be reached before the Naples meeting. An accord on automobile and auto parts, which account for the bulk of Japan's trade surplus with the United States, is not expected.
 
Slow Progress on Deregulation
 On deregulation of the economy, another goal sought by the United States, Japan announced an initial plan in March but left some key points unresolved, including a permanent income tax cut to spur consumer spending, an increase in public works spending to help stimulate the economy and draw in imports, and concrete details on deregulation.
Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, dismissed the March plan as "half-finished work" that was "of limited substance."
The new deregulation plan is expected to be announced as scheduled. While it is stronger and more comprehensive than the March plan, it might still disappoint Washington. The problem is not Mr. Hata's resignation, but his inattention to the issue over the last two months.
"If we had had a stronger Cabinet, we would have had a much stronger deregulation package," said a bureaucrat who worked on the plan.
The tax cut might be left for the next administration since tax reform was one of the issues that kept the Socialists from rejoining Mr. Hata's coalition and averting his resignation as Prime Minister.

The change in the Government in Tokyo is likely to cause a setback in United States trade negotiations with Japan at a final critical phase. (Associated Press) (pg. 8)

HEADLINE: 'Pangs of Conscience' as Man Admits Raping Girl

BYLINE: By NORIMITSU ONISHI 

 BODY:
A man walked into a police station and turned himself in early yesterday in the rape of an 11-year-old Queens girl, the police said.
With police sketches of the rapist plastered throughout Queens Village and other sections of the borough, the man, Creston L. Harry, 25, went to the 112th Precinct station house shortly after midnight and gave statements implicating himself in the June 1 assault, the police said. 
 Mr. Harry lives at 99-30 212th Street, a few blocks from the site where the girl was raped, the police said.
"He had pangs of conscience," said Capt. Robert Cea of the Queens Detective Bureau at a news conference late yesterday afternoon. "He went to a friend and told him that this bothered him tremendously."
 
Police Weren't Close
 "After a conference with friends who wished to stay anonymous, he decided to go to the police," the captain said, adding that the police had not been close to making an arrest.
Captain Cea said the suspect, whowas unemployed and had worked previously in a hospital cafeteria, was a Canadian citizen who immigrated here in 1983.
On June 1, around 2:30 P.M., the girl was walking home from Saints Joachim and Anne School on Hollis Avenue when a man accosted her near 214th Street, threatened her with a knife and forced her to go behind an abandoned house, the police said. There, in a backyard overgrown with shrubbery, the man assaulted her, then took her to a nearby lot and assaulted her again, the police said.
After he released her, the girl staggered home and was later treated and released from Mary Immaculate Hospital.
Mr. Harry was charged with rape, sodomy, sexual abuse and unlawful imprisonment, said Sgt. Anthony Barlanti, a police spokesman.

HEADLINE: UConn Seeks 2% Higher Tuition

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WEST HARTFORD, Conn., June 25

 BODY:
University of Connecticut leaders have proposed a 2 percent tuition increase, the smallest in 13 years.
If approved by trustees, most students would pay slightly less than $10,000 a year to attend the university in fall 1995. 
 University administrators proposed raising tuition for full-time, in-state undergraduates by $76 in 1995-96, from $3,824 to $3,900.
With room, board and most other fees set to rise 2 percent as well, in-state undergraduates living on the Storrs campus would pay $9,980 a year to attend the university in 1995-96, up from $9,784 this fall. Six straight years of double-digit increases have made the university the eighth most expensive public university in the country in some surveys.
President Harry J. Hartley said it was important to keep the cost below $10,000 a year.
The university trustees' budget and finance committee unanimously approved the tuition increase on Friday. The entire board will vote on it on July 15. The committee also approved general fund budgets of $168.2 million for 1995-96 and $169.5 million for 1996-97, which still must be approved by the Legislature.
The second year of that budget carries a tentative tuition increase of 3.5 percent, or $136.
A year ago, the university's trustees had pledged to try to keep the tuition increase below the rate of inflation. This month, some trustees, including the board chairman, Lewis B. Rome, had been pushing for no increase, out of concern that Connecticut residents were being priced out of their state university. But Mr. Rome said he would not oppose the 2 percent increase.

HEADLINE: BASEBALL: NOTEBOOK;
The Owners Are of One Mind, So They Use the Same Words

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Members of the Cincinnati Reds received a letter from their owner last week. "Love, Marge Schott," she signed it. Players on the Oakland Athletics' roster received a letter from the chairman of their team. "Very truly yours, Wally Haas," he signed it. The owner of the Florida Marlins wrote to his players, too, but opted to be more informal. "Sincerely, Wayne," he signed his letter, omitting his last name, Huizenga.
To say that those owners and the other 25 wrote to their players would not be literally correct. They sent their players letters with their signatures, but they didn't write the letters. That is, they didn't write them unless they all had the same exact thought to write the same exact words. 
 The form of the letters resembled the mass letters the White House and members of Congress receive from groups lobbying for action by the President or enactment of a particular bill. In this instance, the form letter was provided by the Player Relations Committee. Addressed to each player individually by his first name, the letters began:
"Enclosed is a memorandum from Dick Ravitch, the Owners' Chief Negotiator, summarizing the economic proposal we made to your union on June 14. Because I believe that the current round of bargaining is crucial to both sides, I have decided to communicate to you directly the terms of the proposal we gave to the Union."
The letter goes on to say that "communications can be difficult" because owners and players are "spread across the country." Then each owner purports to say, though he or she really is being less than forthright, "For instance, I have read statements indicating that, under our proposal, four- and five-year Players would have less bargaining leverage than they do today."
Did the owners themselves actually read those statements? Probably only if their labor negotiators included the statements in a package with the form letter they were told to send to their players under their names.
The letters are so identical that in the third paragraph of each, the word "each" is underlined.
"It is my hope," each owner said in conclusion (and collusion?), "that you will stay fully informed on the important matters at issue in these labor negotiations so that you will be able to make decisions that are fair to you but also in the best interests of baseball. I will keep you posted on the progress of the negotiations."
As the clinching gimmick on these letters, the alleged authors had their secretaries type their initials as the person who wrote or dictated the letter. Except HWH and WJH didn't write their letters. The initials should have been RR or CPO or RM or LM or JW, whichever management labor executive or lawyer wrote it.
Last week's correspondence might have been the first sent directly from management to players since Peter Ueberroth, then the commissioner, bypassed the Players Association, which had rebuffed him, and wrote to the players in 1985 appealing to them to accept a drug-testing program. They didn't.
 
That Curious Salary Cap
 What the owners didn't tell the players in their letters last week was some of the more curious details of their salary cap proposal.
For example, in the three-year transition from this year's payrolls to a fully implemented cap in 1998, clubs that are over the cap would have to reduce the excess each year -- by 25 percent, by 50 percent, by 75 percent. But if a club is under the minimum payroll, it would not have to narrow by similar ratios the distance it sits from that minimum.
Instead, the proposal would allow clubs to reduce their payrolls even further. In 1995, clubs could have payrolls totaling only 25 percent of the minimum; in 1996, 50 percent of the minimum, and in 1997, 75 percent.
The Padres currently have a $13 million payroll. Add other player costs, and the total would be more than $15 million. But that still would be well under the minimum that would be established for next season. If that figure were $30 million, the Padres would need to have a 1995 payroll of only $7.5 million, which means salaries would be even less.
In another provision of the proposal, any club caught trying to circumvent the salary cap or hiding any part of a contract with a player could be fined $1 million. The proposal, however, does not include a provision that would impose a fine on any team or the owners as a group if they were caught hiding revenue so that the players' share would, in reality, be less than the proposed 50 percent.
The unions in the National Basketball Association and the National Football League have discovered revenue those leagues did not include in the pool to be shared with the players.
Maybe these details will be in the next friendly letters the owners write.
 
More Striking to Others
 The threat of a strike hangs over the season, but its shadow appears more ominous for some teams and players than others.
The Cleveland Indians, for example, have built a surprisingly healthy lead in the American League Central Division. They could be on the verge of winning their first division championship ever and be in position to win their first pennant in 40 years, but what happens if the players strike and the two sides fail to reach an agreement in time to salvage the rest of the season and the post-season?
The Yankees are in first place in the A.L. East. They haven't won a divison title since 1981, and George Steinbrenner is reminded frequently that this has been the longest stretch the Yankees have gone without a first-place finish since they started finishing first in 1921.
The Texas Rangers have neverwon a division championship, and they lead the A.L. West. Of course, they lead it with a record well under .500 -- five games before last night's game. That's one area where a strike might benefit baseball. It would spare it the embarrassment of having a losing team compete for a spot in the World Series.
On an individual basis, Ken Griffey Jr. has the most to lose from a work stoppage of any length because he has propelled himself into position to chase Roger Maris's home run record.
But one individual could benefit from an abbreviated season. While Paul O'Neill has attracted widespread attention for his batting average of over .400 -- which now has shrunk to under .400 -- the hitter to watch is Tony Gwynn of the San Diego Padres.
Gwynn has not been at .400 since May 15, but he took a .384 average into last night's game, and he's the best pure hitter in baseball. He is capable of staying in the high .300's, then spurting onto .400 and beyond. If he should follow that pattern and reach the elusive plateau just before a strike, a season-ending stoppage could give the major leagues their first .400 hitter in 53 years.
Would Gwynn's feat be tainted? Certainly, but his average for the 1994 season would start with a 4 forevermore.
 
Who Would Figure Colorado?
 With divisional realignment, the popular preseason view went, all the San Francisco Giants had to do to win the National League West was show up. Nearly halfway through the season, it's not so popular anymore.
"I'm one of the few who didn't buy that argument," Bob Quinn, the Giants' general manager, said. "First, the Dodgers won 81 games last year. I figured they'd win more than 81 this season. Then there's Colorado."
The Rockies? A team in its second season has a chance to challenge the Giants, who won 103 games last season?
"Don Baylor and Larry Bearnarth deserve credit," Quinn said. "The Rockies don't have Ellis Burks, and Howard Johnson isn't producing. But they've been getting great seasons out of their role players. I feared them because Bearnarth has done a great job with the pitching staff. You get visiting staffs coming in fearful of throwing the ball over the plate there. But their pitchers have done a tremendous job."
The Giants, on the other hand, continue to struggle.
"The team went from having the best batting average in the league to where we're last, not by a little bit but a considerable number," Quinn said, referring to their drop from .276 to .240. "We're also last in driving in runners in scoring position."
The Giants have suffered an assortment of serious injuries, but Quinn said: "Injuries are for alibi guys. You have to play through them."
Perhaps the departure of Will Clark as a free agent has had more of an effect on the Giants than his 1993 performance would indicate.
"I don't think that's an issue here at all," Quinn said. Some of the players, however, think differently, though they won't discuss it publicly.
The drastic drop in offense prompted the Giants to sign Darryl Strawberry. The club expects him to be ready to play just before or after the All-Star break. Preparing for his comeback, Strawberry works out at an undisclosed location accompanied by two baseball people, a conditioning coach and a fourth person overseeing his after-care program after his stay in a drug rehabilitation center.

HEADLINE: Health Care Horror Stories

 BODY:
Critics labeled them horror stories, and they were. On Tuesday night, NBC presented a two-hour news special on health reform that began by focusing on the human disasters caused by a system that denies families the guarantee of adequate health coverage.
Rick Reckoway works hard as an electrician. When his 12-year-old son was born with respiratory and cardiac problems, he thought he did not have a financial worry because he had bought family coverage. Mr. Reckoway discovered that his insurance benefits were capped at $100,000, leaving him $700,000 in debt. He played by all the right rules, and found himself impoverished. 
 Cathi and Perry Thoorsell both work. Yet they do not have $7,000 for health insurance for themselves and their two children. When Cathi suffered a difficult pregnancy, they were hit by whopping bills and went $30,000 in debt. Their lives are haunted by the threat of more medical bills they cannot afford. And they have abandoned any thought of having a third child.
These horror stories, some opponents of reform say, slander the overall excellence of U.S. health care. But the issue is coverage, not quality. The system is riddled with holes through which tens of millions fall -- forcing them to scrounge to get by with no insurance or inadequate insurance. Though the vast majority of Americans say they are happy with their health care, families like the Reckoways and Thoorsells are the tip of a large problem -- a problem that has no place in a wealthy country.
President Clinton has it right: Universal coverage is needed for humanitarian reasons, and economic reasons as well. Without it, health care markets will not work properly. Most reform bills would require insurers to sell to all applicants, healthy or sick, at identical rates. That is fine, but unless there is a parallel obligation for all individuals, healthy families will not buy coverage until someone gets sick. Premiums will thus rise drastically for everyone else. The way around this problem is universal coverage.
There is room to disagree about tactics. Mr. Clinton would impose an employer mandate, requiring employers to pay most of the cost of the policies chosen by workers. That makes sense because it would build upon the system that already provides coverage to most Americans. It is also feasible because, with time to adjust and the help of Federal subsidies, companies would be able to avoid massive layoffs by passing along the costs to workers in the form of lower wage hikes.
Yet furious opposition by small employers has bottled up the idea in the Senate Finance Committee -- a crucial battleground because its membership mirrors that of the full Senate. A group of moderates on the committee -- led by John Breaux, Democrat of Louisiana, and John Chafee, Republican of Rhode Island -- put together a measure that avoids an employer mandate. Some of their ideas are sound, primarily a provision to tax lavish health care policies as a way of driving consumers toward cost-effective plans. But their plan fails to lock in universal coverage now or in the future.
Universal coverage can be delayed to give businesses time to adjust to voluntary market reforms. The idea can even be dropped until, say, five or more years from now when it would automatically kick in if voluntary reforms did not achieve universal coverage. But universal coverage cannot be denied unless the country wants to tolerate the spectacle of millions of Reckaways and Thoorsells.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

HEADLINE: The Economy Will Work Its Way Out of This;
Misleading Data

 BODY:
To the Editor:
What amazes me is that "The Job-Killer Policy," Bob Herbert's June 8 column on the loss of jobs in America, and the concern with an invisible inflation, is so atypical today. If anything, I suspect that the job picture will emerge as one of the penultimate challenges facing our society in this decade. The fact is that the employment picture is suffused with misleading data that either misstate or obfuscate the real issue. 
 What must be recognized is that leading businesses are continuing to move their manufacturing into areas of lower labor costs, and this trend means continuing job erosion in the United States.
For a while, it was believed that this was a natural evolution and a balance would be provided by the service sector. But this simplistic view overlooks some fundamental truths that apply to both manufacturing and the services.
First, many high-paying service jobs are directly related to manufacturing. When a manufacturing job moves elsewhere, the corresponding service-sector job tends to disappear and not be replaced. Then, the lower paying jobs in the service sector do not have the corresponding multiplier effect in the economy that their manufacturing counterparts had. And manufacturing jobs tend overall to be long-lasting and higher-paying than the bulk of service sector jobs.
Compound these realities with the downsizing of industry and now the military, a growing level of immigration, relatively high birth rates among the poor and disenfranchised, a growing number of college educated unemployed and other factors, and we find ourselves with a prescription for crisis, if not now, then later. The big variable at this time is whether small business can balance the ledger. Considering the attitudes of local, state and Federal government to small business, it seems doubtful.
The solution, and what is in short supply these days, is a combination of a spirit of innovation and collective will by our leadership. Unfortunately, it appears that our leadership would rather fiddle with the mundane and irrelevant while our futures go up in flames.
 
LESLIE AARON-FRIEDLIEB
Brooklyn, June 16, 1994
The writer is a management and marketing consultant.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: The Economy Will Work Its Way Out of This

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re "Leslie Fay's Logic" by Bob Herbert (column, June 19): Mr. Herbert complains about the "loss" of low-skill jobs in the United States to low-wage countries overseas, especially in Central America and Asia. However, he is silent on what he thinks the United States should do about the problem, common to all advanced industrial nations. 
 His latest target is Leslie Fay, a garment manufacturer now in bankruptcy court. To salvage its remaining jobs in distribution and administration, Leslie Fay plans to shift its United States production overseas. In protest, the International Ladies Garment Workers Union struck on June 1, the first such shutdown in the company's 50-year history.
The column, which omits that Leslie Fay is in bankruptcy, suggests that at heart Mr. Herbert is a protectionist, who would raise tariffs and other trade barriers to "protect" jobs in this country. Such a cure would be much worse than the disease.
Blocking textile imports would lead to higher clothing prices, a de facto tax on consumers to subsidize the wages of a tiny minority in the work force. It would be a subsidy for an industry where productivity has been subpar, despite steady improvement over the post-World War II period.
The United States economy is in constant turmoil. Some industries and some companies decline and die, replaced by others that grow and prosper. In the week that 1,000 Leslie Fay workers went out on strike, more than 400,000 other people were hired for new jobs or recalled to old ones.
There were wrenching changes two generations ago, when textile makers moved south from New England. But with new investment, the New England economy emerged stronger than ever, with new skills in new areas. The same will be true in northeast Pennsylvania where Leslie Fay's plants are.
Despite a 19 percent drop in production employment in manufacturing since 1979, output has risen 43.5 percent. That's a gain of more than 75 percent in product per worker.
 America is the world's largest exporter, with merchandise exports among the strongest sectors of the economy. Exports are more than 21 percent of goods produced in the United States, up sharply from 13 percent a decade ago. Excluding oil, outside the usual framework for trade analysis, our international accounts are remarkably close to balance.
High-wage countries such as the United States must recognize they have no alternative but to trade, and no choice but to use low-cost capital at home to compete with low-cost labor abroad. The last thing Washington should do is inhibit the process of creative destruction and renewal that has made this the richest nation on earth.
 
H. ERICH HEINEMANN
New York, June 20, 1994
The writer, a former New York Times reporter, is chief economist of Ladenburg, Thalman & Company, investment bankers.

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: COLLEGE POINT;
Megastore Plan for Corporate Park Draws Fire

BYLINE: By LYNETTE HOLLOWAY 

 BODY:
A city plan to lure megastores to the thriving College Point Corporate Park is being hotly contested by local merchants concerned that the stores would crush prospering mom-and-pop businesses.
Next month, the city's Economic Development Corporation will solicit proposals for large-scale retail development on 500,000 square feet of vacant land at the center, which borders the Whitestone Expressway. Merchants are scheduled to meet next week with officials from Mayor Giuliani's office to discuss the plan. 
 "We are interested in large-scale retail operations which will draw customers from a large market area rather than from the adjacent neighborhood," said Clay B. Lifflander, president of the development corporation. "I believe there is room in this market for both large and small stores."
The proposal comes at a time when the Giuliani administration is grappling with whether it should revise zoning restrictions that make it difficult for giant stores to come to the city. Planning officials recently completed a study that showed there is a shortage of large-scale retail stores, supermarkets and shopping centers in the city. Researchers showed that was due mostly to the lack of appropriately zoned commercial sites.
In recent years, some giant stores have been able to sidestep the laws by narrowing their market focus. Home Depot, which is scheduled to open a store at the former Taystee bakery in Flushing, succeeded by defining its project as a hardware store. That definition, which generated a great public outcry, lets the company avoid the public approval process often necessary for large-scale projects.
In College Point, local merchants say they fear the megastores, which have been cropping up throughout the region and around the country, because they would ravage established operations at the corporate center and surrounding community. Merchants are mobilizing their forces.
"We want to let everybody know that there is opposition to this plan," said Fred J. Massarello, president of the College Point Board of Trade, which represents 175 businesses.
Under the plan, the builders would develop a city-owned parcel that runs along the Whitestone Expressway and two privately owned sites on 20th Avenue. More than 100 businesses, including offices and manufacturers, occupy the corporate center and employ about 4,000 people. Some tenants include Waldbaum's, the Department of Motor Vehicles and the Postal Service, and The New York Times is planning to build a printing plant there. Besides megastores, city officials hope the plan would also attract a movie theater. L.H.

GRAPHIC: Map of Queens showing location of College Point Corporate Park.
