HEADLINE: Your Wedding

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STEINHAUER 

 BODY:
If you will settle for nothing less than 16 bridesmaids costumed at Vera Wang, a four-tier butter-cream cake and the Gipsy Kings playing while you skip the light fandango across the Rainbow Room floor with a rose clamped firmly between your teeth, stop reading.
And if your idea of a perfect wedding is a quick lunch-hour stop at City Hall followed by a pastrami on rye on the N train back to the office, you should probably put down the paper right now and do some work, even if it is Sunday. 
 But if you want to be married in New York surrounded by your friends, family and a few people you've never met, feed them all decently and actually have fun in the process without plunging yourself, your future spouse or your parents into deepest penury, what follows was made for you.
Of course, planning, and pricing, a wedding, is a very arbitrary business. Almost any professional wedding planner will swear that you can't be married properly for less than $50,000 -- and that Rainbow Room affair would cost a good deal more. Any number of actual recently married people will insist that they had perfectly wonderful weddings for tens of thousands less. Still, if you've come this far, accept this proposition: an apparently fancy wedding for 100 guests, with sit-down meal, for less than $10,000, with enough permutations and combinations to satisfy any couple who need help but want to maintain at least the illusion of the personal touch.
What makes this artifice possible is the mercantile variety of New York, and the mercantile resourcefulness of New Yorkers. For if on the surface, the city seems prohibitively costly in almost every party category, it is also a town full of consignment shops, wholesalers and secret, inexpensive nooks for dancing. And it is home to hundreds of ethnic restaurateurs who are dying to give you an option to grilled chicken, for half the price.
Finally, since you live in New York, you are, a priori, peculiarly equipped for what might seem like the most difficult planning challenge since Normandy.
You've braved the Department of Motor Vehicles at lunch hour -- what snippy caterer hell-bent on serving mashed potatoes is going to strong-arm you? You spend your summer weekends in your un-air-conditioned studio apartment, so an afternoon plowing through the flower markets in search of cheaper blooms won't phase you. And you're happy to schlep all the way to the South Bronx for a better deal on the antique wicker chair of your dreams, so why wouldn't you be willing to shelve a June wedding in favor of one, say, later in the summer, when all the hotels will be begging for your (discounted) business?
 
Taking the Vows
 First, you must make five prenuptial vows:
1. I will plan ahead with a realistic budget that I intend to stick with. This may mean a few compromises. "You have to pick the most important elements of your fantasy and learn to work with it," said Dennis Wilson, who owns Wilson & Krulen Party and Event Design in Chelsea. "For example, if you want the Rainbow Room but can't afford it, pick out an Art Deco style restaurant."
2. I will beg shamelessly. Now is the time to call in old favors, bargain with friends and start off phone conversations with, "You know, what I'd really most treasure as a wedding gift from you is your time."
3. I will not invite more than 100 people. "People understand that times are tough," said Kenneth L. Gutwillig, vice president of marketing and special events for Locations Etc., an event coordinator on West 26th Street. "If you don't invite your third cousin Irving, he's not going to hate you for the rest of your life."
4. I will not give my guests favors. "Favors are O-U-T, out," said Matty Stein, a co-owner and wedding consultant at Fancy That, a party planning concern on Central Park South. "People junk them on the way home, and the last thing people need are two Godiva chocolates after they've stuffed themselves."
5. I will not be married in June. Actually, June isn't even the most popular month to apply for a wedding license in New York; August is, with June a not-so-close second. (Is this because New Yorkers have caught on?) And this is where cost-cutting begins.
"The best time to get married in New York is in July or August," said Susan Bell, who plans parties through her concern, Saved by the Bell, on the Upper West Side. "Hotels are starving for your business and are more than delighted to book your party." January and February are also good for bargaining, she says, and "Friday nights have also come out of nowhere as a choice for weddings."
Just how good a deal you can expect will, of course, depend on the hotel and your tenacity, but Ms. Bell advises that a place that charges $135 a head in June will often come down to $100 or less in the off-season.
 
Exploring Space
 Logistically and economically, it is a good idea to have your ceremony and reception in the same place. Not only will all your guests thank you for not forcing them to hail cabs en masse under the blazing sun, but you save yourself one rental fee and one set of flowers.
What you are looking for is a beautiful spot that will let you bring your own food and drink. (Reception halls and hotels usually place a severe markup on low-end liquor.)
Some good, simple options are photography studios, which generally have great light, lots of clean, open space and often interesting architectural details. They start at about $800 for an evening, and if you can stomach the idea of calling a photographer for a reception room closer to the last minute, you might be able to get the space for less.
There are also many lofts and private mansions for rent. Some of them advertise, but a good source is Locations Etc., the directory produced by the party planners and available at many bookstores.
One such space is the Ukrainian National Home, on Second Avenue between Ninth Street and St. Marks Place, which seats 300. For $1,200, "I give you tables, chairs and air-conditioning," John Wynnyt, the manager there, said, with an emphasis on the air-conditioning.
 
Catering to You
 Approach a caterer with a budget, and work together to create something you like and can afford, rather than just asking what the caterer offers and figuring out which corners you can cut to get it.
"A good caterer will work very hard to work within your budget," said Mr. Wilson. "If you want caviar and can't afford it, then they should put a dab of caviar in a little new potato," he said. "Remember, this isn't about your caterer. This is about your family, your friends, your wedding. Don't settle for something you don't want."
While you should expect to pay at least $35, and as much as $200, per person, not including tax, for a traditional caterer, many ethnic restaurants offer much lower prices for larger amounts of what may be more interesting food. For roughly $16 a person, for instance, you can offer your guests a spread of Indian food from Passage to India, on East Sixth Street. Not a few slices of nan and some chicken curry, mind you, but several appetizers, bread and a 10-course meal with dessert.
For about the same price, Takada's Catering, a Japanese catering company and cooking school at 131 East 23d Street, will give you a two-and-a-half-hour sushi buffet, complete with a chef who will slice, roll and serve, much to your guests' delight. Increase your budget to $25 a head, and you can have traditional Moroccan couscous, with salads and hors d'oeuvres, from Casablanca Ritz Cafe, 170 Thompson Street in the Village.
 
Bartending
 Luckily for you, people are drinking less, or at least would like to give the impression that they are, and therefore it is socially acceptable for you to serve only wine, perhaps with a few bottles of vodka for good measure.
"You should concentrate on areas that have great wine value," said John Osborne, the buyer for Astor Wines and Spirits in the Village. "Chile and Argentina and the south of France are good. Be careful with wines from California, because not everything on the low end is such a great bargain."
Shop ahead and take a few bottles home to make sure they are acceptable, and then try to bargain a multi-case price. As for Champagne, be smart and use it just for the toast.
(A tip: If you offer your guests a glass of wine the minute they walk in the door, wedding experts say, two-thirds of them on average will stick with wine all for the rest of the night.)
 
All Together Now
 If you have your heart set on the traditional hotel reception, you're almost certain to break the $10,000 budget. Still, you might as well make the best of it.
Try to negotiate bringing your own liquor, and make sure you have fully discussed every detail included in the price. For the average $150 a head, are you getting a cocktail hour? Does the price include the cake and Champagne, the tax and gratuities? Also, ask the hotel if it will base its liquor charge on the actual consumption at your reception, rather than forcing you to estimate your party's capacity and buy it up front.
Some hotels give better deals. For instance, for $115 a person, the Hotel Macklowe offers a wedding banquet in its Hudson Theater at 145 West 44th Street. You can have your ceremony in a foyer surrounded by Tiffany stained glass, then head off for a cocktail hour. This is followed by a four-course meal, cake and an open bar. The real treat here is that you can offer your guests dancing on a 30-by-30-foot Broadway stage, which is nicer than waltzing around a bunch of vinyl chairs that have been shoved off to the side of the room.
Or, consider a nice twist on a hotel reception: the wedding brunch or tea. The average cost of a sit-down dinner reception at the Carlyle Hotel at 76th and Madison is $200 a head, but equally elegant is a wedding high tea. For about $100 a person, Ruth Trager, the director of banquet sales at the hotel, offers tea, Champagne, fruits, scones, smoked salmon and those tiny sandwiches that everyone likes.
For the same price, the Carlyle will do a breakfast with eggs, crepes and Champagne. No extra liquor costs, and everyone goes home full. Your favorite brunch spot might also be able to do a wedding brunch for roughly half the price of a dinner wedding.
As for restaurants, there are as many as there are love stories in New York, and couples may choose a certain spot because they love the food or memories they've consumed there. If that's the case, there's no arguing with romance. But if you're willing to be reasonable, and you know you want a restaurant reception, consider calling places in the financial district that do a Monday through Friday business only, and are willing to open on a weekend for you.
For example, St. Maggie's Cafe, a Victorian-style restaurant on Wall Street, will do weddings on weekend nights, with a five-hour open bar, hors d'oeuvres, a four-course meal of American cuisine and cake for $65 a person, including tax and gratuities.
Have you considered a Chinese wedding banquet? The Sheraton La Guardia East Hotel on 39th Avenue in Flushing offers a traditional 11-course banquet, including the mandatory longevity noodles, for $350 a table (an average table seats 10). Included in the price is a honeymoon suite.
One restaurant that can provide a similar spread for $30 a person (and you can bring your own liquor) is the Triple 8 Palace, a glittering Hong Kong-style room on East Broadway in Chinatown.
 
Cakewalk
 Everyone wants a wedding cake that actually tastes good; few ever really have one. If you've made the mistake of flipping through a bridal magazine, you are probably already suffering acute cake envy. A top-of-the-line cake from Colette's Cake on West 11th Street, for example, is a towering piece of edible architecture, with its sugar flowers, lattice windows and cherubs abounding. Of course, at $12 a slice, you aren't having it.
So why not buy the sugarwork floral bouquet that graces the top for $200, and throw it on a considerably cheaper, non-wedding-specific cake bought elsewhere?
If it must be a wedding cake, top to bottom, there are deals to be had. For $175, Veniero Pasticceria on East 11th Street sells a three-layer yellow sponge cake filled with cannoli cream and decorated with the usual roses, which serves 100. "Some people get their cake here just because their parents and grandparents did," said Robert Zerilli, who works in the cake department.
 
Everything Else
 Even if they are to be modest, you'll want some flowers. The number of flower options, of course, will be directly related to the amount of time you have. You may very well want to have your bouquets made professionally, and do the centerpieces yourself. (Though a few lovely flowers bound with an elegant piece of ribbon will cost you about $5 a bridesmaid.)
Anne-Stewart Hamilton, who was married last November and now plans weddings for others, grew ivy on her terrace and clipped it all off to use as garlands for her entire hall and her cake. She tied up her invitations, bouquets and napkins with ribbons bought in the millinery district (the upper 30's between Fifth Avenue and Avenue of the Americas).
Jennifer Farrington and Sean O'Neill, who were married last month, saved their funky, old-fashioned-style Italian tomato cans for several months before their wedding, then filled them with blooming plants that served as very hip centerpieces for their family-style Italian reception. The cost: $30. "I didn't include the price of the tomato cans," said Ms. Farrington, "because we used them for sauce, anyway."
There are those who believe that the music is actually the most important part of the reception, but that doesn't mean you have to spend a mint on a band that specializes in weddings. For every jazz quartet that wants to charge you $800, there is a good band made up of otherwise-unemployed musicians that can be plucked cheap from your favorite bar.
A lot of people, of course, simply prefer to make their own tapes; they get all the songs they want and have a wonderful tape to play on each anniversary.
There are several approaches to hiring photographers, and of course you will have to look at their books to make sure you like their work, because awful wedding pictures are much worse than, say, bad cake.
You can call an art school's photography department and ask to see the books of some fourth-year students. Also, many couples hire a photographer only for the ceremony, and ask their guests to bring cameras to shoot the candids at the reception, which often end up being just as good, if not better. Finally, skip the parent albums. Your family can pick out what they like and order prints themselves.
For invitations, it is best to bypass the retailers and deal with a printing house. But if you have the time (or can appropriate someone else's), make your own. Ms. Hamilton went to Jam Paper and Envelope, that paper nirvana with stores all over the city, and picked out cream stock paper and envelopes that looked like the expensive kind, a few gold calligraphy pens and some cream ribbon from the millinery district, again.
Her sister wrote them out, tied them up and sent them out. Total cost: $60.
In the end, you will most likely exceed your budget in some areas, and may need to make some arrangements.
If your second cousin is a florist, this is the time to invite her to dinner. If you have friends who are professional photographers, caterers or wine merchants, start thinking of creative ways to ask for their services. Consider barter, even with total strangers.
Jordana Rothschild, 24, who works in public relations, saw herself creeping over her floral budget, so she offered her professional skills in a trade for extra greenery. She somehow dragooned five journalists over to the publicity-hungry florist's loft space, and the florist gave her the flowers she had always dreamed of.
So, the recipe for an affordable, fun wedding in New York: the ceremony and reception in the Ukrainian National home, with a spread of Indian food, a jazz band ferreted out from the subterranean, flower arrangements made yourself and invitations you bribed you best friend to make in exchange for baby-sitting. And bag the limos. If Tony and Tina can walk from their wedding to their reception every night, so can you.
 
THE WINE
 What would a wedding be without Champagne? Cheaper, usually. But not necessarily right now. In fact, Champagne lovers, never was there a better time to tie the knot. Most New York shops are selling French Champagne for as little as $13 and good California sparklers for even less.
If you plan only a reception, you can easily get by with just white wine. If there's going to be a meal, though, you'll need some red. Neither needs be expensive. Some attractive whites might include Georges Duboeuf's Macon-Villages 1993 from France, Hawk Crest Chardonnay, 1992, from California or Seppelt Chardonnay Bin 92 from Australia. Reds might include Mr. Duboeuf's Beaujolais Villages 1993, Ruffino Chianti Classico 1992, or Fetzer Vineyards Valley Oaks Cabernet Sauvignon 1991. None should cost more than $7 a bottle.
If money is extra tight, consider "wine in the box," the 90's answer to jug wine. There are lots of extra grapes in California these days, and some good ones find their way into these five-liter packages with built-in spouts. The cost: $9 to $12, or $1.80 to $2.40 a bottle. FRANK J. PRIAL
 
RESTAURANTS
 Here is a short selection of restaurants ready and willing to put on relatively inexpensive wedding feasts.
* Gascogne, 158 Eighth Avenue (at 18th Street), (212) 675-6564. A charming, out-of-the-way, rustic spot with a delightful garden. Specializes in lusty food of southwest France.
* Arqua, 281 Church Street (at White Street), (212) 334-1888. A big, airy room where the northern Italian fare is authentic, ever-changing and uniformly good.
* Park Bistro, 414 Park Avenue (at 28th Street), (212) 689-1360. Authentic French bistro specializing in Provencal fare and regional French wines.
* Gage & Tollner, 372 Fulton Street (at Jay Street), Brooklyn, (718) 875-5181. Old oyster house ambience, moderately priced American fare.
* Symphony Cafe, 950 Eighth Avenue (at 56th Street), (212) 397-9595). Big, airy and theatrical space with recently upgraded American fare.
* Le Comptoir, 227 East 67th Street, (212) 794-4950. Handsome brass-and-wood bistro. Standards done well.
* Remi, 145 West 53d Street, (212) 581-4242. One of the best northern Italian restaurants in town. BRYAN MILLER
 
GREENERY
 This time of year, the best and easiest places for do-it-yourself flowers are the Greenmarkets.
 I go to the one at Union Square, which is open Monday, Wednesday, Friday and Saturday, from 8 A.M. to 6 P.M. The earlier you go, the better, of course. But almost any time of year, you will be able to find interesting plant material, whether it be branches of pear and cherry trees to force into bloom in late winter, lilacs and mock oranges in the spring, roses and irises in June, and then, come midsummer, daisies, sunflowers, black-eyed Susans.
 The wholesale flower and plant dealers along 28th Street (and some also, along 26th and 27th Streets) between jSixth and Seventh Avenues, will often sell to individuals if they are not to busy with their regular customers, the retail florists. These people do business, though, inthe wee hours, so show up about 7 A.M., when the professionals have gone home.
 Adventurous types may want to explore the wholesale dealers at the New YOrk Terminal Market at Hunt's Point in the Bronx and at the BrooklynTerminal Market at Foster Avenue and East 87th Street. These markets are open seven days a week, 7 A.M. to 6 P.M., and some wholesalers will sell to individuals. ANNE RAVER

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.;
Street Theater

BYLINE: By MONIQUE P. YAZIGI 

 BODY:
INSIDE the Cort Theater, where Anna Deavere Smith has been portraying people affected by the 1992 Los Angeles riots, it is intermission. The lights come up. The theater doors open. And the audience flows out of the lobby into the night.
Immediately, the drums begin. 
 Then five guys from Harlem, dressed in T-shirts, shorts and high-priced sneakers, begin to dance and flip through the traffic as it tries to move through West 48th Street between Sixth and Seventh Avenues. The group's moves draw the theater crowd closer, many seemingly touched by this scene -- a combination of gymnastics, acrobatics and break dancing -- on a New York street.
"If you like us, just clap," a member of the group tells the crowd, "and if you don't like us just clap."
When the bucket is passed, many theatergoers give, usually about $2 or $3 apiece.
"I think this play is bringing real life to the stage, and then you walk outside and you're hit in the face again with it," said Denise Wallace, a lawyer from Miami who had dropped $20 into the bucket. "They deserve it if they're going to work that hard."
The Cort's ushers, ticket takers and even the security guards have come to call these pavement performances "the 10-minute half-time show." But, though the show may seem an impromptu distraction for theater crowds, the young men, who call themselves the New York City Float Committee, are deliberately working within the city's underground economy, constantly dodging theater managers, security guards and the police as they seek to take themselves off the streets and onto the world stage.
"They've been coming here for years," said Joan Gallagher, chief usher at the Cort. "I remember them through "Sarafina!" and "The Grapes of Wrath."
"It's a business," said Ross Eastwick, 26, one of the performers. His dancemates, musicians and fellow tumblers included Ayan Williams, 34, the group's manager and drummer; D. J. Spivey, 25; Larry Wright, 19, and Darrl Poboy, 26.
There have been many low points and a few highs. The group has had a professional choreographer, conducted auditions and even had a private audience with Michael Jackson. Six weeks ago, "Michael saw us playing in Central Park from his hotel room at the Plaza," said Mr. Spivey, who has been with the group for 10 years. "A guy from the hotel asked us to meet Michael in one of the ballrooms. We performed for him, while one of his people videotaped us.
"Michael told us he would show the tape around."
"It's true," said Mr. Jackson's manager, Sandy Gallin.
The New York Float Committee was formed 11 years ago by a group of Harlem friends that included Mr. Williams and Mr. Spivey. There are now 10 people on the dance roster; they perform all over Manhattan. Over the years they have had members ranging in age from 4 to 34, all male.
"We try to be good role models for the kids on the street," said Mr. Eastwick, a former drug addict who lives with relatives in Williamsburg, Brooklyn. "We find a lot of raw talent in the ghettos."
Seven days a week, they maintain a schedule that includes a 10 A.M. meeting at Mr. Spivey's apartment on 113th Street and Lenox Avenue -- where he lives with his wife, Dana, and four children -- to decide where and what to perform. A two-hour rehearsal at Herald Square park at 34th Street is followed by performances, starting at 1 P.M., around Broadway and Times Square, during intermissions and when the "flow comes out of the plays," Mr. Spivey said.
Right now, business is not that lucrative.
"On a very, very good day we can make up to $60 apiece," said Mr. Williams, who grew up in Harlem with the rest of the group but left to study percussion at the New England Conservatory of Music. Mr. Williams has performed on the streets of New York and Los Angeles for more than 10 years. He has also given drum lessons and done odd jobs to make ends meet. Often the group takes home only $20 each after working from 10 A.M. to midnight.
"This is the only way we can make money using our talents," he said.
The troupe can never stay in one place too long. Like other street performers, they work without city permits; none are issued for such purposes, city officials say.
Police officers can give a summons at their discretion for various offenses, from unreasonable noise to disorderly conduct, or for blocking the sidewalks, said Sergeant John Clifford, a police spokesman. A summons can mean a fine of up to $250 in court.
While performing, the Float Committee members are chased away up to 20 times a day, Mr. Williams said. The group likes to work in front of the Cort Theater because they are not chased as often. In fact, on this night, a security guard from the Shubert Organization, owners of the theater, seemed to enjoy the show.
"If they're not under the marquee and no one asks us to move them, then they can stay," he said.
Even the star of "Twilight in Los Angeles, 1992," Ms. Smith, had kind words for her half-time rivals at the Cort. "I love them," whispered Ms. Smith, who was losing her voice. Her assistant, standing nearby, said the actor had often sent money out to the group.
But the one-woman play closed last Sunday after 10 weeks, and nothing is scheduled at the Cort until September.
That night, after leaving the Cort, the group set up in front of the Winter Garden Theater on Broadway between 50th and 51st Streets, where the musical "Cats" is playing. But only moments after they started dancing in front of about 100 people, the music was abruptly stopped by a passing police officer.
"It's illegal," the officer said. "When they gather that big of a crowd, they bring out the pickpockets, and it's not safe for the pedestrians."
The five performers stood quietly, watching the people walk by them. "We could have made a lot of money," Mr. Williams said, "but we'll just try again tomorrow."

GRAPHIC: Photo: D. J. Spivey turns intermission into a pavement performance in front of the Cort Theater on West 48th Street. (Norman Y. Lono for The New York Times)

 Here are a list of some other street performers in the theater district.
 
FROM RUSSIA WITH FUN: Alex Vosk and his four miniature poodles, Inky, Porky, Piggy and Loxy perform tricks.
WHERE: The east side of Broadway between 45th and 46th Streets.
WHEN: 5 to 10 P.M. Wendesdays jthrough Sundays.
 
SPACE AGE ART: Denis Grdovic (GER-doh-vitch), a Croatian artist also known as Penichardo, creates artworks using spray paing in just 10 seconds. All the paintings have an outer-space theme.
WHERE: The East side of Seventh Avenue, between 49th and 50th Streets.
WHEN: 9:30 P.M. to midnight, seven days a week.
 
YIMIN TAN AND HIS FRIENDS: More than 35 Chinese artists, trained in Beijing and Shanghai, sketch charcoal and chalk portraits. The sketches take 10 to 15 minutes and cost $15 for black and white, $30 for color.
WHERE: Seventh Avenue between 43d and 44th Streets every day.
WHEN: 8 p.m. to midnight, seven days a week.
 
VINCENT TAYLOR: The musician who plays the steel pan, drums that are the national instrument of Trinidad. The theme song from the movie "Love Story" is his favorite.
WHERE: Seventh Avenue between 47th and 48th Streets.
WHEN: 9 P.M. to 1 A.M., seven days a week.
 
SAX-E-1: James Butch Rooks plays jaxx, funk and disco on his saxophone. "You name it I'll play it," Mr. Rooks said.
WHERE: Seventh Avenue between 50th and 51st Streets.
WHEN: 9 P.M. to 2 A.M. Tuesdays through Fridays.

HEADLINE: TELEVISION;
Two Ways to Replace a Familiar Face

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
KEN BODE HAS A BEARD. HE also has a resume that includes a Ph.D. in politics, a decade as a national political correspondent for NBC News, nearly five years as politics editor of The New Republic, and stints as a reporter and analyst for CNN in the 1992 Presidential campaign.
But since Friday, March 11, when he sat down at the head of a slate-blue, suede-topped table to replace Paul Duke as host of "Washington Week in Review" on PBS, Mr. Bode and his colleagues have heard a lot about the beard from the fiercely loyal viewers of one of the nation's most venerable video salons. 
 "It's the damnedest thing," said Mr. Bode, who grew the beard as a lark five years ago when he left network television to head the Center for Contemporary Media at DePauw University in Greencastle, Ind. He was specifically asked to keep it when he returned to the air part time on CNN, then considered shaving it off when he took the "Washington Week" job -- until WETA, which produces the show, took a lot of expensive publicity photos of him wearing it.
Viewers have written worrying that it makes him look old, a computer hacker sent E-mail calling it "very distinguished," and one regular panelist on the program bumped into a 77-year-old woman in an airport who complained, ever so gently, that she was a bit deaf and facial hair made it harder for her to read lips.
The fuss over Mr. Bode's beard is just one sign of how closely fans have scrutinized the subtle changes in the longest-running program on PBS, which the sonorous, patrician and clean-shaven Mr. Duke headed for 20 of its 27 years on the air. It built a national audience of four million viewers with its four rotating panelists' sober-sided dissections of the capital's weekly irrationalities. It also spawned a gaggle of louder imitators who quickly shed its civility for what Mr. Duke dismisses as "ideological food fights."
When Mr. Duke decided to retire at 67, Mr. Bode, 55, beat out a host of contenders, including some of the program's longtime panelists, as the sort of erudite, experienced yet common-sensical conversationalist who could carry the program into the millennium.
"There's no question that this was an opportunity for us to look at the program pretty hard and decide what we thought was at the heart of it and how to reinvigorate it," said David McGowan, WETA's senior vice president for news and public affairs. "The most important thing here is reporting, and there's a natural tension in the program between the need for beat reporters on particular stories, and retaining that sense of family with certain regular reporters that viewers have come to rely on."
Mr. Bode's mission has been twofold: to maintain the tradition of giving "reporters an opportunity to empty their notebooks and give perspective" on the week's top stories, while also drawing younger viewers by using occasional graphics, live remote hookups, news footage -- and at least one elliptical but racy reference to the President.
Mr. Bode began with a bang, devoting all four segments of his first show to the Whitewater affair, a topic Mr. Duke had largely eschewed as overblown. Mr. Bode and the show's producers conceived the program as an effort to put the issue in perspective by parsing out its origins. "Everybody thought it was a very strong show," he said, but he failed to make it clear that such departures would not be routine.
"The viewers worried about that," he said. "The mail was very polite: 'Mr. Bode, we like you on the program, but we certainly hope that you don't do this every week.' "
He hasn't, but he has made changes, most recently by welcoming a visitor from outside the regular circle of 15 panelists: Bob Woodward of The Washington Post, who came to discuss "The Agenda," his new tell-all book on the battles over President Clinton's economic policies last year. That show ended with Mr. Bode quoting a British newspaper's waspish comment that Mr. Clinton's visit to Oxford would give the world a chance to "focus on one of the President's less well-publicized organs: his brain."
It was Mr. Duke, now living in London for a yearlong retreat, who called to tell Mr. Bode about the clipping that afternoon, but longtime panelists said Mr. Duke would never have repeated it on the air. Indeed, the program's regulars say Mr. Bode has made a point of carrying on the Duke mission without trying to imitate Mr. Duke.
"I think it's human and real and probably very good that Bode isn't like Paul Duke," said Charles McDowell of The Richmond Times-Dispatch, one of the program's panelists for almost two decades. "Paul had an unusual, unusual sort of cool image. He asked the questions; he stayed out of it; he wasn't this animated, involved personality. I think Ken is more animated, more involved in the discussion -- more willing to let it go to a discussion, and override the Q. and A. a little bit.
"I think a lot of people are very relieved at that, as a matter of fact."
A newer panelist, Michel McQueen of ABC News, went so far as to hold a referendum on the beard at a recent luncheon of the National Woman's Democratic Club (it lost overwhelmingly, though she personally thinks "it's very now").
"It's like having your older brother go away to college, and the middle one taking over," Ms. McQueen said of the transition from Mr. Duke, who segued from segment to segment with unabashed (and sometimes labored) wordplay. Now, she said, panelists are almost expected to jump in -- politely -- at any time. "It's enjoyable," she continued. "You have to stay on your toes. You can't zone out during somebody else's segment, just because you think you have some great point you want to make in yours."
Mr. Bode, a gently bearish man who smiles easily and speaks in sound bursts, not bites, has a way with a phrase. (On one early show, he described a Supreme Court vacancy as constituting "one-ninth of one-third of the Government.")
THE BRINGS NOT ONLY the experience of years covering national politics but a childhood in Middle America -- Hawarden, Iowa, population 2,500, where his father owned first a small dairy and then a dry-cleaning business. He wound up at the University of South Dakota, then earned advanced degrees at the University of North Carolina, where he was drawn to the civil rights movement of the early 1960's.
Teaching assignments followed at Michigan State and the State University of New York, Binghamton, along with fellowships at Princeton and Yale. At the 1968 Democratic National Convention, he was floor director for the short-lived Presidential bid of George McGovern, whom he had known from South Dakota. In the mid-70's he found himself covering politics for The New Republic. One appearance on a political special organized by some public television stations led to a test at NBC and, eventually, to 10 years that included "Bode's Journal," his weekly political report for the "Today" show.
He loved that job but gave it up because "I was raising my kids from 100 airports a year," said Mr. Bode who, with his wife, Margo, a high school social-studies teacher, has two daughters, Matilda, 16, and Josephine, 11. "I really had no control."
Today he keeps his perspective by spending better than half of each week overseeing the media center and teaching at DePauw, a private liberal-arts institution. He checks in daily by phone with the "Washington Week" producer, Sue Ducat, reads the news wires and flies to Washington on Thursday mornings in time for each week's live broadcast at 8 P.M. Friday at WETA's studios in suburban Shirlington, Va. (In summer, he travels from the Delaware shore, where his family has an old farmhouse.
In Greencastle, he says, he is kept honest by his buddies in a daily cribbage game, held in an auto-repair garage that he discovered when he arrived in town and his limping Volvo needed a fix. "I can't tell you how much I appreciate it," he said. "I don't think there's a year of college at that table," he added, but there is a lot of life and concern about national problems. "They're all potential Perot voters."
Because Mr. Bode believes that "a lot of Washington stories can best be told from outside Washington," he intends to use remote hookups with correspondents in other cities more often. To that end, there is at least one more change in the works: a new set this fall, in part to get a table that will not look empty if there are not four panelists.
Mr. McDowell, whose genial Virginia drawl often serves as the program's voice of experience, said the basic mission hasn't changed.
"I think 'Washington Week' will continue to be the old reliable, trying to be a little more contemporary all the time, but not getting into open editorializing and loudmouthing and showboating," he said. "But we haven't been all that stuffy, either. There are times when I'm just gassing along like some Southerner at a hardware store."

SECTION: Section 13CN; Page 17; Column 1; Connecticut Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 974 words

HEADLINE: THEATER;
For the Season Past, Awards Imagined and Real

BYLINE: By ALVIN KLEIN 

 BODY:
WHAT to make of a theater season that promised discovery and adventure but delivered retreads and tours? Such is the disappointment reflected in the Connecticut Critics Circle awards for the 1993-94 season that are being presented tomorrow night at 8 at the Norwalk Community-Technical College.
Of awards in 10 categories for achievements in the season just ended (covering the year from June 1 to May 31), a shared prize for set design is the only one being given to home-grown productions at the Yale Repertory and Long Wharf Theater in New Haven. 
 For other awards, reviewers covering four high-visibility regional theaters (including Hartford Stage Company and Goodspeed Opera House in East Haddam) and several enterprising small professional companies, chose a national tour and its star: Nigel Hawthorne and "The Madness of George III." Also among those chosen were two summer stock productions, the leads in Connecticut's umpteenth revival of "Me and My Girl" and the star of a solo show fresh from a Broadway run, Lynn Redgrave. "Madness," selected as outstanding production of a play, arrived from the Royal National Theater of Britain for its American premiere in Stamford. Bookings in Brooklyn, Boston and Baltimore followed. The outstanding musical production "Phantom" was imported from Long Island and exported to Florida.
What's the story? Is the Connecticut theater in a state of crisis or transition? Is the state a nurturing place for theater or just another road stopover, not to mention a recycler of popular musicals? Is the signature regional theater production, supposedly the American theater's lifeline, in decline? Is the voice of Yale Rep, Long Wharf, Goodspeed and Hartford Stage taking featured billing under bookings, packages and generic reruns? Does the critics' group need to redefine categories and ground rules?
For sure, several new Connecticut-based productions were bypassed by the critical consensus, notably "Keely and Du" (the season's most harrowing work) and "Bailey's Cafe," (the most sumptuous adaptation of a novel) both at Hartford Stage as well as "The America Play" (the most stimulating enigma nobody figured out) at Yale Rep and finally "The Swan" (the best anthropomorphic play in decades) produced by Theater Works in Hartford. Still, the season was constrained by an air of non-event, a sure cause for critical malcontent.
Perhaps accolades are in order for categories not yet considered -- like Outstanding Host. This could go to Arvin Brown for holding open house at the Long Wharf for Broadway producers ("The Times"), more Broadway producers ("Broken Glass"), the Abbey Theater of Dublin ("The Faith Healer"), and for the Alliance Theater of Atlanta and the Indiana Rep of Indianapolis in co-production ("Flyin' West").
Anna Deavere Smith in an easy-to-pick-up production of a mono-docudrama also dropped by Long Wharf; so did the National Theater of the Deaf of Chester in "Under Milkwood." With all that, there was room for two actual Long Wharf productions: "Sight Unseen" and "Misalliance."
While there may be no awards for the following categories, they rate as season high points.
Most Deserved and Encouraging News in a Bleak World: The $900,000 grant to the Downtown Cabaret Theater of Bridgeport by the Connecticut Department of Economic Development for major capital renovation and improvement.
Most Affirmative Digression: The Hartman-Tenney Musicals in Workshop series at the Stamford Theater of the Arts produces "The New Yorkers" by Cole Porter.
Most Crushing Let-Down: Christopher Plummer cancels a one-night, one-man show at Stamford Center for the Arts's Palace Theater.
Runner-Up Letdown: Theatre de la Jeune Lune's "Green Bird," at Yale Rep, after "Children of Paradise," the previous year's singular event.
Third Place Letdown: A misguided Marivaux ("False Admissions") at Hartford Stage.
Most Tedious Unanswered Question: Who is Jane Martin? This season it was twice again speculated upon. ("Keely and Du" in Hartford and "Criminal Hearts" at Stamford Theater Works).
More Interesting Unanswered Question: Is Jane Martin waiting for a Pulitzer Prize to own up? ("Keely and Du" was the Pulitzer committee's third choice this year.)
Most Unconditional Display of Loyalty by a Theater to a Playwright: Theater Works in Hartford to Lee Blessing.
Unsolicited Advice to Theater Works: Get over it.
The real winners of the Connecticut Critics Awards follow.
Outstanding production of a play: "The Madness of George III" at Rich Forum in Stamford.
Outstanding production of a musical: "Phantom" at Candlewood Playhouse in New Fairfield.
Outstanding female performance in a play: Lynn Redgrave "Shakespeare for My Father" at Rich Forum.
Outstanding male performance in a play: Nigel Hawthorne "The Madness of George III" Rich Forum.
Outstanding male and female in a musical: Tracey Ann Moore and BK Kennelly "Me and My Girl" Downtown Cabaret in Bridgeport.
Outstanding direction of a play: A tie between Kenny Leon for "Flyin' West" at Long Wharf and Nicholas Hytner for "Madness of George III" at Rich Forum.
Outstanding direction of a musical: Dom Ruggiero for "Phantom" at Candlewood.
Outstanding choreography: Sean Thomas Morrissey "West Side Story" at Candlewood.
Outstanding technical achievement: Myung Hee Cho for set design of "The School for Wives" at Yale Rep and Michael H. Yeargan for set design of "Misalliance" at Long Wharf.
The following special awards are also being presented.
Outstanding playwright: Arthur Miller for his body of work.
Tom Killen award for outstanding contribution to Connecticut Theater: Richard and Susan Hallinan of Downtown Cabaret in Bridgeport.
Special award to Kay McGrath, formerly of Goodspeed Opera House in East Haddam, dean of Connecticut theater press representatives.

HEADLINE: Superstores Fueling Quest for Books

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER 

DATELINE: EASTCHESTER

 BODY:
THE sea change taking place in bookselling across the country was happening here, too, at Encore Books last week, as Lisa Singer hurried to the checkout counter with an armload of children's books and Louis Perri tarried in the aisles of the new fiction section.
Mrs. Singer, a homemaker from New Rochelle who was buying books for her daughter, said she was becoming a regular at the so-called superstore, lured by the vast selection of titles and the steep discounts on most books. 
 Mr. Perri, who owns a limousine service in Scarsdale, also was no stranger to the store. Although pleased with the discounts -- 20 percent on most hardcovers and 30 percent on best sellers -- he said he was equally drawn to the soothing atmosphere and the feeling that here, at least, lost among the shelves, he could unwind from the cares of the day.
Similar scenes were happening at Borders Books and Music in White Plains and at Barnes & Noble stores in Hartsdale, Yonkers and White Plains. While booksellers were discovering that bigger was better in terms of end-of-year revenues, book buyers were scooping up specials, unashamedly forsaking the independent retailers of their neighborhoods.
"I love a small bookstore," Mrs. Singer said, "but I love the discounts even more."
Ilse Ginsberg, an art teacher from Scarsdale, said she was willing to endure "the din of children and the smell of coffee" for the right price.
 
Crazy About Discounts
 "It's not that I don't like children, or coffee, but I'm not crazy about them in bookstores," she said. "I am crazy about the discounts, though."
While superstores have muscled into the increasingly profitable retail book market across the country, they have been Johnny-come-lately's to Westchester.
They began making inroads in Connecticut by the late 1980's, but it was not until 1992 that Barnes & Noble, for one, opened its first superstore in the county in White Plains.
Encore Books opened here in 1992 and expanded a year later, going from a stock of 40,000 titles to 75,000. Borders opened in December at the new Westchester Pavilion mall in White Plains, carrying 130,000 book titles, along with 50,000 music titles and laser disks and videos.
The superstores were initially deterred by the high cost of real estate in the county as were "big box" chain discounters like Tops Appliance City in Hawthorne and Bradlees department store in Yonkers.
"But real estate prices finally came down," said Noreen L. Preston, an economist with the County Office of Economic Development, explaining that the county was now able to compete with areas like Fairfield County and Bergen and Passaic Counties in New Jersey. "What we are seeing are retail outlets, which had been developing elsewhere, now coming here."
Ms. Preston said the superstores had become "drawing cards for malls and other small retail clusters and definitely a plus for business in the county."
Like other merchants, Tom Simon, Encore's general manager, said Westchester was proving to be a fruitful market, and he described the chain's typical customer as "having a high income and education level, along with a high amount of discretionary income -- terrific ingredients for a bookstore."
 
Concerts and Poetry Readings
 Discounters though they may be, the new breed of superstore has eschewed no-frills marketing tactics. Most superstores have soft carpeting underfoot, rich wood-grained cabinets and tables, plump sofas and tucked-away reading sections. Borders has a carpeted amphitheater for children along with a large-screen television that shows movies.
Offering more than just books, Encore has a 1,000-square-foot cafe, which sells coffee, tea, sandwiches and soup. At Borders' Expresso Bar, double cappuccino and Pecan Meltaway cookies are on the menu.
The cafes also provide the backdrop for afternoon and evening miniconcerts and poetry readings. Borders this month was the host of a Voices of D-Day book signing, the Java Jive! folk-rock band, David the Magician, a fiction-writing workshop and a Transcendentalist poets' night.
Encore, which has a superstore in Princeton, N.J., and another in Harrisburg, Pa., holds poetry readings and book signings two and three times a month and presents live music at the store every Friday and Saturday night, catering to what Mr. Simon called "a varied crowd -- singles, couples, groups of all ages."
 
Record Sales Reported
 Barnes & Noble's chief financial officer, Irene R. Miller, said the national chain is seeking to establish its superstores as "quasi community centers," places where children's pajama storytime parties, singles nights, poetry readings and other events are held.
The superstore retailing brew has resulted in record sales. Barnes & Noble, for example, said 1993 was the best year in the history of the company, with $250 million in new revenues. Borders reported that sales in stores that were open for more than a year were up 18 percent during the first quarter of 1994 from the same period the year before. Borders, a wholly owned subsidiary of Kmart with 18 superstores in 1992, has 55 superstores now and expects to close the year with 72, said Dan Conetta, vice president for marketing.
In general, the bookselling industry is experiencing a surge, with a recent survey by the United States Commerce Department showing that sales in bookstores nationwide increased at a faster rate than total retail sales. In all, last year, bookstore sales reached nearly $8 billion, the study revealed.
Borders, typical of the superstores, offers a large selection of books, knowledgeable service and a comfortable shopping environment, Mr. Conetta said. He added that customers are allowed to browse unimpeded.
 
Mimicking the Independents
 "Even if a customer is lying on the floor reading, assuming he is not imposing a safety hazard, our booksellers are instructed to leave him be," Mr. Conetta said. "It's not unusual to see people propped up in all kinds of positions throughout our stores."
Avin Mark Domnitz, president of the Tarrytown-based American Booksellers Associations, a trade organization representing 8,000 members and 4,500 bookstores, said the new superstores were "mimicking the small, independent bookstore, only doing it bigger and better."
But Mr. Domnitz also said that the new breed of bookstores was "putting a lot of pressure on the marketplace" -- and in some places usurping markets previously controlled by the independents.
At Good Yarns in Hastings-on-Hudson, the co-owner, Sooky Kyle, said the 20-year-old neighborhood bookstore was trying hard to fight back.
"We definitely have been touched by the superstores and the economic hard times, and I don't blame people for trying to save money," she said, "but it has been hard for us."
Good Yarns has been offering its customers an incentive card, which entitles them to a $20 gift certificate after $200 in purchases. That combined with 20 percent off best sellers is equivalent to a 30 percent discount, Ms. Kyle said.
 
Lawsuit Filed
 "But can we afford these discounts?" she asked. "Publishers prefer to deal with the big guys, to give them the bigger discount. We are trying to match discounts without the same favors, and that's tough."
Concerned with such trends, the Booksellers Association filed a lawsuit in May in Federal District Court in the Eastern District of Pennsylvania against five publishers -- St. Martin's Press, Penguin U.S.A., Houghton Mifflin, Routledge Hill Press and Hugh Lauster Levin Associates -- charging that they discriminated against independent booksellers by offering special discounts to some chain stores.
While independents like Good Yarns struggle to survive and the superstore chains bring in profits, all the players in the bid for readers' dollars agree that interest in reading continues to grow. It is a trend, they say, that flies in the face of other studies indicating a shift in reading habits. Some data suggest that people rely more on bits and pieces of visual and aural information, like that provided by television and computer information services, rather than the written word.
 
Looking Inward
 "But our studies show that reading is up," Mr. Domnitz said. "They are reading everything -- trends, ideas, how-to's. They are trying to keep up with the world."
Mr. Simon at Encore Books noted an increased interest in philosophy, science and religion. "Readers are looking inward at the meaning of their existence," he said. "And they want answers."
Ms. Miller, the chief financial officer for Barnes & Noble, described books as an expression of a life style:
"People want certain books in their homes," she said. "They become part of the furnishings, even if they aren't always read from cover to cover. They are an entertainment alternative."

LENGTH: 1302 words

HEADLINE: A Gay World, Vibrant and Forgotten

BYLINE: By George Chauncey; George Chauncey teaches American history at the University of Chicago and is author of "Gay New York."

 BODY:
It would have been unthinkable 25 years ago for thousands of openly gay fans to cheer openly gay athletes at Yankee Stadium, for openly gay artists to perform to the acclaim of openly gay audiences at Carnegie Hall, or for the mainstream media to provide extensive and sympathetic coverage of it all. Today's march and the Gay Games and Cultural Festival are testimony to the legacy of the Stonewall rebellion of June 28, 1969 -- when a police assault on a Greenwich Village gay bar turned a small civil rights campaign into a mass liberation movement.
But the enshrinement of Stonewall as the genesis of gay culture threatens to deny the richness and resiliency of gay and lesbian life before the late 60's and to obscure the long history of gay resistance that made the gay-rights movement possible. 
 Pre-Stonewall lesbians and gay men are often held up as passive victims of social hatred who lived solitary lives (in the "closet") that kept them vulnerable to anti-gay ideology. Many gay people blame previous generations for not having had the courage to come out of the closet. Or they condescendingly imagine that their predecessors internalized society's hatred of homosexuality and became self-loathing.
But the systematic suppression of the gay community was not due to some age-old, unchanging social antipathy, nor was it a sign of passivity and acquiescence by gay people. Anti-gay forces created the closet in response to the openness and assertiveness of gay men and lesbians in the early 20th century.
Beginning in the 1890's, an extensive gay world took shape in the streets, cafeterias, saloons and apartments of New York City, and gay people played an integral role in the social life of many neighborhoods. Openly gay men drank with sailors and other working men at waterfront dives and entertained them at Bowery saloons; well-known gay people casually mixed with other patrons at Harlem's basement cabarets; lesbians ran speakeasies where Greenwich Village bohemians -- straight and gay -- gathered to read their verse.
These men and women, who saw themselves as part of a visible, largely working-class gay world, forged a culture with its own language, customs, folk histories, heroes and heroines. In the 1920's and early 30's, gay impresarios organized drag balls attracting thousands of gay dancers and straight spectators. Gay writers, actors and musicians produced a distinctive gay literature and performance style. This cultural outpouring was so popular by the late 20's that gay performers moved from the margins of the city and briefly became the darlings of Broadway.
This flourishing gay world has been forgotten. It was wiped into historical oblivion by a fierce backlash in the 30's -- part of a wider Depression-era condemnation of the cultural experimentation of the 20's, which many blamed for the economic collapse. With millions of male breadwinners losing their jobs, people were fearful of any additional threats to traditional family hierarchies.
In New York, laws were enacted prohibiting homosexuals from gathering in any state-licensed public place. Bars, restaurants and cabarets were threatened with loss of their liquor licenses if they employed homosexuals, allowed them to gather on the premises or served them drinks -- and the State Liquor Authority closed hundreds of establishments for tolerating a gay presence. This continued for decades: nearly every gay bar in the city was closed in the winter of 1959-60 in response to an anti-gay campaign by the newspaper columnist Lee Mortimer, and again in 1964 in a pre-World's Fair "cleanup."
The public discussion of gay issues was also censored. In the early 30's, after a generation of films had dealt with gay images, the new Hollywood production code prohibited gay characters and even talk of homosexuality in films. In the theater, the backlash had started even before the Depression: after the appearance of a lesbian drama on Broadway and Mae West's threat to stage a farce about transvestites called "The Drag" in 1927, a state law was passed prohibiting the representation or discussion of homosexuality on the stage.
In the 30's, the New York City police, using a 1923 state law that made it a criminal act for one man to invite another to have sex, began sending good-looking plainclothes officers into gay bars to strike up conversations with men, lead them on and arrest them if the victims suggested going home. (Between 1923 and 1967, when gay activists persuaded Mayor John V. Lindsay to end most entrapment, more than 50,000 men had been arrested on this charge.)
Anti-gay policing around the country intensified in the 40's and especially the 50's, when Senator Joseph McCarthy claimed that homosexuals in the State Department threatened national security. Thousands of gay Federal employees were dismissed. Equally without substance, police departments and newspapers around the country began to demonize homosexuals as child molesters; arrest rates increased dramatically.
The degree to which gay men had to fear arrest -- and the subsequent exposure of their homosexuality to their families and employers -- is almost impossible to understand today. Although New York's gay world grew in the post-war years, gay life became less visible and gay meeting places more segregated and carefully hidden from the straight public. The state built the closet in the 30's, and the isolation of homosexuals made it easier for them to be demonized.
Still, some gay people fought for their rights. In the 1930's, gay bars challenged the prohibitions against them in the courts (unsuccessfully), and in the 1950's a handful of courageous souls organized political groups, such as the Mattachine Society and the Daughters of Bilitis, to advocate the homosexual cause. Although most did not speak out so openly, taking this as evidence that they accepted the laws against them misinterprets silence as acquiescence. It construes resistance in the narrowest of terms -- as only the organization of formal political groups or protests.
Threatened with police raids, harassment and the loss of their jobs, families and reputations, most people hid their participation in gay life from their straight associates. But this did not necessarily keep them hidden from one other. They developed a sophisticated system of subcultural codes of dress, speech and style that enabled them to recognize one another and to carry on covert conversations. "Gay" itself was such a word until the 60's, when its homosexual connotations began to be known to nongay New Yorkers.
 The tactics gay people devised for communicating, claiming space and affirming their self-worth did not directly challenge anti-gay repression in the way the post-Stonewall movement would, but they allowed many gay people to form a supportive community despite the larger society's injunction against their doing so. This enabled many lesbians and gay men to build happy, self-confident, loving lives.
That the openness of gay life in the early 20th century was brought to an end after a few decades, and that the memory of it was systematically suppressed, reminds us that the growth of tolerance in recent years cannot be taken for granted. Then as now, increased gay visibility produced a powerful reaction.
But the relative tolerance of homosexuality in the early 20th century also shows that America has not been monolithically and inevitably homophobic, and that social conventions of sexuality are no more natural or timeless than those of race or gender. Attacks on gay men and lesbians have often resulted from broader anxieties in American culture as much as from fears about homosexuality itself. Above all, the last century shows us that attitudes toward gay people can change -- and can be changed.

HEADLINE: Can Low S.A.T. Scores Lead to High Grades?

 BODY:
To the Editor:
I am not in a position to contest Donald Kennedy's assertion in "What Grade Inflation?" (Op-Ed, June 13) that "Stanford undergraduates are significantly brighter and better prepared than their 1977 counterparts." However, in light of the decision by the College Board to adjust upward the mean Scholastic Assessment Test score (front page, June 11), it would be difficult to deny that systemwide, today's undergraduates are on average less prepared than students of a generation ago in their verbal and mathematical skills -- two indispensable ingredients for success in college and later in the fiercely competitive global economy.
Therefore, assuming average S.A.T. scores have the same predictive power today as they had a generation ago and that academic standards are the same as then, if grade inflation is not endemic in United States higher education, average grades in the 1990's should have fallen. This is certainly not the case. 
 As for the causes of grade inflation, unfortunately, there are many guilty parties.
 
IRA SOHN
Professor of Finance
Montclair State College
Upper Montclair, N.J. June 17, 1994

HEADLINE: Space Station Faces Danger From Flotsam

BYLINE: By WILLIAM J. BROAD 

 BODY:
Dead satellites, shattered rocket stages and thousands of other pieces of man-made space junk speeding around Earth could destroy a planned international space station, and engineers are struggling to reduce the danger.
NASA estimates the chance that debris could smash through the shield of the space station, an orbital outpost for the world's astronauts, at 1 in 5 during its construction and expected 10-year life. That probability means that if there were five such stations, experts would expect one to be hit. 
 While the station is to have redundant gear to maximize safety and interior hatches to let astronauts seal themselves off from shattered areas that lose air, the overall risk of a catastrophe that would result in death or destruction of the craft is still estimated at roughly 1 chance in 10 over the same time.
Officials of the National Aeronautics and Space Administration say they are confident that the risk of penetration can be reduced, perhaps to 1 chance in 10, making the risk of catastrophe about 1 chance in 20. Although the design already calls for much shielding, more may be added, they say, even while conceding that such a remedy adds cost and weight to an already heavily laden project.
"We'll do whatever is necessary to get adequate safety," the space agency's Administrator, Daniel S. Goldin, said in an interview. "If we need more shielding, we'll put more up."
But Mr. Goldin also said danger and death were inevitable in space exploration.
"We'll never be able to guarantee total safety," he said. "We could have loss of life with the shuttle, and the station as well. If you want to guarantee no loss of life, it's better not to go into space."
Still, a station designer, who spoke on the condition of anonymity, said the bureaucracy was playing down the problem and courting disaster.
"The traditional design philosophy says the mission catastrophic risk should not exceed a few percent," the designer said. "Now, they've got it in the range of 10 percent. That violates due diligence. If you're working in an uncertain environment, your bias should be on the side of safety."
Bigger than a football field at 361 feet in length and 290 feet in width, the station would have a six-member international crew to study Earth, the heavens and human reactions to weightlessness in preparation for lengthy voyages to Mars and beyond.
 
Decade of Use
 The United States, Europe, Japan and Canada are longtime partners in the project, and Russia joined recently. The outpost would cost American taxpayers $43 billion, including $11 billion already spent on design studies. Eighty-five launchings of gear and people will be needed to assemble and outfit the station. Assembly flights are scheduled to begin in late 1997 and end in 2002, after which the completed outpost is to be used for a decade or more.
The recent addition of the Russians to the plan is an important foreign-policy initiative of the Clinton Administration. It is intended to symbolize a new era of East-West accord and to engage Moscow in constructive space work in return for ending practices that upset Washington, like exporting advanced rocket gear to developing countries.
 
Rising Tide of Junk
 The plan faces a potentially close vote on the House floor this week. Some members fear that Russia's economic woes may jeopardize the project, while others see Russian participation as a way to help reduce the odds of political upheaval in Russia.
The tide of junk in orbit near Earth is rising at a rate of 2 percent to 5 percent a year, experts say.
The American military has found in orbit about 7,000 objects ranging from the size of a school bus to the size of a baseball, and it tracks all of them from the ground with radar. Smaller objects cannot easily be tracked. Experts estimate that circling Earth are perhaps 150,000 objects that could penetrate the space station. Because of the enormous speeds of everything in orbit, a tiny flake of metal can pack the punch of an exploding hand grenade.
In general, the collision risks for orbiting stations are high because of their large size and long stay in space. The shuttles are much smaller, going up for days instead of years, but they have nonetheless been moved on four occasions to dodge debris and have been hit by some objects that dug tiny craters.
For any single shuttle flight, NASA estimates the overall risk of catastrophic failure at about 1 in 78 missions. In contrast, the Air Transport Association estimates that a single airline flight has 1 chance in 2 million of ending in fatalities.
The space station program, begun by President Ronald Reagan in 1984, has tended to play down the threat from debris, partly because costs would rise if heavy shielding was chosen as the remedy. In 1991 NASA adopted an updated estimate of the threat, and in 1992 it adopted a plan for heftier shielding after the General Accounting Office, the investigative arm of Congress, criticized the agency for ignoring the problem.
The risk of penetration for that station over its lifetime was to be 10 percent, or 1 chance in 10, then.
Today, design changes to accommodate the Russians have added a new degree of danger. For one thing, the areas for astronauts in the new station are about 40 percent larger than in the old plan, making crew quarters a bigger target for whirling debris.
The new outpost is also to fly in more of a north-south orbit, making it more prone to hit debris head on, since most debris at the station's altitude of 250 miles is in a north-south orbit. Engineers say the move increased the most probable speed of impact from 6.2 miles a second to 7.5 miles a second.
In March, NASA's Aerospace Safety Advisory Panel, an independent group of aerospace experts, urged the agency to get a better grip on the possibility of the station's being destroyed by space junk and the practicality of moving it around to dodge larger pieces of debris.
It also said detailed studies should be done to find out what types of injuries astronauts might suffer and how fast a crew could evacuate the station in an emergency, fleeing in a pair of crew-rescue vehicles.
"It's hairy," Seymour C. Himmel, a panel member and retired NASA engineer, said in an interview. "They're worried about it and working on it. But it comes down to good judgment and risk management. There ain't nothing in space that's going to be perfect."
 
Shielding Astronauts
 William M. Shepherd, deputy manager for the station program at the Johnson Space Center in Houston, said each module for astronauts was getting about 3,000 pounds of added shielding to help protect it from the greater velocity of orbiting junk. The shielding is a fabric of woven alumina and glass backed by layers of Kevlar, the material used in bullet-proof vests.
Mr. Shepherd said such safeguards would allow the American side of the station over its lifetime to achieve a risk of penetration of only 10 percent, the original goal. But, he said, the addition of the Russians increases the risk to 19 percent.
The question the program is now struggling with, Mr. Shepherd said, is whether to try to bring the overall level of protection back to 10 percent, which would require still more shielding.
"Part of the debate is whether that would make the station itself more survivable," he said. "If you're standing on a large ship and take an impact from a missile or torpedo, you may be in better shape than if you were on a small ship. That's the kind of thing we're thinking about. There are trade-offs."
NASA officials say they are eagerly awaiting a study by the National Academy of Sciences, due later this year, on the dimensions of the threat from debris. Only then, and after further study of crew safety issues, will the detailed design of the space station be firmed up.
Mr. Goldin, NASA's Administrator, questioned the reliability of the current risk estimates, saying the empirical data were too sketchy. "People make all their projections based on the absence of evidence," he said. "We're working to get better information."
 
'Overly Conservative'
 He also noted that Russia's Mir station, which has been in orbit for eight years, had apparently suffered no penetrations from space junk. "They think we're overly conservative," he said of the Russians.
Mr. Goldin said that when a space shuttle rendezvoused in the near future with the Mir station he would want a camera on the shuttle's robot arm to examine every inch of Mir for dents.
"We're not going to do anything irresponsible," he said of the plans for the new station. "We're not going to launch anything that has an unacceptable risk. But we also can't afford to use space as a dumping ground. We need an international agreement for controlling the contamination of space."

HEADLINE: Currency Turmoil Weighing on U.S. and Japan;
Dollar Declines To a Record Low Against the Yen

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Monday June 27

 BODY:
The dollar plunged to a record low against the yen in Tokyo trading this morning following the failure of 17 central banks to halt the dollar's slide on Friday, and the resignation of Japanese Prime Minister Tsutomu Hata the next day.
Shortly before noon, the dollar was quoted as low as 99.50 yen, below the 99.90 level that had been reached in New York last Tuesday. This was the lowest level since the modern currency trading system began after World War II. Later in the day the dollar had recovered a bit, to 99.95 yen. 
 The fall of the dollar today was viewed is a repudiation of the multibillion-dollar rescue effort mounted Friday by the Federal Reserve Board and other central banks. It is likely to force Washington to take even more aggressive steps, analysts said. Further intervention might be needed today, and the pressure for the Fed to raise interest rates is mounting.
 
 
Double Blow
 In Japan, the rise of the yen could extinguish the economic recovery that was just starting to grow. It only adds to the sense of turmoil here caused by Saturday's resignation of Prime Minister Hata, which could bring about a period of policy paralysis.
Indeed, the yen's rise and the political uncertainty dealt a double blow to the Tokyo stock market today. The Nikkei average of 225 selected stocks plunged 465.79 points, or 2.24 percent, to 20,300.96.
"This is a psychological shock," Eijiro Hata, the Trade Minister in what is now a lame-duck Cabinet, said today. Hirohisa Fujii, the Finance Minister, pledged to take "tenacious" measures in line with other nations.
 
 
Market Intervention
 But there seemed little to be done except more market intervention and, perhaps, having the Bank of Japan lower interest rates, which would make yen-denominated investments less attractive.
The Bank of Japan was reported to be intervening in the market today to prop up the dollar, but some traders said it was not being very aggressive. "It has been quite weak," said Yasuhisa Morikuni, corporate adviser for foreign exchange at the Bank of America's Tokyo branch.
The dollar had opened at 100.10 yen, its lowest level ever in Tokyo and down 0.30 yen from Friday's close. It seemed to hover there for most of the morning as fears of intervention by the Bank of Japan counterbalanced a bearish market sentiment toward the dollar. But at about 11:30, a dike seemed to burst, and the yen fell steadily to 99.50 before recovering to 99.70 around noon.
The resignation of Mr. Hata was a factor in the decline. Normally, when a country has a weak or unstable government, the value of its currency declines. But in the case of Japan, some analysts think, the yen will rise because political turmoil will make it more difficult for the nation to reach trade agreements with the United States in the next few weeks. Japan's towering trade surplus has been a major factor in the yen's appreciation over the last few years.
Currency traders will be watching to see whether the Federal Reserve intervenes in the market again today. On Friday, the Fed and 16 other central banks spent an estimated $3 billion to $5 billion to buy dollars.
But unlike two previous such interventions, last August and on May 4, the concerted effort on Friday failed to halt the dollar's decline. In New York trading Friday the dollar closed at a weak 100.45 yen and at 1.584 marks, its lowest level against the German currency in 15 months.
Analysts said the intervention on Friday was too little and too late. However, if the banks act aggressively today, it might be more effective. "We may see a bigger effect than last time," said Kazuo Takayama, director of foreign exchange at Barclays Bank in Tokyo.
 
 
Stock Market Slide
 The stock market, meanwhile, continued a slide it began last week. Two weeks ago the Nikkei average climbed above 21,500, its highest level in more than two years, as investors perceive that Japan was about to recover from its three-year-long economic slump. But last week, the index fell 736.33 points, or 3.4 percent, to 20,766.75 because of fears that the rising yen and political instability would nip the recovery in the bud.
The strong yen hurts Japan's export-oriented companies by making their products less price competitive.
Mr. Hata resigned after only two months in office rather than face a no-confidence vote in Parliament that he was certain to lose. The alternative to resigning would have been to dissolve Parliament and call new elections in about one month.
Business leaders said resignation was the best of the two evils because dissolution of the Diet would bring about a "political vacuum." But as it is, the jockeying among political parties to choose the next prime minister will make it difficult to concentrate on economic policy.
 In connection with its trade talks with the United States, Japan had promised to announce by this week a plan to deregulate parts of its economy, cut income taxes and increase public works spending over the long term.
The package of deregulation measures is expected to be announced Tuesday as scheduled, and so might a 10-year plan to increase public works spending. But a decision on the politically sensitive tax issue might have to wait until a new administration takes power.
Still, Japan's economy is in a better position now to withstand political turmoil than it was last July, when Prime Minister Kiichi Miyazawa was booted out of office, and in April, when Prime Minister Morihiro Hosokawa resigned.
The economy is stronger and there are no short-term pump-priming fiscal packages needing to be formulated. An income tax cut for this year is already in effect. And to some extent, markets are getting inured to the now quite frequent upheavals in Japanese politics.
"The uncertainty is clearly weighing down on the market, but long-term it won't affect the market environment," said Kathy Matsui, vice president and chief strategist for Goldman, Sachs & Company in Japan.

The Economy
A wave of mergers and alliances is transforming the nation's trillion-dollar health care system. Without waiting for the outcome of the Congressional health debate, providers of medical care -- including hospitals, physician groups and nursing homes -- are joining to form bigger and bigger networks. The goal is to become large enough to offer the comprehensive services and the reasonable prices needed to assure a steady flow of patients from the increasingly demanding groups that buy medical care. [Page A1.] 
 When an American-led effort by 16 central banks failed to prop up the dollar against the German mark and Japanese yen, many analysts wondered if the Federal Reserve would be forced to raise interest rates as the only way to stop the slide.
