HEADLINE: Administration Is Faulted Over Conference on Africa

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
An Administration-sponsored conference on Africa that opened today immediately became a lighting rod as black members of Congress seized on it to criticize the President's policies toward Rwanda and other African nations and to criticize the conference itself as poorly planned.
After accusations in recent months that the Administration was neglecting Africa, Anthony Lake, the President's national security adviser, who orchestrated the two-day conference,promised in his keynote speech an increase in attention and assistance to Africa. 
 Appearing before African officials, experts on Africa and business executives involved there, Mr. Lake enumerated poverty, civil wars, rapid population growth and AIDS among the problems that need to be tackled in Africa.
"Caring is not enough," he said. "Caring must be translated into conceptual clarity about the nature of the problems that Africa faces, and then care must be translated into practical action."
But conceptual clarity and practical action are exactly what has been lacking, the Administration's critics argue.
 
No 'Focus on Africa'
 "I don't think there has been a focus on Africa ever in any Administration including this one," said Representative Donald Payne, a New Jersey Democrat who is the chief foreign policy spokesman for the Congressional Black Caucus. "I have no idea what the focus of this conference is or what its intended goals are."
Like most members of the Congressional Black Caucus, Mr. Payne boycotted the conference, complaining that it was poorly and hastily planned, that guests were invited only a few days before it took place, and that the Administration's Africa policy is a mess.
Caucus members also were angry that the White House did not solicit their advice on what the focus of the conference, Mr. Payne said.
 
Criticism on Rwanda
 He faulted the Administration for being slow to condemn the killing in Rwanda as genocide and for dragging its feet on arranging for African nations to intervene in Rwanda.
In a stinging report timed to coincide with the conference, Human Rights Watch said the Administration's "failure to make the prevention and punishment of genocide in Rwanda a priority reflects the low level of American interest in Africa."
The report asserted that the Administration was paying too little attention to human rights in Angola, Liberia, Rwanda, the Sudan and Zaire.
Rejecting this criticism, Mr. Lake said, "Throughout Africa, we have left not doubt in the minds of autocratic leaders that we insist on a rapid transition to democracy, return to civilian rule and respect for human rights."
 
Clinton and Gore to Speak
 Through today's conference, the Administration was visibly trying to open a new chapter in American relations toward Africa. President Clinton and Vice President Gore are scheduled to speak at the conference on Monday.
Mr. Lake said repeatedly that he hoped the conference would help the Administration refine its policies on Africa. But Administration officials said the White House would not announce any new policies or economic aid at the session.
While he had lofty words to describe Washington's commitment to Africa, Mr. Lake acknowledged that its willingness to help is restricted by the Federal budget deficit, an overstretched United Nations, and the American public's uncertainty on when and where to get involved abroad.
 
Genocide in Rwanda
 Mr. Lake pleased the Administration's critics by declaring that genocide has taken place in Rwanda. At the same time, he acknowledged that the United States and other nations had done too little to prevent the killing there. But he had no answers about how to prevent such mayhem.
"It's a story of ethnic hatred transformed into genocide, the story of unspeakable physical horror," he said. "It's a test case of one of the great security challenges of the post-Cold War era -- conflicts within, rather than among, nations. And it's a warning of what can happen if African nations and all of us do too little to stop simmering conflicts before they boil over."
In a videotaped message shown at the conference, President Nelson Mandela of South Africa called on the United States to help Africa build democracy and pull out of poverty by "becoming the leading partner in the most novel and challenging international project since the Marshall Plan."
 
'New and Great Beginning'
 Mr. Mandela urged Americans not to be paralyzed by the false perception that Africa is in permanent decline. "There is no better time and place to play a pioneering role," he said. "For many, many generations, praise will be unto those who rose to the challenge as Africa set on a new and great beginning."
Pointing to the democratic elections that brought Mr. Mandela to power, Mr. Lake asserted that Africa offers plenty of hope.
"The terrible problems of African nations and the pessimism they can breed are matched only in scope by the continent's huge potential," he said.
As evidence of the Administration's concern about Africa, Mr. Lake said Washington had sent food and troops to combat hunger in Somalia. Other evidence, he asserted, included the appointment of special envoys to work on the crises in Angola, Somalia and Sudan, as well as what he said was Administration support for peacekeeping efforts in seven African nations, including Angola, Liberia, Mozambique and Rwanda.

HEADLINE: As Christians Pull the G.O.P. to the Right, Its Leaders Argue Over Holding the Center

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
As they seek to unify their party for the elections this November and in 1996, Republican leaders are arguing over how to handle the growing power of the Christian right. They are clashing over whether to soften their outright opposition to legalized abortion and debating their stands on health care changes, welfare policy and family values.
Only last week, the Senate minority leader, Bob Dole of Kansas, rose in the Senate to denounce the Democrats as "appealing to religious bigotry" by condemning Christian conservatives' influence over Republicans. Yet the next day, a Republican colleague, Senator Arlen Specter of Pennsylvania, accused his party of intolerance because it had allowed that faction to gain power. Last weekend, prominent Republicans sparred over the issue at Iowa's state party convention. 
 The fractures are evident in a party that is preparing for its first Presidential election in 16 years as the opposition. Even though Republicans are poised to pick up many Congressional seats in the November election, their bigger concerns are about 1996. At issue is how the Republican Party can hold onto its base without pushing itself so far toward the political margins that it nominates a Presidential candidate who cannot win.
There are powerful memories fueling these concerns: the Republicans' nomination of Barry Goldwater in 1964; the Democrats' nomination of George McGovern in 1972, and the 1992 Republican convention in Houston, a display of right-wing fervor that helped doom President George Bush's re-election.
But the problem surfaces most dramatically in the Republicans' ambivalence over the Christian right, the term applied to people who are involved in politics to advance what they see as Christian principles. Many of them attend fundamentalist churches and have an agenda that emphasizes social issues.
 
Influence on the Rise
 While religious conservatives have been active in Republican politics for years, their ranks are growing. Now that the party is no longer shaped by a Republican in the White House, the religious conservatives, a small segment of the party, can wield a disproportionate amount of influence.
Richard Bond, who was chairman of the Republican National Committee during the 1992 convention, worries that the party could get stuck with an image of intolerance. "My biggest fear is that the Democrats and the media will convince the American people that the Republican Party is being taken over by a bunch of nuts, when this is far, far from the truth," he said. "To suddenly affix the adjective 'Christian' to a political party is a death wish."
Last week, each day brought a new round of charges or countercharges between Democrats and Republicans and within the Republican Party over the issue. Mr. Dole and the other 43 Senate Republicans sent President Clinton a letter demanding that he repudiate Democrats who have criticized the religious conservatives.
But beneath the oratory, self-described moderates like Mr. Specter fear that the party may be viewed as so extreme that it may doom its prospects to regain the White House.
Religious conservatives now dominate several state Republican parties, pushing them to adopt more conservative platforms. They are getting elected to local offices like school boards and are beginning to have an influence on higher offices. Conservative Christians played a major role, for example, in the nomination of Oliver L. North as the Republican Senate candidate from Virginia.
"What some are trying to do is take over the party," Mr. Specter said. "That's bad for the Republican Party and bad for the country."
Few other well-known Republicans have spoken out. Many party leaders, even those who do not subscribe to all the beliefs of the Christian conservatives, believe that they have no choice but to sanction, if not encourage, the participation of these Christians.
"The last thing I want," said Representative Jim Leach, an Iowa Republican, "is a story that says I'm opposed to these people."
Many Republican leaders are following the strategy of William Kristol, a Republican theoretician and former chief of staff for Vice President Dan Quayle. Mr. Kristol rejects the idea that the religious conservatives are unduly influencing state Republican parties. He accuses the Democrats of trying to "demonize religious conservatives" as a means to "scare voters into line" against them.
The Democrats have fanned the partisan flames. On a radio talk show on Friday, Mr. Clinton took on evangelical Christian broadcasters, singling out the Rev. Jerry Falwell.
Earlier last week, Representative Vic Fazio of California, chairman of the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee, harshly attacked what he called the "radical right." Then, in a speech to a lesbian and gay conference in New York, Dr. Joycelyn Elders, the United States Surgeon General, attacked what she called the "un-Christian religious right."
Such remarks brought a torrent of outrage from Republicans. Responding to Mr. Fazio, Representative Henry J. Hyde, an Illinois Republican and leading opponent of abortion, declared that "a calculated smear campaign has been launched against millions of decent and loyal Americans."
In interviews, leading Republicans said the ultimate influence of the religious conservatives would depend on whether they would carry only narrow messages against abortion and homosexuals or if they could broaden their appeals to issues like the economy and health care.
As evidence that the religious conservatives are not dividing the party, some Republicans point to the religious right's support for two Republicans who favor abortion rights: Senators Paul D. Coverdell of Georgia in 1992 and Kay Bailey Hutchison of Texas in 1993. They say that support demonstrates a growing flexibility among the Christian right as it seeks to broaden its appeal.
"The plus side of it is we're bringing people into the party, and they are middle class with strong family values," Mr. Dole said in an interview. "If I thought they were all directed at one or two issues, I'd be worried about it."
Gov. Pete Wilson of California, who has been at odds with Christian conservatives, is also reluctant to criticize them in an election year. If the religious conservatives try to force their views on other Republicans, he said, "they're going to have a quarrel with a lot of people who consider themselves mainstream conservatives -- but I'm not sure how much of a danger that really is."
 
'Rhetoric of Fear'
 Still, while the views of religious conservatives can vary, many have adamant views on emotional issues like abortion and have already pulled Republican parties to the right in some states. In Texas, for example, the Christian right was able to get a platform that endorses the teaching of creation science and has particularly strong anti-abortion language.
A new study by the Anti-Defamation League concludes that the religious conservatives are a dangerous political force that "brings to cultural disagreements a rhetoric of fear, suspicion, even hatred."
Some Republicans attribute the domination of the Christian right in several state parties to a lack of action by the self-described mainstream Republicans.
"I blame my own ilk," said Senator James M. Jeffords, a Vermont Republican. "If they don't get off their duffs and attend the caucuses and attend the conventions, then they'll lose control. I just hope that we learned in 1992 that we have to have a broad party, and if we try to narrow it down, we'll lose elections."
Richard Wirthlin, who was President Ronald Reagan's pollster, said Republicans would be wise to appeal to the religious conservatives in churches but not be too public about it. "Americans don't like extremes," he said, "and the extent to which the religious right does -- both by tactic and position -- place itself in that class as out of the mainstream, it would hurt the Republicans."

HEADLINE: In Japan's Imperial Roadshow, Signs of a More Relaxed Era

BYLINE: By CATHERINE S. MANEGOLD, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: HONOLULU, June 26

 BODY:
For two weeks, from the East Coast to Hawaii's sun-drenched shores, the Japanese Emperor's visit to the United States played with all the sweet-tempered monotony of a summer movie intent upon unrumpled distraction.
No politics, please: By definition, the cross-country journey of Emperor Akihito and Empress Michiko was purely ceremonial. Even a stop at Pearl Harbor was rejected as too controversial. 
 Yet behind the ceremony lies nuance. Behind the nuance lies meaning, and behind the intricate planning for this trip were subtle messages for a Japanese audience rethinking the role of the imperial household and Japan's relationship with the United States.
The trip, which ended on Saturday, came amid heightened military tension over North Korea, a dramatic slide in the value of the dollar, another round of political upheaval in Tokyo and continuing angst over trade. Students of the imperial household said the trip was a reminder of mutual interests that range from military interdependence to the abstract ideals of democracy.
 
Moving Out From the Shadow
 Its message, they said, is that Japan can move beyond the long shadow cast by Washington after World War II and the ferocious economic competition of the last decade, toward a more sophisticated relationship based on mutual respect.
"The symbolism is that you can come here and you don't have to fight," said John W. Dower, a professor at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology who has studied the Japanese-American relationship for years. "It's exhausting that every time these two big capitalist partners get together, they are basically savaging each other. In a way, this is a strong ritual statement that we can actually find something in common."
Just as important, perhaps, was the message that the United States can charm, not just offend; that it is not the racially divided, violence-prone monster that it sometimes seems when viewed from afar.
"We are in a stage of really profound reconsideration on both sides," said George R. Packard, a former dean of the Johns Hopkins School for Advanced International Studies and now the president of the International University of Japan in Nagata. The Emperor's visit, Mr. Packard said, "has satisfied a deep desire on the part of the Japanese to symbolically explain the solidarity they feel with America."
 
Food and Fear
 Also, he noted, it took place as the Japanese Constitution, written in 1947, is up for review and, perhaps, revision.
To some extent, Japan's re-evaluation of its relationship with the United States has been driven by necessity. Japan's economic boom has ended, leaving the country with a protracted recession and new insecurities about American competitiveness. Its nearly obsessive refusal to import rice was broken by a bad harvest that opened market shelves to California grains. Events in North Korea have brought a tough reminder that the Japanese archipelago is within that nation's target range.
Enter the imperial household. The trip "is a ceremonial gift to [calm] the general carrying on," said Merry I. White, a sociologist who is working as a visiting scholar at Harvard University. "For Japan, long-term relationships are built on a series of ceremonies and rituals that are extremely important, probably more important than 'yes' or 'no.' "
One symbol behind the ceremony, she said, is that the United States, for all its bluster, can be a good friend. "One of the basic messages is that if the Emperor can go, you can, too. Your children are safe here."
By emphasizing the positive and traveling with a sizable press corps, the Emperor beamed home a feel-good America. It was an America made of smiling children at every airport, Scarlett O'Hara lifting her skirts to show the Empress her pantalets in an Atlanta museum and a San Francisco dinner attended by Joe DiMaggio -- the latter of particular interest in baseball-mad Japan.
For the most part, Japanese press coverage of the Emperor's American tour was muted. Other than the news of his meeting with President Clinton, Tokyo's major newspapers ran reports of the trip on their inside pages. O. J. Simpson got more space. Television stations broadcast short spots. Still, the images spoke volumes.
 
Royalty Redefined
 Sharon A. Minichiello, the director of Japanese Studies at the University of Hawaii, said that much of the import of the trip lay in a redefinition of the imperial family.
"I think this is an attempt to say that the Japanese people do really want to be considered part of the world," Ms. Minichiello said. "It is the notion of kokusaika, or internationalization, that the government has been talking about for years."
In fact, as structured and exquisitely controlled as the cross-country trip was, it was a pretty loose affair for a royal family so constrained that the Empress, as she explained to one friend, was told not to drink too much water before long flights so that she would not have to make needless trips down the airplane's aisle.
At almost every stop, Japanese residents of the United States clustered for a glimpse of the royal couple and then explained to reporters that there was virtually no chance to get that close to the pair in Tokyo. The couple stopped at several events to talk at length with their American hosts.
That image of accessibility was beamed home to television sets across Japan. When the Emperor and Empress stayed in a home in Longmont, Colo., a Japanese television crew walked room to room to show it. An even more unusual image would follow. At the Rocky Mountain National Park, the Empress shocked the royal retinue when she asked her driver to stop and then strode across a field near Sheep Lakes to "feel what the sheep feel." That day, she was wearing tennis shoes and pants.
 
'Mom-and-Apple-Pie Thing'
 That very unimperial attitude moved Americans at every stop. "This is the transformation of a faceless role into a bourgeois monarchy," Ms. White said. The Emperor and Empress, she said, were "doing the mom-and-apple-pie thing so that we can all have a good feeling."
Carol Gluck, a professor of Japanese history at Columbia University, said: "Change in the imperial household is glacial, by design. There is a growing normalization of the monarchy, if you can say that monarchy is normal in the 20th century."
Meanwhile, Americans who crossed the royal couple's path moved in precisely the opposite direction, adopting levels of decorum that included curtsies, adoring stares and awkward bowing. Even President Clinton succumbed, caught by photographers in an impromptu bow to the Emperor while the two men stood behind the White House.
American awe in the face of things royal was at a peak. In Brighton, Colo., Robert Sakata, a second-generation Japanese-American who runs a huge vegetable farm, started his morning in work pants and a blue-jeans shirt but met the Emperor in a neat gray suit, even though the Emperor's advance men had told him to be casual.
At every stop, the royal couple met with Japanese nationals stationed in the United States for business and with second- and third-generation Japanese-Americans.
To Japanese who have become United States citizens or have worked in this country only to find themselves somewhat ostracized upon their return home, the Emperor's attention was a reassuring sign that Japan's borders might finally become more porous.

HEADLINE: Errors Inflated New York Success At Affirmative-Action Contracts

SERIES: Hidden Partners, Phantom Contracts: Second of two articles.

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER 

 BODY:
Santa Fe Construction, owned by a black man, got two contracts, each worth $6.5 million, to renovate police station houses. The K-Pam Trucking Corporation, owned by a woman, won a $4.6 million subcontract for work on the construction of New York City's third water tunnel.
Those were among hundreds of examples that enabled Mayor David N. Dinkins, in the middle of his re-election campaign last October, to announce proudly the results of a new effort to award more city contracts to companies owned by women or minorities. 
 Mr. Dinkins said the city had nearly doubled the value of contracts going to such companies in the program's first year. The results were hailed by his allies as a major achievement, emphatically ending a longstanding pattern of discrimination and providing a powerful tool for economic development in the city's poor neighborhoods.
But a detailed analysis of the program by The New York Times found that Santa Fe Construction won only one $6.5 million contract, not two, and that the $4.6 million subcontract for K-Pam Trucking was in fact never awarded.
The bookkeeping for the city's affirmative-action effort was riddled with similar discrepancies and errors, the analysis found, and as a result, the new program's achievement was substantially overstated.
In hailing the program's success, Mr. Dinkins and his aides said that $270 million in contracts had been awarded to women- and minority-owned companies last year, increasing their share of the lucrative market in municipal contracts to 17 percent from 9 percent.
But the Times's examination determined that city officials could not identify nearly $70 million of those contracts.
Half that $70 million, municipal officials acknowledged recently, represents their estimate of the value of subcontracts that were supposed to have been awarded by prime contractors, most of them white, to companies owned by women or minorities. But the Department of Business Services, the lead agency in the affirmative-action effort, cannot say with certainty that any of these subcontracts actually went to such companies, nor can it provide their names.
The Times also found that an additional $34 million in contracts that the department did identify were never really awarded or have been canceled since they were awarded a year ago.
There were repeated patterns of errors and misrepresentation in the city's bookkeeping. Seven large contracts were simply counted twice; city officials attributed them to a "computer glitch." More than 30 construction contracts that the department said had been awarded in fact never were. A half-dozen additional contracts were counted improperly, overstating their value.
It is unclear how these errors occurred. Officials from the Department of Business Services and other city agencies blame one another.
Some supporters of the Dinkins initiative speculate that resistant prime contractors and agency bureaucrats embellished their affirmative-action achievements to mask their true reluctance to include more companies owned by women or minorities in city construction business, which has long been dominated by white contractors.
 
Question of Responsibility
 And some opponents of the effort theorize that members of the Dinkins administration used creative accounting to make their achievement look more impressive, particularly in an election year.
Mary Colon, who was on the Business Services staff that created the program under Mr. Dinkins, and who now serves as the program's director, said her agency was not responsible for the bookkeeping problems. All the contract data were supplied by other city departments, like Parks, Transportation and Environmental Protection, which actually award and oversee contracts, she said.
But officials in a dozen city agencies insisted that many of the contracts listed by Business Services had not been awarded. No work was done and no money changed hands, the officials said.
The agency officials said that they had provided accurate information to the Business Services Department, and they suggested that any errors or distortions must have been made by Business Services.
 
'When You Ask Bureaucrats'
 Bookkeeping problems similar to those found in New York City have occurred in other cities and states, several experts said.
"When you ask bureaucrats their story, they will tell you that they were doing their jobs and then a mandate falls from the sky," said Timothy Bates, the chairman of the graduate program in urban policy analysis at the New School for Social Research, who has studied affirmative-action programs around the country. "So they inflate the numbers."
Rudy Washington, the new Commissioner of Business Services, said he had long suspected that the statistics produced by the Dinkins administration had overstated the results of the city's effort.
"I'd bet that if you looked at the participation of the Asian, Latino and African-American people of the city, the numbers would be around 8 or 9 percent," Mr. Washington said. But he, too, could offer no explanation for all the errors in his department's accounting.
 
Covering Up Foot-Dragging
 Wallace L. Ford 2d, who led the department under Mr. Dinkins, said he believed that some inaccuracies "can be ascribed to less-than-enthusiastic support from personnel."
Mr. Ford said that some officials resisted affirmative action. And because the Dinkins administration exerted considerable pressure on agencies to open up their contracting to more women- and minority-owned companies, he said, some resistant officials may have tried to cover up their foot-dragging by creating fictitious contracts.
Whatever the explanation, Mr. Washington said that after learning of the results of The Times's examination he asked the city's Department of Investigation to look into the program's bookkeeping and procedures. He said he also asked the department to investigate some companies owned by women or minorities that were found by The Times to have close ties to white businessmen.
Mr. Washington also said he might change the city's reporting system. It might be more reliable, he said, to identify contracts awarded to minority- and women-owned companies by using records of city payments to contractors for work performed. His department now relies on reports from city agencies about contracts that they have awarded.
 
Contracts Never Awarded
 A comprehensive list produced last year by the department states that more than 3,800 contracts, worth $235 million, were awarded to 568 companies owned by women or minorities during the program's first year, the fiscal year that ran from July 1992 through June 1993.
The Times's examination focused on 135 relatively large construction contracts that the Department of Business Services said had been awarded by various city agencies to 56 companies.
Of these, 31 contracts, nearly one in four, were never awarded, according to the municipal agencies responsible for letting the contracts. In addition, 11 contracts were either counted improperly by the city, inflating their value, or have been canceled since being awarded a year ago. K-Pam Trucking, for example, never got a $4,564,000 subcontract, according to the Department of Environmental Protection, the city agency that was said to have granted the award.
Others were double-counted. The Department of Business Services' records show that Santa Fe Construction, which is owned by Desmond Emanuel, was awarded a contract for $6,535,000 on May 20, 1993, to be construction manager for the renovation of police stations. A second contract for the same amount was awarded 10 days later, the department said.
 
Some Values Overstated
 But the Department of General Services, which is overseeing the renovation, says only one $6.5 million contract was awarded to Santa Fe Construction.
The values of other contracts were simply overstated. Unique Support Services Inc., which provides temporary office workers, was said to have won two big contracts last spring, one for $3.2 million from the Fire Department and one for $648,178 from the Human Resources Administration.
But fire officials said that the contract was actually for three years, and so it was worth only about $1 million in the last fiscal year. Officials from the Human Resources Administration said its contract was worth only $198,000 last year.
Some minority contractors have already defaulted on contracts awarded to them last year. The Climex Construction Corporation defaulted on two contracts, worth a total of $830,000, that it won in May 1993 to renovate city playgrounds, according to the Parks Department. The relatively small company was overextended, parks officials said, and the contracts had to be canceled.
 
Award Is News to Owner
 Perhaps no company better illustrates the shortcomings of the city's oversight of affirmative action than K-Pam Trucking. Mary Brooks, its president, said she had never heard about the $4.6 million subcontract that she was said to have received. Her company did win a $200,000 subcontract in May 1993 to work on two street reconstruction projects in the Flatbush section of Brooklyn.
K-Pam Trucking's standing as a woman-owned company is also in doubt. When Mrs. Brooks created the company in 1981, her only business experience consisted of owning and operating a Carvel ice cream store from 1963 to 1966. She had no experience in trucking or construction, but her husband, Robert, had spent years driving a dump truck, Mrs. Brooks said in a recent interview.
In September 1991 -- just four months after the company was certified by the state as a woman-owned enterprise -- the State Department of Taxation and Finance ordered K-Pam's corporate status dissolved because of a failure to pay an unspecified amount of taxes.
The corporation's dissolution did not prevent K-Pam Trucking from being certified by the city in 1992 or being awarded the $200,000 subcontract last year.
Mrs. Brooks said she knew nothing about losing her corporate standing. "I'm not aware of it," she said. "I was never notified of anything about this."

HEADLINE: Currency Turmoil Weighing on U.S. and Japan;
Fed Officials Get Confusing Signals on Interest Rates

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 25

 BODY:
Top Federal Reserve officials found mixed signals in this week's turmoil in the currency markets, which they believe has left their course on interest rates unclear.
When an American-led effort by 16 central banks on Friday failed to prop up the dollar against the German mark and Japanese yen, many analysts wondered if the Fed would be forced to raise interest rates as the only way to stop the slide. 
 But the Fed sees a far more complicated picture, one that would appear to have it prepared, but not poised, to take action.
 
Little Fear of Inflation
 The Fed officials do not see any inherent inflationary pressures stemming from the week's events that would require such an action. They noted that the dollar was stable against many currencies, and actually rose against those of two key United States trading partners, Canada and Mexico.
In addition, they believe that the inflation rate in the United States is falling as economic growth becomes more subdued.
But they worry that the drop against the mark and yen could be a warning sign that international investors have come to expect inflation in the United States and are less willing to hold assets here as a result.
A determination to stamp out such expectations, which tend to become self-fulfilling, was a prime reason for the series of interest-rate increases that the Fed undertook this spring to keep the markets satisfied about the pace of economic recovery.
 
The Effect of Rates
 Raising interest rates again might appease the banks, companies and professional traders who have sold billions of dollars in exchange for yen and marks and who on Friday brushed aside an Administration-led effort by the Federal Reserve and 15 other central banks to prop up the dollar.
Higher rates would make American bonds more attractive, providing an incentive for traders to buy the dollars needed to buy those bonds.
But higher rates would also put extra drag on the United States economy, which most economists and many Federal Reserve officials say will grow less quickly in the next six months than it did in the winter and spring.
Another increase in interest rates might also irritate the Clinton Administration, although the dollar's perilous condition could limit that irritation.
Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen said in an interview on June 15 -- before the dollar ran into serious trouble -- that given the recent signs of slower consumer spending and economic growth, "I don't see any pressure on the Fed to increase those rates."
To avoid roiling financial markets, senior Fed officials do not like to discuss publicly their plans for changing interest rates and perceptions of currency rates. But they have let their analysis of the market's conflicting signals be known privately.
 
Keeping Tabs
 Top Fed officials watch a broad 10-currency index of the dollar's value that shows the United States currency has fallen very little this year. The Fed's staff presents the index and other statistics to the Federal Reserve Board each Monday morning at a closed-door meeting on monetary policy.
The index shows the dollar's value in comparison to the currencies of Belgium, Britain, Canada, France, Italy, Germany, Japan, Sweden, Switzerland and the Netherlands. The weight of each currency in the index depends on the volume of American trade with each country. Fed officials also keep an eye on other currencies like the Mexican peso.
Even though the yen has been trading at record highs lately, the overall index is higher now than it was in 1987 and during much of the Bush Administration. The index has edged down a couple of percentage points this month -- the latest compilation of the index will be presented today.
A popular rule of thumb among monetary policy experts is that a drop of 10 percent in the trade-weighted value of the dollar produces an increase of 1 percentage point in the inflation rate, but some economists now say the effect is smaller, despite the globalization of the world economy. These economists point to the rising share of American trade with the Latin American and East Asian countries that formally or informally link their currencies to the dollar, and with the oil-producing countries that price their shipments in dollars.
 
Global Factor Played Down
 During Congressional testimony on Wednesday, Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, minimized the effect on inflation from the globalization of the economy. "At this point, we have little aggregate evidence that the increased openness of the United States economy over the past several decades has substantially altered the process of domestic price formation," he said.
But during the same hearing, Mr. Greenspan said the Federal Reserve needed to be ready to raise interest rates in response to expectations of inflation, particularly when the expectations were associated with an economy operating at or near full capacity.
Since World War II, Mr. Greenspan said, "tightened markets became increasingly associated with rising inflation expectations and burgeoning credit demands, which we were sometimes too slow to counter."
Clinton Administration officials, including Alan S. Blinder, a member of the White House Council of Economic Advisers who was approved by the Senate on Friday to become the next vice chairman of the Federal Reserve, have estimated that the economy is operating at 98.5 percent of full capacity -- or better.
The Federal Reserve directly controls only short-term rates, but changing them strongly influences the level of long-term rates. Central bankers try to hold down long-term rates to encourage investors to borrow money for long periods of time and build factories, instead of borrowing for short periods of time and speculating in markets for gold, commodities and other goods. Raising short-term rates tends to dampen inefficient short-term bank lending, economic growth and inflation, eventually pushing down long-term rates as lenders conclude that inflation will not erode their returns.
 
Defensive Policies Abroad
 To be sure, part of the recent fall in the American dollar reflects the strengthening of the German mark and Japanese yen against currencies all over the world, Wall Street economists say. By keeping interest rates fairly high, the German and Japanese central banks have guarded against inflation in their own countries but drained investment money from other countries like the United States, where slackening growth may make higher interest rates less appropriate.
During the last few years, such an international discrepancy in interest rates has often been handled through coordinated changes in rates by central banks. But Robert E. Rubin, the President's national economic adviser, said on Friday that there would not necessarily be talks on such an effort when the leaders of seven industrial democracies meet next month in Naples.
 
Not-So-Distant Memory
 The worst-case chain of events, which no policy makers were willing to discuss publicly, is that the American economy is replaying the events of 1978. As now, the United States economy was then growing faster than those of other large industrial countries and imports soared, so foreigners were being paid more dollars than they were spending for American goods. The value of the dollar began to fall, a problem worsened by an international skepticism about the Carter Administration's foreign policy, similar to the current doubts about the diplomatic skills of the Clinton Administration.
"We're close to that, and certainly the parallels will be drawn, and that will make it more difficult for Secretary Bentsen and President Clinton," said John H. Makin, an economist at the American Enterprise Institute, a research organization in Washington. "We've been over this ground in the living memory of a lot of people in the market."
The Federal Reserve chose in 1978 to raise interest rates fairly gradually, a decision that has been heavily criticized as inadequate since then by monetary economists. When inflation jumped to 11 percent in 1979, the central bank was forced to raise short-term interest rates to 20 percent to bring it under control, provoking a deep recession by 1981 and a long, slow reduction in inflation during the 1980's.
But the current situation is different in that, as Mr. Greenspan noted in his Congressional testimony on Wednesday, the amount of money and loans available in the economy is fairly tight. The money supply was expanding quickly in the late 1970's, and inflation was rising then, rather than falling as it has been during the last year.

HEADLINE: Clinton Aide in Quest Of Latin Trade Riches

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: RIO DE JANEIRO, June 26

 BODY:
Declaring Latin America the world's fastest growing region for American exports, Secretary of Commerce Ronald H. Brown today started a weeklong tour of South America with executives from 22 United States companies.
In particular, Mr. Brown. arriving here on the first stop in a tour of Brazil, Argentina and Chile, said that those three countries "represent immense market opportunities for U.S. businesses." 
 Latin America, the only region where the United States consistently enjoys a trade surplus, doubled its imports of American products in the last seven years, reaching $80 billion last year.
The importance of Latin America to United States trade was underlined last week by the announcement that the trade deficit for April jumped 22 percent compared with March, to $8.4 billion.
But in Latin America, radical decreases in tariffs continue to spur import growth, especially of goods from the United States. In the first four months of 1994, Brazil's imports rose 12 percent and Colombia's imports, 23 percent.
 
Optimism From Unisys
 "There has never been a time when opportunities have been better," said Robert H. Cook, president of the Brazilian subsidiary of the Unisys Corporation. "We are selling branch automation services to banks and document-imaging technology for Brazilian insurance companies."
Describing his mission as "unabashedly pro-business," Mr. Brown said he planned to lobby with Brazilian officials in favor of the Raytheon Company, which is bidding against a French consortium made up of Thomson and Alcatel for a $1 billion radar contract for the Amazon region.
Also hoping for a special hearing in Brasilia was Stephen D. Chesebro, president of Tenneco Gas. The Houston company is interested in a $4 billion project to build a gas pipeline in the late 1990's from Bolivia to Brazil.
Mr. Brown plans to meet this week with the presidents of Argentina and Chile, two countries that he described as leading candidates for admission to an expanded North American Free Trade association. In parallel diplomacy in Washington, President Clinton met with Argentina's President, Carlos Saul Menem, on Friday, and is to see Chile's President, Eduardo Frei Ruiz-Tagle, on Tuesday.
 
Praise for 2 Neighbors
 "Chile is first line for obvious reasons," Mr. Brown said of South America's most open and most prosperous economy. "Argentina would be close behind. There is no question that President Menem has done an extraordinary job with economic reform, privatization and liberalization."
Last week, the Clinton Administration submitted legislation that would allow for fast-track approval of new members to an expanded hemisphere trade pact. Mr. Brown said that South American countries now have to decide whether they want to join the accord's partners or to enter into two-way trade agreements with the United States.
Trailing Argentina closely as a candidate for membership, Mr. Brown said, was Colombia, which "has undergone a good deal of economic liberalization and economic reform."
"We have looked favorably upon that," the Commerce Secretary added.
United States exports to Colombia have virtually doubled since 1991, making the nation the United States' third-largest export destination in South America, after Brazil and Venezuela.
"Colombia already has virtually free trade with the United States," said Ambassador Morris D. Busby, in an interview in Bogota last week. "They have let all imports in -- just look at the American cars on the streets, the Haagen-Dazs in the supermarkets."
This trade opening has not been lost on the Europeans, who have watched their exports to Latin America increase by one-third in the last two years. Last week, technicians for the European Union started to draft a proposal to create, in stages, a free trade zone between Europe and Mercosul, the emerging common market composed of Argentina, Brazil, Uruguay and Paraguay.
To further raise Latin America's profile in world trade, the leaders of a dozen and a half big Latin countries agreed on June 15 to support Mexico's President, Carlos Salinas de Gortari, for the post of Secretary General of the new World Trade Organization.
Speaking in Brazil, where the Finance Minister is also a candidate for the post, Mr. Brown declined to endorse any candidate.

HEADLINE: Tumen Journal;
Wall Around North Korea Has Big Hole in It Here

BYLINE: By PATRICK E. TYLER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TUMEN, China

 BODY:
A convoy of ancient North Korean trucks, some listing precariously under their brimming loads of sacks of Chinese rice, rumbled home recently over the one-lane bridge that spans the Tumen River here.
The bridge and a half-dozen others like it along the 310-mile frontier with North Korea have become a critical lifeline for the Government of that country's President, Kim Il Sung. 
 The $300 million in border trade between China and North Korea, part of the $735 million trade relationship between the two Communist stalwarts, is one reason China was reticent as the United States talked about sanctions on North Korea in response to its nuclear program.
Ever more isolated these days, North Korea is leaning more and more on China to prop up its economy. That is evident here with the increasing shipments of food, fuel and other essential commodities across this frontier.
So far, China's Communist Party leaders are signaling that they intend to stay as close to their longtime ally as "lips to teeth," an old party slogan that the Government has resurrected.
A tough economic embargo would force China to shut down trade along this border, a move that local officials say would wipe out a third of this border region's economic output.
"Any sanctions will only do harm to the peace of the area and it will not make the people of the region happy," said Chao Zhizhong, the deputy director of the state enterprise running the largest trade and barter business. "I am engaged in trade with North Korea, so of course I worry about it."
Sun Jinhu, an official in the Government agency that supervises border trade, added, "We have the same political system and a long history of friendly relations with North Korea, and they depend on China as their elder brother."
There are questions about whether China could enforce sanctions here, where smuggling is rampant. The Communist Party recently removed the mayor of a city in a neighboring province who was running a car smuggling operation.
Clues to the porousness of the border are as common as the right-hand-drive taxicabs in the cities, all smuggled from Japan or Hong Kong.
The river is a thin and unguarded boundary that in the winter can be breached by a short walk, or drive, across the ice.
A tour of the Yanbian Korean Nationality Autonomous Prefecture, as this border enclave in Jilin Province is called, reveals a growing hub of commerce. Local industries manufactured $140 million worth of goods in 1993 that were sold across the border.
Forty percent of the 2.1 million inhabitants of the Chinese prefecture are ethnic Koreans. Most residents speak Korean, which is taught in the schools and is pervasive in street signs, billboards and news broadcasts.
Even local officials are astounded at the tenfold expansion of border trade in the last several years.
Travel agencies in Seoul, South Korea, have been sending more than 15,000 tourists a year to Long White Hair Mountain, a volcanic peak that straddles the border and is the legendary birthplace of the Korean nation. With recently relaxed visa restrictions, the number is expected to reach 100,000 in 1994. Until China and South Korea re-established diplomatic relations last year, it was very difficult for South Koreans to make the pilgrimage.
Now the most popular restaurants and nightclubs in Yanji, the prefecture's capital, are joint ventures with North or South Korean companies.
The Korean market in Yanji is bursting with North Korean goods, proof of economic integration. Its vendors hawk the bountiful array of North Korean seafood, pastries and household goods.
"I have been here 10 years now and business is very good," said Cui Hui Shu, a peddler, holding up the pelt of a North Korean red fox for which she was asking $75.
At Man Kang Hotel in Yanji, a North Korean waitress was wearing a Kim Il Sung button.
In Tumen, the number of convoys moving across this border is increasing, local officials say.
During a recent visit to the border crossing, convoys carrying other staple grains followed the rice trucks. Then came four truckloads of Chinese beer.
The trucks rumbled under a modern Chinese archway and onto the narrow 1,500-foot span to the North Korean border town of Namyang, which appeared drab and lifeless in the distance. The only sound from the North Korean side was the shrill whistle of a steam locomotive, whose antiquity sharpened the contrast with the bustling Chinese side.
Tanker trucks of gasoline also pass this checkpoint to supplement North Korea's critically short supply. The shortage is shutting factories, causing the economy to contract further. Loads of North Korean timber, steel, cement and seafood come back as barter payment.
North Korea's mineral and seafood resources, under strict Government control, are exported because the state gets a good price for them. But people at the border reported that the workers get a meager dole of rice that is barely enough for two meals a day.
A local official in Tumen said that all the evidence suggested that shortages were getting worse across the border. "But even though they have a food problem," he said, "they would never admit it."

GRAPHIC: Photo: For proof of economic integration at the Chinese-North Korean border, all that is necessary is a visit to the market at Yanji, China, where most goods have been imported from North Korea across the Tumen bridge. (Patrick E. Tyler/The New York Times)
 
Map of North Korea highlighting Tumen

HEADLINE: South Jersey: Nice Shore, but Definite Attitude

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: BARNEGAT LIGHT, N.J., June 24

 BODY:
Kathy Larson-Gutowski was chewing her tuna salad the other day and reminiscing about the year she was away.
"I lived there for a year while I was going to school," she said, "but I couldn't wait to get back home." Had she been in brutal towns like Chicago or New York, or distant cities like Paris or Rome? No, she had lived in Bergen County, in North Jersey. To many South Jerseyans, it might as well have been Bombay. 
 New Jersey may be known to night-show hosts as the state with a turnpike for a spine, but within its 21 diverse counties there are sub-categories of Jerseyness that go well beyond the loyalties split between New York and Philadelphia, Giants and Eagles, subs and cheese steaks. This second fault line is the one that divides South Jerseyans from their fellow citizens to the north. If anything, it is growing wider.
"The great growth surge of the 80's didn't do anything to unify the state; it only confirmed their separateness from each other," said James Hughes, a professor of public policy at Rutgers. If anything changed, Dr. Hughes said, it was that the north became an economic center in its own right during the 1980's, and for the south that achievement is still nowhere in sight.
 
Lower Wages and Prices
 "The northern economy that was originally tied to New York has become somewhat more autonomous and independent," he said. "Since north Jersey is the largest suburban office market in the country, it is therefore a much stronger component of the national economy than the southern economy."
Comparable houses in the north fetch half again as much as those in the south, which some say starts at Exit 8 on the New Jersey Turnpike, or by Interstate 195 from Trenton to the shore. Wages are lower in the south, and outside of the north's embattled cities, the 10 poorest towns in New Jersey, measured in per capita income, are all in the south, according to the 1990 Census.
Distinguishing north and south in pure money terms makes a lot of sense to people down here, at the northern tip of Long Beach Island. For if ever north and south shall meet, it is here on the the Jersey Shore -- only outsiders call it the beach -- when the northerners who journey down to their summer homes or crowd into rented bungalows come face to face with southerners for a few weeks or months.
The proximity mixes mutual affection and mutual need with a dose of regional reserve.
 
Looked At Like Yokels
 "I think the feeling on the part of North Jerseyans is that they are the sophisticates and the educated people, and we're the local yokels who never get out of the county and who leave the keys in our cars at night," said Jamie Faurot, a local craftswoman and shop owner. "It's like we're not moving fast enough for them."
A. Jerome Walnut, clerk of the Barnegat Light borough building inspector's office and long a resident of the southern shore, deals with homeowners and builders from both ends of the state when they walk in seeking permits and paperwork.
"North Jersey has always been more industrial and commercial, the pace of life is more intense, and I think when they come down here they often want a faster pace of service," he said. "I think we're a little more methodical down here, instead of wanting everything done in the last minute."
But Lynne Goldmark, a northerner from farthest Passaic County who rents each summer at the shore, argued that visitors did not have time for patience.
"If we're only going to be here for two or three weeks, we need to get things done in a hurry," she said. "I think that's how we got a reputation for being pushy. Anyway, I've known people down here for years, and it doesn't keep us from being friends."
 
Environmental Frustration
 South Jersey is currently up in arms about a thornier issue than how some people behave in a checkout line. Much of the state's southern half is governed by strict environmental laws that southerners believe the northerners enacted. One is the Pinelands Act, which has sharply curtailed development in a forest sanctuary covering a quarter of New Jersey's land. It is a constant source of frustration for and complaint from inland landowners.
Here on the barrier island, the issue is the state's Coastal Area Facilities Review Act, or Cafra, which restricts development in shore areas. It is unfailing fodder for conversation in local bars and coffee shops. Callers to the Long Beach township offices hear a recording referring all questions about Cafra to the state Department of Environmental Protection and Energy before listing extensions for different town offices.
"People from North Jersey are my customers, my co-workers and my friends, so I don't want them to think I don't like them," said Ron Fella, a local real-estate agent who also operates Broadway Pizza, in the morning shadow of the historic Barnegat Light here, during the summer.
 
North Makes the Laws
 "But the Cafra law is a law made by northern environmentalists and forced on South Jersey and it's been very bad for us," he said. "It's gotten so that I couldn't sell a house once because Cafra would not let the buyer put in a dock he wanted."
Thomas J. Keller, a local builder who went in to Mr. Fella's place for a slice, agreed.
"We're absolutely connected to the north, because North Jersey makes the law," he said. "But we're absolutely separate entities from them."
Even the weather has put the south in thrall to the north, Mr. Fella said, only half-jokingly. Last winter's heavy snows forced so many school cancellations that many North Jersey schools did not begin letting out until this weekend. In the south, most children have been free for a week.
With school still in, northern parents are postponing their trips to the shore, Mr. Fella said. The result: a hot spring, good beach weather, but slow business.
"So," Mr. Fella said, "we even have to worry about how much it snows up there."

GRAPHIC: Photo: "I lived there for a year while I was going to school," Kathy Larson-Gutowski, a resident of Barnegat Light, N.J., said of the year she spent in Bergen County in North Jersey. "But I couldn't wait to get back home." (Photograph by William E. Sauro/The New York Times)
 
Map of New Jersey showing location of Barnegat Light.

HEADLINE: Office Market in the Suburbs Is Showing Signs of Recovery

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WHITE PLAINS, June 23

 BODY:
The troubled office market in Westchester County is showing the first signs of a rebound, following the lead of the office markets in New York City and Fairfield County in Connecticut.
Westchester's vacancy rate is now 25.4 percent, down from 26.9 percent six months ago and 27.1 percent at midyear 1993, according to a survey by the Rostenberg-Doern Company, a real-estate concern. 
 White Plains continues to have a huge surplus of office space, but the vacancy rate has dropped to 33.7 percent from 35 percent six months ago, the survey found.
"After several difficult years, the Westchester office market is moving in a positive direction with steady leasing activity, higher absorption and lower vacancy rates," said John H. Rostenberg, a principal of Rostenberg-Doern, which has offices in Purchase, N.Y., and Stamford, Conn.
One of the largest owners of office space in downtown White Plains, the Robert Martin Company of Elmsford, N.Y., said leasing activity has been robust. Michael Grossman, a vice president of the company, said its White Plains office occupancy rate was now 95 percent.
Mr. Rostenberg noted that the decision by New York State to grant $13.5 million in incentives to keep the Ciba Corporation in Westchester may encourage business relocations in the area.
"Westchester County is starting to benefit from the same kind of aggressive economic development efforts that have rejuvenated Fairfield County," he said.
Another trend, Mr. Rosentenberg said, is that the commercial office market in New York City has improved, with rents in Manhattan rising to a point where some companies will consider Westchester as a relocation option.
The vacancy rate in Manhattan now stands at 15 percent, according to figures supplied by Newmark & Company Real Estate of New York City, compared with 16.36 percent a year ago.
The New Jersey office market, too, shows signs of improvement, said Seena Stein, president of Newmark Partners of Mountainside, N.J.
"Average rents are on the increase after many years of decline," she said, "and most markets throughout New Jersey are experiencing a positive net absorption." The office vacancy rate in New Jersey is 21.8 percent, down from 22.2 percent at the beginning of the year.
One suburban office market that was hit early by the effects of the recession was Fairfield County. But the county and one of its chief office markets, Stamford, have seen two years of improvement.
In 1989, Fairfield County's office vacancy rate stood at 29.8 percent, while Stamford's was 29.6 percent. Since then, the county and the Stamford office markets have made a slow but steady recovery. Fairfield County's office vacancy rate is now 19.7 percent while Stamford's is 18.1 percent.
Mr. Rostenberg credited the turnaround to stable building ownership and competitive asking rents, as well as tax reform and an aggressive economic development program waged by the state of Connecticut.
"In the last year or two there haven't been many corporate downsizings in Fairfield County, so the market has been able to catch its breath," Mr. Rostenberg said.
For places like Westchester it is an encouraging indicator that large corporations from New York City may be looking at the suburbs as a relocation option. One large New York City employer -- Mastercard International -- is considering moving most of its operations out of New York City to sites in Westchester or Fairfield.
John McCarthy, a principal of the real-estate brokerage firm of McCarthy O'Callaghan, said the troubles experienced in Westchester over the last few years have been due in part to cutbacks by some of the nation's largest employers, like Nynex and International Business Machines.
Barring any further large corporate cutbacks, he said, the office market is expected to continue to recover.
"It's a slow process," Mr. McCarthy said. "We're starting to scratch away at it, but there is no quick fix."

HEADLINE: HEALTH INDUSTRY IS CHANGING ITSELF AHEAD OF REFORM

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM 

 BODY:
A wave of mergers and alliances is transforming the nation's trillion-dollar health care system. Without waiting for the outcome of the Congressional health debate, providers of medical care -- including hospitals, physician groups and nursing homes -- are joining to form bigger and bigger networks.
Some of the combinations are being put together by hospitals, like the $1.08 billion deal in which the country's largest for-profit hospital chain, Columbia/HCA Healthcare, agreed to buy the largest chain of surgery centers, Medical Care America, last month. Another such combination is New York Hospital's regional alliance with seven other nonprofit hospitals, two nursing homes and four walk-in clinics, all of which send their most complicated cases to the big Upper East Side hospital. 
 Other deals are being struck by physicians, who have been abandoning solo practices to join medical groups and now fear that even those groups are not strong enough. One example: Mullikin Medical Enterprises, owned by 200 physicians in Southern California, is acquiring the practices of other medical groups around the state. It is also negotiating an alliance with a West Coast hospital chain under which the hospitals would lower their fees for patients admitted by Mullikin doctors.
Insurance companies and health maintenance organizations are also racing to form larger medical networks, signing up hospitals and groups of doctors and sometimes even joining with each other. Two weeks ago, Metropolitan Life Insurance and Travelers announced a joint venture that will provide health insurance for 13 million people nationwide; the companies hope that many of these people will receive their care through the new venture's medical networks. And FHP International and Takecare, two California-based H.M.O.'s, recently combined to cover 1.6 million people in eight Western states.
The goal of all these combinations is to become large enough to offer the comprehensive and conveniently available services and the reasonable prices needed to assure a steady flow of patients from the increasingly demanding groups that buy medical care.
Those buyers are themselves joining in larger and more powerful groups. For example, employers in dozens of areas, from Long Island to San Francisco, have formed alliances to buy medical care for their workers, the better to negotiate satisfactory deals with the growing medical networks.
But what does it mean when both providers and purchasers of health care coalesce, when a system in which total spending is expected to exceed $1 trillion this year is increasingly controlled by large entities?
For the patient, care could improve as the growing medical networks invest in sophisticated computerized systems that analyze the care received by their many thousands of patients and find ways to improve it. The systems could also save money for patients and their insurers by identifying the kinds of treatments that do little good.
"The quality of care will inevitably improve as we begin to gather these people under the same roof," said Jon Glaudemann, a vice president of Aetna Health Plans, one of the largest medical networks.
 
'Who Will Discipline Those Guys?'
 But the consolidation, taken to the extreme, could also cause problems. "If two or three health care companies have sewn up all the doctors and the hospitals and the health system gets oligopolized, who will discipline those guys?" said Uwe E. Reinhardt, a Princeton University health economist.
"The trouble with oligopolies, where there are only a few buyers and sellers, is that they become very sloppy," he added. For example, he said, doctors will say that managed-care companies raise prices and keep the difference. Some California insurers already allot only 70 cents of every premium dollar for health care. "The rest they keep," he said.
And unlike other industries, Professor Reinhardt warned, the health care business may not have outside competitors to push it to improve itself, the way, say, Japanese car makers pushed Detroit to improve its performance.
The poor may also suffer, as groups of employers insuring many people in a community gain the size to negotiate harder with hospitals and reduce the subsidies for patients without health coverage, said Bruce C. Vladeck, who directs the Government's Medicare and Medicaid programs.
 
Fewer Hospitals, Fewer Workers
 Other potential losers include many people in the medical business, like nurses and other hospital employees, who would lose their jobs as the industry consolidated and eliminated, for example, the 250,000 underused hospital beds, 30 percent of the total. At least 15 percent of the 5,500 community hospitals may close, Government officials say. The endangered-species list may also include thousands of "surplus" physician specialists who could not find a place in any of the medical networks being formed.
Finally, several hundred small insurance companies and many regional health maintenance organizations are at risk. "Even among the largest insurance companies, who will survive is in doubt," said Kevin Moley, a former senior health policy maker in the Bush Administration.
The consolidation trend is most evident in places where managed health care is strong, like Minneapolis-St. Paul and Southern California. But even in New York, 47 of 57 hospitals surveyed in March had already joined local hospital networks or systems. The survey, by the Greater New York Hospital Association, recorded ties among hospitals and with nursing homes, neighborhood health centers, home health programs and physician groups.
To be sure, the fragmented health care industry is still a long way from the concentration of an industry like automobiles. But in some places, the consolidation has been striking.
In the Minneapolis-St. Paul area, for example, 1.7 million people -- 70 percent of the total -- are now members of just three big health maintenance organizations, each of which owns its own medical facilities. Each of the H.M.O.'s is the product of merger after merger.
 
The Path of Consolidation
 For doctors and their patients, the consolidation has meant frequent change. Take Dr. Eric Anderson, a Minneapolis internist. His longtime patients have followed him as he moved from a neighborhood one-man office that was affiliated with several hospitals to a larger hospital-owned system that in turn will be absorbed next month by an even larger health care network called Allina.
Allina and the two other big health networks, Health Partners and Blue Cross, compete for the business of two employer groups. One concentrates the buying power of the largest 25 companies; the other represents small businesses.
Badgered by the large buyers, the big H.M.O.'s have held the line on prices this year. Indeed, the state announced on Friday that Allina had reduced its insurance rates for state employees by 25 percent.
"We're in the middle of a major cost war," said George Halvorsen, chief executive of Health Partners. "The hospitals have announced plans to close half of their remaining beds."
Among the most visible players in the consolidation of the health care business is the Columbia/HCA Healthcare Corporation. The giant hospital chain has already assembled the leading medical care network in 10 Sun Belt markets -- Miami, Tampa, the Florida panhandle, Atlanta, Richmond, Nashville, Dallas, Houston, El Paso, and Louisville, Ky. -- according to Richard L. Scott, chief executive.
Investor-owned hospital chains like Columbia are also buying venerable nonprofit community hospitals and closing some of them. Other nonprofit hospitals have formed managed-care ventures and alliances with Blue Cross and Blue Shield associations in Philadelphia, Kansas City, Mo., and a number of states.
Seventy-one percent of 1,200 hospitals recently surveyed by Deloitte & Touche, the accounting and consulting firm, said they were joining an integrated system that included other hospitals, outpatient units or physician group practices. In the survey, released last week, one-third of the hospitals said they had acquired or were acquiring physician practices.
Some other traditions are crumbling in medicine as doctors abandon their one-person offices to join group practices. And some physicians stand to profit. For example, many primary-care doctors are selling their practices to hospitals or insurance companies for hundreds of thousands of dollars.

GRAPHIC: Chart: "Putting It Together" lists big health care companies that have merged to increase their power (Source: Irving Levin Associates) (pg. D4)

HEADLINE: Tool Orders Off in May

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
United States machine tool orders fell 18.5 percent during May as higher interest rates slowed economic activity, an industry report showed today.
Economists monitor tool orders to gauge factory output and business investment. 
 The Association for Manufacturing Technology, which prepared the report, said orders declined to $336.85 million in May after rising 11.6 percent in April, initially reported as an 11.7 percent gain.
Compared with a year earlier, tool orders were up 6.7 percent in May.
While higher interest rates are slowing domestic economic growth, foreign demand for goods made in the United States has been restrained by recessions in Germany and Japan.
"If exports begin to turn around, this could be a very good year," Albert Moore, president of the industry association, said.

HEADLINE: On the Right Track With Russia

 BODY:
Whatever problems President Clinton may face elsewhere in foreign affairs, his Russia policy is yielding significant advances.
Last week Russia joined NATO's Partnership for Peace, intended to foster military cooperation between once-hostile alliances; Mr. Clinton and President Boris Yeltsin agreed to meet in Washington in September; the U.S. and Russia signed agreements to build a space station together and to have an American consortium develop oil and gas reserves on Russia's Sakhalin Island. They also made progress on curbing nuclear dangers. 
 Mr. Clinton is betting that continued cooperation will help Russia grow into a country its neighbors can live with. To revert to the old cold-war policy of containment now would only aid the ambitions of nationalists like Vladimir Zhirinovsky, confirming their view of the West as an enemy.
Military ties with Russia are critical. The Russian Army is going through a crisis of confidence brought on by a lost war in Afghanistan, a lost empire in Eastern Europe and a lost country, the Soviet Union. Mix that with loss of military pay and stature, soldiers who are returning home with no place to live, corruption and draft-dodging, and the result is a bitter if not explosive brew.
The demoralized army at first objected to joining the NATO venture. It could not bear to settle for the same terms as Latvia, Poland or others in the Partnership for Peace. It sought a broader role more suited to Russia's stature, but in the end accepted the same formal relationship with NATO as the others, along with a pledge of "enhanced dialogue" on matters like Bosnia. If the military partnership succeeds, it could give the Russian Army a new sense of itself, turning it into a force for peace and heading off a second cold war.
Brokering the deal on oil and gas exploration is the least the U.S. could do to help revitalize Russia's devastated economy. Building a space station gives gainful employment to Russian scientists who otherwise might put their skills to dangerous ends.
American encouragement has been decisive in getting Russia and Ukraine to pick up the pace of dismantling their nuclear arsenals. Last week Russia agreed to shut down plutonium production reactors lest the material be stolen and sold abroad.
Mr. Clinton gets little attention or credit for his prudent Russia policy. He has made few speeches defining and defending his quite sensible approach. That has left the field open to his detractors and allowed the public to focus on Somalia, Haiti and Rwanda. But those problems are far less important than a solid relationship with a healthy Russia.

HEADLINE: Protectionism, Alive in Washington

 BODY:
President Clinton preached open trade in April when he signed an international trade agreement in Morocco -- proclaiming it would add $1 trillion to the U.S. economy over the next few years. But when the Administration withdrew from public scrutiny to translate the Marrakesh accord into U.S. law, it lurched toward protectionism.
At issue are anti-dumping rules, which countries use to retaliate against foreigners who sell goods at prices below their production costs. The Marrakesh accord puts limits on the rules so that countries will no longer be able to exploit them by finding dumping where none exists. But the Administration's draft language includes provisions that either change the words of the Marrakesh accord -- which the U.S. signed along with more than 100 other nations -- or twist their meaning in such a way that the U.S. would be able to exclude more imports than the accord intended. 
 To prove dumping, the U.S. typically must show that the price of an import falls below its production cost; the higher the estimated cost, the higher the price the foreigner must charge. The Marrakesh accord permits new foreign companies to base prices on production costs at the end of a start-up period. By that time, costs have usually declined. But the Administration changes the accord to include some earlier and higher costs. That makes dumping easier to "prove."
Another Administration provision would have the effect of forcing foreigners to raise prices when output is low -- during a recession, for example -- just when domestic companies are cutting prices to survive. The Administration also plans to waive part of the accord that would rule out retaliation for minor dumping infractions -- where prices are set within 2 percent of costs. Finally, the accord permits retaliation only to the extent that dumped imports have injured domestic industry; but the Administration would allow retaliation beyond the injuries caused by goods sold at unfair prices.
The pattern here is to rewrite Marrakesh for protectionist purposes. And while this pattern provides new evidence of the Administration's split personality on trade, it comes as no surprise to those who have witnessed other internal battles. Officials like Laura Tyson of the Council of Economic Advisers have fought from the beginning to promote more open trade; others seem determined to protect steel mills and other politically powerful industries from competition.
The draft document is an assault on consumers, who pay higher prices to domestic companies when they are insulated from competition. It is also an assault on domestic exporters -- like pharmaceutical and computer companies -- whose products will be shut out of foreign markets when trading partners mimic the U.S. rules. That reflects poorly on a President who claims Nafta and the Marrakesh accord as hallmarks of his first term.

HEADLINE: The Church Can Learn From Its Daughters;
'Cultural Imperialism'

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Your June 15 article about the Vatican's stand on the United Nations international conference on population and development calls it a conference on women's rights. The draft of the document discussed during the New York preparatory session is 82 pages long, and it covers topics on population control policies. Only 7 pages are dedicated to development. The agenda being pushed includes a heavy load of population control, allegedly on the assumption that reducing population will reduce poverty. A scientifically respectable proof of this thesis is yet to be put forth.
One may ask who is pushing this agenda and why. The effort to impose the values prevailing among United States delegates to the conference is a case of "cultural imperialism" and an attempt at social control of third world countries. (Nor are those values a unanimous consensus among the people of the United States.) 
 Complying with the "recommendations" on the limit to the number of children allowed per woman will be rewarded with economic aid, while those countries that fail to implement the policies will be deprived of aid. What else is this but imperialism?
As you point out, support for the Vatican's position is high among the Islamic countries, some of Europe and much of Latin America. Clearly, it cannot be assumed this support is based on religious values, but rather on a respectful approach to the dignity of the human person. Perhaps it is time to hear the real political and economic reasons for wanting to curb population growth in the third world.
Undoubtedly, there are problems to be solved in population, development and women's rights and health. The United Nations can do better than this.
 
TONA VARELA
New Rochelle, N.Y., June 17, 1994

 CORRECTION:
The Pulse chart in the Metro Section yesterday included incorrect figures in some editions for the number of lawyers per 100 people in New York, New Jersey, Connecticut and the United States. The correct figures are: New York, 0.4 in 1987 and 0.5 in 1993; New Jersey, 0.3 in 1987 and 0.4 in 1993; Connecticut, 0.3 in 1987 and 0.5 in 1993; United States, 0.3 in 1987 and 0.3 in 1993.

GRAPHIC: Chart: Disciplinary sanctions against lawyers have risen steadily. Public discipline of attorneys, which can range from reprimand to disbarment, jumped nationally from 1987 to 1993 and by even more in New York, New Jersey and Connecticut. Locally, legal experts point to be the recession and the competitive nature of the legal business as reasons for the increasing misuse of client funds and other ethical lapses. The figures also reflect greater efforts by state bar associations and the courts to root out dishonest lawyers. Of all the nation's lawyers, one in six practices in New York, New Jersey and Connecticut. (Sources: American Bar Association, New Jersey Office of Attorney Ethics, New York State Bar Association, Census Bureau)
