HEADLINE: U.S. Hopes North Korea Can Be Induced to Extend Nuclear Freeze

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration is confident that it will able to verify the freeze on the North Korean nuclear progam in the next round of talks, to begin on July 8. But the big issue is whether the monitoring can be extended after the negotiations.
To monitor the freeze, two officials of the International Atomic Energy Agency are stationed at the Yongbyon nuclear site. From there, they conduct inspections to make sure that the 8,000 fuel rods the North Koreans have removed from the Yongbyon reactor and dumped into a cooling pond are kept there and not diverted to produce plutonium, an ingredient in atomic bombs. 
 When the inspectors are not present, the agency relies on cameras to keep an eye on the stockpile.
 
Frozen for the Moment
 "Based on our understanding at this moment, the major elements of the program are frozen," the State Department spokesman, Michael McCurry, said today. But the crucial issue is not freezing the North Korean program in the near term. It is the long-term disposition of the the rods.
The three-foot-long rods embody the future of the North Korean nuclear program. If taken to the North Korean reprocessing plant, Administration officials say, the rods could be used to produce enough plutonium in six months for four to five bombs.
In an effort to persuade the North Koreans to make the temporary freeze permanent, the Clinton Administration is considering a package of economic and diplomatic inducements, including an initial exchange of liaison offices.
The best way to cap the North Korean program, Administration officials said, would be to procure the rods and take them to another country for reprocessing. Another approach would be to store them in North Korea in a way that would make it hard for the material to be diverted quickly, like encasing them in concrete.
Whatever arrangement Washington proposes, the North Korean nuclear potential to lurch forward has cast a shadow over the coming talks. Since it takes time for the rods to cool before they can be processed to make plutonium, experts say, North Korea's short-term freeze will not have a major effect on its nuclear ambitions. North Korea's offer to freeze its program is only for the duration of the high-level talks in Geneva.
 
Schedule for the Talks
 Mr. McCurry said today that the first days of the talks will be devoted to presentations by each side. After initial discussions, the two sides will take a break. They will reconvene the following week to discuss each side's position, Mr. McCurry added.
One of the Clinton Administration's fears is that after the talks end, the North Koreans may insist on taking the rods to their reprocessing plant to make more plutonium. The North is allowed to reprocess the plutonium under the Nuclear Nonproliferation Treaty and might even allow inspectors to be present.
But such a move would be worrisome from Washington's perspective, even if inspectors were present, since it would make an important advance in North Korea's nuclear program. Washington has drawn the line at further processing of plutonium.
Even as the the Administration prepared for its talks, military planning is continuing. Officials said today that the Navy was planning to send two minesweepers and an amphibious vessel from Hawaii to Japan this week as part of Washington's effort to quietly reinforce its military presence on the Korean peninsula. Traveling at about 8 knots, the minesweepers are scheduled to arrive by the end of July.

Koreas Set Date for Summit
 PANMUNJOM, Korea, Tuesday, June 28 (Reuters) -- North and South Korea agreed in principle today to hold the first summit meeting between their two Presidents in an effort to end half a century of hostility. The meeting will be held on July 25 in Pyongyang, the North Korean capital.
A senior South Korean Government official at this border village to monitor North-South talks on arranging the summit meeting said the date had been set and confirmed by both sides. But he said Seoul wanted a date and venue for a second round of summit talks before committing itself unreservedly to a historic first round.
The South Korean official said the South wanted to setle today on a date and venue for a second round of talks. The North wanted to work out details of a second summit mmeting only after the first one had taken place.
South Korea, although keen to see a summit meeting, is determined that it will not be held at the expense of concessions to the North over its suspected development of nuclear weapons.

HEADLINE: Haiti's Strongman Reported Ready to Retire in October

BYLINE: By GARRY PIERRE-PIERRE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, June 27

 BODY:
Haiti's military strongman has said he will step down when his term as commander of the Haitian armed forces expires in October, a high-level military officer said today.
The officer, who spoke on condition that he not be identified, said the commander, Lieut. Gen. Raoul Cedras, had merely reaffirmed a long-held intention to retire and was not acting out of fear that the United States would make good on its threat to use force to oust him. General Cedras was a leader of the coup that toppled the elected President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, in September 1991. 
 The officer said the thinking was that if General Cedras resigned after serving out his term, that would show him to be a strong leader who did not buckle under international pressure. But his voluntary departure could also provide a face-saving solution to the three-year crisis.
"The general knows that he has to leave," the officer said. "The question has always been when and under what circumstances."
 
Three-Year Terms
 The Haitian Constitution provides three-year terms of office for the armed forces commander, but the term is renewable by the President.
In a letter to General Cedras dated Dec. 16, 1993, Father Aristide nominated Maj. Gen. Jean-Claude Duperval as the next commander in chief. But General Duperval, the deputy assistant commander of the army, does not seem to have any real power in the inner circle of the high command.
It was unclear whether the officer describing General Cedras's plan was floating the idea of his departure to elicit a response from the United States, which has been leading the international effort to restore Father Aristide. American diplomats insist that General Cedras should depart immediately so the country can organize parliamentary elections scheduled for November and pave the way for the return of Father Aristide.
"He thinks we're bluffing," a United States diplomat said of General Cedras when informed of the unidentified officer's remarks. "We've got cards he does not know we have. We're asking him to throw in his cards before it's too late."
Economic sanctions, including a near total ban on commercial air traffic and all financial transactions with the United States, have been imposed on Haiti to force the military leaders to surrender power.
Critics of the military have compared recent defiant statements by General Cedras to those of Jean-Claude Duvalier, the former Haitian dictator, who in February 1986 drove around the city telling the Haitian people that his hold on power was as strong as a monkey's tail. A few days later, he was whisked away to exile.
 
On His Own Terms
 But the officer said General Cedras remained confident that public sentiment in the United States did not support an invasion and that having seen the worst of the sanctions, he planned to leave on his own terms.
Father Aristide's recent statements criticizing the Clinton Administration's policy on Haitian refugees, and his seemingly wavering support for a United States-led military intervention, are seen here as having emboldened the military.
In an interview on National Public Radio on Saturday, Father Aristide said that he would not return to Haiti if there was a military intervention. In other interviews, he has said that he would support a "surgical strike" to remove the military leaders.
"It does complicate the equation," the American diplomat said. "This is one of the reasons that this has been such a tough nut to crack."
The officer said that if General Cedras resigned, other elements of the Governors Island accord would be invoked. Under the terms of the accord, signed by General Cedras and Father Aristide last July, the general was to retire, the military and the police were to be separated into different forces and the powerful police chief, Lieut. Col. Joseph Michel Francois, was to be transferred from that post.
 
Balks at Resigning
 But that accord stalled in October after General Cedras refused to relinquish power. The officer said General Cedras was willing to honor his promise but had stayed on because of pressure from officers and soldiers who felt their future would be clouded after his departure.
Some Haitian analysts say that the United States is not eager to dismantle the Haitian Army, which despite its shortcomings is the only functioning institution in the country.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Battered Dollar
Ten years ago, a dollar bought 250 Japanese yen; now, it buys slightly more than 100. The dollar's slide against the yen has evoked cries of alarm from Wall Street. But for many Americans who are not engaged in trading currencies or securities, the dollar's woes have not meant very much. Economic Analysis. [Page A1.] 
 The dollar hit another record low against the yen in Tokyo but later rebounded in New York, buoyed by falling commodity prices, a rising bond market and a belief among some investors that a weak dollar is a bigger problem for Japan than for the U.S. [D6.]
A waiting game was played in banks and investment houses around the world, as currency traders were again left guessing whether or how far the dollar might fall. [D6.]
How can the yen be so strong when Japan's economy is still scraping bottom and its once-seamless political order has unraveled for the second time in a year? Economic Analysis. [D6.]
 
Companies
The U.S. Supreme Court, in a 5-4 decision, endorsed a framework for Federal regulation of cable television but stopped short of upholding a 1992 law that requires cable systems to retransmit the signals of local broadcasters. [D1.] Both sides claimed some element of victory in the decision, but the effect of the ruling won't be felt for perhaps a year or more. [D18.]
RSA Data Security, which controls a coding technique that secures computer-data secrecy, has won industry support. But officials fear RSA's system could limit their control over national security access and are promoting another security standard. [D1.]
Kemper directors voted unanimously to accept a $3.2 billion purchase offer by Conseco, a Carmel, Ind., life insurer whose proposal exceeded an earlier bid by General Electric. [D1.]
General Motors is planning to appoint G. Richard Wagoner Jr., its chief financial officer and head of worldwide purchasing, to run its North American automotive operations. [D3.] The F.B.I. is said to have sought German prosecutors' help in an inquiry of G.M.'s claims that a former official committed industrial espionage. [D2.]

Novell and Wordperfect completed their merger and said they would show their first product at New York's PC Expo. [D3.]

HEADLINE: SHAKE-UP AT THE WHITE HOUSE: CHIEF OF STAFF -- Man in the News;
Once at Odds With White House, Now at Its Helm: Leon Edward Panetta

BYLINE: By NEIL A. LEWIS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Leon E. Panetta, the man now charged with making the White House more efficient and focused, learned early in his career about the power a White House chief of staff can wield.
Mr. Panetta, who began public life as a Republican, was the director of civil rights for the old Department of Health, Education and Welfare in 1969, in the early days of Richard M. Nixon's Presidency. He sought to deal quickly with more than 600 lingering school desegregation cases but was told bluntly to back off to preserve the White House's "southern strategy," the effort to woo the South from the Democrats. 
 When Mr. Panetta pressed ahead anyway, he was dismissed, largely at the instigation of H. R. Haldeman, Mr. Nixon's chief of staff, Mr. Panetta recalled in a 1971 memoir, "Bring Us Together: The Nixon Team and the Civil Rights Retreat."
Today, in a markedly different political climate, Mr. Panetta was appointed White House chief of staff. But the job he has assumed still carries great authority to enforce Administration policy and political consistency.
 
From Nixon to Lindsay
 "I now have the opportunity to help direct the office of the Presidency and to try to make it serve the President in an effective and efficient manner," he said. "It will not be easy. Changes will be made in consultation with the President, but they will be made in the spirit of making the best use of the talent and abilities that are here."
After he was dismissed from the Nixon Administration, Mr. Panetta began working for a man who would provide a model of a Republican-turned-Democrat, Mayor John V. Lindsay of New York. Mr. Panetta was Mayor Lindsay's coordinator for Federal and state aid, working out of Manhattan, Albany and Washington.
Mr. Panetta returned to his native California in 1971 to practice law, seemingly destined to do so for the rest of his life. But in 1976, by then a Democrat, he ran for Congress and won a narrow victory in California's rich agricultural belt.
The district, the state's 16th and the heart of John Steinbeck country, follows the coastline down past Monterey, Santa Cruz and the Big Sur region inland to the fertile fields that first drew Mr. Panetta's immigrant parents.
 
Son of Italian Immigrants
 In the House, Mr. Panetta first gained prominence on the Agriculture Committee, tending to his local constituents' interests, like promoting the legalization of "guest workers" from Mexico to help with the harvest. He was returned to the House election after election with ever wider margins.
In 1978 he began serving on the Budget Committee. For four years, until Mr. Clinton tapped him in 1992 to be director of the Office of Management and Budget, he was the committee's chairman. In that job he demonstrated that mastering the details of legislation and policy can be a source of formidable power.
Leon Edward Panetta was born in Monterey, Calif., on June 28, 1938, the son of Carmelo and Carmelina Panetta. His parents had immigrated from Italy, first to Sheridan, Wyo., where they joined a small Italian community that worked the copper mines, and then to California.
The Panettas eventually settled in Monterey where they opened a restaurant, Carmelo's Cafe, where they shared cooking duties. When Carmelo Panetta became ill in 1947, he bought a walnut ranch, where Leon Panetta grew up among the gnarled trees.
He received his bachelor's degree from the University of Santa Clara in 1960 and was an editor of the law review at the University of Santa Clara's law school, from which he graduated in 1963.
 
A Frugal Philosophy
 In his remarks at the White House today, Mr. Panetta paid tribute to his parents, saying their experience as immigrants had fueled his interest in public service.
Mr. Panetta was not a natural choice for the job of budget director because he had criticized Mr. Clinton's economic proposals during the campaign. He contended that Mr. Clinton was not paying enough attention to reducing the deficit, a position he has taken with varying degrees of alacrity in behind-the-scenes economic fights within the Administration.
Mr. Panetta is known to his friends as frugal in personal habits as well as policy preferences. Most weekends during his 16 years in Congress, he flew home to California. His wife, Sylvia, whom he married in 1962, ran his district office there. They have three grown sons.
During the week, Mr. Panetta roomed with three other members of the House in a small two-bedroom apartment near Capitol Hill.
Mr. Panetta shared the living room with Representative Charles E. Schumer, Democrat of Brooklyn, while Representatives George Miller, Democrat of California, and Marty Russo of Illinois each had a bedroom.
 
Long Hours in the Office
 Mr. Miller said today that the foursome spent much of their time debating politics and policy, but Mr. Panetta did so the least for the simple reason that he often returned to the office at night.
"I think he really will substantially improve the Administration's understanding of Capitol Hill," said Mr. Miller. "He's someone who knows when it makes sense to stand and fight, and when it makes sense to make a deal."
When Mr. Panetta became budget director and was no longer a member of Congress, he moved out of the group apartment and got his own place. It was, he told friends with a smile, his personal nod to the separation of powers.

HEADLINE: THE BATTERED DOLLAR: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Falls But Rallies Some at End

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
The dollar hit another record low against the Japanese yen in Tokyo early today but later bounced back in New York, buoyed by falling commodity prices, a rising bond market and a new sense among some investors that the weak dollar is a bigger problem for Japan than it is for the United States.
Currency traders say there is a growing belief that the dollar has hit bottom for now, trading lower than the economic fundamentals would dictate, and is thus due for at least a temporary bounce upward. 
 Given that, many traders began moving in today to buy up the cheap dollar, betting that the currency's sharp slide is over -- at least for now.
 
Links Among Markets
 The dollar's rise in New York trading both fed, and was fed by, a surge in the bond and stock markets. Those in turn were being fueled by a tumble in commodities prices, which are closely watched by bond and stock traders as a harbinger of inflation.
The dollar started today's trading by hitting new post-World War II lows in Japan and Europe. Despite intervention by the Bank of Japan, the dollar finished in Tokyo at 99.93 yen, after trading as low as 99.50 yen.
Although the dollar dipped below the 100-yen level last week, it always bounced back and never, before today, ended trading at less than one yen to one cent.
The dollar tried to rally in Europe, but ultimately ended there below 100 yen as well. In New York, however, the dollar rebounded, ending at 100.45 yen. Still, that was down slightly from 100.65 yen on Friday.
The dollar also ended at 1.5840 German marks in New York, down slightly from 1.5850 on Friday, but above its recent low of 1.5730 marks.
In the currency markets, there is no long run, only the short run, measured in hours or at the most days. And today's short-run attitude was that the dollar has gone below a level that makes sense in economic terms.
Many analysts are beginning to express the view that the dollar's slide is now a much bigger worry for Japan than it is for the United States. The yen has become so expensive against the dollar that it is pricing some Japanese exporters out of the market. At the same time, the cheap dollar is a boon for American exporters and multinational companies.
"There is more of a yen crisis than a dollar crisis," said Robert Giordano, chief of economic research at Goldman, Sachs & Company. "Japanese exporters are writhing in pain because many of them simply are not competitive at one yen equals one cent. For them this is disastrous. For us what's the big deal?"
That view was shared by the Washington-based nonpartisan Economic Policy Institute, which issued a statement today signed by a number of top economists. "There is no dollar crisis," the statement said. "The current problem is mainly of the yen, not the dollar. The dollar is not collapsing with respect to other currencies."
Of course, if the dollar was to continue its slide, the Federal Reserve might feel compelled to raise interest rates to make dollar-denominated investments more attractive to international investors. But it is still unclear whether the Fed will make such a move at its next policy-making meetings scheduled for July 5 and 6.
On Friday, currency traders were dismissing as "lame" the Clinton Administration's enlisting of help from 15 other nations to buy dollars to prop up the currency. But that move may not have been as ineffective as it first seemed to market watchers.
"The truth is that intervention is not a panacea, but it can be helpful in breaking a slide that has little economic foundation," Mr. Giordano said. "If something is really wrong, it will have to be corrected with policy changes. But if something is not fundamentally wrong, then breaking a slide, or at least slowing it down, can be an effective use of intervention to help people see what is actually going on. It is still premature, though, to say that the dollar is out of the woods."
Some market analysts said the dollar may also have been helped slightly by the news late in the day that President Clinton had decided to shake up his White House and bring in his budget director, Leon Panetta, to be chief of staff
"The market liked what it saw," said John Lipsky, chief economist for Salomon Brothers. "What it saw was that the Administration was not walking away from the issue, saying, 'We gave at the office Friday and don't bother me anymore.' "
Instead, Mr. Lipsky said, President Clinton was clearly "taking steps that are designed to enhance an image of decisiveness."
Mr. Panetta has a reputation as a budget hawk, and a strong advocate of restraining Federal spending.
 
No Big Rally Expected
 Still, even if the dollar has hit bottom, there are a number of structural factors that suggest it is not likely to surge soon.
The weekend resignation of Prime Minister Tsutomu Hata of Japan makes it even more unlikely that Japan will have a strong enough Government to make the tough decisions to open its markets more to the world and reduce its $130 billion annual global trade surplus. The piling up of dollars in Japan is one of the strongest drags on the American currency.
Also, with Germany and Japan coming out of recession, interest in those nation's stock and bond markets is growing Investors attracted to those markets will be selling dollars and buying yen and marks.

HEADLINE: THE BATTERED DOLLAR: THE TRADERS;
Moving Money and Awaiting the Fed

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LONDON, June 27

 BODY:
Steve Dickinson and Mike Clark hunched before a bank of computer screens on the currency-trading floor at Midland Bank's global markets division here today, watching for any sign that the Federal Reserve or the European central banks were stepping in to calm the world's financial markets by halting the sharp slide in the dollar over the last week.
The American currency had slumped to another postwar low against the Japanese yen during trading in Tokyo overnight. European stock markets, nervous about the turmoil, had opened lower. 
 It was a waiting game being played in countless banks and investment houses around the world. A concerted attempt by central banks to prop up the dollar had failed on Friday. The Clinton Administration's commitment to strengthening the currency remained very much at issue, at least in the minds of participants in the foreign exchange markets.
"If the Fed doesn't come in today, I don't know what will happen," Mr. Dickinson said just after 2 P.M. here.
In the end, neither the Fed nor the European banks came into the market and bought dollars. The American currency continued its decline against both the yen and the German mark, and traders, banks, investment firms, multinational corporations, speculators -- all those who buy and sell currencies -- were again left guessing about how far the dollar might fall and about official American policy toward the decline.
Still, an afternoon spent peering over the shoulders of Mr. Dickinson and Mr. Clark provided a glimpse into the combination of psychology, expectations and economic fundamentals that drive currency markets.
As dealers on Midland's interbank desk, the two men match requests by other banks to buy and sell currencies with prices offered by Midland's traders at desks surrounding them.
Despite large-scale purchases of dollars by the Japanese central bank earlier in the day as the dollar slumped below 100 yen, the Midland dealers in London did not expect any action from the Europeans at least until the start of the business day in New York.
"Because this is deemed to be an American problem, the C.B.'s won't come in until the Fed shows its hand," Mr. Dickinson said, referring to the European central banks.
The pace of trading was almost languid at times. But there were bursts of furious activity. A big customer wanted to sell $250 million for marks. The dollar was trading around 1.58 marks at the time. The interbank desk dealers punched their keyboards and shouted into phones as they sought buyers. They were able to sell $50 million at 1.5790.
There are few buyers of dollars these days. The dollar's weakness may not be justified by the economic fundamentals, Mr. Clark said, but the fundamentals seem to mean little at the moment in the markets. All that matters is identifying the prevailing buying and selling trends and riding them for all they are worth, and right now the trend is clearly downward for the dollar, he said.
"The sizable transactions are people selling dollars," Mr. Clark said. "If every time you buy a dollar you lose money, you stop buying them after awhile."
That process of matching buyer and seller, and taking speculative positions for the bank's own account, occupies the minds of Midland's traders and dealers. While they are by no means unaware of the effects of currency instability on the stock and bond markets and on national economies, market participants give little thought to them while engrossed in the second-to-second world of the data flickering on their screens.
"At the end of the day the idea is to make money for our shareholders, for the room, for our management team, for the end-of-year bonus," Mr. Dickinson said.
Over the last week, most market participants had been making money by betting that central banks, and particularly the Federal Reserve, were not committed to an all-out defense of the dollar. Mr. Dickinson and Mr. Clark said that Secretary of the Treasury Lloyd Bentsen, who over the last year had left traders with the impression that he favored a weak dollar in relation to the yen, might be happy to see the dollar stay weak as a way of putting pressure on Japan to make trade concessions.
"This is not a market that's very impressed by central banks drawing a line in the sand," Mr. Clark said.
As the afternoon wore on, it became apparent that the Federal Reserve was not going to give the markets a chance to test its mettle for at least another day. The key factor, as far as Midland's dealers could tell, was that stock prices on Wall Street had been stable in early trading, suggesting that the investment markets were recovering their equilibrium despite the dollar's continuing slide.
The Fed may also have been helped when the London stock market, Europe's largest, recovered strongly after falling sharply in the morning. The Financial Times-Stock Exchange index of 100 leading shares closed up 23.3 points, at 2,899.9.
"I think the Fed is probably taking comfort from the fact that the Dow is quite steady," Mr. Clark said. "The lack of central bank activity and the calm in the equity markets adds support to the view that what they're really interested in is stability in the markets rather than the absolute value of the dollar."
The dollar had been gyrating between 1.5810 marks and 1.5750, a spread that provided enough margin for speculators to bet on the market's ticks up and down. But if there was to be wider speculation, Mr. Clark thought it would come later in the day. "The speculators might want to wait until Europe has gone home," he said, "to foreclose the possibility of coordinated intervention by the central banks, and then go Fed-hunting."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mike Clark, a dealer at Midland Bank's global markets division, following the dollar during currency trading in London yesterday. (Jonathan Player for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE BATTERED DOLLAR: THE JAPANESE;
A Puzzling Strength Amid Turmoil

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, June 27

 BODY:
As the Japanese yen soared above a crucial benchmark today, the incongruities here seemed even more striking: How could Japan's currency be so strong when its economy is still scraping bottom and its once seamless political order has unraveled for the third time in a year?
For the first time in history, the yen closed in Tokyo worth more than a penny. All day, the market's movements swirled and intermingled with the political maneuverings set off by Prime Minister Tsutomu Hata's resignation on Saturday. Rumors of new combinations of parties and personalities, of secret meetings and imminent divisions in the Liberal Democrats and the Socialists, the country's two largest political parties, only drove the markets into greater fury. 
 The more that it appeared disorder would reign, the more traders here seemed convinced that Japan's $60 billion annual trade surpluses with the United States would not end anytime soon. The result, everyone appeared to agree, would be a still weaker dollar, perceived here as the Clinton Administration's only truly effective trade policy.
 
Demands for Action
 By the hour, more and more leaders of the Japanese business community denounced the confusion, loudly protesting that if their exports were made more expensive, Japan's fledgling recovery risked being choked off. By day's end, they were arguing that unless the politicians straightened themselves out quickly, a wave of red ink would obliterate whatever hope of rebound remained.
"I have grave fears in the face of the political vacuum and the abnormal rise in the yen," Takeshi Nagano, the top official of the Japan Federation of Employers' Associations, said in a statement.
He continued, "The whole of our country's industry will be forced to a standstill."
Of course, any Japanese industry dependent on the import of ever-cheaper raw materials from the United States was not entirely unhappy. But so closely linked were the yen and the politics that this afternoon Japan's chief Government spokesman, Hiroshi Kumagai, who had served as chief Cabinet secretary in Mr. Hata's coalition Government, abandoned customary circumspection. He used the threat of further yen appreciation to try to head off an alliance between the Liberal Democrats, ousted from power last year after a 38-year reign, and the Socialists, the party that defected from the ruling coalition and ultimately forced Mr. Hata to resign.
Traditionally, the Socialists have been viewed as a threat to Japan's stability, because of their opposition to the 34-year-old security pact between Tokyo and Washington, their sympathies for North Korea and calls for protected markets.
"If the L.D.P. and the S.D.P. are allied it would rise to 80 yen" to the dollar, Mr. Kumagai said. Those two parties met today, inconclusively.
Meanwhile, Mr. Hata and his closest ally, Ichiro Ozawa, the shadowy but brilliant strategist who runs the Shinseito (Japan Renewal) party with him, were working furiously behind the scenes to split the Liberal Democratic and Socialist parties and attract defectors into a new conservative coalition.
One thing that is worth remembering is that the political imperatives on one side of the Pacific look pretty remote on the other. American analysts have been saying for some time that investors are expressing their lack of confidence in the Clinton Administration. But there is a mirror issue: Why would anyone have more confidence in Japan's Government?
Some analysts say that the political upheavals here should make no difference for exchange rates; after all, Japan is run by the bureaucrats. But two weeks from now, when the Group of Seven industrialized nations meet in Naples, Tokyo will have to find something to say about how it will stimulate domestic demand and how speedily it will move to deregulate its economy. Both of those are political decisions, and no politician wants to take them.
There are other logical gaps in the conventions about political stability and the strength of the yen. Four years ago, when the yen was rising steadily and the United States was in recession, analysts said that the combination of Japan's growth prospects and its stable government created a natural environment for investment.
Now the reverse is being argued: the lack of growth in Japan and the instability of its Government are combining to keep the trade gap open.
Either way, the yen goes up.
 
A Price Break on Soda
 The argument -- that a higher yen will automatically lead to a flood of American imports -- has its flaws. Some items are cheaper: American-made cars, Compaq computers and private-label soda, which is now being sold at 48 cents instead of the $1.10 for regular brands. But the stronger yen has also led to a surge in imports from China, Southeast Asia and India, and the result can be seen on Tokyo's streets.
More discount goods are now available, including some at "100 yen stores," where many items sell for 100 yen, now exactly $1. Unfortunately for Washington, little of it is made in America.
The Clinton Administration's other hope -- to get more American companies to invest in Japan and thus penetrate its market from within -- is already being undercut. Even at a time of falling property prices, the costs in the 100-to-1 era seem even more insurmountable.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Currency traders in Tokyo as the dollar hit 99.50 yen, a postwar low. (Agence France-Presse)

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Economic Analysis

HEADLINE: Divorced Father Accused Of Killing His 2 Children

BYLINE: By ROBERT HANLEY, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TEANECK, N.J., June 27

 BODY:
A 50-year-old divorced father, apparently distraught that his former wife and two children were moving to Florida, murdered the children Sunday and then drove with the bodies to the police headquarters here and parked, the authorities said today.
The man, Avi Kostner, an unemployed cabdriver under psychiatric care for depression, was arrested in the police parking lot after a friend found him slumped over the steering wheel of his Isuzu Trooper and the bodies of the children hidden under a sleeping bag in a rear seat, John J. Fahy, the Bergen County Prosecutor said. Mr. Kostner may have been feigning unconsciousness. 
 The children, Geri Beth, 12, and Ryan, 10, had both been suffocated several hours apart on Sunday, Mr. Fahy said. The girl was killed sometime about 4 A.M. and the boy about 1 P.M., Mr. Fahy said. Where the murders occurred and where Mr. Kostner had the children during a regular weekend visitation remain uncertain, Mr. Fahy said.
 
Children in Custody Battle
 Since their parents' divorce in April 1988, the two children had been at the center of a feud over custody and visiting rights. The children and their mother, Lynn Mison, were scheduled to leave their home in Wayne, N.J., for a new one in Florida on Friday. The planned move apparently left Mr. Kostner deeply troubled.
"We suspect that may have had something to do with this particular crime," Mr. Fahy said.
Merchants near Mr. Kostner's apartment on Teaneck Road here said today that he often complained bitterly about the Florida move and what he considered unfair treatment by judges in the divorce and custody proceedings over the last six years.
"He seemed like a man being pushed over the edge, and it made him goofy," said Jayne Ross, the cosmetics manager at Teaneck Drugs, a pharmacy near Mr. Kostner's apartment.
Ms. Ross said Mr. Kostner often carried a file bulging with divorce and custody papers. He would either show or give them to anyone willing to listen to his complaints.
Recently, she said, Mr. Kostner came to the store, stunned that Ms. Mison had received court permission to move to Florida with the children.
"He said, 'Can you help me find a lawyer? I don't have any money,' " Ms. Ross recounted.
Sam Ahmed, assistant manager of a bagel shop across Teaneck Road from Mr. Kostner's apartment, also said Mr. Kostner often complained about the courts and his ex-wife. During a stop in the shop on Thursday, he said he feared he would never see the children again.
 
'Two Beautiful Kids'
 Mr. Kostner's neighbors said he was a leader in a Boy Scout troop in Teaneck and occasionally worked as a counselor at the Jewish Community Center in neighboring Englewood. They described him as soft-spoken, but often appeared unhappy and despondent.
"They were two beautiful kids, very attractive," one neighbor, Aaron Weinberg said. He said he was stunned by the two murder charges filed against Mr. Kostner.
Under a 1990 court order, Mr. Kostner was allowed custodial visits with the two children every other weekend. This summer, he was to have had custody of them for four weeks, Mr. Fahy, the prosecutor said.
Last Friday, Mr. Kostner picked up his children at their schools in Wayne. Where he took them is still unclear, Mr. Fahy said.
The prosecutor said the times of their deaths was established during autopsies.
 
Telephone Messages
 About mid-afternoon Sunday, Mr. Fahy said, Mr. Kostner began calling friends in New York City and leaving messages on their answering machines that he had killed the children and was going to commit suicide.
One friend came to Teaneck at about 7:45 P.M. Sunday to look for Mr. Kostner. The friend, whom Mr. Fahy refused to identify, and a Teaneck police officer went to Mr. Kostner's apartment about a half mile from the Teaneck police headquarters. No one was home.
They returned to the headquarters about 8:30 P.M. The friend left the station about 9 P.M. At that point, he noticed Mr. Kostner's Isuzu Trooper parked in the lot. The vehicle had not been there at 8:30 P.M.

HEADLINE: SHAKE--UP AT THE WHITE HOUSE: THE OVERVIEW;
CLINTON SHUFFLES HIS AIDES, SELECTING BUDGET DIRECTOR AS WHITE HOUSE STAFF CHIEF

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
President Clinton today announced the most extensive staff shake-up of his 17 months in office, redrawing the lines of power in the White House as he searched for a way to give an air of strength and professionalism to his Administration.
Mr. Clinton removed a fellow Arkansan and lifelong friend, Thomas F. McLarty 3d, as White House chief of staff and replaced him with Leon E. Panetta, the President's budget director. He also installed his counselor, David R. Gergen, to whom he turned in another time of political crisis, in a powerful new position to help refine the Administration's foreign policy. 
 To succeed Mr. Panetta as budget director, the President nominated Alice M. Rivlin, the No. 2 official at the Office of Management and Budget.
Beginning with the setting -- the Oval Office instead of the more informal press room -- everything about today's announcement seemed intended to project leadership and authority in a White House whose stumbles have left an appearance of ill management and whose initiatives have sometimes floundered for lack of a strong central voice.
"I think the job of the President is to make the White House as effective as possible," Mr. Clinton said this afternoon, "which means you have to use people at their highest and best use."
Mr. Clinton portrayed the changes as nothing more than a midterm realignment meant to help the White House in its campaigns to overhaul the health care system, gain respect for its foreign-policy posture and overcome stiff Republican challenges in the November elections.
The President went out of his way to paint Mr. McLarty as an architect himself of the shake-up. And he said his boyhood friend, whom he has known since kindergarten, would remain his "closest and most trusted personal adviser" in a new senior role as counselor on political and legislative matters.
But Mr. McLarty has never pretended to the role of a major legislative strategist, and even among his friends and subordinates in the White House the shuffle was regarded as a sidetracking of the unfailingly courteous former businessmen who had been increasingly described as an ineffectual chief of staff.
White House officials said Mr. Clinton had made plain to Mr. McLarty that their partnership was not working the way it should. Even the august setting of the Oval Office this afternoon could not hide the look of vague unease on the face of Mr. McLarty, the latest among the Arkansans appointed to top jobs in Washington to have fallen from grace.
In Mr. Panetta, a former Congressman from California noted for his mastery of the Federal budget and legislative struggles, Mr. Clinton has turned to a consummate Washington insider whose fiercest battles within the Administration have been fought to keep control of the budget deficit.
Mr. Gergen's standing was also enhanced by the changes. His appointment as a top adviser to both the President and the Secretary of State gave him the role in foreign-policy formulation that he had sought, and been denied, since joining the Administration a year ago.
Mr. Gergen, who served as an aide to three Republican Presidents, had told associates that he intended to leave the White House during the next few months. But White House officials said he would now stay on at least through the end of the year, having been promised a seat on the Administration's highest-level foreign-policy council, the Principals' Committee.
 
Enviable Access
 Mr. Gergen will have offices both at the Old Executive Office Building, a part of the White House complex, and at the State Department, a level of access that is not enjoyed even by the Secretary of State or the national security adviser.
It has been just over a year since the President invited Mr. Gergen to leave the business of political commentary to join the White House staff as part of what until today was Mr. Clinton's most drastic personnel reshuffling. Today's announcement, which was kept secret until this morning from even the most senior White House officials, was an implicit acknowledgment that the current lineup had left Mr. Clinton ill equipped to fight the political wars that he must wage this year.
Senior Presidential aides said tonight that Mr. Panetta's years in Congress had equipped him with the expertise and shrewdness to lead efforts to win passage of a health care bill and other legislation and to help the Democrats avoid what could be a disastrous loss of Congressional seats in the midterm elections this fall.
But they said it remained uncertain whether Mr. Clinton, whose habit is to seek counsel from far and wide, would be willing to cede enough power to allow Mr. Panetta to operate as a traditional chief of staff.
 
'Relaxed Exchanges'
 In an interview tonight, Mr. McLarty said Mr. Clinton made clear to him several months ago that he believed it was important that the two engage in more of the "relaxed exchanges and discussions" that were a fixture of their relationship during the Presidential transition but had grown infrequent since Mr. McLarty became chief of staff.
Mr. McLarty said that he viewed that assessment "as an honest comment from a person who normally speaks his mind very clearly to me," and that he and the President had generally agreed by early this month that he could be more valuable in a counselor's role. The final details were worked out in a lunch between him and Mr. Clinton last Wednesday, and at a dinner on Sunday for which Mr. McLarty invited Mr. Panetta to his house.
Mr. McLarty, who, like Mr. Clinton, was born in Hope, Ark., said he expected to play a major role in reaching out to business leaders, lawmakers and others with whom he has forged a close relationship. Other White House officials said his value as a Presidential emissary and confidential adviser should not be underestimated.
But some officials also described Mr. McLarty's performance as having been limited by a certain deafness to political rhythms, especially on Capitol Hill. They said his courtliness had also seemed to prevent him both from being an effective spokesman for the Administration and from managing the White House with a firm hand, rendering him almost invisible in the public eye except in the wake of embarrassments like the dismissal of the White House travel office staff and a senior official's recent use of a Presidential helicopter for a golf outing.
 
Small Circle
 With his reassignment, Mr. McLarty joins that official, David Watkins, as another of the small circle of high-ranking Arkansans who have been ousted or demoted in unflattering circumstances since Mr. Clinton brought them to Washington. Webster L. Hubbell, the No. 3 official at the Justice Department, was forced to resign after his fellow partners in a Little Rock law firm accused him of improprieties; William Kennedy, an associate White House counsel, was reassigned after his handling of security checks on White House employees came into question. Another Arkansan who was close to the Clintons, Vincent W. Foster Jr., the deputy White House counsel, committed suicide last July.
Mr. Gergen had known he was getting a new job. But except for Mr. McLarty and Mr. Panetta, no one within the White House staff was told of the entire shake-up until this afternoon, and even tonight such details as office assignments remained to be worked out.
While Mr. Gergen was expected to spend most of his time in a new seventh-floor office at the State Department, White House officials said it was unlikely that Mr. McLarty would be relegated to Mr. Gergen's current spot in the basement. They said he would almost certainly be offered work space adjoining the Oval Office.
Mr. Panetta, who will mark his 56th birthday on Tuesday, will move into the corner suite on the main floor of the West Wing that Mr. McLarty has occupied since the President took office.
 
Substantial Clout
 Mr. Panetta has already wielded substantial clout in internal White House battles over economic policy and the shape of health care legislation. The priority he has attached to reducing the budget deficit has frequently put him at odds with White House political advisers and their advocacy of increased Government spending, but even his opponents within the Administration have generally spoken of him with admiration.
Ms. Rivlin, the former director of the Congressional Budget Office, is one of the country's leading scholars on fiscal policy. She was regarded during the Presidential transition as a leading contender for budget director, but was said by aides to Mr. Clinton to have failed then to establish a rapport with him. She has maintained a low profile since being chosen as Mr. Panetta's deputy.

HEADLINE: HAITIANS TAKING TO SEA IN DROVES, ALARMING THE U.S.

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
The tide of Haitian boat people surged today to its highest level in more than a year as the Coast Guard picked up more than 1,000 people on the high seas who were seeking to seize the opportunity the Clinton Administration has given them to apply for refugee status.
The flood is swamping the bureaucracy that the Administration set up to accommodate Haitians who hope to be granted such status. 
 Today's actions bring the number of Haitians intercepted since Friday to more than 1,800. That means more Haitians in their flimsy boats and rafts have been picked up in the last four days than in the first four months of this year.
The surge is alarming American officials, who fear that the 1,000-bed Navy hospital ship they are using to process Haitian boat people off Kingston, Jamaica, will be overwhelmed. They also fear that the larger number of Haitians granted refugee status will create political and economic strains in Florida and other states at a time when immigrants are becoming a major campaign issue.
"The numbers are reaching a level that they have to be a concern," said Duke Austin, spokesman for the Immigration and Naturalization Service. "If they continue to increase, it's going to strain our ability to handle them."
One official said he expected that the Administration would soon be forced to use the Guantanamo Bay naval base in Cuba to process Haitians. But Administration officials said their ability to handle the flood of Haitians would improve when they open an interviewing center in the Turks and Caicos Islands in mid-July.
Administration officials are uncertain about why the number of Haitian boat people has exploded. The most important factor, they say, is that word has filtered back to Haiti that the United States is granting refugee status to a far higher percentage of Haitians than before.
Since the United States began processing Haitian boat people off Kingston two weeks ago, almost one-third have been granted refugee status. That compares with an acceptance rate of one in 20 for Haitians who sought refugee status in the past year.
Why the acceptance rate has risen is unclear. But before President Clinton announced a change in refugee policy on May 8 in response to pressures from blacks and liberals, his Administration was forcibly repatriating Haitians without granting them a hearing.
 
'Pretty Good Odds'
 "Right now if you're in Haiti looking at what you got there and you see you have a 33 percent chance of getting in, you realize those are pretty good odds, so you take a chance," an Administration official said.
Another official said the Haitians had also taken advantage of a calm sea and excellent weather.
Administration officials have long acknowledged that their fear of a wave of Haitian refugees is one of the factors driving their Haiti policy, especially their efforts to oust Haiti's military leaders, who seized power in September 1991.
This week's flood of boat people is expected to increase pressure on the Administration to act decisively to restore Haiti's exiled President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide. Several officials have said a flood of boat people could increase the chances of military intervention and accelerate the timetable for such intervention.
Coast Guard officials said that after picking up 260 Haitians on Sunday and more than 1,000 today, they had more than 1,300 Haitians on board cutters headed for Kingston, where the Navy hospital ship Comfort is anchored. Before today's actions, the Administration had taken 1,148 Haitians picked up at sea to the Comfort.
Interviews with 477 Haitians have been completed, with 130 of them receiving refugee status. State Department officials said 347 had been denied refugee status, and 267 had been repatriated to Haiti.
American officials noted today that apparently in response to the new processing procedures, Haitians seem to be departing in smaller, less seaworthy boats -- a signal that they are not trying to make the journey all the way to Florida, but expect to be intercepted close to the Haitian coast.
"These are tiny little boats that never had a chance of getting to Florida," said Stanley Schrager, spokesman at the United States Embassy in Port-au-Prince.
After the Coast Guard stops the boats, those on board are taken to the Comfort, where they are interviewed to determine whether they warrant refugee status. Those granted refugee status are taken to Guantanamo Bay for medical tests; those rejected are returned to Haiti.
Michael McCurry, the State Department spokesman, urged Haitians today not to flee in flimsy rafts and risk drowning. He said they should instead apply for refugee status at processing centers in Haiti, even though fewer than 10 percent of the Haitians applying there have been granted refugee status.
In its latest step to increase pressure on Haiti's military rulers, the Administration is to announce this week that it will stop granting visas to virtually all Haitians and will cancel outstanding visas, officials said today. Because most Haitians cannot afford to travel, the decision to deny visas is directed primarily at wealthy Haitians and is intended to push them to abandon their support of Haiti's military rulers.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Amid tightened economic sanctions against Haiti and a higher rate of acceptance of refugee claims, the United States has reported a sharp rise in the number of Haitians trying to flee their country by boat. In Port-au-Prince yesterday, residents mobbed a food handout at a local church. (Agence France-Presse) (pg. A6)

HEADLINE: THE BATTERED DOLLAR: THE IMPACT
A Primer on the Dollar;
The Currency's Well-Publicized Decline Hasn't Meant Much for Most Americans

BYLINE: By PETER PASSELL 

 BODY:
Ten years ago, a dollar bought 250 Japanese yen; a month ago, it bought just 105. Now, it buys about 100.
The dollar's slide against the yen, as well as against the German mark, in recent weeks has evoked cries of alarm from Wall Street and has been read by some as a judgment of American policy -- and policy makers. "The market is saying your economy is not in good shape," said Georges de Menil, a senior editor of the European-based journal Economic Policy. 
 Yesterday, the dollar fell to a new record low below 100 yen, before recovering in late trading. [Page D6.]
But for many Americans who are not actively engaged in trading currencies or securities, the dollar's well-publicized woes have not meant very much. The economy is growing moderately, new jobs are being generated and inflation is modest.
In the view of some economists, the biggest worry now is that the Clinton Administration, embarrassed by the symbolic weakness of a declining American currency, will care too much about the exchange value of the dollar and too little about the jobs and income, and will lean on the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates, impeding further growth.
"The whole idea of having a floating-rate exchange system is to avoid currency crises" that force governments to alter otherwise desirable domestic policies, said James Tobin, a Nobel laureate in economics at Yale University.
While there are, indeed, some effects from a falling dollar, the impact on Main Street is real but relatively minor. When a dollar buys fewer yen or marks, it buys fewer goods made by foreign workers who are paid in yen and marks. Hence, the depreciating dollar is bad news for Americans who are shopping for a Sony camcorder or a bottle of Kirin beer or a Mercedes-Benz, unless the producers are willing to take smaller profits, and hold their export prices flat, as some have done.
But even if prices of imported goods do rise, all those camcorders, cars and what-not imported from Japan and Germany represent just a small percent of American consumption. And at the same time, the value of the dollar has held firm against the currencies of many of the country's trading partners, and even appreciated against the currency of the biggest, Canada.
Over all, a dollar buys just 3 percent less of the mix of goods and services Americans imported last year. Thus, even assuming that Japanese and German exporters were able to pass along their entire 10 percent rise against the dollar since the start of 1994 -- which they have not -- the impact on American living standards would be hard to discern.
Of course, the direct price increases from higher import prices are not the only source of inflation for American consumers. Economists note that American companies with a history of less-than-aggressive price competition may use higher import prices as a lever to raise their own prices. "I would be unhappy if Japan were forced to raise car prices," said George Perry, an economist at the Brookings Institution, who expressed fears that higher prices for Toyotas and Nissans, for example, would give Detroit an excuse to raise its own sticker prices.
It is far from clear, however, that many German or Japanese industries have this much freedom to raise their prices without fear of losing customers in a global economy that is increasingly competitive in high-technology goods. German machinery exports, for example, now face competition from economies ranging all the way from Italy to South Korea to Brazil.
But if inflation is not a significant concern, why have both the stock and bond markets responded so badly to the dollar's decline? American securities prices may have been expected to rise since the depreciated dollar makes American goods more competitive in world markets and thus makes American corporations more profitable.
 
Reflection on Policy?
 One answer -- one apparently taken to heart on Wall Street -- is that the decline of the dollar and the parallel decline in the value of American securities are both symptoms of what Mr. de Menil calls a "lack of credibility in American economic policy."
For all the hoopla over deficit reduction last year and the Federal Reserve's pre-emptive strike against inflation this year, he argues that America's budget and trade deficit problems remain serious and that Washington's will to squash inflation as the economy approaches full capacity remains in doubt.
That is impossible to disprove. But it does not mesh well with the widely held view that the American economy is relatively healthy and relatively resistant -- for the moment, at least -- to the cumulative cost pressures that led to spiraling inflation in the late 1970's.
Nor is it easy to reconcile the timing of the dollar's slide against the yen and the mark with any changes in American policy that would affect confidence in the economy. It is noteworthy that the dollar fared particularly poorly in June -- a month in which forecasters detected signs that the vigorous economic expansion of the last few months was running out of gas -- and therefore less prone to overheat.
The very focus on exchange rates frustrates Mr. Tobin. The virtue of a free market in currencies, he said, is that it permits governments to pursue divergent economic policies that lead to different rates of growth and inflation rather than trying to keep them artificially in line so their currencies stay in tandem.
In this case, he noted, flexible exchange rates have permitted the American economy to recover from recession even as Japan's and Germany's economy remained in the doldrums. Under the post-World War II system of fixed exchange rates, which held until the early 1970's, such divergence would have resulted in a currency crisis and the formal devaluation of one currency against another.
Not surprisingly, Mr. Tobin's prescription for coping with the diminished dollar -- seconded by many others -- is to do "roughly speaking, nothing." And from his perspective, the irony is that American policy makers may take to heart implied criticism from financial markets, whose driving logic is, at times, questionable.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Justices Back Cable Regulation

BYLINE: By LINDA GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
A deeply divided Supreme Court endorsed a broad framework for Federal regulation of cable television today but stopped just short of upholding the 1992 law that requires cable systems to retransmit the signals of local broadcasters.
Instead, the Justices held in a 5-to-4 decision that the Government had to present more evidence to justify the law's assumption that the retransmission requirement was necessary to preserve the economic viability of broadcast television. The case now goes back to a special three-judge Federal court, which upheld the law last year.
 
A Return Is Inevitable
 The split nature of the decision enabled both the cable and broadcast industries, locked in bitter economic and political combat, to claim a measure of victory -- assertions that will not finally be resolved until the case makes its inevitable way back for another Supreme Court decision a year or more from now.
But it was clear that the broadcasters, despite being on the losing end of today's decision in a technical sense, had won a great deal. The one-vote majority accepted the rationale Congress had used in crafting what are known as "must carry" rules, as well as the analysis that the special Federal court applied last year in upholding the retransmission requirement.
In fact, most of Justice Anthony M. Kennedy's 45-page opinion read like an all-out affirmance of the lower court's judgment. But in the final pages, Justice Kennedy suddenly switched gears and announced that the case had to be sent back for additional factual findings. That conclusion may have been necessary to hold one of the five members of the majority for an opinion that may have gone through several incarnations since the case was argued more than six months ago.
 
 
Rules Remain in Effect
 The must-carry rules will remain in effect as the case proceeds.
Justice Kennedy said that the must-carry provisions "are justified by special characteristics of the cable medium: the bottleneck monopoly power exercised by cable operators and the danger this power poses to the viability of broadcast television."
The "bottleneck" image was central both to the approach Congress took in enacting the Cable Television Consumer Protection and Competition Act of 1992 and to the majority's analysis today.
Once a home is wired for cable, as are 60 percent of American households with television sets, viewers no longer have the option of watching broadcast television directly without special equipment. Instead, as a practical matter, viewers are limited to the offerings that the cable system chooses to provide. Congress found that the cable industry, competing with broadcasters for the same advertising dollars, had an incentive to limit broadcasters' access to their systems. Congress also found that cable operators' affiliation with programmers provided an extra incentive to limit broadcasters' access.
The 1992 law, which was passed over President George Bush's veto, requires most cable systems -- those with more than 300 subscribers and 12 channels -- to devote a third of their channel capacity to carrying the signals of local commercial broadcast channels. If the number of local broadcast stations exceeds a third of a cable system's capacity, the system is not obliged to carry them all. A separate provision also provides guaranteed access for public television stations.
As expected, the cable industry immediately went to court to challenge the provisions on First Amendment grounds, on the theory that the Government could not interfere with cable operators' judgment by requiring the transmission of particular speech. A fast-track provision in the law sent the challenge to the special panel of three judges here, with a direct appeal to the Supreme Court.
 
 
Broad Implications
 The case, Turner Broadcasting v. Federal Communications Commission, No. 93-44, had broad theoretical implications as well as enormous practical and economic importance. The Supreme Court had never defined a place for cable television along a First Amendment spectrum that gives the most complete constitutional protection to the press and much more limited protection to broadcasters, on the theory that the scarcity of broadcast frequencies makes regulation necessary.
Justice Kennedy said today that because cable had no comparable physical limitations -- and with the advent of new technology, will soon have no practical limits at all -- the rationale for limited First Amendment protection that applies to broadcasters does not apply to cable.
This was only a limited, theoretical victory for the cable industry, however, because Justice Kennedy's opinion relied on another rationale for justifying Government interference with cable programming of the sort the Court has explicitly rejected with respect to the press. Citing cable's role as "bottleneck or gatekeeper," Justice Kennedy said: "Simply by virtue of its ownership of the essential pathway for cable speech, a cable operator can prevent its subscribers from obtaining access to programming it chooses to exclude."
"The potential for abuse of this private power over a central avenue of communication cannot be overlooked," Justice Kennedy continued, adding: "The First Amendment's command that government not impede the freedom of speech does not disable the Government from taking steps to insure that private interests not restrict, through physical control of a critical pathway of communication, the free flow of information and ideas."
Justice Kennedy's opinion was joined in full by Chief Justice William H. Rehnquist and by Justices Harry A. Blackmun and David H. Souter. Justice John Paul Stevens joined the central part of the analysis, but wrote a separate opinion to say that the must-carry rules should be upheld without the requirement of further proceedings. Justice Stevens said he was nonetheless joining the Court's judgment in sending the case back to the lower court so that there would be a majority for that disposition.
The dissenters were Justices Sandra Day O'Connor, Antonin Scalia, Clarence Thomas and Ruth Bader Ginsburg. In a dissenting opinion by Justice O'Connor, they said the must-carry rules were "an impermissible restraint on the cable operators' editorial discretion as well as on the cable programmers' speech."
Much of the constitutional debate between the two sides was over whether the must-carry rules have the effect of regulating speech on the basis of content. Under the Court's precedent, content-based regulation of speech is the most serious First Amendment violation, requiring the highest level of justification. "Content neutral" regulations, on the other hand, are justified as long as they serve "an important or substantial governmental interest" and restrict no more speech than necessary.
In concluding that the regulations are content-neutral, Justice Kennedy said that "Congress's overriding objective in enacting must-carry was not to favor programming of a particular subject matter, viewpoint or format, but rather to preserve access to free television programming for the 40 percent of Americans without cable."
Addressing the Government's justifications for the policy, he said, "Assuring that the public has access to a multiplicity of information sources is a governmental purpose of the highest order, for it promotes values central to the First Amendment."
 
 
Dissenters' Argument
 But in the dissenters' view, the Government's choice to favor broadcasters by assuring them access to cable was inherently based on content. "The interest in insuring access to a multiplicity of diverse and antagonistic sources of information, no matter how praiseworthy, is directly tied to the content of what the speakers will likely say," Justice O'Connor said. No interest the Government put forward could justify that interference with cable operators' First Amendment interests, she said.
In sending the case back to the lower court, Justice Kennedy said the Government must demonstrate that "Congress has drawn reasonable inferences based on substantial evidence." Specifically, he said, the three-judge panel must compile evidence to test the Government's assertion that without the must-carry rules, cable operators would drop broadcast stations, which would deteriorate or fail as a result.
The lower court had not conducted a full trial before reaching its original decision, instead granting summary judgment to the Government on the basis of briefs that had been filed. The new proceeding will not necessarily have the character of a formal trial, but many more documents are likely to be filed.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Prices Gain As Commodities Drop

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities were sharply higher yesterday after rebounding from earlier losses connected to the falling dollar.
The 30-year bond rose 22/32 , to 8524/32 , for a yield of 7.45 percent, down from 7.52 percent on Friday. 
 The market rebound followed a sharp drop in commodity prices, which offset investors' fears that a weaker dollar would raise inflation pressures and bring on higher interest rates. As the dollar stabilized and gold prices skidded by as much as $8 an ounce, bond prices gained steadily.
Shorter-maturity bills were mixed, and at yesterday's weekly auction the average rate for new three-month paper was 4.20 percent, up from 4.18 percent the previous week. The average rate for a six-month bill was 4.60 percent, up from 4.55 percent.
 
Commodity Index Falls
 The Commodity Research Bureau index, a closely followed barometer of future inflation pressures, dropped by 2.21 points, to 227.77. Gold for August delivery fell $6.50, to $386.40 an ounce on the Commodity Exchange in New York.
Treasury securities followed the foreign exchange market as the dollar tried to recover from record lows reached in overnight trading. Volume was light as uncertainty in the debt and currency markets kept many investors sidelined.
"Following the dollar seems to be the game plan," a bond dealersaid.
Darwin L. Beck, a financial economist for CS First Boston, noted that as the dollar stabilized yesterday it also reduced the need for the Federal Reserve to take some kind of action. "The Fed was looking for a reason not to alter monetary policy," Mr. Beck said. "If it did, it will be because of the economy, and not be strictly based on the dollar."
 
Will the Fed Act?
 He said that the dollar, while at a lower level yesterday, was at an exchange rate at which the central bank could feel comfortable in not taking immediate action. "If the dollar remains at current levels, the Fed may wait through its July Federal Open Market Committee meeting," Mr. Beck said. The committee, which sets the Fed's monetary policy for months ahead, meets next week.
"As far as the general outlook," Mr. Beck said, "we think the tightening will be in August when the F.O.M.C. meets again, after the June and July economic data. That is, of course, if the dollar remains stable and the political and economic climates produce no surprises."
Yesterday, comments from Fed and ex-Fed officials threw early jitters into the bond market, along with an initial spike in the commodities index.
According to Bloomberg Business News, Wayne D. Angell, a former Fed governor who is chief economist at Bear, Stearns, said the Fed was likely to raise rates 50 basis points next week. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
The Fed president in Richmond, J. Alfred Broaddus Jr., told Market News that foreign exchange rates were getting more consideration than they used to in policy deliberations, but that they are only one variable the Fed watches. The Fed's best policy course, he said, is to persuade the markets of the Fed's long-term commitment to price stability. Mr. Broaddus said the Fed needed at least to see June data before it could gauge the strength of demand, but he did not see any significant weakening in spending.
 
Frost in Brazil
 The commodities index briefly caused anxiety when it jumped more than a point in early trading, as coffee and cocoa prices soared. Reports of frost in Brazil caused coffee prices to soar, but a drop in gold and lower prices over all in other commodities quickly reassured th market. "If not for gold, the dollar and coffee would have devastated bonds," a bond trader said.
Concerns about a Fed interest rate increase will likely continue to plague the market right up to meeting of the open market committee. Some analysts expect the Fed to raise interest rates by 25 basis points, based on economic factors rather than the dollar's slide.
Greg Jones, an economist with MMS International, said the dollar was not irrelevant in the Fed's policy discussion, "and while it will not be a catalyst for a tightening, it may prompt a half-point rate hike and a more aggressive funds rate hike of 50 basis points," he said.
In the municipal market, Los Angeles County sold $1.15 billion of short-term debt through CS First Boston. The tax and revenue anticipation notes due on June 30, 1995, have a 4.50 percent coupon and were initially priced to yield 3.85 percent. Underwriters said the issue carries the highest short-term rating of MIG-1 by Moody's and SP-1+ by S.& P.
The following are the results of yesterday's auction of new three- and six-month Treasury bills:.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Most Commodities Tumble And Bureau Index Falls 2.21

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Most commodity prices fell sharply yesterday, despite a huge surge in coffee prices.
The selling in grains, livestock, metals and oil pushed the closely watched Commodity Research Bureau index down 2.21 points, to 227.77. 
 "Lumber and pork bellies are limit down, cotton is sharply lower and sugar took a dive," said Warren Seeley, a futures.
"The outlook is grim for speculators who are long in the commodity markets," he said. "If it wasn't for the coffee frost, the C.R.B. would have been down an additional three points."
A severely damaging frost in Brazil, the world's leading coffee producer, set off panic buying of coffee futures yesterday, sending prices soaring more than 25 percent, to seven-and-a-half-year highs.
Coffee for July delivery jumped 33.8 cents, to $1.5975 a pound, after peaking earlier at $1.80, its highest price since November 1986. The more active September coffee contract gained 33.5 cents, to $1.6160 a pound on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange in New York.
But coffee was the exception to the deflationary trend.
"There are very few plus signs in the commodity world," said Jeff Wilson, editor of the Brock Report, a farm marketing newsletter. He noted that the sharp upward spikes in the prices of a host of commodities had receded.
"Worries about currency fluctuation, improved weather in the U.S. and concerns about higher global interest rates have combined to reduce the amount of asset reallocation to commodities from financial assets," Mr. Wilson said.
Rain in the Midwest depressed grain prices. At the Chicago Board of Trade, July soybeans lost 8.25 cents, to $6.59 1/4 a bushel; July corn fell 7 cents, to $2.48 1/2, and July wheat declined 1.75 cents, to $3.13 1/4 a bushel.
Meat prices also fell, with July pork bellies falling 1.575 cents, to 38.95 cents a pound at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange. Improving rainfall in cotton-growing regions and a general liquidation of agricultural commodities also depressed cotton prices, with the July contract sinking another 1.9 cents, to 71.58 cents a pound.
Signs of weakening home sales helped depress lumber futures. July lumber fell the $10 trading limit at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange to settle at $339.50 for 1,000 square feet.
Earlier yesterday, the National Association of Realtors said sales of previously owned homes slipped seven-tenths of 1 percent in May, the first dip in three months, as higher mortgage rates began to curb the market for existing housing.
Inflation-sensitive precious metals reflected the downturn in commodity prices. August gold fell $6.50, to $386.40 an ounce at the Commodity Exchange in New York, while July silver dropped 15.5 cents, to $5.245 an ounce.
Energy prices also retreated. August crude oil finished 31 cents lower at $19.01 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. July gasoline lost 0.98 cent, to 52.99 cents a gallon, and July heating oil fell 0.85 cent, to 49.54 cents.

HEADLINE: Sales of Existing Homes Declined 0.7% Last Month

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Sales of previously owned homes slipped seven-tenths of 1 percent in May, the first dip in three months as higher mortgage rates began to curb the market for existing housing, a real estate trade group said today.
The National Association of Realtors said sales nationally were at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.09 million, down from 4.12 million in April. It was the first decline since February, when severe winter weather kept shoppers inside. 
 Although sales rose in the East and South, they were flat in the Midwest and had a big drop in the West.
 
Still a Busy Month
 Robert H. Elrod, president of the association, said some consumers chose to delay home purchases in May because of higher mortgage rates, although many remained in the market. May was the seventh month in the last eight in which sales exceeded a rate of four million.
"Despite the rise in mortgage interest rates, affordability conditions remain at one of the best levels of all times and are still a strong driving force behind this healthy housing market," Mr. Elrod said.
Surveys by the Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation showed 30-year fixed-rate mortgages averaged 8.6 percent in May, up from 8.32 percent a month earlier and the highest since 8.67 percent in May 1992.
Sales of existing homes had edged up 1.2 percent in April, though sales of new homes fell 6.8 percent that month. Analysts attributed the decline to rates that have risen from 25-year lows in October.
The median price of a previously owned home in May was $109,900, up 3.2 percent from a year earlier, when the price was $106,500. The median is the midpoint in the price range, meaning half of the homes cost more and half cost less.
Regionally, sales jumped 10.5 percent in the Northeast, to an annual rate of 630,000. The median price was $138,300, down 1.4 percent from May 1993. Sales were up 1.9 percent, to a 1.57 million rate, in the South, where the median price was $97,400, up 2.5 percent from a year earlier.
But sales were unchanged in the Midwest, at 1.04 million, and plunged 12.4 percent in the West, to a rate of 850,000. The median price in the Midwest was $87,100, up 2.5 percent from May 1993, and $148,600 in the West, up 5.8 percent from a year earlier.

HEADLINE: The Declining Dollar

 BODY:
The dollar continued to fall against the German mark yesterday and hovered near a post-World War II low against the Japanese yen. But a lower dollar poses no crisis and, for now, warrants no dramatic attention. Indeed, intervention by Washington is likely to be ineffective or damaging.
True, a lower dollar means lower U.S. living standards -- Americans can afford fewer German cameras or Japanese VCR's. And a lower dollar, by driving up the price of imports, adds to inflation. However, these impacts are small because imports account for only about 10 percent of the nation's economic activity. Besides, the dollar will almost surely continue to fall as long as the U.S. racks up huge trade deficits. 
 In any case, there is little useful the U.S. can do about the dollar. Last week the U.S. and its allies launched a coordinated effort to buy dollars and thereby raise their value. But the effort fizzled because even governments cannot easily overcome the torrent of a trillion dollars that sloshes through currency markets each day.
The only quick way to stop the dollar's slide would be to make dollar-denominated assets more attractive to investors by shifting monetary policy toward higher interest rates. But if the Federal Reserve Board were to do that, it would slow the economy and possibly raise unemployment. Given the small benefit of halting a modest fall in the dollar, Fed tightening is not warranted.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial
