HEADLINE: Getting Real On Central Europe

BYLINE: By Zbigniew Brzezinski; Zbigniew Brzezinski was national security adviser to President Jimmy Carter.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
President Clinton's visits to Latvia and Poland next week, as well as his participation in the Group of Seven summit meeting in Naples a few days later, signal a more comprehensive and strategically coherent U.S. policy toward the former Soviet bloc.
Generated by a working alliance between the National Security Council and the Defense Department, the policy seeks to sustain realistic cooperation with Russia while also enlarging the scope of European security. Both the symbolism and the substance of the President's trip are likely to underline these important themes. 
 The emerging strategy recognizes that the post-Communist transformation in Central Europe has moved beyond the economic phase; the central issue is now the region's relationship to the rest of Europe, especially with regard to security. And this strategy recognizes that in the former Soviet Union, a stable and cooperative relationship between Russia and Ukraine is critical to the successful political and economic transformation of both countries.
A product of serious debates within the Administration and outside it, the new, more realistic U.S. policy seeks to consolidate and formalize the new landscape that has arisen since the Soviet Union's collapse. Its critical objective remains the fashioning of a cooperative relationship between the West and Russia, but it recognizes that this goal is compatible with the progressive deepening of Europe's political unity and the expansion of its security perimeters. Indeed, simultaneously promoting friendly links with Russia and enhancing Central Europe's security is likely to prove more productive than concentrating on one or the other.
The schedule for Mr. Clinton's trip has a strategic logic. In Riga, he is bound to underline American sympathies for the three small Baltic democracies -- echoing the Scandinavian countries, which have become impressively committed to the survival of these former victims of Stalinist expansion. In a remarkable and obviously deliberate formulation, the Swedish Prime Minister recently referred to them as Sweden's "near abroad," adding that his country would not be indifferent if their independence was again threatened.
In affirming U.S. support for the Baltic republics, Mr. Clinton would not be offending the Russians. Except for extremists, most Russians accept the separate status of these republics. Russian troop withdrawals are likely to be completed on schedule; even the large Russian minorities in Estonia and Latvia are developing a stake in the continued independence of these countries, given their impressive economic performance.
In Warsaw, Mr. Clinton will face a more complex issue. The Poles fear that U.S. policy toward Central Europe has become a function of U.S. policy toward Russia. They resent this, and so do the Hungarians and Czechs. At the same time, Russia has made it clear that it opposes the expansion of NATO to Central Europe.
The President's visit is unlikely to resolve the issue of Central European membership in NATO, even though some progress is likely. When Russia joined the Partnership for Peace last week, the alliance made it absolutely clear that Russia did not gain a veto on NATO decisions regarding membership, while Germany in particular has indicated that it favors the expansion of NATO into the geopolitical no-man's land between itself and Russia.
Under these circumstances, the President will have the opportunity to stress two critically important themes: that the expansion of NATO is a natural process, closely connected with the widening of Europe's unity, and that any such expansion will reinforce a stable relationship with Russia. After all, the reconciliation of Central Europe and Russia is more likely in a setting of security than in a geopolitical vacuum. Mr. Clinton might add that an expanding NATO would be prepared to undertake a special treaty relationship with Moscow, commensurate with Russia's status as a major power and now also a friendly one.
The theme of reconciliation will be especially resonant in Warsaw. The Poles are about to observe the 50th anniversary of the 1944 Warsaw uprising, which was suppressed by the Germans after 63 days of fighting while the Russians sat passively on the other side of the Vistula River. By contrast with the Normandy events, the Poles have wisely invited both the German and the Russian leaders to symbolize their desire for genuine reconciliation. The President will thus have an ideal setting to make it clear that reconciliation and wider security reinforce each other and that the U.S. is prepared to clarify the criteria and time frames of NATO membership.
Finally, in Naples, the Group of Seven industrial powers are likely to address the theme that political instability and economic crisis in Ukraine will benefit no one. President Boris Yeltsin will be there -- all to the good, since Western concern for Ukraine's future should not be viewed as an anti-Russian preoccupation.
A more stable and non-imperial Russia is more likely to emerge if there is a stable and secure Ukraine. That is why the Group of Seven, and the U.S. in particular, are preparing to affirm their interest in Ukraine's well-being and to offer aid as long as Kiev shows its willingness to undertake long-delayed economic reforms.
So far, it has not done so. After the presidential runoff election between President Leonid Kravchuk and Leonid Kuchma, the former Prime Minister, that has been mandated by Sunday's election, the Group of Seven should twist the arm of the next president to appoint a dynamic economic reformer as prime minister and give him full political support. In taking the lead on this issue, the Clinton Administration would show an important evolution in its thinking.
The signs of greater sensitivity to Central European security concerns and the increased emphasis on Ukraine are part of a larger and welcome adjustment in U.S. priorities. Ukraine is no longer ignored or slighted. The White House is consciously stressing its support for Ukrainian independence; Secretary of State Warren Christopher has gone out of his way to emphasize U.S. support for Ukraine's territorial integrity on the sensitive issue of Crimea, and Defense Secretary William Perry has been truly energetic in furthering U.S.-Ukrainian military cooperation.
As of this year, aid to the former Soviet Union is no longer ignoring the non-Russian states; in fact, about half of it is now directed to them. This major reallocation reflects a growing recognition that a stable group of nations in the former Soviet Union is infinitely preferable to a renewed imperial structure -- indeed, that a return to empire would probably even doom the still uncertain prospects for Russian democracy.
At a time when the Clinton Administration is under assault for its foreign policy, it is good to be able to welcome the makings of a more realistic and coherent strategy for post-cold-war Europe.

HEADLINE: Let's Not 'Treat' the Problem of Homelessness With Drugs

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Your June 17 article reporting growing support in New York State for involuntary commitment to mental hospitals describes "a bill that would place those released from mental hospitals under court order to take medication and get counseling." 
 A major aim is to force treatment on street people, and in almost all cases, that means drugs without counseling. In my interviews with street people at drop-in centers, I have found nearly all of them would rather endure the risks of homelessness -- including starvation and physical abuse -- than subject themselves to psychiatric drug treatment. Is their viewpoint entirely irrational, as the proposed laws would suggest?
In most cases, the drugs forced on street people are categorized as neuroleptics or antipsychotics. These drugs cause a neurological disorder, tardive dyskinesia, which is usually untreatable and irreversible. It involves involuntary movements that can be disfiguring, disabling and sometimes painful. Conservative estimates indicate tardive dyskinesia strikes 25 percent or more of drug-treated patients. Among the homeless, who are likely to be exposed for longer periods, the prevalence can exceed 50 percent.
As I document in "Toxic Psychiatry" (1991) and elsewhere, proof is growing that many of these patients will also undergo drug-induced permanent damage to their mental processes, tardive dementia. Beyond that, the neuroleptics do not "tranquilize," as some other medications do. They typically produce painful and sometimes agonizing physical and emotional reactions, including akathisia, which can drive the victim to move compulsively in a vain attempt to alleviate the inner agitation.
While the neuroleptics do cause lobotomy-like apathy and docility, this serves the interests of institutional care more than the patient's needs.
Even if, by a seemingly objective standard, drug treatment might "help" some of these people, should we force it on them? In addition to the civil liberties issues, there are social and economic ones. The problems of homelessness should not be "treated" psychiatrically. Homelessness escalated under 12 years of economic policies that reduced the income of the poor and produced unemployment, while raising housing's cost.
Instead of psychiatrically sweeping the streets, let us increase the opportunities for America's poorer and less able citizens. As a group, they are most in need of unskilled or entry-level jobs and affordable housing. And those few who are too disabled to work need our sympathy and support, not involuntary drugs and giant lockups.
 
PETER R. BREGGIN, M.D.
Executive Director, Center
for the Study of Psychiatry
Bethesda, Md., June 17, 1994

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: SHAKE-UP AT THE WHITE HOUSE: BUDGET DIRECTOR
Woman in the News;
A Hawk on Budgets -- Alice Mitchell Rivlin

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Many young Americans dream of becoming President someday, but Alice M. Rivlin had a different dream: to become director of the Office of Management and Budget.
Much of her life has been pointed in that direction, and today President Clinton named her to that post. As soon as Bill Clinton was elected President, her many friends started mentioning her as a logical budget director. 
 And at the news conference announcing her appointment, she was bursting with pride that she will be the first woman to head the powerful office whose tentacles reach into every corner of the Government.
Ms. Rivlin, a 63-year-old Philadelphia native, comes to her new job well prepared, having served as Mr. Clinton's deputy budget director and the first director of the Congressional Budget Office.
 
'A Tremendous Knowledge'
 In her 18 months as deputy to Leon E. Panetta, Ms. Rivlin was a numbers-cruncher-in-chief while Mr. Panetta did a lot of the political work, handling members of Congress and facing off with Cabinet members who usually wanted more money for their agencies.
"She clearly brings a tremendous knowledge of the Federal budget, of deficit reduction and of the economy," said Robert Reischauer, the current director of the Congressional Budget Office. "Having worked on the Hill, she brings an understanding of the political dimension and a tremendous knowledge of the intricacies of different Federal programs."
More than a few Administration officials were surprised that President Clinton turned to Ms. Rivlin to replace Mr. Panetta, who was named White House chief of staff. The reason: Ms. Rivlin was in the President's doghouse for several months last year after she questioned whether billions of dollars in his national investment program was being well spent.
To the President's face, she sometimes suggested that it might be wiser to devote those billions to cutting the deficit than to spending it on programs like building highways. Among the President's top econonomic aides, Ms. Rivlin was always considered one of the staunchest deficit hawks.
For a while, there was talk of pushing her out of the O.M.B. by offering her a seat on the Federal Reserve Board. But she insisted on staying in the budget office.
Over the past year, Ms. Rivlin has worked her way back into the President's good graces through hard work and her mastery of the budget's details, other officials say.
Some economists say that naming Ms. Rivlin as O.M.B. director was an astute political move because it will bolster Mr. Clinton's deficit-fighting credentials. "Now it's to the White House's advantage to have someone who is viewed as tough on deficits," said John H. Makin, an economist at the American Enterprise Institute.
Georgianna Alice Mitchell -- she disliked and later dropped her first name -- was born on March 4, 1931. She graduated in 1952 from Bryn Mawr College, and six years later she received a doctorate in economics from Radcliffe College. In 1955, she married Lewis A. Rivlin, a Washington lawyer.
 
With Brookings Institution
 Even though her three children kept her busy, she rose steadily in the economics profession. After receiving her doctorate, she became an economist at the Brookings Institution, where she did research on the budget and social programs. In 1966, President Lyndon B. Johnson named her deputy assistant secretary for program analysis at the Department of Health, Education and Welfare.
She returned to Brookings after President Richard M. Nixon was re-elected in 1972. In 1975, when Congress was looking for a centrist Democratic economist who was not daunted by the Federal budget, it picked her to run its new budget office.
She ran the office for eight years before returning once again to Brookings. At the Congressional Budget Office, she earned high marks for her ability to analyze the economy and work with Republicans and Democrats alike. She also earned respect for telling powerful committee chairmen that budget constraints would not allow them to build every last pork-barrel project.
Now the challenge will be to face up to the Cabinet Secretaries in the annual budget battles. Having Mr. Panetta as White House chief of staff will of course strengthen her hand, although, her economist colleagues say, she does not need anyone's helping hand.
"Alice is pretty tough and pretty independent," said Mr. Makin. "I'm sure there are some people who have plans that will find themselves frustrated by Alice."

CORRECTION-DATE: June 29, 1994, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about Alice M. Rivlin, the newly appointed director of the Office of Management and Budget, referred incompletely to her marital status. While she married Lewis A. Rivlin in 1955, that marriage ended in divorce in 1977; she married Sidney Winter in 1989.

HEADLINE: SHAKE-UP AT THE WHITE HOUSE: NEWS ANALYSIS
Job for a Capital Insider;
In Calling on Panetta as Staff Chief, Clinton Opts for a Doer Rather Than a Doorkeeper

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Marlin Fitzwater, who was the President's spokesman in the Reagan and Bush White Houses, has just finished writing a chapter in his memoirs about the seven chiefs of staff he worked under -- starting and ending with James A. Baker 3d.
One of his conclusions is that Washington insiders have a better chance of succeeding as chief of staff than do outsiders. "The White House," Mr. Fitzwater said in an interview today, "operates on a set of rules and principles applicable in no other setting." 
 President Clinton has doubtless come to the same conclusion. Thomas F. McLarty 3d, his childhood friend, was admired in the White House for his congenial nature. But he was a corporate executive from Arkansas who knew neither the policy nor the players of Washington, and that proved to be his downfall.
"Clinton knew Mac's shortcomings, but he wanted him to be basically a doorkeeper," a senior White House official said. "Now he sees he needs something more than a friend who can organize the staff and his schedule."
By contrast, few people in Washington and certainly no one in the Clinton Administration has more of a grasp of the intricacies of Government policy than Leon E. Panetta. The main job description of the budget director is to master the details. And from his 16 years in Congress, Mr. Panetta has a wealth of friends and experience in the ways of the capital and election-year politics.
But merely being an insider is no guarantee of success. There is no assurance that Mr. Panetta's knowledge and experience can do what Mr. Clinton hopes, namely repair the Administration's image inside and outside Washington.
Mr. Clinton would like to dispel the notion around the country that the White House is a chaotic operation in which decisions are made largely on impulse. And he would like someone in charge of the staff who can direct all the energy here in the capital toward passing his legislative agenda, particularly his health care bill.
Mr. McLarty is not identified with any particular Administration policy. In fact, the only times he seemed to surface publicly was to take the blame for some misstep by the staff, as when staff members used an official plane this spring for a golf outing. In each case, Mr. McLarty took the blame, though he was probably not personally at fault, and promised that the mistake would not be repeated.
The most a chief of staff can do, said Charles O. Jones, the president of the American Political Science Association and author of a new book, "The Presidency in a Separated System," is to be "an intermediary between the President and what he wants and the rest of the Government."
Dismissing the chief of staff when things seem to be going awry within an Administration is nothing new. It is comparable to the way the manager is the first to go when a baseball team begins to lose.
Sometimes it works. Ronald Reagan sacked Donald T. Regan in 1987 when his Presidency was reeling from Iran-contra disclosures. Mr. Regan's replacement, Howard H. Baker Jr., provided a steady hand that helped put the Administration back on course, at least in terms of the public's perception. The move was instrumental in Mr. Reagan's restoring his popularity, which helped George Bush win the 1988 election.
 
Paralysis of Government
 But other times, it makes no difference who the chief of staff is. Mr. Bush dismissed John H. Sununu in December 1991, and nine months later, when his Presidency still seemed to be in trouble, Mr. Bush sent Mr. Sununu's successor, Samuel M. Skinner, packing and replaced him with James Baker, who had been Mr. Reagan's first chief of staff. Neither move changed the public's view that Mr. Bush's economic and domestic policies were misguided, a notion that probably cost him re-election.
The immediate effect of today's switch could be a few weeks of inaction at a particularly delicate moment in the development of health care legislation. People who have worked in the White House in other Administrations say that the Government is often nearly paralyzed for a month or two when a new chief of staff moves in while staff assistants jockey for position in the new regime.
That may not happen this time because Mr. Panetta is already well known by others in the Administration and because Mr. McLarty was not influential in making health policy. But it is certainly a risk.
If everything works right, White House officials said today, the staff shake-up could help the President in several ways.
Up till now, Mr. Clinton has basically functioned as his own chief of staff, at least as far as policy making is concerned. The one area where he has delegated the authority is in health care, and here it was to his wife, Hillary. In Mr. Panetta, he will have someone he trusts at his right hand to help him make the final call on Administration policy.
Mr. McLarty's most important role from now on, he said in an interview, will be First Friend -- someone who can tell the President without varnish what he needs to hear, however unpleasant the news. Except for Mrs. Clinton, no one thus far has been able to do that, one senior official said.
 
Shift of Gergen
 And by moving to the State Department, David R. Gergen, whose expertise is molding public opinion, can now take on the task of explaining how the Clinton foreign policy makes sense. Foreign affairs is normally one of the least partisan aspects of a Presidency. Mr. Gergen, a fixture under Republican Presidents, is obviously admired by Mr. Clinton, but he was resented in the political cauldron of the White House.
Whether all this works out or not, it is not likely to make or break the Clinton Presidency. The value of a chief of staff, said Mr. Fitzwater, is measured entirely by whether his President is successful.
And a President's success or failure, as Mr. Bush learned, eventually rests on his own shoulders.

CORRECTION-DATE: July 2, 1994, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
A chart on Tuesday listing White House chiefs of staff and their years of service misstated the starting date for Hamilton Jordan. He was President Jimmy Carter's chief of staff from July 1979 to June 1980; he did not start in January 1977.

HEADLINE: THE HEALTH CARE DEBATE: THE DETAILS;
Study Criticizes Subsidy For Insurance for Poor

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
A new study finds serious, unsuspected problems with many proposals for the Federal Government to help low-income people buy private health insurance by subsidizing their premiums.
 Such subsidies are a feature of most of the health insurance bills being considered in Congress. Subsidies have been suggested as a way to make sure that all Americans obtain health insurance, with or without a requirement that employers contribute to the cost of workers' coverage. 
But the study, issued today by the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities, a nonpartisan research institute, says that if subsidies are not carefully designed they will discourage people from working and may encourage companies to drop health insurance they now provide to employees.
 Moreover, the study, which looked at all proposals, says there are serious problems in plans to limit the amount the Government could spend on subsidies in any year: "they make no adjustment for recessions."
 
Problems of Recessions
 To stay within the spending limits, the study says, subsidies would be reduced at the very moment when the need was greatest, in recessions, as the numbers of poor and unemployed people rose.
 Government subsidies to help people buy insurance have been in health plans proposed by President Clinton; Representative Jim Cooper, Democrat of Tennessee; Senator John H. Chafee, Republican of Rhode Island, and Senator Edward M. Kennedy, Democrat of Massachusetts. Spending on such subsidies would be subject to limits set by Congress. None of the proposals would increase the spending limits in time of recession.
 Robert Greenstein, director of the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities, said the cost of subsidies would be much higher if there was no requirement for employers to pay any of the premiums for their workers. But, he observed, in the absence of an employer mandate, companies would have "powerful incentives" to drop coverage for their employees and to shift costs to the Government, which would subsidize the premiums.
 
On Providing Subsidies
 Describing what would happen without an employer mandate, Mr. Greenstein said the study found: "Employers could terminate coverage without jeopardizing poor workers. The workers would continue receiving similar coverage, with their premiums fully paid by the public subsidy system."
 Moreover, it says, "Firms that employ large numbers of low-income workers would be able to make themselves and their employees better off by terminating health coverage and converting some or all of the savings into higher wages or other employee benefits."
 Mr. Cooper defended his proposal, saying, "It is essential that health care reform include subsidies to make coverage affordable for low-income people."
 The study says that the proposed subsidies will become extremely expensive if they are available to large numbers of people in middle-income families. On the other hand, it says, if the subsidies decline sharply as earnings rise, they may discourage people from trying to earn more, especially people with incomes between the poverty level and twice that amount ($15,000 to $30,000 a year for a family of four). In other words they would lose their benefits and be subjected to more taxes as well.
 
Gradual Phasing Down Urged
 People just above the poverty level already pay a high "tax" on additional earnings because they may lose food stamps, tax credits and other benefits as their incomes rise.
 Thus, the study concludes: "Low-income subsidies should be phased down very gradually. Otherwise, incentives to work will be weakened too much."
 A family of four is classified as poor by the Government if it had cash income less than $14,764 in 1993. For each additional dollar earned, the report says, a family of four that has income 25 percent above the poverty level and receives food stamps would lose a total of 76 cents under the Clinton proposal, 78 cents under the Kennedy proposal, 90 cents under the Chafee proposal and 95 cents under the Cooper proposal.
 Put another way, such a family would keep 24 cents of each additional dollar earned under the Clinton proposal, but only 5 cents of each additional dollar under the Cooper proposal.
 
The Use of Overtime
 Such high marginal tax rates could easily discourage extra work effort because people would keep relatively little of their additional earnings, the report says.
 Mr. Greenstein said that an employer mandate could also have some unintended effects. For example, he said, it would create incentives for companies to make greater use of overtime by full-time workers, rather than hiring additional employees. "Use of overtime does not increase health care premium costs, while hiring new employees would do so," he said.
 The study by the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities examines the effects of subsidies on people at different income levels. The work of the center was assisted by a grant of $20,000 from the Henry J. Kaiser Family Foundation.

HEADLINE: THE HEALTH CARE DEBATE;
Virginia's Candidates for Senate Debate, Making Cases to Tough Audience of Voters

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
In a lively free-for-all on worldwide television tonight, the four candidates running for the United States Senate from Virginia clashed in their first joint debate over issues like character, crime and foreign policy.
Reopening old feuds and probably creating new ones, Senator Charles S. Robb and the three candidates who want his seat took the opportunity on CNN's "Larry King Live" to make their cases. 
 Mr. Robb, the Democratic nominee, repeatedly cited his ties to the White House and his ability to work closely with President Clinton. Oliver L. North, the Republican nominee who is best known for his involvement in the Iran-contra affair while working in the Reagan White House, declared that he was the only one of the four who had not spent his "entire life running for office."
Former Gov. L. Douglas Wilder, a Democrat who entered the race as an independent, accused Mr. Robb of being too close to Mr. Clinton and supporting economic policies that hurt the Virginia economy. Another independent candidate, J. Marshall Coleman, a Republican who is a former state Attorney General, went after Mr. North. Mr. Coleman repeatedly said that he could work closely with the state's senior Senator, John W. Warner, a Republican who has refused to endorse Mr. North and is backing Mr. Coleman.
 
The Character Issue
 Both nominees, Mr. Robb and Mr. North, have drawn opposition from within their parties because they are are seen as relatively unpopular in the state and particularly vulnerable on character issues. Mr. Robb has acknowledged marital indiscretions, and Mr. North was found to have lied to the Congress on his role in the Iran-contra matter, although his conviction was overturned on a technicality.
But only Mr. Coleman took up the character issue directly, near the close of the 90-minute program. Responding to a caller from Louisville, Ky., he said, referring to Mr. North: "I think ethics are an issue. This is an issue the people of Virginia need to resolve. I do not think the person to my left is someone we ought to vote for to be in the Senate. He has not set the kind of example we want to set."
Mr. North snapped back: "Oh, come on." He added, "From a guy who has changed his position more often than anybody I know, you ought to get whiplash."
Asked about his character, Mr. Robb said only that "I had some dents in my armor." But he said that "the Almighty forgave me," as did his wife, Lynda Bird.
 
Wilder Versus Robb
 On the Democratic side, Mr. Wilder agreed that character was an issue, but, in assailing Mr. Robb, he concentrated on the one-term Senator's support of Mr. Clinton's economic plan, which raised some taxes, and for his vote to confirm Clarence Thomas to the Supreme Court. "Either you support the rights of women or you don't," Mr. Wilder said to Mr. Robb. "Either you support minorities or you don't."
"Wait a minute," Mr. Robb retorted. "You supported him at one point." The Senator defended his vote on Mr. Thomas and said he had the best record on women's issues of any candidate.
Just the sight of the four candidates sitting shoulder to shoulder in a half-circle made for a spirited program. Unlike the others, who wore suits, Mr. North appeared in shirtsleeves, probably to present himself as a Washington outsider.
Except for an occasional slight, there was relatively little back and forth between the two party nominees. At one point, after Mr. North said he would not support the crime bill before Congress, Mr. Robb asserted, "First of all, Ollie, I recommend that you read the bill."
The fact that Larry King extended his program by 30 minutes to broadcast a Senate debate involving a single state demonstrated the intense interest in the race beyond Virginia. One caller during the second half was from Kissii, Kenya.
The program had its share of odd moments. One of the breaks was for a North commercial. And, after Mr. Wilder admonished the other candidates for accepting out-of-state donations, he blurted out his own toll-free number and appealed for contributions from around the country.
The race is generally viewed as wide open for any of the candidates, although a new Washington Post poll found that at this early stage in the race, Mr. Robb held a comfortable lead, with support from 36 percent of those polled, followed by Mr. North, with 26 percent. Mr. Wilder trailed with 17 percent, while Mr. Coleman received 13 percent. The survey was a telephone poll of 1,000 registered voters in Virginia conducted June 19-23. The poll's sampling error was plus or minus 3 percentage points.
Some of the most interesting responses came when a caller asked the four men to say which candidate they would vote for if they were not in the race. Mr. Robb, who responded first, grudgingly said he would vote for Mr. Wilder, his old nemesis, mainly because Mr. Wilder has traditionally been a Democrat. But Mr. Wilder refused to return the favor and would not say whom he would support. Mr. Coleman also refused to answer the question. Mr. North dodged the question by saying he would have voted for James Miller 3d, whom he defeated for the Republican nomination.
Mr. North said he had "high hopes" that former President Ronald Reagan would endorse him, although Mr. Reagan put out a letter this spring accusing Mr. North of misstating his role in about Iran-contra.
"Don't hold your breath," Mr. Coleman responded to Mr. North.

HEADLINE: THE HEALTH CARE DEBATE: NEGOTIATIONS;
2 Senate Leaders Propose Health Plans

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
Two major Senate players on health care legislation today laid out their own long-awaited legislative proposals but without strong hopes that the Senate Finance Committee would accept their main ideas.
When the committee meets on Wednesday afternoon, its chairman, Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, will put his proposal before it. The New York Democrat's plan would require employers to pay for their workers' insurance if other steps did not produce universal coverage by 1999. That provision appears to have a committee majority against it. 
 Asked what he would do if his plan was defeated, Mr. Moynihan said, "Let's find out whether we can't win."
Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the Republican leader, put out a tentative draft proposal that would emphasize changes in insurance laws but that also would provide $100 billion over five years in subsidies to help the poor buy insurance.
 
Differences in 2 Plans
 Mr. Dole's plan rejects the employer-paid insurance that is central to President Clinton's proposal to insure all Americans and is in most Democratic bills. It includes no requirements that anyone buy insurance, though like every other bill it seeks to make insurance easier to purchase.
Neither leader can count on the support of all members of his own party in the committee. A group of moderate Democrats and Republicans offered its own plan last week, and substantial parts of that plan reappear in both the Moynihan and Dole measures. The moderates are likely to oppose both plans, holding that the Dole plan does not go far enough and that the Moynihan bill goes too far.
Mr. Moynihan today attributed his difficulties to this alliance of more conservative Democrats and more liberal Republicans. He said his fallback requirement for employers to buy insurance for their workers "had the votes a month ago, but the coming together of the two groups makes it very chancy."
"It's close," he said. "We'll find out."
The leaders of that group of six, Senators John H. Chafee, Republican of Rhode Island, and John B. Breaux, Democrat of Louisiana, met with their colleagues this evening to plot how to advance their proposal if Mr. Moynihan's fails.
Mr. Dole was not committed to offering his proposal before the committee. He called a caucus of Republican Senators to discuss it at 8 A.M. Wednesday. But because some Republicans would probably vote against it in committee, he may not offer it there at all.
The Dole plan would provide subsidies for low-income people to buy a Government-designed package of benefits. People at or below the poverty level would receive full premium subsidies; smaller subsidies would be offered to people whose income was up to 150 percent of the poverty level. Mr. Dole proposed to pay for his plan by raising the Medicare tax on people with high incomes, slowing the rate of growth of Medicaid spending and additional "Medicare savings (to be determined)."
 
Dole on the Attack
 Senator Dole, who has complained about President and Mrs. Clinton's criticism of Congress for accepting Government contributions to its health insurance while refusing to vote for employers' contributions for all Americans, struck back in his plan. He proposed eliminating the Government payments for insurance for the President (but not the Vice President), all members of Congress and the Cabinet, and political appointees confirmed by the Senate.
The Moynihan plan's central element of requiring employers to pay for 80 percent of their workers' insurance would take effect gradually. If, by 1997, insurance coverage for workers, and their dependents, at companies employing 100 or more people did not rise to 98 percent from the current 89 percent, then all employers in that category would have to insure their workers.
For employers of 25 to 99 workers, insurance coverage would have to rise to 96 percent from the current 79 percent by 1998, or the companies would have to insure workers and their dependents.
Finally, employers of fewer than 25 workers would have until 1999 to raise the coverage level to 93 percent from the current 74 percent. If that did not happen, they would have to provide insurance.
The Moynihan bill would provide subsidies to low-income individuals and families and to small businesses to help pay for premiums. To pay for them, it would raise the tax on a pack of cigarettes to $2, and, as would Mr. Dole's measure, it would raise Medicare premiums for individuals making more than $90,000 and families making more than $115,000.
Senator Edward M. Kennedy of Massachusetts, chairman of the Senate Labor and Human Resources Committee, and leaders of 14 black organizations held a news conference today to announce support for universal coverage. They contended that 55 percent of black Americans were either uninsured or underinsured.
The groups promised to lobby members of the House and Senate, a tactic that is likely to get the attention of Democrats, especially in the South, who depend on black votes to win.
 
Disappointment for Dingell
 Another committee chairman, Representative John D. Dingell of Michigan, officially gave up any effort to draw up a bill in the Energy and Commerce Committee, which he heads. Mr. Dingell, one of the House's most skilled deal makers and a passionate advocate of national health insurance, could not overcome united Republican opposition to any bill requiring employer payments.
In a letter to the House Speaker, Thomas S. Foley of Washington, Mr. Dingell promised to help develop legislation and work with leaders of other committees. Mr. Dingell called his decision a disappointment. He has championed national health insurance since he came to the House in 1955, succeeding his father, also Representative John D. Dingell, who introduced the first national health insurance bill in the House in 1943.
The House Ways and Means Committee, continuing work on a comprehensive bill, endorsed the idea of price controls on the cost of doctors, hospitals and prescription drugs if spending exceeded certain amounts. Under the plan, the Federal Government would set annual spending goals for each state. The price controls would take effect in 2001 and later years in any state exceeding its goal.
Representative Michael A. Andrews, Democrat of Texas, tried but failed, on a vote of 20 to 18, to eliminate the price controls idea.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Senator Bob Dole, at left, has drafted a tentative health insurance plan that would not require employers to pay the premiums. DEVELOPMENTS YESTERDAY
 
Congressional committees continue their efforts to move legislation forward for debate in the full House and Senate.
 
Congress
 
The Senate: A Moynihan Proposal . . .
 
Sponsor -- Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, Democrat of New York, chairman of the Senate Finance Committee.
 
Highlights -- Senator Maynihan's second variation on the Clinton plan. Among other things, it would require employers to provide insurance for their workers if industry reforms did not meet universal coverage goals by 1999
 
. . . And a Dole Alternative
 
Sponsor -- Senator Bob Dole, Republican of Kansas, the minority leader and a member of the Finance Committee.
 
Highlights -- Would make changes in insurance regulation to make coverage easier to obtain, and offer subsidies to help the poor purchase it.
 
The House -- Representative John D. Dingell, Democrat of Michigan, the chairman of the Energy and Commerce Committee and a passionate advocate of guaranteed health care, announced that he had given up any attempt to pass a bill in his deadlocked committee. Democrats on the House Ways and Means Committee caucused through the day on another proposal, especially its cost-control provisions.
 
The White House -- Hillary Rodham Clinton addressed the Economic Club of Washington, a business group, and predicted that if Congress did not approve a Clinton-style plan, a grass-roots movement will force Congress to enact Government-paid health care.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Again Falls Below 100-Yen Level

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
The dollar fell to another record low against the Japanese yen in Asian trading today.
The decline came even though the Bank of Japan intervened in the currency market to support the dollar and despite Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bensten's renewed promise of support for a stronger American currency. 
 In Tokyo this morning, the dollar hit a new post-World War II low of 99.10 yen. Traders said the Japanese central bank began buying dollars for yen after the dollar fell below the previous low of 99.50 yen.
The Asian selling came at about the same time that Mr. Bentsen spoke to the Foreign Policy Association in New York yesterday evening. "We believe a stronger dollar is better for our economy and better for the world's economy," he said.
In his remarks, Mr. Bentsen denied that the Clinton Administration still wanted a weaker dollar as leverage against the Japanese in bilateral trade talks.
"Let me say clearly -- and I speak for the entire Administration -- this is not the case," he said. "The dollar is not a tool of our trade policy."
But some traders in Tokyo apparently remained unconvinced of the United States' resolve to support the dollar. The Federal Reserve has been absent from the currency market so far this week, after a concerted round of intervention on Friday failed to push up the dollar.
By the afternoon in Tokyo today, the dollar was quoted at 99.35 yen. In New York late yesterday, the dollar had ended trading at 100.02 yen, down from 100.45 yen late Monday.
The dollar also moved lower against the German mark, but in a broad trading range. Some dealers said the wider range suggested that the market was prepared to take the dollar lower against the mark.
The dollar was last quoted at 1.5787 marks in New York late yesterday, down from 1.5840 late Monday. In afternoon trading in Tokyo today, it was quoted at 1.5745 marks.
"For the moment, the dollar doesn't look wrongly valued" against the yen, said Richard E. Witten, a partner at Goldman, Sachs & Company. "But the feeling in the marketplace is that the dollar can continue to decline against the European currencies."
Referring to the central banks, Mr. Witten said, "There does not appear to be any significant interest on either side of the Atlantic if the dollar declines as much as another 5 percent against the mark."
Such a drop would send the dollar down to about 1.50 marks.
Trading has been light so far this week, and some currency traders say the selling pressure on the dollar may be beginning to ease for now.
In part, yesterday's bout of weakness was caused by fresh signs of strength in the American economy, signs that at another time might have tended to bolster the dollar's value.
Strong underlying growth in the economy tends to raise the currency's value relative to others, especially those of countries whose economies are not growing as rapidly. At the moment, the United States economy is expanding at a much more rapid clip than those of its leading trading partners, including Japan and Germany.
 
Still Some Uncertainty
 But there is considerable uncertainty about economic growth in the United States and in other leading industrialized countries. In addition, financial markets are worried about how the Federal Reserve and other big central banks will respond to coming economic reports.
Lately, the nervousness has been manifested most clearly in the foreign exchange market, where the dollar has weakened despite attempts by central banks to shore up its value.
Despite the relative stability of the dollar early this week, dealers and analysts say the currency may still face some turbulent days.
"There is no sense that there is a big change in market sentiment or that there has been a permanent standoff" between foreign exchange traders and the central banks, said John Lipsky, chief economist for Salomon Brothers. "The next few weeks will be immensely complicated."
The run-up to the Fourth of July holiday will probably act as a further depressant to trading activity this week.
But next week, the Fed's policy-making Open Market Committee will hold an important meeting to determine the course of interest rates, and the Government will release June's employment data, which are expected to give indications of whether economic growth is too fast or too slow.
 
Finance Minister to Meet
 In addition, the finance ministers from the Group of Seven industrial nations will meet in Naples to discuss the dollar, among other matters.
Traders said they expected the finance ministers to make comments about the recent upheaval in the currency markets before the Group of Seven meeting, and the market could move on the comments.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:
Business Technology
 Freedom of expression has always ruled in cyberspace. But the companies that control the computer networks, and some individuals, are beginning to play the role of censor. [Page A1.] 
 United Technologies has developed a flywheel battery that, among other virtues, would give an electric car greater bursts of power and a longer range before recharging. [D1.]
 Companies National Medical Enterprises said it would plead guilty to paying kickbacks and bribes for referrals and would pay $362.7 million in a settlement with the Government. [D1.]
General Motors made several executive shifts at its North American automotive operations, showing a willingness to trade seniority for perceived ability. [D1.]
Metropolitan Life is being investigated by the U.S. Attorney's office in Tampa, Fla., state officials said. [D2.]
St. Jude Medical, a maker of heart valves, agreed to acquire the cardiac device business of Siemens for $500 million. [D4.]
Mobil plans to reduce jobs in its chemical operations by a fifth. The company will also take a charge of $315 million. [D4.]
Daimler-Benz is expected to name the head of its aerospace division to succeed Edzard Reuter as chairman. [D4.]
Bergdorf Goodman abruptly eliminated the position of fashion director, putting Ellin Saltzman out of a job. [D4.]
Unocal has put its California oil and gas assets up for sale. The company plans to use the money to invest overseas. [D4.]
Viacom's deadline for first bids for Madison Square Garden, the New York Knicks and New York Rangers passed. [D17.]
Two Western electric and gas utilities agreed to merge in a deal that would form a company with assets of $3.4 billion. [D17.]
 International Brazilian coffee growers tried to assess the damage from a cold snap that destroyed about a third of next year's crop, causing coffee prices to soar on world commodity markets. [D1.]
Japan announced a plan aimed at eliminating dozens of arcane regulations that have irked trading partners. [D2.]
 
The Economy
 Home sales surged 4.2 percent in May to the briskest pace of the year, apparently shrugging off a rise in mortgage rates. The Conference Board's confidence index advanced. [D1.]
The House approved legislation that would permit greater competition between local and long-distance telephone companies, as well as between telephone and cable television. [D2.]
The F.D.A. Commissioner said he hoped to avoid a ban on cigarettes, as health officials and tobacco executives moved toward agreement that some regulation is needed. [A1.]
 
Markets
 The dollar fell to another record low against the Japanese yen in Asian trading. [D1.]
 Blue-chip stocks retreated from a strong gain on Monday. The Dow Jones industrial average dropped 15.86 points. [D6.]

HEADLINE: 2 KOREAS SET DATE FOR FIRST MEETING OF THEIR LEADERS

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, June 28

 BODY:
North and South Korea agreed today to a three-day summit meeting starting on July 25 in Pyongyang, the North Korean capital. It would be the first meeting of the top leaders of the two countries since the Korean Peninsula was divided in 1945.
The North and the South announced agreement in principle last week for a summit meeting between South Korea's first civilian President in more than three decades, Kim Young Sam, and North Korea's 82-year-old dictator, Kim Il Sung. But the setting of a date today substantially increases the prospect that the meeting will take place. 
 At that meeting, South Korean officials said, the date and location of a second summit meeting, likely in the South, is to be negotiated.
Today's accord came in a meeting that was remarkably free of bitterness, given the continuing accusations over the considerable evidence that North Korea is trying to build nuclear weapons. The meeting was at the border village of Panmunjom, along the Demilitarized Zone where the armistice was declared in the Korean War 41 years ago.
Only three months ago, at the same table, a North Korean official was threatening to turn Seoul, 30 miles to the south, into a "sea of fire" for bringing international pressure and the threat of sanctions against the North for its failure to open its nuclear sites to international inspectors.
Many in South Korea have been deeply suspicious of whether the North's suddenly cooperative, friendly behavior is sincere, a result of the new openings former President Jimmy Carter appears to have made during his trip to Pyongyang 10 days ago, or whether it is merely another attempt to stall for time.
The speed with which the two sides settled the details of the meeting, with few of the angry exchanges that usually punctuate such discussions, suggest that both sides were eager to capitalize on Mr. Carter's trip.
The agenda was not defined, and it is likely to be affected by the outcome of a crucial meeting between the United States and the North on July 8 to discuss the nuclear issue.
There are a range of long-pending issues between the North and South, including opening the border for visits of families that have been divided for more than 40 years, economic cooperation and possible steps to lower troop levels and tensions.
 
Nuclear Issue in Foreground
 Resolution of most of the issues is likely to depend on steady progress on the nuclear disagreement.
For Kim Il Sung, now the world's longest-serving leader, it is a political triumph: the leader of the South will be coming to treat him as an equal in his own capital.
For Kim Young Sam, it is an equally rich moment. Millions of South Koreans have relatives in the North, and seeing Seoul's leader in Pyongyang will clearly give many hope that Asia's Berlin Wall is falling.
If the summit meeting is successful, it will also redeem Mr. Carter, whose travels to Pyongyang earlier this month were a source of constant controversy. Mr. Carter emerged declaring that the "nuclear crisis is over" and that his two days of talks with the Communist leader had been a "miracle."
Many of Mr. Carter's critics suggested that he was naive in seeming to accept everything that Kim Il Sung said so uncritically. But others suggest that he was shrewdly trying to box the North in and make Mr. Kim's aides carry through on whatever the "Great Leader" declares.
Leaders of the two Koreas have never met since the peninsula was divided in 1945, though they have talked about it periodically since 1972. But there is considerable wariness on both sides that the meeting could turn from a celebration of Korean brotherhood to another bitter argument between two countries still technically at war.
 
Criticism of Site
 Already there are charges that Seoul has caved in to the North. Chosun Ilbo, South Korea's leading newspaper, suggested in an editorial that the South's President was being manipulated by the North and should not have agreed to allow the first summit meeting to occur in Pyongyang rather than some neutral location.
Until now, the highest-level contacts were a series of meetings between the Prime Ministers of North and South Korea in 1990 and 1991, culminating in an agreement to remove all nuclear weapons from the Korean Peninsula and establish greater contacts among the people of the divided country.
The denuclearization agreement also bans reprocessing of nuclear fuel on either side, yet the North has built an extensive reprocessing plant.
The July 25 meeting is to follow the high-level meeting of American and North Korean officials, to begin on July 8 in Geneva. That meeting, set up by Mr. Carter, will discuss North Korea's willingness to open up to full nuclear inspection in return for a package deal of economic and diplomatic incentives.
North Korea has agreed to a temporary freeze on its nuclear development activity while the talks are on, including the transporting of spent fuel rods from a nuclear reactor to a reprocessing plant, where bomb-grade plutonium could be extracted.
 
Step Toward Reconciliation
 In contrast, the goals of the North-South meeting are likely to be more symbolic: a step toward national reconciliation after a half-century of conflict. North Korea's chief representative at today's meeting, Kim Young Sun, said to his South Korean counterpart, Lee Hong Koo, that the meeting would bring "bright hopes of reunification in the 1990's."
In fact, reunification is one topic both men are expected to steer gingerly around, because they have widely differing views of how it may take place, and on whose terms.
As he arrived at the meeting site today, Lee Hong Koo, South Korea's Unification Minister and Deputy Prime Minister, told his Northern counterparts that he hoped that the six-lane highway he had taken to the Demilitarized Zone would soon be extended to Pyongyang. The North Korean delegation smiled, but said nothing about opening up their society not only to the South's vehicles, but to its wealth and its ideas.

HEADLINE: Enduring Crises to Graduation Day;
New York High School Seniors Turn Tassels and Toss Books

BYLINE: By CHARISSE JONES 

 BODY:
In the years it took to reach her graduation day, Catherine Williams had a baby and named him Duvall. She peeked at him between classes, cuddled him while doing homework, and now, after long days and bookish summers, is preparing to go off to a community college.
In the years it took Flora Tartakovsky to don her cap and gown, she worked her summers away in a hospital and did medical research worthy of a national prize. Now, the 18-year-old, born in Ukraine and raised in Queens is preparing to leave for Harvard. 
 And a young man named Gregory Zieba will lay down his school books and search for work, because he would rather his family send his sister to college than spend their limited resources on him.
In ceremonies that culminated yesterday -- the last day of school for New York City's public schools -- some 34,000 high school seniors turned their tassels, tossed their books and crossed a threshold that separates childhood from the grown-up world.
They are what remain of the more than 80,000 students who entered high school in 1990. On average, less than 40 percent complete their secondary education within four years. Some of this week's graduates include students who have spent five, six or seven years in high school. Literally thousands of others drop out or move away.
To paint a picture of these graduates' high school years is to speak of rising enrollment and shrinking classrooms, of deep budget cuts and crumbling schools. Indeed, their senior year began and ended two weeks late because of an asbestos crisis last fall. To sketch their future is to show an ever more demanding job market, and college fees that for too many will be too high. Yet a portrait of their faces is more hopeful.
If, as state education officials say, the percentage of students who plan to go on to higher education is roughly the same this year as last, about 19,000 students will attend a four-year college and roughly 7,000 students will attend a two-year college. About 700 will go to technical training schools.
Most will take advantage of public institutions here in New York, with 41 percent saying they would attend a school in the City University of New York system, and another 12 percent planning to go to college within the State University of New York system, said Robert Terte, a Board of Education spokesman.
Precise statistics will not become available until August.
The high number of students planning to go to college belies the low expectations some have of the New York City school system. The reality, said Peter Caruso of the New York State Education Department, is "if the kids stay in school long enough to get to the 12th grade, most go on and graduate and go to college."
The City University of New York, which charges a maximum annual tuition of $2,450 for New York residents, expects 57 percent of its first-time freshmen this fall to come from the city's public schools, said a spokeswoman, Rita Rodin.
 
Enlisting or Working
 "It's always been a door to a better life," Ms. Rodin said of CUNY, noting that the system's open-admission policy guarantees a place to all students with a high school diploma. "For many students who can't afford higher tuition, it is their only chance for higher education."
Of those not planning to go to college last year, 1.5 percent said they were going into the military, and 6.8 percent said they had either already found work or were looking for jobs, Mr. Terte said. About 9 percent said they had other plans, while 4.1 percent did not respond.
Whatever their plans, this year's graduates have weathered changes and crises in high school. In four years, enrollment in the nation's largest school system crept above one million, filling many of the students' classrooms to capacity and putting an extra burden on their teachers. Three different chancellors held forth over the city's schools, and many students said they saw their books crumble and classes disappear as more than a billion dollars of the system's budget were chopped away.
Despite the singular challenges it endured, the class of 1994 is like many of years past. Within its ranks are three winners of the prestigious Westinghouse Science Talent Search, and hundreds of other students who fought the pull of the streets and lethargy of mediocre grades to make it to graduation day.
And like teen-agers everywhere who stand on the cusp of adulthood, there are those who approach the future with naive ambition, hoping that they can save the world -- or at least their own neighborhood.
Miriam Lora's dreams are rooted in a nightmare. The 17-year-old graduate of Brandeis High School said two people have been killed in front of her family's Bronx apartment building. So she wants to be an F.B.I. agent, and "take the criminals out of the street." She will attend the John Jay College of Criminal Justice.
Others, while giddy with the anticipation of new challenges, admit that they are feeling a little fear.
Annette Stamatelatos, 17, is an aspiring singer who said she was born with a song. So when she thinks of college, and life beyond, she envisions it as if it were a performance.
"I think of it like I'm going on stage," said Miss Stamatelatos, a graduate of John Bowne High School in Queens who will attend Queensborough Community College in the fall and hopes to eventually go to Juilliard. "I'm nervous, but sooner or later, I will get through it."
Eighteen-year-old Miss Tartakovsky knows she will be fine as well. But that does not make leaving home any easier.
Four years ago she was a freshman at the Bronx High School of Science. Two weeks ago, she was wearing a dress of green velvet and dancing at her prom. Today she is a high school graduate bound for Harvard.
 
Missing High School
 "The fact you're still considered a child," is one of the things she said she would miss most about high school. "Even when you do irresponsible things, they say: 'Hey, it's O.K. You're a teen-ager.' It's different in college. You have to bear a lot more responsibility for your actions."
A stellar student, Miss Tartakovsky won a $15,000 scholarship this year in the Westinghouse Science competition for her experiment on early detection of primary biliary cirrhosis, an inflammatory disease of the liver.
But despite her academic success, she said that she, like so many New York City students, did not escape mammoth budget cuts unscathed.
"Some of my books were literally in pieces," she said.
For some, the obstacles to graduation went far beyond a lack of textbooks.
Images of Miss Williams's twin struggles dot her Brooklyn apartment. On a wall there is a poster, "Be a Math Monster." On the window sill is a container of baby powder.
It took the 19-year-old six years to graduate from Springfield Gardens High School in Queens. When she was 15, her mother died. When she was 17, she had a baby.
She still must pass one more of the state regents competency tests required for all high school students to get their diplomas. But in the fall, she plans to enter a pre-nursing program at the Borough of Manhattan Community College.
 
'I Have to Get a Good Job'
 As a freshman, she failed every course. But two months after having her son she was back in Springfield, taking eight classes a day and participating in a Board of Education day-care program for teen-age mothers.
"The way I feel is if I'm not educated, how can I educate him?" she said. "So I got serious. I have to take care of him until he's good and grown. So I have to get a good job."
She would rise at 5:30 A.M., scramble out of the door by 7 and be in school by 8. She would drop Duvall in the school's nursery, and head to class, peeking in on him when she got the chance. Good-natured taunts from classmates laced her day. ("Cathy, how old are you now? 25?") But in January, she completed high school.
Mr. Zieba, 18, has made an adult's choice, though in many ways, he is a child still.
"I had a lot of time to think about it," said Mr. Zieba, who graduated last Friday from Automotive High School in Brooklyn. "In school, I haven't been a great student, and my sister is the third best student in her school. She really wants to go to college. It's better for my father's money to be spent on her than me."
So he will join thousands of other high school graduates who go straight from high school to the job market. It will not be easy, labor experts say.
"In New York it's not just finishing school that's an issue," said Samuel M. Ehrenhalt, middle-Atlantic regional commissioner for the United States Bureau of Labor Statistics. "It's your ability to take on additional training, to adapt to change. It just takes more to make it these days in the world of work and a lot of youngsters leave school with the bare minimum."
Though the bureau did not have statistics on how many New York City high school graduates who skip college end up unemployed, figures through March 1994 show that the rate of unemployment in the city for 16- to 19-year-olds was 36 percent compared with 19 percent nationally.
 
Joining the Marines
 Still, Mr. Zieba is optimistic. If you've got a job in car mechanics, water-proofing or carpentry, he'll take it.
There was one invaluable lesson he learned in the hallways of his school. One of four whites out of 1,100 students, Mr. Zieba once feared his fellow students would be unkind. But he found many friends, like Narindra Lakhicharran.
Mr. Lakhicharran, an immigrant from Guyana, has shiny black hair, and a movie star smile. But possibly as soon as next month, he will be in boot camp, watching his locks fall to the floor, and learning the rigors of being a marine.
"I don't have a choice to go straight to college because I have financial problems," said Mr. Lakhicharran, who plans to go back to school in 1995, with the military paying for his education.
Nixon Mendez hopes to go onto college one day as well. But he has yet to cross high school's finish line.
At 20, he is older than what many would expect a high school senior to be. He hoped to have graduated this year, but he must take three more classes in summer school before he can lay his hands on a diploma.
He is unsure of his destination. But the path to get there, he said, is clear. "When I first started, I thought I would just have fun," said the Brandeis High School student. "But I realized that that can't continue. So I decided to try and graduate."
Here's to the class of 1995.

HEADLINE: China Migrants: Economic Engine, Social Burden

BYLINE: By PATRICK E. TYLER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: BEIJING, June 28

 BODY:
With his possessions bundled in plastic and hung from his shoulders, and his trousers rolled up to beat the heat, 30-year-old Ren Jun drifted into Beijing this month, part of a migrant tide of 50 million peasants that is threatening to swamp China's urban landscape.
To the Communist Party leadership, they are the engine of China, an inexhaustible supply of cheap labor, a floating population helping to build the country. They are one reason that 5,000 factories can be simultaneously under construction in China's coastal provinces. 
 But they also are becoming a huge and at times unstable and exploited force rampant on the fringes of China's overcrowded and polluted urban centers, where crime, corruption and unemployment threaten stability.
The migrants are easy to spot. Most large cities along China's coast have a million or two living in shanty towns, dormitories or public spaces. At Beijing's main railway station, a thousand migrants an hour pass through the green metal gates. And vast numbers of laborers like Mr. Ren appear each morning here at an impromptu labor market, hoping local coal-mine supervisors will pick them to work.
 
'A Positive Development'
 "So far, I think it has been quite a positive development," said Fan Gang, a leading economist at the Chinese Academy of Social Sciences. "It has helped to transfer wealth from rich to poor areas of the country."
Richard Baum, a political science professor at the University of California at Los Angeles, called the mobile population "a shock absorber" that can flow from one sector of the fast-changing economy to another "to cushion the transformation of the Chinese economy."
At the same time, today's migration could be the harbinger of an even greater one to come, Chinese and Western specialists say. With 130 million surplus workers in China's farm belt and a surge in population growth that will push that number to 200 million by 2000, China's migrants are an X factor in China's future.
"In five years, this could become a very big problem," Professor Fan said.
 
Estimate of 70 Million
 In March, Agriculture Minister Liu Jiang estimated that 50 million peasants had left their farms in 1993 to seek employment in the cities, more than double the 24 million who set off for the cities in 1992.
Some estimates put the "floating population" at 70 million to 100 million, said Dorothy Solinger, a China scholar at the University of California at Irvine. She also argued that the migrant problem "may not be as bleak as it is made out to sound."
"First, they are not all going to the big cities," she said. Many migrants simply move off farms into township enterprises nearby, or even far away, but not necessarily to large cities.
Second, she argued, where migrants do cluster, crime is the largest potential worry, not political rebellion. She pointed out that the Mayor of Zhuhai, the special economic zone adjoining Macao, recently said 75 percent of the crime in his city could be attributed to migrants.
But much is simply unknown, she said. The growth of the migrant phenomenon came from nowhere in a country that enforced rigid residency rules just a decade ago, making it impossible for peasants or city dwellers to stray from their work units.
What seems undisputed is that Pearl River delta in Guangdong Province has the largest concentration of migrants, an estimated 10 million. At least 500,000 are child laborers, Chinese surveys have shown; many work in sweatshop conditions.
 
Dangers of a Downturn
 In Jiangxi Province, the outflow of farmers leaped from 200,000 in 1991 to more than 3 million last year. Shanghai's 13 million residents are now supporting 2.5 million rural workers attracted from all over the Yangtze valley to construction work on the city's vast redevelopment.
As long as China's economic growth gallops along at more than 10 percent a year, this floating population is likely to remain relatively well employed, prosperous and stable. But an economic downturn or recession could easily leave the migrants stranded and aggrieved.
"This is the labor of an exploited class," said a longtime Western diplomat here. "There are no wage laws to protect them, and they can be fired on a whim."
Professor Baum added: "It is a double-edged sword to be sure. They may be an efficient buffer helping to transform the economy, but they are also a large pool of marginal people subject to the vagaries and insecurity of having no rice bowl they can count on. They are dying in grotesque industrial accidents, they are locked in the dormitories at night, and they create a pocket of potential human misery wherever they cluster."
 
Building Subways and Hotels
 Farmers from Sichuan Province are building Beijing's new subway, just as peasants from Zhejiang Province are building new freeways, hotels, office buildings and ministries. Many leave their villages because $1.25 a day on a sweltering construction site is more than they can earn working the land.
Others are "environmental refugees" from northern and western China where the land has just given out from degraded soil, dried up water resources or the relentless advance of the Central Asian deserts.
Many go home with money in their pockets, but many also go home nurturing grievances.
On a recent afternoon, six unkempt men lying on bedrolls in Beijing's main railway station explained why they were returning to Jiangsu Province after less than a month at work here. Their spokesman was a 24-year-old farmer named Liu with a face lined by weather and worry.
"We just don't want to work here any more," he said. "We left our construction team yesterday without notifying the boss. It serves him right."
The construction boss had come to Mr. Liu's hometown and promised to pay the villagers $1.72 a day to lay bricks and pour concrete. He said he would also pay for their train tickets. But when they got to the site, near Beijing's international airport, the boss cut their wages to 57 cents a day and told them they would have to pay for their own train tickets.
 
The Uses of Money
 Their tempers simmered for a month before they decided to head back to the farm, where their wives were looking after the rice crop.
Speaking in a tone that reflected their intimidation in the big city, Mr. Liu said: "We dare not walk in Beijing alone. We are new here. Some people might find faults with us, or fine us."
Remittances from peasants who do find work are dramatically redistributing the new wealth.
Professor Fan said that in one county in Sichuan Province, migrants remitted $138 million last year, more than the total economic output of the county, which was $115 million. A recent study of China's rural economy said migrant farmers from Anhui Province sent home $862 million, which exceeded the provincial government's annual revenue by $230 million.
What is most remarkable, however, is the sheer mass of migration.
During Lunar New Year celebrations, when all 1.2 billion Chinese are drawn home to their families, train stations became encampments for hundreds of thousands of travelers a day, Woodstock-sized crowds of peasants waiting for trains in dozens of rail hubs.
 
52 Trampled to Death
 At the New Year in February, China's overburdened rail system pressed special freight trains into service, each one bulging with human cargo packed so tightly that some passengers became hysterical with claustrophobia. In one accident, 52 people were trampled to death when 10,000 migrants were herded onto a freight train in Hunan Province.
Mr. Ren arrived recently in Beijing hoping to land a job in the local coal mines. He made his way to the labor market, becoming one face in a sea of laborers for hire.
Alarmed at the rapid growth of Beijing, the municipal authorities have posted signs saying the market has been closed. This has not stopped the peasants from coming, nor has it stopped gangs of criminals from prowling the market, looking for women and children to abduct and sell into China's booming trade in prostitutes and wives for sale.
 
From One City to Next
 Policemen on foot patrols often scatter the workers like so many chickens.
One laborer who kept well away from the police was Wang Xinmin, 24, who has migrated from one city to another since he left Henan Province at 17. Too much work on not enough land put him on the road, he said.
He has worked in the far south, at a garment factory in the special economic zone of Shenzhen, adjacent to Hong Kong, but it did not last.
"The local people there bullied us because we were from the countryside," he said. And the factory boss, who had promised high wages, delivered only half of that on payday.
"The working conditions were really unsafe and chaotic," he said, "and though we were supposed to work eight hours a day, actually we had to work much longer."
Having lost his most recent job, as a busboy, Mr. Wang said he would try for two more weeks to land another job in Beijing before heading home.
 
Farming 'Not Worth It'
 Mr. Wang sat cross-legged under a pine tree near the labor market, and when he finished telling his story, he listened to one from Mr. Ren, who comes from a line of farmers in the hardscrabble hills of Hebei, 120 miles northwest of Beijing.
"People like me don't want to work on the land anymore," Mr. Ren said. "It's backbreaking work. I've tried it. The land is not fertile, and it is far from any water. It's just not worth it."
If he stayed home and worked the land, Mr. Ren said, he might earn $345 a year if rain were plentiful. But working in a Beijing coal mines, he might take home nearly $700.
In either case, it is not enough to get a wife these days. "I would like to have a wife," he said. "But the dowry demands of girls are very high," higher even than the wages of a migrant coal miner.
