HEADLINE: Sabi Sand Journal;
Trying to Bridge South Africa's Rich-Poor Chasm

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: SABI SAND GAME RESERVE, South Africa

 BODY:
The worlds on either side of the wire fence that surrounds this mammoth private game reserve could not be more different from each other.
Inside the fence is a 140,000-acre wildlife refuge where wealthy tourists, most of them white, luxuriate in opulent surroundings, sip drinks on shaded verandas and gambol about the bush in Land Rovers photographing cheetah, lions, rhinos, elephants and other game. 
 Just outside the fence is grinding poverty in this stretch of the former tribal homeland of Gazankulu, one of the 10 ostensibly self-governing areas for blacks set up under the old apartheid regime. Some 500,000 people crammed into about 140 square miles in the immediate vicinity try to wrench a meager living from the agriculturally marginal land by raising cattle, goats and corn.
For years the two worlds rarely intersected except for the few local residents who worked at the game lodges as cooks, cleaning women, drivers or game trackers. But in the last 18 months, some of the private landholders in the reserve have made tentative steps to develop black-owned businesses to provide goods and services to the game lodges and to create alternatives to traditional subsistence farming.
"The idea is to attract wealthy international travelers and connect them with economically devastated areas," said Dave Varty, chairman of the Conservation Corporation, which runs a 24-room lodge called Londolozi on the reserve.
So far the efforts have been modest. The Conservation Corporation has helped start a business to make earthen pots for plants that decorate its lodges. It has also helped local women to get a $28,000 loan from a foundation to start small sewing cooperatives that make clothing for the local people and for tourists to buy in the gift shops. A group of landowners contributed about $10,000 to buy a water pump and to build a water tank so that a nearby community could create an eight-acre community garden that they hope will supply fresh produce to some of the lodges.
There are more ambitious plans as well. A community group has asked the Conservation Corporation to lend it capital to build a landing strip for aircraft bringing visitors to the nearby Kruger National Park. And there are plans for laundries, auto repair shops and film developing companies.
"The people here are not looking for a type of affirmative action that says: we're black, give us your business," said Grant Hearn, a rural development specialist from the University of Natal whose work in the area is financed by landowners in the reserve. "They're saying: train us and we will compete as suppliers."
Landowners in the reserve see their efforts not as charity but as a kind of insurance policy now that political power has shifted to a black majority that is still largely poor. Some businesses realize that unless black economic development occurs, they may face harsher consequences, like expropriation of land by frustrated black residents.
"The people also are saying that if you don't give us education and training, then we'll demand the land back," Mr. Hearn said.
Neither Mr. Hern, nor Solly Mohaule, the community liaison officer from Conservation Corporation, have any illusions that the tourist industry in the reserve will ever be a big enough market to meet the needs of an area where unemployment is about 60 percent.
But with the old tribal homeland structure wiped out by the new order and with the new national Government not planning to set up local authorities until next year, this small-scale economic development is one of the few signs of change in the area.
Like many ideas in South Africa at the moment, these must overcome a host of obstacles. The old system of education for blacks provided few business skills. The old tribal homelands policy denied black residents clear title to the land, making it difficult to use land as collateral for loans.
And efforts to achieve a partnership in economic development must overcome the black suspicion of whites that is a legacy of apartheid.
"People say to me, 'the white man has had many plans before that have tricked the blacks,' " said Mr. Mohaule, a black who grew up in the region and later became a lawyer in Johannesburg.
Suspicion is heightened by the fact that the reserve was carved from land that once belonged to the local people. "The forced removal of people from the game reserve is a problem," said Sam Nzima, who owns a liquor store near the fence. "People still believe that that is our land."
That wariness also surrounds an idea being floated by the Conservation Corporation to expand the Sabi Sand Reserve by getting local people to cede control -- but not ownership -- over some of their land. In return the community would receive an equity stake in the company and representation on its board of directors.
While company officials see the plan as a way for the local people to gain further revenue, the idea has not yet been embraced by the local residents.
"We're not sure about the concept," said Mac Mnisi, an adviser to a local chief of the Shangaan, the ethnic majority in the area. "The matter is still being talked about. The most important question is who will benefit."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Tourists enjoying a meal in the outdoor dining area at Londolozi Game Lodge, part of the Sabi Sand Game Reserve in South Africa. The reserve's owners are trying to help local people set up businesses to supply it. (Carolina Salguero for The New York Times)
 
Map of South Africa shows the location of Sabi Sand Game Reserve.

HEADLINE: Clinton Seeks Chile Trade

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
President Clinton predicted today that Congress would give him the authority to negotiate a free trade agreement with Chile, but he left unclear what type of pact the two countries would pursue.
Mr. Clinton, meeting for the first time with Eduardo Frei Ruiz-Tagle, the Chilean President, told reporters that winning "fast-track authority" from Congress to expedite an agreement was a top priority. 
 But both leaders said they were unsure whether to pursue a bilateral trade pact or whether Chile should join the North American Free Trade Agreement linking the United States, Mexico and Canada.
Fast-track authority would require Congress to vote on the treaty, without amendments, within a specified time period. The authority was used in negotiating the North American trade pact.
"The most important thing for me now is to get the Congress to approve the fast-track negotiations with Chile so that we can accelerate this economic partnership," Mr. Clinton said. "I think they will."
Mr. Frei told a news conference that no decision had been made. He said his Finance Minister, Eduardo Aninat, and Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, had been charged with coming up with the best option for both countries.

HEADLINE: Proposals Could Erode Fiscal Gains, Giuliani Aides Say

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

 BODY:
With a $31.6 billion city budget in place, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani faces a new battle in Albany over an attempt by the Patrolmen's Benevolent Association to be removed from the jurisdiction of the city's collective bargaining law, as well as several proposals to supplement pensions for retired public employees.
Public employee unions are pushing hard for the pension increase, which they say is needed to allow retired workers to keep up with inflation. And the P.B.A. bill is part of that union's continuing effort to win parity in pay with neighboring suburban police forces.
 
Negating State Aid
 But city officials said the set of bills, if passed, could cost the city $300 million or more, eroding the fiscal gains achieved when the state approved $285 million in additional aid to the city. 
 "This is an issue that we have to take very seriously," said Abraham M. Lackman, the city budget director. "It will undercut all of the aid that we got." He said that the cost of the pension supplement alone could force the city to lay off up to 15,000 workers.
Mr. Giuliani is to meet with legislative leaders in Albany today to bring the message that the city cannot afford substantial new costs in the new fiscal year. Aides said he would also be seeking approval for a measure that would save the city $127 million by the end of June 1995, by allowing it to make reduced contributions for several years to city pension systems and to increase the amount in later years.
Mr. Giuliani's lobbying came as the Legislature moved into its end-of-session flurry, and city officials said the Albany pension bills were their biggest concern.
The State Legislature is considering several bills that would for the first time provide an annual cost of living adjustment to the pensions of retired state and city workers and teachers. Most of the versions being discussed would tie the increase to the consumer price index.
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has said such an adjustment would be difficult for the state to afford, but in an election year in which public employee and teachers unions can provide critical support for legislative candidates, there is significant backing for the measures in both the Senate and the Assembly.
As an alternative, legislative leaders and the Cuomo administration are also discussing a one-time pension supplement that would give retirees a raise in payments, but not on a recurring basis. Deputy Mayor John S. Dyson said that the various proposals pending in the Legislature could cost the city from $226 million to $500 million annually.
 
Fighting P.B.A. Bill
 The Giuliani administration is also fighting legislation sought by the Patrolmen's Benevolent Association that would remove the police union from the authority of the city's collective bargaining law, and put the officers under the jurisdiction of the State Public Employment Relations Board. The same bill has been approved by the Assembly and Senate in the past, but was vetoed by Mr. Cuomo. It has already passed the Republican-run Senate this year.
Aides to the Mayor said the measure would hinder the Giuliani administration's attempt to hold the P.B.A., the city's largest and most powerful police union, to the same pattern established in wage settlements this spring with the housing authority police and the firefighters. Those contracts conformed to the modest wage increases negotiated with other municipal employees a year ago.
City officials say that if the police union came under the jurisdiction of the State Public Employment Relations Board, the officers could find more support for their attempts to win wage settlements comparable to their better-paid suburban counterparts instead of being compared with other city employees. The P.B.A. has been without a contract since October 1991.

HEADLINE: Jews Revisit Shanghai, Grateful Still That It Sheltered Them

BYLINE: By PATRICK E. TYLER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: SHANGHAI

 BODY:
They stood in the pouring rain not long ago, a few dozen Jews from all over the world and their Communist Chinese hosts, to commemorate Shanghai's role as a safe haven for Jewish refugees fleeing the Holocaust.
In the late 1930's, Shanghai was the only destination in the world that did not require an entry visa. And so the Jews came -- 20,000 or more. And Shanghai, with an already established Jewish population of about 10,000, took them in. 
 "Those of us who grew up in Shanghai felt no anti-Semitism," said David Zysman, vice president of Yeshiva University.
Mr. Zysman arrived here in 1934. His family came from Europe by way of Panama. His father was in the silk trade, and they stayed through the war. He returned recently with a small group from New York led by Rabbi Arthur Schneier.
Benjamin C. Fishoff, today an electronics importer in Manhattan, escaped from Poland to Shanghai with his entire Hebrew school. But he had to leave his parents behind and never saw them again.
Joseph Ganger, who runs a garment business with headquarters in the Empire State Building, got to Shanghai with his parents after the war began.
If there had been room on an Italian steamship sailing to Shanghai in late 1938, Rabbi Schneier said, he, too, would have fled to China instead of to Hungary, where most of his family were killed.
The Japanese, allied with Germany, had already taken most of eastern China. Shanghai's streets, bustling with rickshaws and Bentleys, throbbed with frenetic wartime commerce.
Rena Krasno was born in Shanghai in 1923 to Russian immigrant parents and stayed until 1949. But she never learned to speak Chinese and was mostly oblivious to the currents of Chinese nationalism there.
Ms. Krasno's father edited one of 30 Jewish newspapers published in Shanghai in those days.
"When the refugees came, my father was totally involved with them," she said. "Anybody with a social conscience spent every hour trying to help them survive because we knew Jews were being killed like flies in Europe and we wanted this small number of Jews here to survive."
Japanese and German archives later revealed that the fate of the Shanghai Jews was almost sealed in July 1942 when Col. Joseph Meisinger of the Gestapo in Japan arrived in Shanghai with a plan to kill all the Jews there.
Papers by Chinese academics from the Center for Jewish Studies in Shanghai asserted that when news leaked of the German plan, Japan backed away from it. The Japanese occupiers declared the Jews stateless refugees and set up a ghetto instead.
The establishment of diplomatic relations between China and Israel in 1992 made the recent commemoration possible, even though the Jewish population of Shanghai had been dispersed.
Sensitive to the religious themes of the commemoration, city officials barred reporters from the events. But they were not shy about the economic implications. They conducted an investment seminar for the visitors.
On a tour, the group visited the old Ohel Rachel synagogue, now vine encrusted from disuse. Rabbi Schneier has been lobbying Beijing on religious freedom issues, including the restoration of the synagogue.
"Shanghai is going to become a major center of trade and finance in the future, so we have to dream and I firmly believe there will again be a Jewish community here," he said.
But for those who returned on the recent trip, a tour of the old Jewish sites of the city at times raised clouds of memories.
Mr. Fishoff, examining a photo exhibit, found himself staring at his own image on an identification card.
"I was so shocked to see myself up there on the wall," he said, "I got quite emotional."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Grim View From Brazil Coffee Fields

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: RIO DE JANEIRO, June 28

 BODY:
Wanderley Cintra Ferreira, president of the coffee growers cooperative of Franca, in the interior of Sao Paulo state, flew over the cooperative's blighted coffee fields today, to survey the damage from Brazil's worst cold snap in nearly 20 years.
"It was clear that the damage was very great," Mr. Ferreira reported. 
 Agricultural experts here estimate that the Antarctic cold front that swept through southern Brazil, the world's largest coffee producing region, has destroyed about one-third of next year's crop. Brazil is the leading supplier of coffee, accounting for about a quarter of world production.
The impact was felt on commodity exchanges around the world, where coffee-futures prices continued to rise today after soaring more than 25 percent on Monday. Coffee for July delivery jumped 10.55 cents, to $1.703 a pound in New York, the highest in seven and a half years.
With some producers defaulting today on delivery contracts, hoping to ride the wave of rising prices, Brazil and Colombia, the largest growers, temporarily suspended exports for July and waited for notoriously volatile commodity prices to stabilize.
Many Brazilian growers walked through their fields today, trying to assess the losses. "The cold wind hit 100 percent of our area," said Carlos Melles, a grower in Minas Gerais state. "We believe that 50 percent of the bushes have been damaged and that there will be a 30 percent drop in next year's harvest."
But specialists said the sharply rising prices were being spurred by more than the prospect of losing 7 percent of the world's 1995 crop. Prices started to tumble in 1989, and by the early 1990's reached the lowest real levels of the century. The crash, provoked by overproduction, has resulted in losses by producer nations since 1989 estimated at $10 billion.
While world prices dropped in half, consumer prices declined one-third, allowing coffee roasters to increase profits. The roasters argue that coffee beans account for only one-quarter of coffee prices, with processing, packaging, transportation and marketing making up the rest.
"The roasters enjoyed incredible profit margins," said Judith Ganes, a coffee analyst for Merrill Lynch in New York.
As coffee became barely profitable, Brazilians tore out bushes or cut back on pruning and fertilizing. In five years, Brazil reduced the number of bushes by almost 25 percent, to about 3.2 billion today.
Coffee's dominance of exports has decreased much more. From 74 percent of Brazilian export revenue in 1952, coffee accounted for only 3 percent last year. In one traditionally coffee-producing state, Espirito Santo, coffee has dropped from 33 percent to 8 percent of economic activity.
The neglect was felt when the frigid weather hit. "Without maintenance, many trees were weak and vulnerable to last weekend's frost," said Francisco Ourique, a partner in Flavor Coffee, a trading company here.
Still, with most Brazilian beans ready for harvest, analysts expect only a 5 percent loss in this year's crop. But the southern part of the country is blighted by dead and damaged bushes, and the 1995 crop is expected to be 18 to 20 million sacks, down about one-third from forecasts.
The frost's effect was quickly felt at supermarket counters in the United States. Roasters, raising prices as much as 20 percent, followed a lead set Monday night by Procter & Gamble, which increased the price of its Folgers coffee 17 percent.
With international coffee supplies relatively low, consumer prices could continue to rise through the summer. "I was expecting $2 a pound, without a freeze," Ms. Ganes of Merrill Lynch said. "If the extent of Brazil's freeze is severe, the price could hit $2.70.
The freeze last weekend struck as the Brazilian Government favors low coffee prices. This is because it is changing the cruzeiro to a dollar-backed currency called the real on Friday and embarking on a plan to radically reduce inflation, now 45 percent a month. Coffee makes up a key part of the basket of consumer prices the Government wants to stabilize. Even before last weekend's freeze forced up world coffee prices, officials announced a plan to auction 1.5 million sacks to local roasters in an effort to push down domestic prices by 15 percent.
In addition, the Government would like to lower world prices to prevent recurrence of the boom-and-bust cycle that has plagued coffee growers for generations. "Frosts always provoke production increases," said Ruy Barreto, president of one of Rio de Janeiro's largest trading companies. "This one will only work as an incentive for new planting in Africa, Central America and Indonesia -- and Brazil will lose market share."
Lawrence Eagles of GNI Ltd. in London agreed, saying: "If prices rise too high, they are going to cause a flood of investment in coffee production. There will be an impact on consumption, and the producers will be back in boom-and-bust cycle."
On paper, Brazil has a stock of 17 million sacks -- almost equal to a year's consumption in the United States, the biggest consumer. But Brazil has dragged its feet in recent years in conducting an internationally accepted audit. Many brokers fear that the country's supply is no more than 10 million sacks. Of those, only 2 million would be of export grade.
In Colombia, where each rise of one cent in the price of coffee generates $15 million in foreign-exchange earnings, growers have also been caught with low production and cannot make up for Brazilian crop losses.
The Colombian harvest this year is expected to be 11 million sacks -- the lowest in five years. By comparison, Colombia produced 18 million sacks in 1992. With Colombian growers currently receiving 64 cents a pound for their coffee, officials there announced today that the Government would raise producer prices in July.
Colombia, Brazil and other producers from Central America are to meet next week to try to reach an agreement on a uniform price policy.
In Africa, the world's second-largest growing region, production has gradually fallen in recent years.
In the meantime, consumption has increased in the United States. After dropping nearly in half from 1962 to 1988, American consumption has risen 12 percent since 1988. Last year, Americans each drank an average of 1.87 cups of coffee a day.

HEADLINE: Higher Taxes? No Problem. Voters Rescue School.

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: FAIRHAVEN, Mass., June 28

 BODY:
A white elephant of a building mixing turn-of-the-century French, Flemish and Tudor Revival styles, Fairhaven High School, known locally as the Castle, is not your conventional schoolhouse.
So it was perhaps appropriate that when the school was placed on accreditation probation last June, the town's residents responded in a most unconventional way: they voted to raise property taxes. 
 "The high school was going to get the official stamp 'Rejected,' like a car that doesn't pass inspection, said Thomas P. Crotty, a lawyer for the town. "It's kind of embarrassing."
The embarrassment was apparently shared by most of the voters in this picket-fence-pretty fishing town with its 21 miles of seashore and 16,000 mostly blue-collar residents on Buzzards Bay, 60 miles south of Boston.
In a special ballot on June 13, they approved, by 2,765 to 959, a 20-year increase in property taxes to help finance an $18 million expansion and renovation of the high school, which was built with a bequest to the town by a native son, Henry Huttleston Rogers, a millionaire oil man and Wall Street financier. It is one of five buildings financed by Rogers that give Fairhaven a skyline more typical of Europe than New England.
 
A Failing Grade
 The school, which has 630 students, boasts hand-carved gargoyles on ceilings and walls, floors of Italian marble and two-story stained-glass windows. Despite its architectural opulence, the New England Association of Schools and Colleges found that the high school failed to meet standards for library services, faculty, education programs and the building itself and threatened to take away its accreditation if the problems were not fixed.
In the June 13 ballot, Fairhaven voters agreed to finance about $6 million of the necessary renovations through an increase in the local property tax. The state will chip in $12.3 million. Elaine Rocha, Fairhaven's Town Clerk, said the decision would raise taxes $69 a year over 20 years, based on the average valuation of $112,000.
That may not seem like a great deal of money, but the decision comes at a time when voters around the country are increasingly reluctant to raise property taxes to pay for education and, in some cases, are turning to other sources of financing altogether.
In Massachusetts itself, in fact, schools operate under Proposition 2 1/2, a referendum approved by state voters in 1980 that limits property taxes to 2.5 percent of assessed valuation. Fairhaven got around that limit by voting to exclude the debt for the renovation from the 2.5 percent ceiling.
 
Corny but True
 Why is Fairhaven different? The answer most often cited by residents is not likely to signal a national change of heart on education financing: "Nobody volunteers to pay extra taxes," Mr. Crotty said. "It's community pride, the future of the children. All the things that people say that are so corny are true in Fairhaven."
Indeed, at a time when small towns in America appear to be vanishing -- or transforming into sprawling suburbs -- Fairhaven seems a rarity; a population that has remained stable for decades, a place where doors go unlocked and parking meters do not exist.
Town officials and residents alike hailed the tax vote as a symbol of confidence in the community, a stand taken for family values and a new beginning from years of economic recession.
"It's not just to save the building but to prepare the town and the students of the town for the future," said David F. Wood, who graduated from the high school 26 years ago.
School officials were not allowed to lobby voters either way on the tax measure, so Mr. Wood and other residents -- mostly students, their parents and Fairhaven High School graduates -- formed a political action committee that spent $4,600 to rally support for the vote.
Lawns were peppered with blue-and-white signs (the colors of the Fairhaven Blue Devils) backing the tax increase, and voters turned out at an unusually high 41 percent rate to cast their ballots, all of which gratified Mr. Wood, who headed the citizens' group.
"With the competitive nature of the world," Mr. Wood said, "my children and every other child in town need the best in order to survive in life."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Residents of Fairhaven, Mass., have voted to increase their property taxes to finance an upgrading of the turn-of-the-century building housing the local high school, which was facing loss of accreditation. The principal, Dr. John Newborn, had the school's ornate auditorium to himself yesterday. (Photographs by Evan Richman for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Parents of Slain Children Had Bitter Disputes in Court

BYLINE: By ROBERT HANLEY, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: HACKENSACK, N.J., June 28

 BODY:
The 50-year-old divorced father who is charged with murdering his two children during a weekend visit had fought bitterly in court with their mother over money as well as over custody and visitation.
Family Court records here show that the man, Avi Kostner, a former cab driver under psychiatric care for depression, owed his former wife $8,200 in child support and won a judge's ruling in April canceling the debt. At that hearing, his ex-wife, Lynn Mison, sought permission to move to Florida with the couple's two children, Geri Beth, 12, and Ryan, 10. 
 The judge, Robert E. Hamer, granted her request and ordered a new visitation schedule to replace a 1990 court-ordered schedule that allowed Mr. Kostner to see the children every other weekend, on several Jewish holidays and four weeks each summer, records show.
 
Debt Was Erased
 Judge Hamer freed Mr. Kostner from making child-support payments while he was unemployed and erased his accumulated debt of $8,234. Mr. Kostner had been held briefly in the Bergen County Jail here three times -- in December 1987, June 1992 and March 1993 -- on charges of failing to make the child-support payments.
He was found on Sunday night in a friend's Isuzu Trooper parked outside the Teaneck police headquarters with the bodies of the children covered by a sleeping bag in a rear seat, the authorities said.
Acquaintances have said he was deeply troubled by Ms. Mison's plan to leave for Florida with the children on July 1. He complained about the move to merchants near his apartment in Teaneck and to friends at the Teaneck Jewish Center, where he worshiped and served as an assistant scoutmaster of a Boy Scout troop.
"He feared he would lose the children not only geographically, but also religiously and emotionally," said the center's rabbi, David Feldman. Rabbi Feldman said Mr. Kostner had complained several times about his former wife's planned move.
 
Messages to Friends Reported
 John J. Fahy, the Bergen County Prosecutor, said the move may have been a motivating factor in the murders. Before he was found, Mr. Kostner left messages on the answering machines of friends saying he killed the two children and planned to commit suicide, Mr. Fahy said.
Autopsies have shown the two children were suffocated, and Mr. Fahy said today that investigators do not know where the killings occurred.
Mr. Kostner, who is being held in the prison ward at the Bergen Pines County Hospital in Paramus, is scheduled to appear in court for the first time on the charges Wednesday.
According to court records, during the April hearing Mr. Kostner objected to the children's proposed move. In addition to the request for the reduction in his child support, he also asked that Ms. Mison be held in contempt for encouraging the children to call their stepfather Daddy. Judge Hamer rejected that request and also approved the children's move.
Ms. Mison remarried after she and Mr. Kostner were divorced in April 1988 after a nine-year marriage. In her divorce papers, Ms. Mison, a high school teacher with a master's degree in chemistry, said she was the family's major source of support. She complained that Mr. Kostner was unable to hold a steady job, asserting he was willing to work only Sunday mornings and weekend afternoons between 4 and 6 P.M. She said he refused to help with household chores while she worked, at times, 90 hours a week at various jobs.
Since the divorce, Ms. Mison and Mr. Kostner have been in court many times, squabbling about custody, visiting rights, the children's religious upbringing and child support. Mr. Kostner complained that judges treated him unfairly and that his child-support payments of $130 a week were too great a portion of his weekly unemployment payment of $230. He claimed his former wife and her new husband earned $150,000 a year.
Under Judge Hamer's new ruling on visitation, Mr. Kostner would have been allowed to see the children four weeks this summer, over the new year's holiday and for two additional periods of 7 to 10 days. He would have been allowed to call them daily.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Prices Tumble; Variety of Factors Cited

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities fell yesterday, buffeted by another retreat in the dollar, a surge in commodity prices, bearish economic news and concern about the next interest-rate rise by the Federal Reserve.
The 30-year bond fell 21/32 to a price of 85 4/32 , to yield 7.51 percent, up from 7.45 percent on Monday. Traders said most of the weakness was felt in the intermediate and long maturities, with shorter-maturity bills mixed. 
 Rising commodity prices, one of the chief culprits in much of yesterday's selloff, pushed the Commodity Research Bureau index of 21 commodity futures up 2.63 points, to 230.40, only one day after the index slid to a six-week low on Monday. The advance in the index, a closely watched inflation forecaster, was fueled by a sharp rally in grains and soybeans, as talk of hot weather intensified on the trading floors. Gold, a litmus test of interest-rate sentiment, was also higher yesterday, as gold for August delivery rose $1.50, to $387.90 an ounce on the Commodity Exchange in New York.
 
Rebound in New- Home Sales
 Among the economic reports released yesterday was a stronger-than-expected May report on new- home sales issued by the Commerce Department, which weighed on bond prices since it showed strength in the economy when none was expected. The report showed a sharp rebound to an annualized rate of 738,000 units in May following 708,000 units in April, significantly revised from the original estimate of 683,000. Sales were up in all sectors of the country except in the Midwest.
Ronald R. Reuss, chief economist at Piper Jaffray Inc., Minneapolis, said he saw the economy "showing some signs of weakness for the second quarter." He added, "Industrial production, factory orders and durables for the first two months of the second quarter were below the pace of the first quarter."
Mr. Reuss said that while some people expect economic growth of 3.5 to 4 percent on an annualized basis, some components in the economy during the second quarter indicated rate of growth that is less than 3 percent a year. "This should be good news for the markets as they are demanding a 3 percent or less growth mode to allay inflation fears," he said.
"Recent dollar weakness should be overlooked by the Fed because since the initial tightening in February the money supply of various measures have all shown significant reductions in their rate of growth," Mr. Reuss continued. "The Fed's intention is to slow the economy slightly and not kill it."
Concerning the stronger-than-expected new-home sales, Michael Carliner, an economist with the National Association of Home Builders in Washington, was surprised. Indeed he was skeptical of the May increase, and expected that the total for the year would not equal the annual rate reported in May.
"These numbers don't match up with our panel survey of builders," he said, "which has showed activity tailing since early in the year."
Another report released yesterday was the Conference Board's consumer confidence index. Even that rebounded more than expected, rising to 92.0 from 88.9 -- the highest in four years. The May import prices report showed a 1.1 percent surge in import prices, though the gain was a more modest two-tenths of 1 percent excluding oil.
Analysts said all three releases were modestly bearish for the market, and the combined effect sent Treasury prices down sharply in early trading. It temporarily distracted the market from the battered dollar, which rebounded a bit in overnight trading, but the threat of a renewed slide, which came to pass, held the market under siege.
 
Municipal Bond Market
 In the municipal market, bonds prices also came under pressure as investors remained hesitant about buying paper amid a rise in Treasury yields and an abundant supply of new issues.
Analysts said that with mutual funds and other institutional investors reluctant to buy bonds, brokers' inventories remained near seven-year highs.
Jacksonville, Fla., officials delayed the sale of $74 million in revenue bonds to finance improvement at the city's Gator Bowl stadium because of the increase in interest rates.
Among other offerings yesterday was New York State's $160 million of general obligation bonds, which were awarded to Bear, Stearns and initially priced to yield a maximum 6.30 percent for an issue due in 2024.
Those parts of the financing that are not insured by the Ambac Indemnity Corporation -- and thus triple-A rated by the rating agencies -- are rated A by Moody's Investors Service and A- by Standard & Poor's.

HEADLINE: Home Sales Climbed a Brisk 4.2% in May

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
Shrugging off the upturn in interest rates, sales of new single-family houses climbed 4.2 percent in May to the briskest pace of the year, a better-than-expected performance that combined with an upward revision for April sales to suggest that the economy may not be slowing much.
Separately, consumer confidence as plumbed by the Conference Board rebounded to about the highest level in four years, though expectations for the months ahead improved much less than assessments of currently prevailing conditions. 
 Though economists considered neither report significant enough to alter forecasts, markets reacted as if they feared faster growth. Bond prices dropped, as did stocks and the dollar.
Possibly contributing to the undermining of the dollar was the Government's annual tally of the nation's international investment position. It showed a widening gap between the value of American-owned assets abroad and foreign-owned assets in this country.
The negative position of the United States, which dates from the late 1980's, rose to $555.7 billion last year from $507.9 billion at the end of 1992.
The report on home sales, compiled by the Departments of Commerce and Housing and Urban Development, lifted the annual rate to 738,000, a pace that during the last year was exceeded only in the boom months of November and December.
"Interest rates have not depressed the housing market at all," contended Eugene J. Sherman, director of research at M. A. Schapiro & Company. "There's been no visible impact."
Mortgage rates have been rising since October, moving up on conventional 30-year loans to 8.60 last month from 8.32 in April, according to the Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation. But analysts say affordability is still high and that marginal buyers have adapted by turning to adjustable rate loans.
Still, specialists generally agree that the recent pace cannot be sustained much longer, a view seemingly borne out by the latest poll of members by the National Association of Home Builders, issued in June. The proportion of builders expecting "good" sales six months from now has dropped to 36 percent from 41 percent, while 15 percent, up from 11 percent, expect sales to be "poor."
 
Sales Drop Ahead Seen
 "Much of the effect of higher rates is in discouraging people from beginning to shop," said Michael Carliner, an economist for the association. That suggests that people already in the market carried through with their plans -- and indeed might have been spurred to even quicker action by the turn-up in rates -- but that today's lower traffic in model homes points to a sales decline ahead.
Stephen S. Roach, senior economist at Morgan Stanley, said he already saw "the first signs of flattening out" of interest-sensitive parts of the economy, like housing and motor vehicles.
Sales last month rose 8.4 percent in the South, 6.5 percent in the Northeast (but at a still-sluggish rate) and 5 percent in the West. The Midwest, however, posted an 8 percent decline.
The upward revision for April was to an annual pace of 708,000 new homes from 683,000, thereby halving the initially reported decline to 3.4 percent.
With an inventory of 298,000 unsold houses in May, builders had a supply that would last 4.9 months at current selling rates, slightly lower than the five-month stock in April.
The median sales price of homes sold eased $3,000, to $127,000, last month but analysts said this, as usual, was probably more a reflection of the mix of homes that changed hands rather than a softening of house prices. The average price eased $1,500, to $152,000.
 
Confidence Is Strong
 The Conference Board, a corporate-sponsored research group, said its overall confidence index advanced to 92 in June from 88.9 as sentiment improved in seven regions and fell only in the South Atlantic and mountain states.
"Although consumers continue to express concern with the job situation -- both the present and the immediate future -- confidence is currently at a level which, historically, has been associated with a reassuringly lively economy," said Fabian Linden, executive director the group's research center.
But pessimistic views on future job prospects still outnumber positive ones by a moderate margin, Mr. Linden added.
The Commerce Department's calculation of the $47.8 billion widening in the country's net international debt -- direct investment and portfolio securities combined -- valued factories, farm land and other tangible assets at the current cost of replacing them.
When direct investment is valued at the current stock market value of the owner's equity, however, the net debt of the United States narrowed by $82.3 billion last year, to $507.7 billion.
The department said the divergence in the two measures reflected exceptionally big advances in foreign stock markets.

HEADLINE: Stocks Lose Ground, With Dow Off 15.86

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
Old worries piled on the new worry of the declining dollar yesterday as the stock market retreated slightly after a one-day rally.
Traders returned to agonizing about inflation, interest rates and earnings. Housing data indicate that the American economy, thought to be slowing, may be as vigorous as ever and with it, perhaps, inflation. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average, after leaping 48.56 points on Monday, retreated 15.86 points yesterday, to 3,669.64. Bond prices fell, exacerbating the stock decline.
 
Trade in Dollars Continues
 Meanwhile, currency traders continued to trade dollars down to near-record levels. The dollar dropped to 100.02 yen, from 100.45 yen in New York late Monday. The dollar fell to 1.5787 German mark, from 1.5840, the lowest level in more than a year.
"No one can change the longer-term decline in the dollar," said Kathleen M. Camilli, chief economist for Maria Fiorini Ramirez Inc., an economic consulting firm. "The best you can hope for is a managed decline of the dollar and you can do that through currency intervention and jawboning," or attempting to convince the financial markets that the dollar should be worth more, she said.
Because Ms. Camilli expects the Treasury Department and the Federal Reserve to intervene in the currency markets, she also expects European central banks, especially the Bundesbank, to put pressure on the Fed for a fifth rate increase in advance of the Fed's policy-making meeting on July 5 and 6.
The Fed's multibillion-dollar buying campaign is also expensive for the Bundesbank and other central banks, which join the Fed in huge dollar purchases, she said. "There has to be some quid pro quo."
The fate of the dollar is important because a weakening dollar makes dollar-denominated assets like stocks less attractive, particularly to foreigners. For example, a German investor who bought the stocks that make up the Dow industrial average would have a return of 3.95 percent over the last 12 months, but would post a loss of 3.21 percent when that investment was converted to marks.
Because of the market turmoil, many traders appeared to stay on the sidelines. Volume on the Big Board totaled 267.7 million shares, or almost 9 percent below this year's average daily volume of 293 million shares.
But traders who are worried about an overheating economy received a jolt yesterday. The Commerce Department reported that single-family home sales, instead of retreating because of higher interest rates, rose strongly.
Home sales posted an unexpected 4.2 percent rise in May after a revised 3.4 percent fall in April. The annual sales rate equaled 738,000, while private economists had expected a rate of 682,000. That compares with a revised rate in April of 708,000.
Long-term interest rates rose. The yield on the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond rose to 7.51 percent from 7.45 percent. Commodities prices, driven by higher coffee and corn prices, soared after a recent plateau. The Commodity Research Bureau index of 21 commodities rose 2.63 points, to 230.4. Gold, the refuge of the inflation-jittery, gained. Spot gold futures for June delivery rose $1.50, to $386.20 an ounce. The broader Standard & Poor's fell 1.24 points, to 446.07. The smaller-company Nasdaq composite fell 0.63 point, to 702.05.
The New York Stock Exchange composite fell 0.65 point, to 245.80, with advancing stocks lagging behind declining stocks, 775 to 1,343. The American Stock Exchange market value index fell 2.78 points, to 422.67.
The most active stock, at 17.2 million shares, was the American depository receipts of Vodafone Group, a cellular telephone company in Britain. Its shares rose 3/4, to 73 7/8, on no apparent news.

HEADLINE: Studies Show Small Companies Play Big Role

BYLINE: By TOM REDBURN 

 BODY:
New York has long been identified with the Fortune 500 corporate giants whose names adorn Manhattan skyscrapers or that are based in suburban office complexes around the metropolitan area.
But two new studies indicate that smaller businesses are playing an increasingly important role in generating jobs for New Yorkers. Particularly surprising was that the thousands of small industrial companies doing business in New York proved far more successful than big ones in riding out the harsh economic downturn of the last few years. 
 The research was carried out separately by the Federal Reserve Bank of New York and the local office of the Federal Bureau of Labor Statistics. Together, the studies suggest that the common practice in New York of handing out valuable tax breaks to large companies that threaten to leave -- while popular among political leaders faced with highly publicized job losses -- may be a poor use of tax dollars.
"Our economic development policies are ripe for a new look," said Samuel M. Ehrenhalt, New York regional director of the Bureau of Labor Statistics. "It is time that we had a good evaluation of what works and what doesn't."
 
Thought to Be an Exception
 For years, small business in the United States has prided itself on its image as the prime source of new jobs. But the New York area, with a greater concentration of large corporations than any other region, was thought to be an exception to the rule.
The recent research, however, suggests that smaller companies, particularly fresh start-up businesses, are of growing importance to New York in creating new jobs.
Companies with fewer than 25 employees account for roughly one-fourth of private employment in New York State. Yet they generated two-thirds of the additional jobs in the state during the mid-1980's boom, according to a report released on Monday by economists at the Federal Reserve Bank of New York.
And contrary to conventional wisdom, which generally assigns small business a greater role at the early stages of a economic recovery, their importance increased as the decade continued. For 1984 and 1985, New York companies with fewer than 25 employees produced less than half of the net job growth. But in 1987 and 1988, they accounted for 9 out of 10 of the additional jobs.
"Now that the regional economy is starting to grow again," said Rae Rosen, a senior economist at the regional bank, "we should look to small firms to generate most of the additional jobs."
 
Smaller Is Less Stable
 To be sure, the bank economists found that smaller companies are typically more unstable than big corporations. "This is good news with a caveat," Ms. Rosen said. "More job growth from small business also goes hand in hand with higher turnover."
But even that is not necessarily true for all types of companies. Another study, released yesterday by Mr. Ehrenhalt at a Bureau of Labor Statistics briefing, found that the local recession that lasted from 1989 to 1993 hit large manufacturing companies in New York State much harder than small companies.
Of the 17,000 industrial companies with fewer than 20 employees, 6.3 percent failed or left the state, costing the jobs of about 8.3 percent of their workers. By contrast, about 14 percent of the 8,000 companies with more than 20 employees disappeared. They eliminated more than one in six of their workers during the recession.
The bigger the company, the harder it fell. Companies with more than 100 employees cut their total work force by 20 percent, compared with a 14 percent loss of jobs at companies employing 20 to 99 workers.
"Manufacturing has been in long term decline here for 30 years," Mr. Ehrenhalt said. "But despite that, we're seeing some islands of strength in manufacturing, particularly among small establishments. Many of them appear to have some kind of talent for survival."
Looking forward, the Federal Reserve Bank economists concluded, new small businesses will be "clearly a dynamic part of the job creation process" in the New York area. But the region's "labor market in general will probably enjoy less employment stability during the 1990's than it did in the 1980's."

HEADLINE: FINANCE BRIEFS

 BODY:
Debt
 
* Federal Home Loan Bank is selling $50 million of step-up notes due in 1996 through First Tennessee Bank. The notes carry a 5.5 percent coupon and are priced at 100. If the notes are not called in six months, the coupon increases to 6.25 percent, then twice a year to 6.75 percent and 7.25 percent.
 

* Ferrellgas Partners L.P., Liberty, Mo., is selling $250 million of notes due in 2001 in two parts. One issue is $200 million series A notes priced at 100 to yield 10 percent. The notes are callable in four years. Starting in 1998, they are callable at 105, or $1,050 per $1,000 face value of bonds. In 1999, they are callable at 102.50, and are callable at par thereafter. The other issue is $50 million series B floating- rate notes priced at a yield 312.5 basis points more than the three-month London interbank offered rate, the rate international banks charge each other for loans. A basis point is one-hundredth of 1 percent. The series B issue has a sinking fund that retires $5 million in years five and six. Goldman, Sachs & Company led the underwriting team on the equity sale, while Goldman, Sachs and Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette managed the debt sale. The notes are rated B1 by Moody's Investors Service and BB- by Standard & Poor's.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Two insurers of mortgages may take rising interest rates in stride.

BYLINE: By Barnaby J. Feder 

 BODY:
INVESTORS are so nervous about rising interest rates that they are not thinking clearly about how different financial service companies are affected, according to some analysts. Take the difference between companies that lend money to home buyers and those that sell insurance policies guaranteeing that such mortgages will be paid.
Mortgage companies are clearly in for a rougher ride than in the days when rates were falling. Homeowners are less likely to take out new mortgages in search of lower monthly payments, depriving the mortgage sellers of a major source of income. And rising rates put pressure on mortgage originators to reduce loan spreads, servicing fees or other income sources to keep mortgage expenses from rising so fast that they drive business away. 
 Mortgage insurers, on the other hand, benefited less from the decline in interest rates and will certainly be hurt less from any increase. The size and stability of the revenue from premiums on existing policies is a far bigger factor in their performance than how many new policies they sell.
"A lot of investors have lost sight of the fact that these are insurance companies," said Richard Lane, who advises institutional investment firms for Cleary Gull Reiland & McDevitt in Milwaukee. Mr. Lane said that the MGIC Investment Corporation, the largest independent mortgage insurer, and the CMAC Investment Corporation, the other major pure play in the business, were undervalued.
The two companies compete with units of larger financial businesses, like those of the Allstate Corporation and the GE Capital Corporation, and with the Federal Housing Administration, which focuses on the highest-risk policies and has limits on the kinds of mortgages it can handle. Mortgage lenders typically require homeowners to buy mortgage insurance when they are putting down less than 20 percent of the cost of their home.
The good news for the insurers is that even as the number of refinanced mortgages drops sharply, there are still plenty of first-time home buyers to add to the pool of people paying premiums. And with the economy recovering in most places, the insurers can expect fewer claims.
MGIC, which pioneered the business in the 1950's but suffered big losses as a subsidiary of the Baldwin United Corporation in the 1980's because of policies sold to real estate speculators, is seen as the safer play. It was acquired by management and the Northwestern Mutual Life Insurance Company in 1985 and taken public in 1991 at $24 -- or $12 a share, adjusted for last year's two-for-one stock split.
By early last year, MGIC peaked at $36. It closed on the New York Stock Exchange yesterday at $25.25, down 37.5 cents, but analysts say it should achieve its former peak again within a year, based on a conservative valuation of projected earnings. MGIC is growing not just from new policies but also from buying policies sold to reinsurers in periods when the risk of defaults was higher.
"They are hitting on all cylinders at the moment," Vanessa Wilson, who follows the company for CS First Boston, said. Michael Hughes, who follows MGIC for Merrill Lynch in San Francisco, has $40 a share as his 12-month target. Mr. Lane said $45 "would not be outrageous" if investors returned to valuing its earnings as highly as they did in early 1993.
CMAC, which ended trading unchanged at $25.875 yesterday on the New York Stock Exchange, has a smaller portfolio, less of a track record, and more exposure in the troubled California real estate market. But it also has the potential to climb even more rapidly if that market rebounds enough to cut default rates, Mr. Lane said.

HEADLINE: Japan Taking Steps on Deregulation

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, June 28

 BODY:
Japanese who want to drive large motorcycles must pass an excruciatingly difficult test administered by the police. Those content with a smaller motorcycle can pass an easy test given by a driving school. The United States has complained that these rules impede sales in Japan of American motorcycles, which tend to be large.
But the large-motorcycle test will be eliminated in two years as part of deregulatory plan announced by the Japanese Government today intended to sweep aside dozens of arcane regulations that have irked trading partners and sometimes domestic consumers, as well. 
 Today's plan follows up on a preliminary one announced late in March in an attempt to break Japan's trade deadlock with the United States.
But the new package lacks crucial economic-stimulus measures that Tokyo had promised. One missing element is a substantial increase in public works spending above 430 trillion yen -- now about $4.3 trillion -- that is budgeted for this decade. Another is an extension of this year's income tax cut to future years to stimulate consumer spending.
There is general agreement on the need for an income tax cut, but a bitter dispute as to whether the sales tax should be increased to compensate for it.
 
Group of 7 Meeting Shortly
 Japan had hoped to present the measures to the other leading industrial nations at a meeting of Group of Seven leaders next week in Naples. But the resignation of Prime Minister Tsutomu Hata last Saturday prevents the Government from formulating such policies, which will be left for the next administration.
Even so, the deregulatory steps announced today are likely to be welcomed by Washington, if with some reservations.
"We've seen laundry lists of this sort in Japan before," an American official said. The crucial matter, he added, is how and whether the steps are carried out.
The plan include measures that could help American and other foreign companies sell more in Japan. For instance, the Japanese will allow more foreign data to be used in obtaining approval for the sale in this country of food, pharmaceuticals, medical devices, building material and automobiles, rather than requiring separate tests to be performed in Japan.
However, many of the 279 deregulatory items in today's plan only set a direction, leaving the details to be worked out later by Japanese ministries, which can sometimes thwart politicians' intentions. In other cases, new legislation is required to effect some change in regulations.
"The devil is in the details," said William Farrell, executive director of the American Chamber of Commerce in Japan. "I don't think any of us are putting on our party costumes."
In fact, the package, drawn up by a Government committee, still must be approved by the Cabinet in order to become official policy. But in the turmoil brought about by Mr. Hata's resignation, that will not happen until the next administration .
 
Foreign Ministry Is Positive
 A spokesman for the Foreign Ministry said that the new plan was "much more concrete and substantial" than two previous deregulatory packages announced by the Government in the last half-year.
"The basic line was set and that is very important," the spokesman said, adding that the bureaucrats "cannot escape from this framework." The plan calls for 193 of the 279 items to be acted upon by the end of the fiscal year next March, he said.
With Japan in a three-year economic slump, many business leaders and politicians have been calling for deregulation, not so much to appease the United States as to reinvigorate the Japanese economy by spurring the formation of new businesses. Deregulation is also seen as helping Japanese consumers by giving them a greater choice of goods and services at lower prices.
But there is still resistance, particularly from industries that are now bloated and heavily protected, like agriculture and retailing. And bureaucrats are reluctant to eliminate regulations because that reduces their power.
Japan has more than 10,000 regulations, so today's 279-item plan is only a beginning. The package calls for the Government to develop a five-year plan for further deregulation by the end of this year.
The new measures are concentrated in four areas -- housing and land use, telecommunications and information, financial services and the promotion of imports.

HEADLINE: Dining as the Nation's Founders Did

BYLINE: By FLORENCE FABRICANT 

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA

 BODY:
When it opened more than 200 years ago, City Tavern was Le Cirque of Philadelphia. Started by a group of 53 prominent investors, including Lieut. Gov. John Penn and several signers of the Declaration of Independence, it was decidedly clubby and, as John Adams wrote, created suppers "as elegant as was ever laid on a table." Some meetings of the Second Continental Congress were held there, as were more raucous gatherings where prodigious quantities of alcohol were consumed.
Now, just in time for the Fourth of July, a refurbished City Tavern is reopening, in an incarnation that celebrates its history and the nation's food legacy. The restaurant opens to the public on June 30. On June 29 at noon, Mayor Edward G. Rendell will sit down to lunch with 60 city and business leaders. Had he been Mayor some 200 years ago, he might have done the same. 
 City Tavern, a restaurant with lodgings, was established in 1773 but burned down in 1854. When the National Park Service decided to reconstruct the tavern in 1948, not even the foundation remained. The project was finished in time for the bicentennial in 1976.
It cost $1.5 million to rebuild the tavern, a handsome five-story, Federal-style building on South Second Street at Walnut Street, not far from Independence Hall. To make it authentic, the Park Service used drawings, insurance surveys and other documents of the time and went as far as to have the bricks handmade, as they would have been in the late 18th century.
The rebuilt City Tavern fitted right in with the rest of the landmarks in Philadelphia's Independence Park district. But the food failed to live up to the structure. The restaurant was run by the Canteen Corporation, which initially tried to offer food typical of the era of George Washington. But quality and authenticity eroded under the pressure to turn a profit in what became a seasonal, tourists-only venture. A subsequent operator did no better.
Now, perhaps, gastronomic history will be better served. A year and a half ago, the Park Service closed the restaurant and began a search for a new operator. In February, after considering a number of proposals, it selected one from Walter Staib, a restaurant consultant in nearby Bryn Mawr, Pa. The contract required Congressional approval, and when it was granted on April 15, Mr. Staib was nervously looking at the calendar. "My dream was to open by the Fourth of July, and I began to wonder if we would make it," he said.
Not to worry. The Government has in Mr. Staib, 48, a man whose vision and attention to detail operate in overdrive. "I've been working so closely and intensely with this, I sometimes feel as though I was actually here at the time, in another life," Mr. Staib said last week as he guided a visitor through the building and offered tastes of some of the dishes that will be on City Tavern's menu.
As his executive chef, Mr. Staib hired Derek Banks, 29, who graduated from the Culinary Institute of America and has become nearly as passionate as Mr. Staib about the historic aspects of the project. The two had worked together before, at the Excelsior Hotel in Little Rock, Ark. "I love the history, finding out where the foods and cuisines came from," Mr. Banks said. "And I have become fascinated with Thomas Jefferson, who was definitely the food guru of the era."
The menu will include smoked pheasant and trout, baked crab meat, a lush wild mushroom bisque laced with sherry, pork tenderloin in oatmeal stout, quail roasted with bread stuffing and glazed with a raisin and brandy sauce, pork pie, a seafood stew and several dishes with sausages. Some West Indian dishes like pepper pot soup were popular in 1773 and will appear on the new menu. An old-fashioned taste is suggested in a number of dishes by the addition of sherry and allspice. Main dishes at dinner range in price from $14.25 to $22.50, and at lunch, from $6.75 to $14.25.
Some of the dishes seem startlingly modern -- like an appetizer of artichoke bottoms stuffed with peas, and salad dressings made with olive oil -- yet, they are historically valid. "Jefferson grew artichokes and 30 kinds of peas," Mr. Banks said. "And he loved olive oil so much he brought it back from Italy and wanted to plant trees here. That didn't work out, so he arranged for Africans to bring sesame seeds so they could be pressed for oil instead." Mr. Banks is also using a lot of marinades, he said, "because that's what they did in those days."
Mr. Staib said that there is a fine line between re-creating the old and accommodating modern tastes. "If someone wants a margarita, we'll serve it, but bottles of tequila will not be visible on the bar," he said. "We have no sandwiches on the menu because they were not served in America at the time."
All items on the menu, as well as the furnishings and tableware, had to be approved by Park Service historians. "They insisted on brass candlesticks because they said pewter was not used for candles in the late 18th century," Mr. Staib said. "Their historic window for this is about 1770 to 1790. They would not allow us to put flowers on the table. It's never easy when you're dealing with 50 million curators."
The dinnerware reproduces an 18th-century pattern, as do the goblets, which are made with a metal alloy that looks like pewter, rather than real pewter, which contains lead.
The Park Service did not permit certain historically accurate touches, like a whiff of curry in a sauce. "They were afraid spices like that would confuse the customers," Mr. Staib said. The issue of whether saffron will be acceptable has not been resolved.
Mr. Staib and Mr. Banks have started working with local growers of fruits and vegetables, especially those in nearby Lancaster County, and plan to start their own herb garden behind the restaurant. "The first thing I did when I came in here was get rid of the freezers," Mr. Staib said. "We only have a small one for ice cream."
The chandeliers will be dimly lit with 20-watt bulbs to simulate candlelight. "We're using large type on the menus," Mr. Staib said. He has made mostly cosmetic changes in the decor. The high-ceilinged rooms have been painted in muted colors, the wood floors are freshly scraped and the carpeting was designed from a needlepoint pattern researched by Park Service historians. Mr. Staib will use the basement rooms with fireplaces as private dining rooms.
As in the past, strong drink will be sold. "We know from the data there were parties you would not believe," Mr. Staib said. "They consumed incredible amounts of sherry, port, Madeira and wine." City Tavern will be the only place serving Thomas Jefferson Ale, an amber brew with an alcohol content of 9.8 percent, twice that of most beers. It is being made by Dock Street Brewing Company in Philadelphia according to Jefferson's recipe. "We had been working on the beer, and when we saw work was being done on the tavern, we came by to try to get the account," said Peter Breslow, a sales representative for the brewery. "The coincidence was unbelievable."
The beer will be served in small stemmed glasses because of its strength. It will be available in the dining rooms as well as in the bar room, whose bar is actually fitted with wooden bars to secure it. "That's the origin of the term," Mr. Staib said.
City Tavern is not Mr. Staib's first encounter with American history. The Parker House Hotel in Boston and the Sagamore Hotel in upstate New York are among his previous consulting assignments.
A native of Pforzheim, Germany, a town at the edge of the Black Forest, Mr. Staib began working in his parents' restaurant when he was 6. In 1969 he got a job as a chef in Chicago, and never went back to Europe.
Mr. Staib has no partners in the City Tavern project. It is the first job in which he has invested his own money, nearly $500,000 so far.
Each of the handmade costumes for the servers, from the mobcaps to the historically accurate shoes with no left or right, cost $400. He redid the kitchens and bought all the tableware. He plans eventually to open a shop to sell some of the items as souvenirs, along with baked goods and the like.
Records show that Daniel Smith, the tavern's first innkeeper, a man who straddled the fence politically and ran off to England after the Revolutionary War, purchased all the fixtures and furnishings.
"That even makes our expenses authentic," Mr. Staib said.

Early American Cuisine: Varied, but No Sandwiches
 
West Indian
Pepper Pot Soup
(Adapted from City Tavern)
Total time: 45 minutes
 
 1 link Italian hot sausage
 1/4 pound corned beef in one piece
 1 large onion, chopped
 4 scallions, chopped
 1 bay leaf
 2 sprigs fresh thyme
 1/2 Scotch bonnet pepper or other small hot chili, seeded and minced, or hot red pepper flakes or cayenne pepper to taste
 1 1/2 teaspoons whole allspice
 1/2 teaspoon whole black peppercorns
 6 cups beef stock
 2 cups shredded callaloo, spinach, mustard greens or other greens
 1 medium-size carrot, peeled and diced
 1/2 pound yuca, peeled and diced
 3/4 cup diced chayote or yellow squash
 Salt to taste.
1. Dice the sausage, place it in a 3- to 4-quart saucepan and cook over medium heat until lightly browned. Dice the corned beef, add it and continue to cook a few minutes. Add the onion, scallions, bay leaf and thyme, and continue to cook until the onion is tender. Stir in the hot chili or pepper.
2. In a mortar or spice grinder, crush the allspice and peppercorns and add them to the pot. Stir, then add the stock. Bring to a simmer, and add the greens. Cook for 20 minutes.
3. Add the carrot, yuca and squash and cook about 10 minutes longer. Taste, adjust the amount of pepper if necessary and season with salt. Remove the bay leaf and thyme before serving.
Yield: 6 servings.
Approximate nutritional analysis per serving: 200 calories, 9 grams fat, 30 milligrams cholesterol, 660 milligrams sodium (before salting), 10 grams protein, 20 grams carbohydrate.
 
Seafood Stew
(Adapted from City Tavern)
Total time: 45 minutes
 
 1 cup medium-dry or fruity white wine
 2 pounds mussels, scrubbed and debearded
 1 tablespoon unsalted butter, or more to taste
 1 large onion, sliced thin
 1 bulb fennel, sliced thin
 3 shallots, sliced thin
 12 jumbo shrimp, peeled and deveined
 1 pound bay scallops
 1/4 cup amontillado sherry
 1/2 cup heavy cream
 Salt and freshly ground white pepper
 A few drops lemon juice
 1 tablespoon minced chives.
1. Place 1/2 cup wine in a 3-quart saucepan, and bring to a boil. Add the mussels, cover and cook over medium heat until the mussels open, about 10 minutes. Use a slotted spoon to remove the mussels to a bowl. Strain the cooking liquid through a very fine strainer into a 2-cup measure. Add enough water to make 1 1/2 cups, and set aside.
2. Rinse any grit out of the saucepan. Place it over medium heat to dry it; then, add the butter. Add the onion, fennel and shallots, and saute, stirring from time to time, until they are tender but not brown.
3. Add the remaining wine, and cook over high heat until nearly all the wine evaporates. Add the mussel liquid. Bring to a boil, and add the shrimp. Cover, and cook about 5 minutes, until the shrimp are not quite completely pink. Stir in the scallops, cover and cook about 3 minutes. Add the mussels in their shells, cover and cook 2 minutes longer, until the mussels are reheated and the other seafood is cooked.
4. Using a slotted spoon, transfer the seafood, onions and fennel to a large, warmed, shallow serving bowl. Cover the bowl.
5. Bring the contents of the saucepan to a boil, add the sherry and cream and cook briskly for 5 minutes or so, until the liquid has reduced and thickened somewhat. Season to taste with salt and pepper, and add the lemon juice. Swirl in a little additional butter if desired. Pour the sauce over the seafood, sprinkle with chives and serve.
Yield: 4 servings.
Approximate nutritional analysis per serving: 440 calories, 20 grams fat, 220 milligrams cholesterol, 570 milligrams sodium (before salting), 45 grams protein, 20 grams carbohydrate.

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Drug Use: Still a Hushed-Up Secret in Baseball

BYLINE: By CLAIRE SMITH 

 BODY:
Drug use. It's one of baseball's dirtier little secrets, hidden under rugs, until sad little occasions like yesterday, when Dwight Gooden was suspended for violation of the commissioner's drug policy.
Drugs. The illness and dangers are otherwise not often spoken of in major league baseball. Rather the subject is caught in a D.M.Z. That is the no man's land where issues of substance are pinned down, while fights over money make up most of the volleys exchanged between management and players. 
 Suspensions of players like Gooden, Otis Nixon and Pascual Perez, or revelations of illicit drug use by players like Paul Molitor and Darryl Strawberry, shed light on the topics occasionally. This shows that the game has no formal drug policy.
There are informal arrangements between management and players. Mental health officials on both sides are on hand to examine players said to be in harm's way. Not in harm's way of the drugs that could take their lives. In harm's way of disciplinary action that could take away their livelihood, their salary, weeks, months or even a lifetime, as was the case with Steve Howe.
Howe, the Yankee pitcher, is the player a former commissioner, Fay Vincent, tried to remove from the game for life after Howe was found to have violated the commissioner's drug policy for a seventh time. That Howe is still playing is more evidence of the rift between management and its union than it is of a concerted effort by both sides to make sure that these players can get the best treatment that money can buy.
Major League Baseball once did have a formal drug policy but another former commissioner, Peter Ueberroth, vacated it in the mid-1980's, as it fell victim to yet another wrangle between management and the players.
So it is after Ueberroth acted that baseball stumbles from incident to incident, suspending players and probably praying that none of them die -- at least until their careers are over -- as were the cases with the former pitchers Eric Show and Rod Scurry.
Otherwise, drug dramas sadly play out the same way. Major League Baseball and its commissioner monitor players but only those with a drug history. If these players are found to be in violation of aftercare treatment -- didn't show up for treatment, flunked urinalysis, etc. -- then that player risks suspension.
The commissioner or his stand-in will then gravely announce the suspension, as Bud Selig, the head of the owners' executive council did yesterday. The club that employed that player then makes its own statement, expressing disappointment and surprise.
In Gooden's case, as in the case of Strawberry a few months ago, the player then issues a vague statement of remorse.
Within hours, sometimes minutes, the players' union representative issues his own statement. It usually consists of little more than huddling with said player to see if he agrees to grieve the penalty -- in Gooden's case 60 days without pay.
As this unfolds one cannot help but notice the lack of the word treatment. But then again one learned long ago not to assume that the No. 1 priority is the player as a person not as a potential 20-game winner, producer of 100 runs batted in or the cause celebre of a legal battle.
Where is Gooden? No one knew or cared to say yesterday. But if it was a relapse, isn't it important to know whether he is receiving treatment? If the cases of Scurry and Show proved anything it's that a life could be at stake here. It would be wonderful to see that one day when a crisis disrupts a player's life all statements are waived until both sides are absolutely in agreement as to how best to help the player. But when both sides are preparing to wrestle over the player instead of caring for him it is folly to hope the game will be so enlightened any day soon.
Gooden, like Nixon before him, will likely make more headlines because of the fight for his 60-day pay than for any acknowledgement that other things must now take priority in life. Especially if he failed two successive drug tests, as a highly placed baseball official indicated.
Baseball knows how this will look. The union knows, too. It is a fight for rights to be sure. But is it a fight for Gooden? Baseball, unlike the National Basketball Association, has never been called a drug-infested league. But it has had enough players who have been troubled by alcohol and drugs, from Mickey Mantle to Pascual Perez, who know that an important percentage of its players have trouble. No less than Selig acknowledged this yesterday.
Selig said he doesn't think baseball is anywhere close to being drug-infested, but said, "that's no less reason for us to pursue a thoughtful policy." Said Selig: "We may debate how widespread this problem is throughout baseball. We cannot debate that there are tragedies out there."
So even when one player falls and the game goes one more day without uniting to fight this problem, then the whole game falls as well.
"It's obviously something we need to do, to declare a partnership not only for economic reasons but sociological reasons as well," said Selig. "And this would have to be a thoughtful program with emphasis on education and aftercare. This can't be a happy day for anyone in baseball."
But it's a day the sport could use to make a landmark decision. Players and management could decide that this is one issue where there is no longer any more room for disagreement. If they come together and but one life is saved, it could be the biggest victory baseball ever produced.

HEADLINE: Cuban Tells Of Cinema Troubles

BYLINE: By By ANTHONY DePALMA, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, June 28

 BODY:
The director of the Havana Film Festival, for years a showcase of Cuban culture and a test of Communist tolerance, has left Cuba and vowed not to return, saying he will seek political asylum in the United States because he fears a new wave of repression against Cuban artists.
In an interview here last week, Jose Horta said that he feared for his safety if he returned before there was "a complete change in the policy of the Cuban Government." 
 "The festival has always been very open, very liberal, and now I think for some people that doesn't go down very well," he said.
Dressed in a blue blazer and black jeans, Mr. Horta said that shortly after boarding a plane in Havana on June 18, he was pulled off by security officers, his passport was taken and his baggage was unloaded and searched. He was not interrogated, but neither did his accusers tell him what they were looking for or why. Eventually they returned his passport and allowed him to leave. He flew to Mexico City, and a few days later sought refuge in Miami.
"The only thing I know," he said, "is that it wasn't a mistake and it wasn't a coincidence. It was deliberate and it was directed at me personally." Recently, he said, several paintings have been stolen from the national film institute where he works, and one of his assistants has been detained by the authorities without evidence. But he said he did not believe he was also a suspect in the case.
 
A Guess at the Cause
 Mr. Horta said some of his current trouble might be connected to a film that had its premiere in December's film festival. "Strawberry and Chocolate," by Tomas Gutierrez Alea, is the first Cuban film with an openly gay protagonist. A tale of intolerance in Cuban society, the film became a test of President Fidel Castro's willingness to look the other way.
The film won the top award at the festival, and more than a million Cubans have paid to see it. The film was recently acquired by Miramax Films, which plans to release it in the United States in January.
The Cuban film industry has had the support of the Casto Government almost from the beginning. Within months after the 1959 revolution that brought Mr. Castro to power, a national film institute was established.
The film festival, featuring new Latin American films, began in 1979 and has been a lavish demonstration of Mr. Casto's on-again, off-again willingness to tolerate criticism. International stars like Robert Redford and Arnold Schwarzenegger have attended the festival, held each December, and one of the most prized tickets customarily has been an invitation to a reception given by Mr. Castro at the national palace.
Cuba's artistic freedom has been scaled back since Soviet economic aid was withdrawn a few years ago and the island became increasingly unstable. Mr. Horta said he has had to reduce festival expenses, and last year had to ask the authorities not to cut the electricity while festival films were being shown.
The 42-year-old Mr. Horta has directed the festival for 16 years and has been influential in sustaining Cuba's film industry under Communism. He said that he never felt outright Government censorship but that the system had its own constraints, with artists generally knowing how far they could go without getting into trouble. Three years ago, an irreverent film called "Alice in Wondertown" was released for a few days and then withdrawn by the Government.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Jose Horta, head of the Havana Film Festival, in Mexico City. (Keith Dannemiller for The New York Times)
