SECTION: Section A; Page 22; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 424 words

HEADLINE: Constructive Chaos in Japan

 BODY:
Tsutomu Hata, who resigned last weekend after just nine weeks on the job, is the third Japanese Prime Minister to quit in the past year. Such instability is frustrating to the Clinton Administration, which has to conduct trade negotiations and coordinate Korea policy with officials who may be out of office before deals are ready to be clinched.
But the rapid turnover also reflects something positive, a broad realignment of Japanese political life away from one-party dominance and toward democratic competition. That shift should make future governments more responsive to ordinary consumers and less beholden to bureaucracy and special interests. Over the long run, that ought to ease relations between Tokyo and Washington. 
 More immediately, Mr. Hata's fall is a setback for the broad opposition coalition that succeeded the long-ruling Liberal Democrats last August. Ranging from left-wing Socialists to right-wing conservatives, the coalition was too diverse to be anything but temporary. It splintered the day Mr. Hata took office. Rightist and centrist groups joined forces in an attempt to convert the coalition into a second conservative party, able to compete directly with the Liberal Democrats. The Socialists, feeling squeezed out, then broke ranks, leaving Mr. Hata with only a minority in Parliament. The first serious challenge brought him down.
The Socialists will now likely determine the next Prime Minister. Mr. Hata could conceivably return to office by wooing them back. Or they could cut a deal with the Liberal Democrats, helping the old ruling party back to power. Either arrangement would be a marriage of convenience. But either would make it possible to postpone new elections until the breakthrough political reform bill passed in January takes full effect, bringing more representative districts and tighter contribution limits.
The Socialists are difficult partners, caught in a cold war time warp, opposed to any foreign policy assertiveness, suspicious of market initiatives and hard to please on fiscal policy. But they also represent the pacifist, cautious opinions of many Japanese. Accommodating their views is, at least for now, a parliamentary necessity. Yet unless they can transform themselves into something other than a negative, obstructive force, their electoral future looks bleak.
Several elections under the new system will be needed to complete Japan's political realignment and restore governmental stability. For Americans, as for Japanese, it should be worth the wait.

LENGTH: 1474 words

HEADLINE: Requests by Charities Overwhelm Restaurants

BYLINE: By BRYAN MILLER 

 BODY:
LIKE a benevolent urban stroller who has been confronted by one too many mendicants, restaurants and related businesses across the country are balking at the overwhelming number of requests they receive from charities for free meals and services.
"It's absolutely out of control," said Jeremiah Tower, the celebrated chef and owner of Stars and Stars Cafe in San Francisco. 
 David Bouley, the chef and owner of his namesake restaurant in New York, said, "It's really become crazy."
Both chefs pointed out that solicitations from fund-raisers have surged in the last three to five years to the point where they can hardly acknowledge all of them, much less participate. In 1990, the two chefs said, they received about two or three charity requests a day. Today Mr. Bouley receives five or more, while Mr. Tower gets up to 10. Most requests seek free meals at the restaurant that can be auctioned at a charity event. Sometimes, chefs' services -- cooking in a private home or at a party -- are sold as a way to raise money.
As a result of the increase in requests, restaurateurs say they are forced to turn down more nonprofit organizations than ever. Not only are the requests overwhelming, they say, but restaurant profit margins are considerably lower than they were a decade ago.
To deal with the situation, restaurateurs are much more selective about whom they assist, often preferring local organizations or those with whom they have a special relationship. For Alison Price, owner of the restaurant Alison on Dominick Street in New York, that could be an organization that works against hunger or illiteracy. For Mr. Bouley, it could be Planned Parenthood.
Fund-raisers say small organizations are feeling the squeeze more than large established groups that can offer restaurants high-profile exposure. All charitable organizations are working harder and targeting their potential donors more strategically than in the free-spending 1980's.
Mr. Tower says it is impossible to say yes to everyone. "If you were to say yes to just half of the biggest benefits, you would be spending a fifth of your time outside of the restaurant, not to mention what it would cost you," he said.
Mr. Tower, who says his restaurants have donated more than $1 million just in goods in the last 10 years, says he has begun to curtail his charity work and has cut back on charity-related travel. "In the peak years, I went to 20 to 30 major events a year," he recalled. "This year I may go to 10."
There are no reliable statistics that measure the food industry's charitable activities. Professional fund-raisers across the country, though, agreed that restaurateurs have always been generous. In a way, they point out, restaurants created their own problem by eagerly embracing charities in the 1980's and doing such an efficient job that they became irresistible targets.
Mr. Bouley, who has had to assign an employee to deal with all the requests that come in, said:"Ideally, I'd like to do all of them. But now they come from all over -- a school in Texas, hospitals from far away. You want to give back, but . . . " Auctions at which the prize is Mr. Bouley cooking dinner at the winner's home have fetched as much as $4,000.
But many professional fund-raisers say those days may be coming to an end.
"I think all fund-raising is becoming harder," said Liz Berger, who organizes an annual fund-raising event for Manhattan Dance Space, a nonprofit performance center. This spring the event included Mr. Bouley and fellow chef Jonathan Waxman, the Savoy restaurant in SoHo and Dean & Deluca, the retail store. Ms. Berger's good relations with Mr. Bouley in previous years, combined with the neighborhood aspect of the function, persuaded him to participate. Her personal connections with the others helped as well, she said.
"A small organization needs a big-ticket event, but that takes a lot of money and effort to organize and we are not in a position to do that," Ms. Berger said. "So we use chefs, who have developed sort of a cult status. The difference today is that we are working much harder to get the right chefs to participate."
Mr. Bouley says he now limits his altruistic work to specific groups like the 24 eighth graders from his old elementary school in Willimantic, Conn., that he invited for lunch, or to organizations like Planned Parenthood and hospitals.
Like many leading restaurants in New York City, Bouley helps nationally known organizations like Citymeals-on-Wheels, Save Our Strength and the March of Dimes because it has longstanding relationships with them.
One of the carrots charity organizations hold out to restaurants is the prospect of free publicity and exposure to potential new customers. The truth is, say restaurateurs, that publicity is minimal because the events have become routine, no longer worthy of major coverage by the media. As for new customers, virtually every restaurateur interviewed agreed that the rewards were negligible at best.
"They don't come back, generally," Ms. Price said of the top bidders at auctions. "Many are from out of town; others just come for the free dinner."
Ms. Price, whose small 56-seat French restaurant receives about eight requests a week from charities, remarked that generous giving had become a serious financial strain, especially on modest-size establishments in relatively frugal times.
"People don't realize that when we give away a table for two we lose the potential revenue from that table, and we need the profit from six other dinners to make up for it," she said. Two years ago, Alison on Dominick Street gave away about $25,000 in free meals and services, Ms. Price said. "Today, frankly, I can hardly do any of them, unless it is something I really care about or involves a good customer," she said.
Professional fund-raisers pointed out that food purveyors and wine companies often derive the least benefit from such events because guests pay more attention to the celebrity chefs than to the products.
"If charities worked, we would be selling a lot of wine," said Linda Johnson, executive director of the Sonoma County Wineries Association, in a recent article in Wine Business Monthly, a trade journal. "But charities have a lot of distractions to wine sales. You taste the wine and go talk to people, but you don't stand there and examine the wine."
Lewis Perdue, publisher of Wine Business Monthly, added: "Wineries are getting much more cautious. It has become a problem as wine auctions have proliferated. It used to be we had only two or so, but now all sorts of people -- even TV stations -- see wine auctions as a new way to raise money."
Shafer Vineyards in Napa, Calif., receives some 400 donation requests annually; a neighbor, Beringer Vineyards, receives a similar flood. Beringer is now redefining its charity policy to emphasize organizations within a 25-mile radius of the vineyard.
Some wine makers are even beginning to seek some sort of quid pro quo for their donations. Chateau Montelena in the Napa Valley is spearheading a campaign to make soliciting charities sign a letter expressing support for the wine industry. The charities are also asked to agree in writing that moderate wine consumption is healthful for most people.
Arienne Daguin, an owner of D'Artagnan, a Jersey City purveyor of foie gras and game to fine restaurants, says she receives up to 60 requests a month from charities for food donations. In May alone, she estimated, the company gave away $6,000 worth of products, which is a little higher than the average.
"I am getting tougher; I have to," Ms. Daguin said. "We are getting hit on two sides: by the charity and by the chefs who get involved in charities."
A pioneer in linking charities to the restaurant industry was the March of Dimes, which in 1978 held its first Gourmet Gala in the Waldorf-Astoria. Leading chefs from New York City cooked for the well-heeled guests. The gala just broke even. By 1988, however, the New York Gourmet Gala netted $800,000. The most recent gala, last fall, earned about $700,000, said Albert Hudes, a fund-raising consultant to the March of Dimes. Mr. Hudes attributed the event's diminished revenues to a relatively slack economy combined with possible overexposure of chefs on the charity circuit.
A lot of organizations copied the gala formula in the 1980's, Mr. Hudes said, adding, "It was so easy then."
By going to restaurants for help, fund-raisers say, groups could avoid having to hire caterers, rent spaces and deal with deliveries.
Everyone agrees that new ideas for generating funds need to be developed. Ms. Price, for one, has discussed other approaches with her chef. "We talked about doing all kinds of different things, like going fishing with the chef for a day," she said. "That would be fun, no?"

CORRECTION-DATE: July 4, 1994, Monday

An article in The Living Section on Wednesday about restaurants' effort to cope with requests from charitable groups misidentified a group that raises money through restaurant benefits. It is Danspace Project at St. Mark's Church in the Bowery, not Manhattan Dance Space.

HEADLINE: Senate Votes Bill Widening Pool of Jurors

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, June 29

 BODY:
Seeking to place more members of minority groups and more poor people onto juries, the New York State Legislature today passed a measure that for the first time would allow jurors to be drawn from unemployment and welfare rolls.
Currently, juries are selected using lists of registered drivers, registered voters and state and local taxpayers. But in many parts of the state, particularly urban areas, lawyers and judges have complained that the juror pool is too small or does not accurately reflect the local community. 
 "There are a lot of people in urban communities who aren't reached because they don't drive, don't vote or don't pay taxes," said Judith S. Kaye, the Chief Judge of the state's highest court, the Court of Appeals, and a longtime advocate of improving jury selection. "Obviously the most desirable thing is to have as broad representation as possible. It divides the benefits and burdens fairly.'
The bill, if signed into law by Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, would potentially increase the state's juror pool by over one million adults, state officials estimated.
 
Was Vetoed Previously
 Governor Cuomo vetoed similar legislation last year because he said it breached state and Federal laws requiring strict confidentiality for people receiving welfare or unemployment insurance. Elizabeth D. Moore, Mr. Cuomo's counsel, said today the new version seemed to have addressed Mr. Cuomo's concerns.
"We've been told he'll sign it," said Senator Joseph R. Holland, a Spring Valley Republican who sponsored the bill in the Republican-led Senate, which passed the bill today.
The Democratic-controlled Assembly passed an identical bill sponsored by Assemblyman Arthur O. Eve, a Buffalo Democrat, in March.
The legislation would mark a fundamental change in the way juries are chosen, Judge Kaye and other legal experts said. It will mean not only that juries will be more diverse, they said, but also that people will be called for jury duty less often because more jurors will be available.
But the bill falls well short of the sweeping reforms proposed by a state panel earlier this year. That panel, appointed by Judge Kaye, recommended increasing jury duty pay, ending exemptions for certain professions and reducing the time that jurors wait before being picked for trial.
 
Broad Backing
 The bill has received support from a diverse group that included trial lawyers, advocates for the poor, advocates for welfare reform, the League of Women Voters and the state's leading judges.
Many judges and trial lawyers argued that expanding the juror pool would help speed trials, which now are often slowed by the lack of available jurors.
"I have seen cases delayed for weeks because of the unavailability of jurors," said Stephan H. Peskin, president of the New York State Trial Lawyers Association. "In some counties they dry up; you just can't get them."
Black and Hispanic legislators backed the bill because they said it would help ensure that the court system was more accessible and fairer to the poor and members of minorities. And advocates for welfare reform said the legislation could give people on public assistance a valuable taste of public service.
"In a sense, it brings some people more into the mainstream," said Mr. Holland.
Mr. Holland said the new version of the bill would address Mr. Cuomo's concerns about confidentiality by masking which state agency provided a juror's name. That way, court administrators would not know whether a potential juror was drawn from, say, a list of registered drivers or a list of people receiving unemployment insurance.

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Cuomo Wants Business To See Him as a Friend

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, June 29

 BODY:
They weren't your typical Mario M. Cuomo constituents, these 300 bankers dressed in golfing plaids and Wall Street blues, most of them card-carrying Republicans. But, then, this wasn't your typical Mario M. Cuomo speech.
The Democratic Governor cited Abraham Lincoln but sounded more like another Republican President, Ronald Reagan, as he spoke to bankers recently at Saratoga Springs, advocating deregulation, privatization and maximizing corporate profits. "You are the answer to our problems," he said. "You are the tools we need to meet our challenges." 
 As he has begun campaigning in earnest for a fourth term, Mr. Cuomo is using his speeches, legislative clout and policy mandates to elevate one message above the din of many others: he's a friend to business.
 
Down in Polls
 Those who know Mr. Cuomo best for his passionate advocacy for the poor and his close ties to labor might find such chamber-of-commerce talk perplexing. But consider the politics of 1994: the state is just rising from deep recession, Mr. Cuomo is down in some polls and considered vulnerable on economic issues, and his chief Republican rival is painting him as a tax-and-spend liberal whose policies have cost the state half a million jobs.
"The bang against him will be he's anti-business," said a political adviser to Mr. Cuomo, speaking on condition of anonymity. "That's not true. But he has to make as positive a statement for business as he can."
Mr. Cuomo has done just that in a flurry of recent appearances with business executives and groups to tout three things: an expanding job base, a state budget that includes $470 million in tax cuts and an economic recovery he says will bring the state back to the soaring prosperity of the 1980's.
Along the way, he has promised to hold down state fees, avoid new regulation of business and to sponsor legislation that he says will make New York "aggressively hospitable to business."
"Why do you want to be more hospitable to business?" he said at a Binghamton aerospace company two weeks ago. "Because it is everything. If they succeed, we succeed."
 
Fast Footwork
 At times, Mr. Cuomo's efforts have involved some fast political footwork. Having once called the nation's military spending "excessive" in a memorable 1984 speech to the Democratic National Convention, Mr. Cuomo recently told military contractors in Binghamton that he would work with the Congressional delegations from New York, Texas and California to protect the B-2 bomber program. The bombers, which cost $2 billion each, have been criticized as wasteful and unnecessary by some in Congress.
"I'm absolutely convinced on the merits that it doesn't make sense to end the B-2 program as scheduled," Mr. Cuomo said.
In many forums, Mr. Cuomo has described himself as a victim of George Bush and Mr. Reagan, contending that their economic policies brought on a national recession. Yetin Saratoga Springs Mr. Cuomo made a bipartisan comparison of his declining popularity with that of Mr. Bush, suggesting that both their reputations have been sullied by economic forces beyond their control. .
"If everything is good and you're in place, you're a George Bush with a 90 percent rating because you just beat Saddam Hussein," Mr. Cuomo said. "But if you're there when everything goes bad, you're George Bush with a 29 percent rating because they just announced that my brother-in-law has been fired and he's coming to live with me. And that's called unemployment and you've got to blame somebody."
 
Stepping on Toes
 Mr. Cuomo has done more than make speeches about business, and in the process he has stepped on the toes of some traditional Democratic constituencies, including consumer groups, environmentalists and labor unions.
He was a leading proponent of a package of business tax cuts in the $34 billion state budget passed three weeks ago. Groups representing organized labor have raised concerns that the cuts, worth more than $1 billion when fully put in place over three years, will make it harder to work out a budget and force the State Legislature to reduce services.
He has lobbied for legislation to limit a type of lawsuit, known as derivatives, brought by shareholders against corporate boards for wrongdoing. The bill's leading proponents, General Electric and the Business Council of New York State, say the bill would end frivolous litigation. But consumer advocates like the New York Public Interest Research Group say that it would virtually eliminate an important check on corporate abuses.
He has advocated creating a hybrid business entity -- a limited liability company -- that would possess the liability protections of corporations but have the lower tax rates of partnerships. The proposal would help accounting firms and certain financial-service concerns, both of which have lobbied strongly for it, but some lawmakers contend that it could reduce tax revenues by tens of millions of dollars each year.
 
Attacking Pollution
 And he has said proposed Federal regulations intended to reduce pollution in the Great Lakes could impose unnecessary and costly burdens for towns and industries. Aides to Mr. Cuomo assert that New York already has tough water-pollution regulations and that some of the new rules would be difficult to administer. Several major corporations, including Eastman Kodak and International Paper, have made similar arguments.
But environmental groups say Mr. Cuomo's stance may slow the Great Lakes cleanup. Lee Wasserman, executive director of the Environmental Planning Lobby, said Mr. Cuomo's letter "sounded like it came right out of Kodak's word processor."
Republicans suggest that Mr. Cuomo is a recent convert to ideas like privatization and deregulation, arguing that he has little to show for his contention that "we have led the world in privatization."
"The difference bewteen George Pataki and Mario Cuomo is while Mario Cuomo is still thinking about privatization, George Pataki will get it done." said Caroline Quartararo, a spokeswoman for State Senator George E. Pataki, the endorsed Republican candidate for governor.
 
Market Factors
 In his speeches, Mr. Cuomo says that he has promoted privatization and deregulation since the mid-1980's but that the recession, market factors and a balky Legislature have slowed his efforts. He also bridles against suggestions that it is out of character for a Democrat with liberal roots to sound pro-business.
"I laugh about the labels: Democrats, Republicans, conservatives, liberals," he told the bankers in Saratoga Springs. "That you talk about in campaigns. You don't govern that way."
Some Democrats warn that Mr. Cuomo risks alienating Democratic loyalists like labor unions by working for business groups whose support remains in question. "He's working like mad for guys who will stab him in the back," said one Democratic Assemblyman.
But others suggest that Mr. Cuomo has little to lose because unions have nowhere else to turn this fall.
"His standing with the unions improves every day when they get a closer examination of Rudy Giuliani and Christie Whitman," said Jan D. Pierce, vice president of the Communication Workers of America.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; Foreign Desk 

LENGTH: 1457 words

HEADLINE: TURNOVER IN JAPAN: THE OVERVIEW;
SURPRISE ALLIANCE PICKS A SOCIALIST AS JAPAN'S LEADER

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, June 29

 BODY:
The conservatives who controlled Japan for four decades until their ouster last summer swept back into power tonight, forging an alliance with their oldest ideological enemy by electing the head of the Socialist Party, Tomiichi Murayama, as Prime Minister.
The bizarre partnership between the Liberal Democratic Party and the Socialists stunned the country and prompted immediate fears that whatever halting progress had been made toward deregulating the economy and cleaning up a corruption-ridden political system could be slowed or reversed. 
 In Washington, officials reacted with dismay, saying that they made it virtually impossible to achieve progress soon in divisive trade talks in which the United States is seeking greater access to Japanese markets for American exports.
The currency markets seemed to agree. Betting that the new Government would do nothing to address Japan's $60 billion trade surplus with the United States, traders drove the dollar to a new postwar low of 98.8 yen.
[By early Thursday, the dollar had recovered a bit, to 99.13 yen. Business Day, page D1.]
The turbulent developments also seemed likely to slow or even reverse the deregulation of Japan's economy and efforts to reform its corruption-ridden electoral system.
Income tax cuts to stimulate the economy might be delayed, and there were questions about how much Japan would now cooperate with the United States on possible sanctions to stop North Korea's nuclear weapons program. [Page A10.]
Mr. Murayama, who is 70 years old and has no experience even in a cabinet post, won a dramatic 261 to 214 vote this evening to become Japan's fourth Prime Minister in a year and the first Socialist to lead the country since 1948.
In another strange twist, he defeated former Prime Minister Toshiki Kaifu, who just hours before the vote defected from the Liberal Democrats in outrage at their maneuver and was endorsed by the loose coalition of parties that has led the country for the past year.
The surprise naming of Mr. Murayama, after the resignation on Saturday of Prime Minister Tsutomu Hata, was engineered by the conservative old guard, which knew it would be seen as a cynical ploy, but viewed it as the only hope of once again taking the reins of the country they controlled for 38 years.
"Finally, we have come back to power," Yoshiro Mori, a leader of the Liberal Democrats, said tonight to party members, seeming to make clear by his tone that his party -- and not the Socialists -- planned to call the shots in the new government.
It is unclear who among the Liberal Democrats, a party that is also deeply split, will be pulling the strings of the new Government. The party president, Yohei Kono, is considered weak. Several rightist members of the party defected rather than ally with a Socialist.
Only hours after the vote, the Liberal Democrats agreed to appoint what Mr. Murayama termed a "dovish cabinet" that would, for example, share the Socialists' aversion to a greater Japanese role in United Nations peacekeeping operations.
In Japanese politics, the combination of the Liberal Democrats and the Socialists is as unimaginable as a merger of the American religious right and the left wing of the Democratic Party, and some predict it will lead to the further splintering of both parties.
 
'It Is a Disaster'
 Japanese business leaders and many of the country's leading bureaucrats immediately denounced the election of Mr. Murayama, saying they feared it would mean paralysis in trade talks and security policy at a time of already tense relations with the United States.
"It is a disaster for us," a top official in one of Japan's most powerful ministries said tonight. "We will be laughingstocks at the G-7," he added, alluding to the meeting next month in Italy of the leaders of seven major industrial nations.
In the absence of workable trade agreements, a weaker dollar may be the only option for the Clinton Administration's hopes to dramatically increase exports to Japan.
It was unclear whether the new Government, once formed, would try to dismantle or delay the legislation passed earlier this year to radically change the way electoral districts are organized. The new system would transfer more power to younger, urban voters, and the Liberal Democrats and the Socialists both fear that that shift could doom them by weakening the strength of their traditional constituencies.
Virtually no political players here believe the new alliance of left-wing Socialists and right-wing Liberal Democrats can last longer than a few months. Mr. Murayama and his party have always battled the Liberal Democrats, who have embodied Japan's conservative, anti-Communist establishment since 1955.
Though they have moved considerably to the right in recent years, in the past the Socialists have been supportive of North Korea and its rulers, highly protectionist and steadfast in opposition to Japan's security treaty with the United States.
Until Tuesday, when the first indications of the new alliance leaked out, Liberal Democrats regularly said that the Socialists were totally unfit to run the world's second largest industrial economy.
"I expect some of the party members may be embarrassed," Mr. Kono, the Liberal Democrats' leader, said tonight. "I hope they will understand our decision."
But by virtue of their sheer numbers, the Liberal Democrats will dominate the little-known politician now heading the country. Before tonight's vote there were more than 200 Liberal Democrats in the lower house of Parliament and only 74 Socialists.
Sensitive cabinet posts like the Foreign and Finance Ministries, the Defense Agency and the Ministry of International Trade and Industry are not likely to be entrusted to Socialists.
As recently as Tuesday, top officials were dismissing the possibility that Mr. Murayama, widely regarded as a left-leaning Socialist and inexperienced in policy matters, could actually become Prime Minister.
"Not a chance," a senior defense official here said with a laugh. A mid-level diplomat said that if Mr. Murayama became Prime Minister, "the bureaucrats would revolt, and he would sit at his desk with no one to tell him what to say."
 
A Desperation Move
 But in a crazed, murky day of backroom negotiations, the two parties came together out of desperation, fearing that unless they combined their votes, both parties would be split apart as the old political order here disintegrates.
Mr. Murayama was elected in a dramatic vote on the floor of Parliament just three hours before it was scheduled to go out of session. For Japanese voters, long accustomed to votes whose outcomes are known months in advance, it was a rare moment of real competition.
Many of Mr. Murayama's own party members refused to vote for him, and the first ballot failed to yield a decisive winner. On a second ballot, Mr. Murayama was elected, 261 to 214, over Mr. Kaifu, the former Prime Minister.
Mr. Kaifu, who served from 1989 to 1991, when he was removed by senior party leaders opposed to his support for measures to end corruption, was a surprise candidate because he had been a prominent member of the Liberal Democrats until two hours before the vote.
 
Embraced by Coalition
 He quit the party in disgust rather than endorse Mr. Murayama, and was immediately embraced by the six coalition parties that had governed Japan over the last year. Their hope was that his clean image would lure a number of Liberal Democrats.
Ultimately a number of prominent, conservative Liberal Democrats did defect -- including former Prime Minister Yasuhiro Nakasone and former Foreign Minister Michio Watanabe -- but there were not enough votes to defeat the combined forces of Japan's two largest political parties.
In public, the politicians who led the overthrow of the Liberal Democrats last year were angry and bitter. In private, though, the coalition's leaders said the reversion of power to the Liberal Democrats might create a public backlash that could help the coalition in the next election.
"The sight of Socialists and the L.D.P. as allies is just too Machiavellian, even for Japanese who are very lenient about philosophical differences," said Seizaburo Sato, a professor at Keio University who has strong ties to the country's old conservative leadership. "In the next election, both the L.D.P. and the Socialists will suffer very much."
The last Socialist Prime Minister was Tetsu Katayama, who served for eight months from 1947 to 1948. He was elected in the first general election under Japan's new constitution, during the American Occupation that followed World War II.
The Socialists spent the next 40 years in the political wilderness.

HEADLINE: Fleeing Haiti: For the Poor, Choices Are Limited

BYLINE: By GARRY PIERRE-PIERRE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LEOGANE, Haiti, June 29

 BODY:
Three years ago, five armed soldiers came knocking at Jean-Fritz Salame's door, looking for his father. But Mr. Salame's father, Louitenante, a low-level official in the political party of the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, escaped through a back window.
The next night the soldiers returned looking for the son. But like his father, Mr. Salame slipped away. 
 "It's been a nightmare for me," said Mr. Salame, 17, who said his only chance for survival is a boat trip. "If I don't get out I will get killed."
For Wilson Jean, 26, escape by boat is a matter of economic survival. Mr. Jean, who is unemployed, has a wife and six children.
"I am going to go and take my chances," Mr. Jean said, discounting the fact that even if is picked up by the United States Coast Guard, he will be returned to Haiti regardless of his economic straits unless he can persuade immigration officers that he has been a victim of political persecution.
These men symbolize the pressures that have led thousands to flee since the September 1991 coup that ousted Father Aristide as President. And if thousands more take to sea, as few here doubt, it will bring yet another crisis for the Clinton Administration, which has struggled to find a workable Haiti policy.
Last year hundreds of boats were built here by Haitians waiting for the propitious moment. Now, with the Administration's more generous refugee policy, a punishing international embargo on Haiti and an increase in army-led terror, Haitians are making good on their promises.
All along the coast, fishing boats are being prepared. Most owners have no intention of getting to Florida. Their rickety vessels are barely able to make it 15 miles offshore, where a dozen Coast Guard cutters are on patrol. The refugees are taken to a United States Navy hospital ship anchored at Kingston, Jamaica, for asylum interviews.
On Tuesday, 755 Haitian boat people were picked up, pushing the total past 2,000 in two days.
 
Odds Seem to Improve
 This wave began shortly after President Clinton announced on May 8 that he was rescinding the policy of summarily returning Haitians without granting them interviews to determine whether they should be given refugee status. The exodus gathered steam after word that a third of those interviewed received asylum, a sharp increase from the earlier 5 percent rate among the boat people.
Also contributing to the flow is a further decline of the economy since a near-total ban on commercial flights and a ban on financial transactions with the United States.
While Haitians take to the seas, United States officials are urging those with political problems to seek asylum at processing centers in Haiti. Since February 1992, United States officials in Haiti have received 58,000 applications and interviewed 17,000 people, of whom 4,000 were given refugee status. But with the ban on flights, about 200 Haitians granted asylum have been stranded in Haiti.
Moreover, many Haitians are loath to stand in line outside the processing centers while soldiers drive by. Others say the process is too lengthy.
 
Luck Is a Big Factor
 For others, who readily admit they are fleeing misery rather than persecution, the slightest chance of finding a compassionate interviewer who will allow them into the United States is worth taking a flimsy boat. They have watched too many friends who fled for economic reasons gain entry.
"Everyone has his own luck," Mr. Jean said. "If I get to go to the United States, I could find a job and feed my children."
In Leogane, 20 miles south of Port-au-Prince, almost everyone said they would be taking a boat.
At a glance, Leogane seems quiet. The entrance to town, one of the many ports of departure for the boat people, is lined with two rows of palm trees. The road is unpaved and dusty and the pastoral town is peppered with mud huts, pastel gingerbread homes and cement houses. The people sell charcoal and the catch of the day.
But beneath that tranquility lies a more sinister place. Standing on the black-sand shores of Leogane, where several boats are being built and others stand ready for a voyage, residents describe a pattern of abuse by local military leaders.
They say that indiscriminate shootings have become part of everyday life, and that a group of even five people gathering publicly will be immediately dispersed by soldiers. Uttering the name of Father Aristide is considered the highest treason.
"Too many people have died," said Sauver Ouest, 52, who added that he would take a boat as soon as he could. "Clinton can send Aristide to deliver us. If he wants to free us he can, because Haiti is a little country."
Jean-Claude Foreste, 41, echoed these sentiments. Until the coup, his job was to affix pictures of President Aristide in offices and on poles.
Shortly after the coup, soldiers came looking for him. He fled La Gonave, an island off Leogane, and came here with his 10 children. Mr. Foreste said he had applied for asylum in Port-au-Prince but had not heard from anyone since the application, two years ago.
"The moment I find a boat leaving, I will take one," he said. "What I want the most is peace and security. I want to be somewhere until things change in my country. I want to take my children with me."
But some people, like Jerome Estaril, 45, said they would not take a boat because they know that they will be sent back. "I can't leave," he said. "I will only go if I am sure I will get to stay. I am only hungry and the United States does not want me."

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Continues Its Drop Against the Yen

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
The dollar continued its record-breaking descent against the Japanese yen yesterday. But the drop, which occurred in light, orderly trading, did little to upset other financial markets.
The selling began in Asia after Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen, reiterated something the foreign-exchange market already knew -- that Japan's political turmoil was slowing progress in America's trade talks with Tokyo. 
 "It obviously slows the process down until they get their people in place to negotiate," Mr. Bentsen said in response to a question at a Foreign Policy Association dinner in New York. "We do not see the progress at this point that we were hoping for."
In a speech he delivered at the dinner on Tuesday night, Mr. Bentsen offered a vigorous defense of the dollar and said it was not the Clinton Administration's intent to use a weaker dollar as leverage in trade talks with the Japanese.
Nevertheless, the dollar tumbled. In late New York trading, the dollar was quoted at 98.80 yen, down from 100.05 yen late Tuesday, after hitting its latest post-World War II low of 98.55 yen yesterday afternoon.
In Tokyo early today, the dollar edged up slightly to 99.10 yen.
But yesterday's sharp drop continued a decline that over the last 18 months has lopped off more than 20 percent of the dollar's value against the yen as Japan's trade surplus with the United States has swelled.
Analysts said that yesterday's election of Tomiichi Murayama, chairman of Japan's Social Democratic Party, as the next Prime Minister made it unlikely that bilateral trade negotiations with the Japanese would be resolved anytime soon.
"The Bentsen comments reiterated all that the market was already thinking," one currency trader said. "At this point the market is very negative and very bearish, and is always on the lookout for additional reasons to sell."
Robert Hormats, vice chairman of Goldman Sachs International, attended Tuesday night's dinner. "Bentsen said exactly the right thing, and was stating the obvious," he said. "The Japanese Government is in a period of enormous uncertainty, and that will draw out the trade talks."
In an interview yesterday, Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, said, "How this change of Government in Japan -- the fourth Prime Minister we have had to deal with in less than a year -- is going to affect the negotiations is subject to anyone's guess."
Despite its slide against the yen, the dollar was fairly stable against most other currencies, and even rose a bit against the German mark. But traders said the dollar's rise against the mark was more reflective of a weaker mark against the yen.
"There was not a lot of dollar-buying against the mark," another trader said. "There was a lot of mark-selling against the yen."
In New York, the dollar was last quoted at 1.5865 marks, up from 1.5787 marks late Tuesday.
In another bit of positive news, stock and bond traders, who have been reacting nervously to the dollar's fall against the yen, took yesterday's slide in stride.
Traders agreed that if financial markets were able to maintain a semblance of stability in the wake of further dollar weakness, monetary authorities would be likely to postpone taking any steps that might shore up the currency until sometime next month.
Over the course of the next 10 days, financial markets will need to digest a heavy diet of fresh information, including new economic statistics as well as meetings of the Federal Reserve Board's policy-making Open Market Committee in Washington and the Group of Seven finance ministerial session in Naples.
Many traders believe the Fed will eventually raise short-term interest rates. A rate increase, in turn, would likely push up the value of the dollar by making dollar-denominated interest-bearing investments more attractive to international investors.

HEADLINE: 3.4% U.S. Growth in Quarter

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
The economy grew at an annual rate of 3.4 percent in the first three months of this year, led by surprising continued strength in consumer spending, the Government said today.
While the economy slowed drastically from the growth rate of 7 percent at the end of last year, analysts said the pace of expansion was solid, if unspectacular. Many said they expected that the economy would at least duplicate the performance of the first three months in the current quarter that ends on Thursday. 
 A month ago, the Commerce Department estimated that the gross domestic product, the total value of goods and services produced in the United States, rose 3 percent in the first quarter of 1994. The upward revision today exceeded the expectations of most economists.
The Commerce Department attributed the revision to an additional $5.6 billion in consumer spending and $2.3 billion in business investment. These more than offset a drop in public spending and a decline in exports.
There was only moderate inflation in the first three months this year, the Commerce Department said. An inflation index tied to the gross domestic product rose at a rate of 2.6 percent, compared with 1.3 percent for the final three months of 1993.
Economic expansion was held down by the harsh winter in much of the nation and the Los Angeles earthquake.
The Commerce Department also said that after-tax corporate profits in the first quarter fell to an annualized $287.5 billion, down 2.8 percent from the last quarter of 1993, after a 7.9 percent rise the previous three months. The lower corporate profits were the first since an 11.2 percent drop in the third quarter of 1992.

HEADLINE: THE HEALTH CARE DEBATE: SPENDING LIMITS;
House Panel Backs Health Cost Controls

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
The House Ways and Means Committee has endorsed legislation that would, for the first time, set annual limits on health spending for the nation as a whole and for each state.
If any state exceeded its limit, the Federal Government would enforce strict price controls on doctors, hospitals, prescription drugs and the rest of the private health care industry in that state. 
 The cost controls are part of a comprehensive measure to overhaul the nation's health care system. The bill seeks to slow the increase in per capita private health spending so it grows no faster than the economy as a whole; in recent years, it has been growing nearly twice as fast.
Other Congressional committees working on health care legislation have also included measures to slow the growth of health spending. But the Ways and Means Committee is one of the most influential, and its proposal is the most extensive.
The committee approved these cost controls on Tuesday over strenuous objections from the American Hospital Association and the American Medical Association, which said the new regulations would freeze in place all the flaws and inefficiencies of the existing health care system.
 
Hospitals' Objection
 Michael J. Rock, a lobbyist for the American Hospital Association, noted that the bill would slice up the nation's health care spending into 10 categories and set separate budget goals for each. This, he said, runs counter to trends revolutionizing the health care industry. Under these trends, consumers and employers typically pay a lump sum to an insurance company or a group of doctors or hospitals for all the care needed by a person or a group of people.
But supporters of the measure maintained that doctors and hospitals would not work seriously to keep costs down unless they faced the possibility of price controls.
Another potential problem is that the Federal Government has only rough estimates of health spending in each state. Under current law, the Government is not required to tabulate spending by state, and it has had no pressing need for such data.
But consumer groups and labor unions support the cost controls as precisely the type of shock therapy needed for the health care system.
 
Medicare as Model
 The price controls the committee envisions for the private health care industry are modeled after techniques already used to pay doctors and hospitals treating patients under Medicare, the Federal health insurance program for the elderly and the disabled. Representative Pete Stark, the California Democrat who heads the Ways and Means Subcommittee on Health, said these formulas had effectively slowed the growth of Medicare, a contention many Republicans vehemently disputed.
President Clinton proposed a different type of controls on health spending. Under his proposal, the Government would limit annual increases in health insurance premiums, on the assumption that insurers would then force doctors and hospitals to accept lower payments than they would otherwise get.
The Senate Committee on Labor and Human Resources, headed by Edward M. Kennedy, follows the approach recommended by Mr. Clinton. Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan of New York, chairman of the Finance Committee, wants to set annual goals for increases in private health insurance premiums, but he would let Congress decide at a later date what steps should be taken if the actual increases exceeded the goals.
In the Ways and Means Committee, no aspect of health care legislation has generated fiercer debate than the plan for spending limits and "standby price controls."
Representative Michael A. Andrews, a moderate Democrat from Texas, proposed eliminating the entire cost-control regime. All 14 Republicans supported his effort, but he lost by 20 to 18. The failure of the proposal had the effect of endorsing the cost controls in the original bill, which is expected to come to a vote on Thursday.
The committee's acting chairman, Representative Sam M. Gibbons of Florida, won over a few wavering Democrats by giving Congress a chance to re-examine the issue in the year 2000. But if Congress did nothing, and if health spending in any state exceeded the goal for that state, then the price controls would take effect in that state in 2001.
Representative Nancy L. Johnson, Republican of Connecticut, described the spending limits as Draconian and said no industrial country had curbed health spending as severely as the bill would require.
But Gerald M. Shea, director of employee benefits at the A.F.L.-C.I.O., said a regulatory mechanism, including the threat of price controls, was needed to force providers to bargain seriously with the consumers and purchasers of health care.
"Even with all the competition in the world," Mr. Shea said, "we couldn't get costs in line as fast as we need for the good of the economy" if there was no threat of Government regulation.
 
H.M.O.'s Included
 Mr. Shea said his only regret was that the price controls in the Ways and Means measure could not take effect before 2001. Liberal Democrats agreed to defer price controls to that year to placate more conservative lawmakers and economists who asserted that the cost goals could be met by encouraging competition among insurance companies, doctors, hospitals and drug companies.
Under the Ways and Means bill, health maintenance organizations would also be subject to the price controls. The maximum premiums would initially be computed with the same formula used to calculate Medicare payments to H.M.O.'s enrolling elderly people.
Health maintenance organizations describe current Medicare payments as inadequate. Karen M. Ignagni, president of the Group Health Association of America, which represents 500 H.M.O.'s with 37 million members, said "it would be devastating" to use a similar formula in calculating payments to health maintenance organizations for private patients. Private patients account for the vast majority of the 45 million people in health maintenance organizations. Medicare and Medicaid number fewer than five million.
Representative Benjamin L. Cardin, Democrat of Maryland, observed that several states had been regulating hospital rates for years, with none of the dire consequences foreseen by Republicans.
But Richard J. Davidson, president of the American Hospital Association, said state officials demonstrated much greater sensitivity to local concerns and the problems of particular hospitals than could be expected of Federal officials responsible for a vast national program.

HEADLINE: A Quality-of-Life Offense? It Could End Up in Arrest.

BYLINE: By NORIMITSU ONISHI 

 BODY:
Giorgio Favia was awakened yesterday morning by his mother's knock on his front door. "These nice police officers want to talk to you," said his mother, who lives on the first floor of their apartment building in the Bronx.
Minutes later, he was in a patrol car's back seat headed to the police station. Concerned about appearances, he said, the two officers did not handcuff him until he was out of his mother's sight. 
 His crime: drinking beer out of a bag in Washington Square Park.
Mr. Favia, a father of two, was one of 33 people arrested yesterday as part of the New York City Police Department's crackdown on so-called quality-of-life offenses. Like Mr. Favia, they had failed to pay summonses issued for such transgressions as drinking and urinating in public, playing their radios too loud and riding bicycles on sidewalks.
 
Computer Records
 The police began the program in mid-March in the West Village's Sixth Precinct. Under the system that still exists elsewhere, only a fraction of people given summonses go to court. To combat this, officers in the West Village now bring violators without photo identification to the station house, where computer records make it possible for repeat offenders to be arrested later.
Underlying this policy is the belief of the Police Department, and City Hall, that leniency toward even minor offenses reduces the city's quality of life and creates a culture that encourages greater crimes. And next week the department is expected to expand this policy citywide.
Police Commissioner William J. Bratton said he was pleased with the program's beginning. "It's not going to be just writing a piece of paper so we can get a statistic that we did this, saying we issued 10,000 summonses," he said. "If the summonses aren't having any impact, there's no sense writing the paper."
Since March the police have issued 1,834 summonses in the Sixth Precinct. Of those summoned, 876 failed to appear in court, and the police hope the first arrests yesterday will help reduce the percentage of scofflaws.
As he left Criminal Court in lower Manhattan yesterday, Mr. Favia, 23 and unemployed for the last four months, grumbled about paying a $25 fine (plus a $45 late penalty) and explained why he hadn't in the first place. How could he afford to, he asked, with a wife, two children and only $140 a week in jobless benefits?
John Jollon, 29, was waiting outside the court for his brother Kevin, 34. They too had been drinking outside, near Bleecker and Sullivan Streets, he said. His brother was fined; he wasn't. "They should be out there busting drug pushers," said Mr. Jollon, who lives in Richmond Hill, Queens.

HEADLINE: TURNOVER IN JAPAN: TRADE;
Hopes Dim Over Trade With Japan

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
The rise to power of a hybrid coalition of Socialists and conservatives in Japan has dampened hopes in Washington that the yearlong negotiations with Tokyo to open some of its closed markets can be concluded in time for the Group of Seven summit meeting in Italy next week.
Indeed, American officials are now loath to predict when those trade talks might conclude. American officials now use words like "over time" and "the long run." 
 This dimming of hopes for any resolution of the trade problems between the United States and Japan is not only bad news on the trade front, but is also clearly hurting the dollar, which continues to fall despite Administration efforts to talk up the value of the American currency.
 
Protectionists in Japan
 On Tuesday night and again this afternoon Secretary of the Treasury Lloyd Bentsen declared that the United States was concerned about the fall of the dollar against the yen and wanted to see its slide reversed, but his public declarations had no effect on the markets.
The reason is this: Currency traders know that the Socialists now spearheading the new Government in Japan are the most protectionist party in that country. They long opposed opening the Japanese rice market to foreigners, let alone high-tech sectors, and they generally do not favor making concessions that would open other markets to the United States in ways that might cost Japanese workers their jobs.
With Socialists sharing power with conservatives in Tokyo for the near future, currency traders assume that there is little hope for a negotiated settlement of the American-Japanese trade dispute.
Therefore, many currency speculators conclude, the Clinton Administration will be forced by the rising trade deficit with Tokyo to use a weaker dollar -- whether by design or default -- to try to pressure Japan into concessions -- even if Washington says it does not want to do that. A weak dollar and strong yen makes goods Japan wants to sell in the United States more expensive and makes the goods the United States wants to sell in Japan cheaper.
 
Dollar Holding Up
 The dollar is actually holding up well against most other currencies.
As much as Mr. Bentsen now says "the dollar is not a tool of our trade policy," American officials generally don't seem overly upset by the howls of protest coming from the big Japanese exporters, complaining that they are being priced out of the American market by the soaring yen.
Either way, what is clear for now is that the short-term prospects for a negotiated settlement have been set back. The United States Trade Representative, Mickey Kantor, said in an interview today:
"How this change of government in Japan -- the fourth Prime Minister we have had to deal with in less than a year -- is going to affect the negotiations is subject to anyone's guess. We are continuing to meet with the Japanese side. We even have received some new proposals from the Japanese side in the past few days. " Neverthless, we have to look at this realistically, and obviously it is difficult to assume you can make a tremendous amount of progess under these circumstances."
Mr. Bentsen echoed this view in remarks to the Foreign Policy Association in New York on Tuesday, saying of the talks with Japan: "We do not see the progress at this point that we were hoping for."
Mr. Bentsen told reporters today that he was still optimistic about progress with Japan "over time," but he clearly was not hazarding any predictions about the short term.
American officials note with some bemusement that for the last year they have been complaining about the hold that the permanent Japanese bureaucrats have over that country's negotiating positions in the trade talks, seemingly dominating the political leaders at every turn.
But lately, American officials have seen some of the benefits of that bureaucratic dominance: Despite the political turmoil in Tokyo, the bureaucrats handling the negotiations with Washington are still marching to the beat of their own drummer and showed up this week offering new proposals in the negotiations.
 
A Glimmer of Hope
 Ultimately, though, any final agreement will have to be affirmed by the new political leadership, but the sheer fact that the negotiations are continuing here with the bureaucrats -- on opening the Japanese markets for insurance, automobiles and auto parts, medical equipment and telecommunications -- gives American officials at least a glimmer of hope that a negotiated solution sometime is not completely out of the question.
American officials say there is no hope that a deal can be struck opening the Japanese markets for autos and auto parts anytime soon, but in the field of insurance and government procurement of medical equipment and telecommunications gear the two sides, at least before today, were narrowing their differences.
Japanese officials say that if they could get the green light from the political level, they could probably close a deal on opening the Japanese insurance market by next week.

HEADLINE: U.S., Allies and Russia Agree on Bosnia Map

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PARIS, June 29

 BODY:
The United States, Russia and the European Union have agreed on a map for the territorial division of Bosnia designed to end the 27-month war in the former Yugoslav republic, a spokeswoman for the French Foreign Ministry said today.
The map, the fruit of several months of consultation with the warring factions in Bosnia, is set to be formally approved by Secretary of State Warren Christopher, Foreign Minister Andrei V. Kozyrev of Russia and European Union ministers at a meeting in Geneva on July 5. 
 The map, the latest in a series of diplomatic attempts to draw lines through the ethnic confusion of Bosnia, was not immediately made public, but it is known to offer 51 percent of the territory to a federation of Bosnian Muslims and Croats and 49 percent to the Bosnian Serbs.
Maps said to represent the international plan, published by Serbian newspapers and described as broadly accurate by Western diplomats, suggest that an attempt has been made to link Muslim enclaves in eastern Bosnia to Sarajevo and to insure that Muslims can return to several cities from which they were evicted by the Serbs.
 
Timing Not Fixed
 Previous diplomatic maps have all failed to resolve the central conundrum of Bosnia: how to achieve the return of Bosnia's Muslims to their homes given the fact of the Bosnian Serbs' military victory and determination to hold a coherent body of territory.
The French spokeswoman said it was not yet clear when the map would be submitted to the warring parties.
But she said they might be called to Geneva on July 5 and formally handed the plan. This would give them three days to respond before a summit meeting of of the Group of Seven major industrialized nations in Naples that begins on July 8.
"This plan amounts to a last chance to end the war," the spokeswoman said. "French troops in the United Nations peacekeeping force will not spend another winter in Bosnia like the last one."
France, like other Western nations, has grown deeply impatient with the bloodshed in Bosnia and is determined to withdraw at least part of its large contingent in the United Nations force if the warring parties shun the lastest peace plan.
The spokeswoman said that American, Russian and European diplomats had also agreed on a series of "inducements and punishments" designed to focus the minds of Serbs and Muslims on the consequences of pursuing the war.
Western officials said the broad plan was that if Serbs accepted the map and the Muslim-led Bosnian Government rejected it, an international trade embargo on Serbia might be eased. But if the Bosnian Government said yes and the Serbs no, the arms embargo that puts the Bosnian Government at a disadvantage might be lifted.
A danger of this idea is that it will merely encourage each side to try to insure that it is the other that rejects the plan -- a tactic already explicitly referred to by the Bosnian President, Alija Izetbegovic.
 
'Many Violations'
 The completion of the map came as fighting raged between Muslims and Serbs in central Bosnia despite a one-month cease-fire signed by the two sides on June 10.
"There are many violations of the cease-fire," Yasushi Akashi, the top United Nations official in the Balkans, said today in Brussels, where he attended a meeting with NATO officials called to review how an eventual peace would be enforced.
Bosnian Government forces, fighting in the Mount Ozren area, are apparently trying to secure a strategic road between their strongholds of Tuzla in northern Bosnia and Zenica in central Bosnia.
"There is no peace in Bosnia and no cease-fire," Brig. Andrew Ridgway of Britain said in a statement issued in the central Bosnian town of Gornji Vakuf. "After the end of the cease-fire, there will be at least 20 years of war."
This pessimistic assessment reflected the fact that the Bosnian Government and Croats have been talking openly about using their alliance to crush the Serbs on the battlefield, possibly in a coordinated offensive in the fall.
They have shown no public enthusiasm for a settlement that will inevitably be imperfect, while the Serbs, who hold about 70 percent of Bosnia, have made clear that they regard the territorial concessions being demanded of them as excessive.

HEADLINE: Investors Seek a Haven in Money Market Funds

BYLINE: By LESLIE EATON 

 BODY:
The turmoil in the world's financial markets is giving Americans the jitters, making them reluctant to continue pouring billions of dollars into stock and bond mutual funds, industry officials said yesterday. Instead, investors are heading for the safety of money market funds.
Executives at fund companies suggest that investors are merely parking their cash in money market funds until the markets calm down. 
 For example, Fidelity Investments, the nation's largest mutual fund company, saw 25 percent more cash flood into its money market funds in June than in May, said Neal Litvack, executive vice president of marketing for the firm.
 
Flight From Bond Funds
 "That money is ready to go back in, given some sign of stability," he said, noting that a recent survey sponsored by Fidelity found that 38 percent of fund investors planned to buy stock funds in the next six months.
But some investment advisers think inflow is coming from people fleeing bond funds, which have been falling in value since February, when the Federal Reserve started raising interest rates. Taxable bond funds are down more than 3 percent this year, according to Morningstar Inc.; funds that invest in municipal bond funds have fallen more than 4 percent.
"If you came to me and said, 'I've been battered in long-term funds and I want to take my money out,' I'd say, 'Do it,' " said Kurt Brouwer, president of Brouwer & Janachowski, a San Francisco investment advisory firm. "I wouldn't leave it in long-term bonds, because interest rates probably are going to go up some more."
Such scared investors, he added, need to re-evaluate their investment objectives and the amount of risk they can tolerate.
While investors have been putting money into money market funds, they are also taking money out, according to data from the Investment Company Institute, the mutual fund industry's trade association. From June 1 to June 22, assets in money market funds sold to individual investors fell by about $2 billion; analysts note, however, that Americans use money market funds as checking accounts, and often draw on them to make end-of-quarter tax payments.
Investors continue to bail out of some bond funds, though fund executives said the pace of withdrawals slowed in June and some bond funds attracted modest inflows of cash. Fidelity's long-term bond funds lost $100 million; over all, the firm took in $650 million, far less than the net $1 billion it received from investors in May. The decline came not because more people were pulling money out of funds, but because there was a drop in new purchases, Mr. Litvack said.
Stock funds continue to attract investors' dollars, but at a slower pace than in May. For example, at T. Rowe Price in Baltimore, the inflow into equity funds fell almost a quarter in June but remains "pretty good," Steven Norwitz, a spokesman for the firm, said. "People are just holding back."
Investors who use brokers are believed to react more slowly to market moves, but firms that cater to them, including Kemper Financial Services, report modestly lower net sales. At State Street Research, stock fund sales dropped 18 percent and bond fund sales fell 11 percent, but the inflow into money market funds soared 20 percent, a spokeswoman said.
 
Drop in Stock Fund Sales
 Mutual Fund Trim Tabs, a newsletter that surveys a sample of mutual funds, estimates that for the entire mutual fund industry, net sales of stock funds fell sharply in June and international stock funds showed only very small gains. The outflow out of bond funds continued, the newsletter found, but at a more more moderate pace.
In May, equity funds attracted $11.8 billion, even more than April's $11.3 billion inflow, according to data released yesterday by the Investment Company Institute. But much of May's positive activity came from people who move money around within fund families, many of whom frequently switch from fund to fund. Excluding such activity, stock funds attracted $9.9 billion, the lowest level of net sales this year.
Those investors who did choose stock funds went for the gusto. Aggressive growth funds, a category that includes funds that invest in the smallest companies, were the biggest gainers; growth funds, the next most aggressive group, had their best month in more than a year.
Investors pulled $2.2 billion out of bond funds, far less than the $4.8 billion they removed in April. Again, much of that activity came from in-house switching; looking only at new sales and redemptions, fixed-income funds lost about $1.1 billion, the same amount as in April.

HEADLINE: Stocks Mixed in Light Pre-Holiday Trading

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
The buck stops where? And does the question matter at all to the stock market?
Perhaps not this week, as traders head into the long Fourth of July weekend. Moreover, share trades this week, for portfolio performance in the quarter ending today, will not be settled until next week, another incentive for traders to head to the beach. 
 That might account for yesterday's seeming paradox of a continuing slide by the dollar but a mixed performance by leading stock indexes.
Since June 3, the dollar has lost more than 6 percent of its purchasing power against the Japanese yen and about 5 percent against the German mark. The dollar fell yesterday to a new post-World War II low of 98.80 yen. But the dollar rose slightly against the mark, settling at 1.5865 marks, perhaps because of a modest cut in German interest rates.
 
Bentsen Again Defends Dollar

 
 The dollar's slide came despite a continuing defense of the currency by Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen.
Mr. Bentsen said yesterday that he was "disappointed" that a currency intervention on Friday had failed and reiterated that "we believe a stronger dollar is better for our economy and better for the world's economy." His remarks followed his strongly worded declaration on Tuesday that the Clinton Administration was not using the dollar as a trade weapon.
The fate of the dollar is important because a weakening dollar makes dollar-denominated assets like stocks less attractive, particularly to foreigners. For example, a Japanese investor who bought the stocks that make up the Dow Jones industrial average would have seen a return of 4.21 percent during the last 12 months, but would post a loss of 3.25 percent when that investment was converted to yen.
The Dow fell only 2.59 points yesterday, to 3,667.05, while the broader Standard & Poor's 500 index rose 1.56 points, to 447.63. The smaller-company Nasdaq composite index rose 1.96 points to 704.01.
Thin trading volume exaggerates price movements. Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was again below normal, with 264.4 million shares changing hands, or about 10 percent below this year's daily average of 293 million shares.
"As we get closer to the weekend, our audience is going to get more and more sparse," Bill Allyn, director of listed equities trading at Jefferies & Company, said. "Friday should be quiet."
There are other factors at work. Long-term interest rates fell modestly, making stocks, at least for yesterday, a slightly better buy. The yield on the benchmark 30-year bond fell to 7.50 percent from 7.51 percent.
The inflation outlook as reflected in commodities prices eased, as fears receded of drought conditions in the Midwest. The Commodity Research Bureau index of 21 commodities fell 1.02 points, to 229.38, led mainly by falling contract prices for corn, soybean and orange juice.
The price of gold, the refuge of the inflation-jittery, fell. The gold contract for August delivery declined $1.60, to $386.30 an ounce.
 
A Mixed Bag
 Blue-chip stocks like Caterpillar and I.B.M. fell, but telephone companies like BellSouth and regional banks like Nationsbank rose. Over-the-counter stocks like Cisco Systems and Novell rose.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index rose 0.92 point, to 246.72, with advancing stocks outnumbering decliners, 1,251 to 885. The American Stock Exchange market value index rose 0.81 point, to 423.48.
Of the 30 component stocks that make up the Dow industrials, 12 fell, led by Caterpillar, which fell 2, to 102 1/8.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Are Mixed, Though Dollar Is Weak

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities were mixed yesterday, and despite another weak showing by the dollar, the market exhibited surprising resilience.
Trading was thin ahead of the long Fourth of July weekend, and while the dollar slipped to another low against the Japanese yen, the dollar's performance against European currencies may have provided a stabilizing effect on the bond market. Commodity prices were also lower, and that also helped bonds by easing investors' concerns about inflation. 
 The price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 3/32 , to 857/32 , for a yield of 7.50 percent, down from 7.51 percent on Tuesday. Shorter-maturity bills were also mixed, with the rate of a three-month maturity at 4.13 percent, down a basis point, and a six-month maturity at 4.56 percent, up two basis points. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
 
Tightening Not Expected
 "Today's action was mostly with positive bias, but with little activity in terms of the dollar," said Donald Fine, chief market analyst for Chase Securities. He said he did not think the dollar would fall indefinitely, and at some point foreign investors would look at United States securities in terms of currency appreciation in addition to yield.
As for the Federal Reserve, Mr. Fine said, "I do not expect the Fed to tighten at the upcoming Federal Open Market Committee meeting on Tuesday and Wednesday because it would give the impression that monetary policy is linked to dollar exchange rates."
He added that the Fed's primary concerns were inflation and economic growth, and the Open Market Committee would have ample opportunity to lift the funds rate before its next meeting on Aug. 16.
In early trading, prices were in negative territory. But they soon showed some resilience, possibly helped by position-squaring ahead of the holiday weekend and investors' concerns about central bank intervention in the foreign exchange markets that might catch them leaning too far the wrong way.
Concern that inflation would heat up in the near future was fueled by the continued battering of the dollar and the effect that would have on the United States economy.
 
Events in Japan
 One negative factor influencing trading yesterday was news that Japan's lower house had voted for a Socialist candidate, Tomiichi Murayama, to be prime minister. Market analysts said that should insure prolonged political instability in Japan and a long delay in the resumption of trade talks between the United States and Japan.
In its second and final upward revision, the Commerce Department said yesterday that the United States economy grew at a stronger-than-expected rate in the first quarter. The gross domestic product was revised upward, to 3.4 percent from 3 percent, and from the initial April report of 2.6 percent. Last month, the G.D.P. was revised to 3 percent.
Traders said that even though the pace of growth was stronger than expected, it still put economic expansion at less than half the 7 percent annualized rate of the fourth quarter of 1993. Traders said the strong growth number was overshadowed by the dollar as a factor influencing the market.
Some market analysts said the revisions of the first-quarter G.D.P. would not alter market estimates of a gain of 3 to 4 percent in the G.D.P. in the second quarter. And while consumer spending posted a healthy gain in the first quarter, a moderation in that spending is expected in the second quarter and will depress sales, they say.
Treasuries, which have also tracked with commodity prices lately and were pushed sharply higher this week as a result, found some solace in the Commodity Research Bureau index yesterday, as the basket of 21 commodity prices declined. The C.R.B. index fell 1.02 point, to 229.38.
 
Municipal Bond Issues
 The day's largest offering in the municipal bond market was a $370 million debt issue by the Metropolitan Transportation Authority of New York, negotiated through underwriters led by CS First Boston.
The underwriters said most of the issue, which is divided into two series, was insured by the Municipal Bond Investor's Assurance Corporation and rated Triple-A by Moody's Investors Service and Standard & Poor's. The bonds are priced to yield a maximum of about 6.386 percent for an issue due in 2024.
The maximum yield for an uninsured bond due in 2024 is 6.60 percent. The uninsured bonds are rated Baa-1 by Moody's and BBB+ by S.& P.
A $206 million joint refunding by the Dallas-Fort Worth International Airport was initially priced by Merrill Lynch to yield a maximum 6.30 percent for a bond due in 2012. All the bonds in the financing are insured by Municipal Bond Investor's Assurance and rated triple-A by the rating agencies.
