HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
The U.S. shouldn't be too proud to imitate others' technology.

BYLINE: By Peter Passell 

 BODY:
"PRODUCTIVITY isn't everything," Paul Krugman, an economist at Stanford University, is fond of saying, "but in the long run it is almost everything." And long after the current fuss over the exchange value of the dollar is a distant memory, policy makers will still be worrying about the fundamentals that drive American living standards.
President Clinton apparently got the point, sweeping into office clutching reams of proposals for jump-starting the technological change thought to lead to faster productivity growth. 
 But William Baumol, the director of the C.V. Starr Center for Applied Economics at New York University, suspects that the policy establishment is ignoring the cheapest and least intrusive (and therefore least controversial) role for government in the process. "Imitation," he argues, "is more important than innovation." Hence, he says, Washington should be doing what the Japanese have never been too proud to do: Make it as easy as possible for business to borrow foreign technology.
It has become a cliche to bemoan American industry's failure to capture the benefits of innovation. Who, after all, has not heard the sad story of how the videotape recorder was invented in the land of the Stars and Stripes, and then commercially developed by the enterprising Japanese.
Mr. Baumol does not dispute the video recorder story -- or, for that matter, Japan's legendary agility in using borrowed technology to carve out markets in color film, television sets, autos and quartz watches. But he turns the standard conclusion on its head: If you can't lick 'em, join 'em. Imitators (including American imitators), he suggests, have always had an advantage over the leaders in technology.
While mass-produced steel, jet aircraft and penicillin were all British inventions, German and American companies dominated their commercialization, and relatively little of the profit has ended up in British pockets. And a variety of more systematic studies, he reports, confirm that the rate of return on investments in borrowed foreign technology is considerably higher than the return on home-grown innovation.
The moral, Mr. Baumol argues, in his new book "Entrepreneurship, Management and the Structure of Payoffs" (MIT Press), is not to stop the generation of new technology or to work harder to slow the export of American inventions, but to get better at imitation. And while much of the responsibility ought to fall on the corporations that would profit directly, he believes there is a legitimate -- and cost-effective -- role here for Washington.
One easy initiative would be to finance fellowships and language training for students to study engineering and other applied sciences abroad, just as South Korea, China and a host of other countries send students to America. Another would be to subsidize the immigration of technical workers. Sponsoring businesses or universities would be obliged to make the case that the immigrants were bringing specialized skills.
Yet another would be to give American embassies greater capacity (short of industrial espionage) to assess foreign technology. Mr. Baumol suggests that they might also serve as agents in technology licensing agreements or prepare newsletters that would give businesses back home early knowledge of what was happening, and where.
An obvious question is why Washington should take the initiative when the American companies that stand to gain haven't bothered. Larger corporations (notably multinationals) do, of course, invest in the transfer of technology. But Mr. Baumol notes that left to the market one would expect systematic underinvestment in this sort of activity. A good deal of technological knowledge is simply too abstract to be patented or embodied in individual workers who have the right to change employers, and thus the benefits are not likely to be captured by a single investor.
Martin Baily, a fellow at the McKinsey Global Institute in Washington and longtime advocate of tax breaks for research and development, is not entirely convinced. While he acknowledges that some technology is indeed a "public good" whose benefits flow easily beyond the borders of the individual enterprise, he is skeptical that government would be good at figuring out what sort of knowledge is worth importing and what isn't.
More broadly, he suspects that the availability of advanced technology plays a smaller role in productivity growth than is generally believed. "The real issue," Mr. Baily argues, is changing the regulatory environment and the economic culture of business to encourage best practices."
Arguably, the most intriguing aspect of the Baumol approach is how cheap it would be. It is hard to imagine how even the most determined bureaucracy could spend more than a few hundred million dollars a year on the sorts of programs he envisions -- peanuts compared with magnetic levitating trains, defense industry conversion and a host of other projects being promoted as answers to the productivity doldrums.
Indeed, investing in technology transfer might even save the public money if it absorbed even a fraction of the attention of the industrial planners and high-technology lobbies lurking on the fringes of the Clinton Administration.

HEADLINE: Where Job Hunters Hit the Jackpot

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: SIOUX FALLS, S.D.

 BODY:
With pay bounding ever higher and jobs so plentiful that the local employment benefits office is often deserted, this Plains-hugging city of 105,000 is the closest thing in America to a place where it's the job seeker who says "Don't call me, I'll call you."
With unemployment in Sioux Falls the lowest in the nation -- 1.9 percent in May, according to the latest figures from the Labor Department, and possibly less, compared with 6 percent nationwide -- Karen White, personnel officer at Hutchinson Technology Inc., has had to get used to interviewing prospects for the company's computer-parts assembly line at 6 A.M., before they go off to work. 
 And Scott Simpson, owner of Sun Masters, which tints glass in cars and homes, says he has more business than he can handle and would open another shop if he could find people to work in it.
"I've got to go all over the place to get people -- and then I've got to give them an arm and a leg," Mr. Simpson complained. "They're very demanding."
At a time when policy makers as well as professors -- to say nothing of the Federal Reserve -- are looking warily at the possible negatives of economic strength, South Dakota's biggest city provides a laboratory of sorts in which to test what economic theory predicts of tight labor markets.
Foremost is the traditional trade-off between joblessness and inflation. Workers account for about 70 percent of the typical company's costs, and estimates of how low the nation's unemployment rate can go without stoking inflation range between 5 percent and 7 percent.
But a two-day visit this month showed clearly that pay is not the only thing getting more costly here. The main effect, not surprisingly, is on prices that are locally set, from real estate to haircuts. The leading complaint involves rents, which average about $370 to $750 a month for a two-bedroom apartment, the most popular size; last August, such units went for $350 to $600 a month. "I'd say rents are up about 7 percent over all from last year," said Jeffrey Iverson at the Rental Connection, a locating and property management service. This is triple the national rate of consumer price inflation.
According to Jim Honermann, owner of Barber Harbor, a large emporium on the 41st Street shopping strip, "Everything is going pretty fast and furious here." The basic men's haircut at Barber Harbor rose $1 last year, to $12, and may soon rise again, he said, to "at least $13."
With its its jobless rate down to the so-called frictional pool, composed mainly of people between jobs or new to the labor force, Sioux Falls also fulfills numerous other predictions about the effects of labor shortages. Among these are less qualified job applicants, high turnover, vacancies that on occasion cause work to be contracted out to other areas and intense competition that requires frequent sweetening of pay and benefits.
Business and government people here say employers would be finding things even tougher if the city were unable to draw workers from a region of small towns and farms that is in economic decline.
While blue-collar workers are among the hardest to find, service-sector operations like Citibank's huge credit-card processing complex are also pinched, providing academics and policy makers with another situation with test tube potential. Both are newly interested in the wage relationships of people with different skill levels and in ways of gauging the increased mobility and flexibility of the American work force.
 
 
'One Occupation at a Time'
 "Increasingly, wage levels are set with an eye to the competitive rates found in the local labor market, one occupation at a time," Audrey Freedman, a New York consultant, recently told a House panel examining the distribution of incomes.
In Sioux Falls, though welders and skilled construction workers are in particularly short supply, the fiercest local job battles tend to be fought over the relatively unskilled.
"We have raised our entry-level wage three times in the last year and a half," said Sandy Stoltz, personnel director for Carlisle Plastics, a producer of food and yard bags for Sears and other major retailers. Most recent was the May jump of 50 cents an hour that lifted starting pay to $6.60, with an extra 40 cents for the night shift.
At Bell Paper Box Inc., the larger of only two suppliers of priority-mail envelopes to the United States Postal Service, Mark S. Graham, the president, says that 80 percent to 90 percent of new hires leave before working three months.
 
 
'I'm Out of Here'
 "We need to bring in three or four people because you're only going to have one of them left," Mr. Graham said. When something displeases them, he added, "they just say 'I'm out of here' because they can go down the street" and do better.
Sioux Falls, named for a series of red-rock cascades on the Big Sioux River and boasting the nation's biggest livestock market -- more than 1.1 million hogs, cattle and sheep are expected to be auctioned this year -- is not alone in finding labor an increasingly scarce commodity. To varying degrees, the same pressures are showing up in other metropolitan areas with exceptionally low unemployment rates, a group heavily concentrated in the Midwest, in many cases cities with major universities. "These places historically have had low rates," partly because of a broad mix of recession-resistant business and industry, David Smith, an economist at the Bureau of Labor Statistics, said.
And then there are places like Sheboygan, Wis. -- a city whose 3.1 percent unemployment rate places it below the top 10 -- where strong demand for production workers has inspired efforts to import them. A current target is the 2,000 Briggs & Stratton Corporation employees in the Milwaukee area, 50 miles to the south, who were recently told they would be laid off over the next few years as the company shifts some production to lower-cost areas.
 
 
Hospitable to Enterprise
 Sioux Falls, which like much of the central United States, suffered from the agricultural and energy recession of the 1980's but was largely unaffected by the 1990-91 downturn, is a virtual boom town. It is one of the relatively few places in United States where the factory work force is actually growing. But the a boom is based not on oil or entertainment projects or mega-dollar government projects but mainly on being hospitable to enterprise -- no corporate or personal income tax -- and its manageable size.
Indeed, the city's tiny minority population -- Sioux Falls is 96.8 percent white, mainly of Scandinavian and German extraction -- has benefited from the intense demand for workers, pulling American Indians and blacks, some with checkered employment histories, into relatively well-paying jobs.
Among its other advantages are low-cost electricity and a midcontinent location that saves money for telecommunications businesses.
Virtually everyone here cites the effect of glowing articles in Money magazine, which in 1992 ranked Sioux Falls first on its list of livable locales. Though losing this honor last year, to Rochester, Minn., Sioux Falls still boasted the top-ranked economy.
But as fast as newcomers arrive, many from neighboring Minnesota, where the tax burden is significantly higher, the Sioux Falls job market bids for them -- whether in financial or medical services or at such manufacturers as Hutchinson, which makes suspension assemblies for disk drives, or Aerostar International Inc., a maker of various types of balloons, including nearly all new balloons over the last 10 years for Macy's Thanksgiving Day parade in New York.
 
 
Minimum Wage
 At John Morrell & Company, whose 2,800-worker packing plant has been a pillar of the Sioux Falls economy for 85 years, even $9 an hour to start is not enough to keep the ranks full. Hutchinson's $1-an-hour raise, a 16 percent increase in starting pay, for all its production workers last October forced many other employers to follow. Greg Peterson, manager of the local Job Service office, said that not even high school students could be hired here for the minimum wage of $4.25 an hour; they get at least $4.75.
Another effect of the tight labor market is to force employers to recruit more imaginatively and over ever-greater distances, luring many workers into daily treks of 100 miles or more and threatening the city's 12-minute average commute. Many applicants are already employed, leading to off-hours interviews as people "take a shot," as Mr. Peterson put it, at the most desirable jobs with such regional or national employers as Federal Express, Northern States Power or Burlington Northern.
South Dakotans who had decided to move to Sioux Falls had produced "an uneasy marriage," said Lieut. Gov. Steve Kirby, who, like many officials in smaller states with part-time legislatures, remains active in business -- in his case a venture capital company. He said depopulated rural areas consoled themselves with the "spring of money" that taxes from Sioux Falls provided for the programs of state and local governments.
 
 
National Job Bank
 Robert G. Stanek, president of the SMC Corporation, said that despite raising wages twice so far this year,his machinery company was short 20 hands, workers he said he would be willing to train free and who could make it unnecessary to contract out some production to nearby parts of Minnesota, Iowa and Nebraska.
Perhaps the best thing policy makers could do, Mr. Stanek suggested, would be to create a national job bank that could link employers here with the legions of motivated but marginally skilled unemployed in other parts of the country, particularly in urban centers.
"These people," Mr. Stanek said, "are not finding their way to Sioux Falls."

HEADLINE: Philharmonic's Deficit Persists Despite New Regime's Gains

BYLINE: By DIANA JEAN SCHEMO 

 BODY:
In the nearly three years since Kurt Masur and Deborah Borda took over the New York Philharmonic, they have been hailed as the team to cure an ailing orchestra. As music director, Mr. Masur has thrown open the orchestra's doors for public discussions, open rehearsals and radio broadcasts. Ms. Borda, the executive director, has tried to lure untapped audiences by scheduling cheaper, shorter concerts at unusual times and festivals to highlight specific themes in music. Together, they have mended ties with a fractious, once-demoralized orchestra, winning critical acclaim for restoring its musical excellence.
Despite this string of successes, however, the Philharmonic has yet to shake the fiscal demons that have been haunting it, and other orchestras, in recent years. A deficit that first surfaced in 1990 will not disappear this year, as predicted. Rather, after dipping to $500,000, it is expected to surge past the $1 million mark again next year, suggesting that for all the cushions of a healthy endowment and well-placed friends, New York's premier orchestra is not immune to the trials confronting orchestras across America, and must invent new strategies for survival. 
 Indeed, by some measures, the fiscal strains on the New York Philharmonic appear greater than those of the other members of the country's so-called Big Five orchestras: Boston, Chicago, Cleveland and Philadelphia. By next year, the sum of the Philharmonic's deficits over the last six years, $7.6 million, is expected to exceed those of its sister orchestras over the last 10 years. Each of the Philharmonic's shortfalls is covered by withdrawals from the endowment beyond the regular 5.5 percent annual transfer, the amount its board officially deems sufficient to cover operating expenses and insure the endowment's growth.
 
Deficits 'Not a Problem'
 Officials at the Philharmonic do not appear troubled by the deficits, and point to the steady rise of the endowment to $100 million from $68 million in 1990 as a sign of the orchestra's fiscal health. "It's a new era in that there are financial pressures on the institution," said Edward Sermier, the chief financial officer, "but now that they're understood, they're not a problem." .
He said that a $1 million deficit, given the endowment's size, was "manageable," and could be virtually eliminated by dropping activities peripheral to the Philharmonic's mission, like the summer parks and Rush Hour concerts, educational programs, lectures and extra rehearsals, though the orchestra has no plans to do so.
"The institution has improved its artistic quality enough that the board is willing to accept a small deficit without cutting noncore programs," Mr. Sermier said.
The difficulties confronting the Philharmonic and other American orchestras were first documented in a 1992 report by the American Symphony Orchestra League. The report found that virtually all the costs associated with staging classical concerts had risen faster than the rate of inflation, from musician salaries to administration to promotion, while audiences, for the first time in history, were declining. A second report the following year went so far as to suggest that orchestras woo audiences with jazz and pop music, recruit more black and Hispanic musicians by dropping traditional blind auditions, and try incorporating videoscreens and special lighting in concerts.
 
Conclusions Protested
 The report touched off a firestorm of protest among some orchestra executives, who said that in its worry over the financial condition of orchestras, the league was forgetting their very raison d'etre: presenting classical music of a European tradition.
While the solution was in dispute, however, the problem was not. "It's a societal change we're dealing with," said Ms. Borda. "People used to purchase tickets for 24 Saturday nights with the Philharmonic. I don't know anybody who plans for 24 Saturday nights of anything anymore."
Joseph Kluger, president of the Philadelphia Orchestra, said: "Today, if you can get that same individual to buy six concerts, you're doing very well. So you need more people to get the same revenue base."
Yet even consistently full houses are no longer enough to keep an orchestra in the black. Four years ago, the Philharmonic's ticket sales and royalties of $13.1 million covered 56.4 percent of the orchestra's $23.1 million in expenses. Last season, with more than 96 percent of the seats at Avery Fisher Hall sold, revenues of $14.8 million covered only 50.5 percent of the $29.2 million budget, according to the Philharmonic's financial reports.
"You do max out in terms of what you can generate in earned revenue," said Lois Cohn, marketing and public relations director for the Philharmonic. Next year, there will be an overall 5.7 percent increase in subscription ticket prices; some single-ticket prices will also rise, with orchestra seats for regular concerts running $45.
 
A Strategic Decision
 The stewards of the Philharmonic appear to have made a strategic decision. Rather than muddle through in the hope that a general economic upswing will eventually carry the Philharmonic along, they took drastic steps to improve its quality and heighten its presence. Last year, the Philharmonic reined in, even slightly decreased, administrative costs, while increasing musician salaries and touring by some $4 million. It also increased rehearsal time, and almost doubled the amount of money spent on promotion, from $1.65 million in 1990 to $3.2 million.
The Philharmonic's 106 musicians earn minimum weekly salaries of $1,300, with Glenn Dicterow, the concertmaster, and some principal players collecting significantly more. After an $80-a-week increase in salaries next September, the pay scale will surpass that of the highest-paying orchestra in the country, the Chicago Symphony, which is about to enter contract negotiations. Mr. Masur's $1.12 million annual salary and Ms. Borda's $280,000 are also among the tops in the field.
To some extent, the new policies are beginning to show results, judging by figures in annual reports. Last year, amid the newly won critical acclaim and the lavish 150th-anniversary celebrations, the Philharmonic saw gifts, grants and bequests soar to $10.5 million from $6.4 million in 1992. Paid attendance also rose to 406,739 from 380,214. But the increases were not enough to reverse the deficits and eliminate the need for extra withdrawals from the endowment.
Tom Bacchetti, the former executive director of the Atlanta Symphony who now acts as a consultant to orchestras and performing arts organizations, called the string of Philharmonic deficits "worrisome." Contributors give to the endowment fund so it will serve as an enduring source of income, he noted. Compensating for shortfalls by dipping into the endowment is "a dangerous course" that will "erode its buying power," he said.
"You're just buying trouble down the road," Mr. Bacchetti said. "It tends to diminish the confidence of contributors to the endowment fund."
And overlooking several years of deficits, he warned, ignores the heart of the problem: an unhealthy ratio of revenue to expenses. Because of dwindling grants from both the government and private foundations, Mr. Bacchetti advises his clients that to insure solvency, they should earn 60 percent of their operating budgets.
 
'Really Investiments'
 But Stephen Stamas, chairman of the Philharmonic, said he was not worried by the deficits. He said that some of them were "really investments, because we wanted Kurt Masur and the orchestra to start out in the best possible way."
Ms. Borda, who is 44, works hard to project an upbeat mood, and describes the initiatives the orchestra has taken to reach a wider audience as long-term investments without regard to finances. Responding to the 1992 American Symphony Orchestra League report with notable candor, Ms. Borda said that the initiatives had, paradoxically, cost more money and would not pay off "on an immediate financial basis."
"The truth is that like other American orchestras, we were losing ground and running deficits anyway," she wrote. "Instead, we will try to turn those 'losses' into investments and pray that we made the right ones. What is clear to me is that we can't keep doing 'business as usual.' "
The Philharmonic has replaced some regular subscription concerts with lower-priced Rush Hour and Saturday matinee concerts intended to draw commuters and young professionals. The informal, hourlong concerts present the Philharmonic's subscription program for the week without intermission, and feature Mr. Masur and Ms. Borda fielding audience questions about the music and the orchestra.
At the first such concert, Ms. Borda recalled, briefcases leaned like bookends along every row. Mr. Masur asked the audience how many had never been to the Philharmonic before. About half raised their hands.
"There was an audible gasp -- not from the audience, but from us in the Philharmonic," Ms. Borda recalled.
 
Innovations Praised
 Mr. Bacchetti, who praised Ms. Borda's innovations while saying "the jury's still out" as to their effectiveness, noted that in some ways her job was tougher here than it would be in most other cities, because of the plethora of choices concertgoers face.
The Casual Saturdays, shorter concerts at lower prices, are proving popular among families with children, and the elderly. This season, the Philharmonic will add chamber music and raise subscriptions to $99 from $81 for orchestra seats at the full three-concert series, said Ms. Cohn.
Mr. Masur, 67, has also restored the conductor as a presence in the life of the city. His radio shows, audience chats, children's concerts and open rehearsals are all geared toward making the orchestra more approachable, both intellectually and socially.
These innovations are not, strictly speaking, short-term investments whose success can be measured in a glance at the bottom line the following year. Some of the steps are really an individual conductor's stamp on a city, and some may take years to demonstrate their worth. But it is perhaps a measure of the uncertainty of orchestras these days that they must each watch the moves their counterparts are making, and seize what might seem to be a movable success.
"Orchestras are experimenting," said Tom Hallett, chief financial officer of the Chicago Symphony, which is considering adopting a rush-hour concert series of its own. "That's what you're seeing."

HEADLINE: Buying Air-Conditioners: An Environmental Guide

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

 BODY:
A SHOPPER went to a discount store, Tops Appliance City, in East Hanover, N.J., earlier this month to look for a window air-conditioner. He made a tentative selection, a $700 model, and then asked if the machine used Freon, the Du Pont trade name that is commonly used to refer to chlorofluorocarbons, or CFC's, the chemicals blamed for eating a hole in the ozone layer.
Yes, the salesman said, and the machines are notorious for leaking, he added, so buy our service contract, too, for $149. 
 Well, said the shopper, do you have any that don't use Freon?
Oh, sir, said the salesman, you wouldn't want one of those, because the replacement refrigerant corrodes the machine's innards. Buy the service contract instead, he said again.
What's an environmentally conscious shopper to do?
First, stop listening to the salesman.
"The salesman is flat-out wrong" about the refrigerant used in air-conditioners, said T. R. Reid, a spokesman for the Whirlpool Corporation in Benton Harbor, Mich., which produces window units under the Whirlpool, Kitchenaid, Roper and Kenmore brand names.
(On Monday, the Tops store manager said someone from company headquarters would respond. No one did.)
No window units use CFC's like Freon. Instead, they use a compound called R-22, part of a class of chemicals called HCFC's. HCFC's do some ozone damage, but at the moment, no one markets a completely ozone-friendly window air-conditioner, so the only alternative is to swelter.
With a warm spring and a reasonably strong economy, the industry expects to sell more than three million window air-conditioners this year, all using R-22, and nearly all of that refrigerant will eventually be vented into the atmosphere, either because the machines leak or because they break down and are dumped in landfills.
For now, buy whatever you can find in the stores, because window air-conditioners are not prone to leak, at least not in the first decade or so.
Window air-conditioners are not as big a problem as cars. The 1994 cars and many of the 1993's use a new refrigerant called R-134a, which does not hurt the ozone layer; older models use R-12, which persists in the atmosphere for decades and breaks down stratospheric ozone molecules that protect life on the earth's surface from the sun's ultraviolet rays.
Car air-conditioners are prone to leak: they are powered by a belt from the engine, which spins a shaft, and the seal around the shaft gradually breaks down. In addition, car air-conditioners must be free to bounce and vibrate, so they use hoses instead of pipes, and the hoses leak, too. But production of R-12 will be banned after this year, and repairing air-conditioners, expensive now, will become harder and harder.
Window air-conditioners are more durable because the motors and spinning shafts are inside a sealed box. Most have a life span of 12 to 15 years.
But the refrigerant they use, R-22, is scheduled to be phased out, too. Almost all production will stop in 2020. According to the air-conditioning industry, R-22 does only 5 percent as much damage as R-12.
Not everyone agrees that R-22 is acceptable even in the short-term. John W. Passacantando, the executive director of Ozone Action, a Washington environmental group, said that in the next few decades, R-22 will have an impact much larger than 5 percent, maybe 20 or 25 percent.
The 5 percent figure, he said, was based on damage over the next 200 years, but the most intense period of ozone destruction will occur much sooner, in the next century. Industry executives do not agree, but they anticipate that the current phase-out for R-22 will be accelerated. Thus they all have replacements on the drawing boards or ready to mass-produce.
The problem is that an air-conditioner using R-134a, if it were the same size as current units, would be 30 percent less efficient, said A. Bruce Buchholz, the vice president for window air-conditioners at the Carrier Corporation in Syracuse. At the same time, the United States Department of Energy is proposing regulations to make all air-conditioners more efficient. Manufacturers can make window units more efficient by adding more coils, but that makes them bigger, noisier and costlier.
So the industry, along with the chemical companies that make the refrigerants, is still looking for a better substitute than R-134a.
Companies that make dehumidifiers, which have traditionally used R-12, are switching to R-22 because it won't be banned until 2020, said Robert J. Laurent, a spokesman for the Fedders Corporation in Peapack, N.J.
Carrier, a United Technologies subsidiary, has built a window unit that runs on R-134a and is ready to mass-produce it, but for the European market. "In Europe a room air-conditioner sells for double what it does in the U.S.," Mr. Buchholz said. "There's a margin to work with there."
Whole-house air conditioners also run on R-22, but Carrier introduced a model earlier this year that runs on R-134a. Size is generally not a consideration in whole-house models, because the machinery is outdoors.
Buying a whole-house unit that runs on R-134a would be better for the environment, but people who buy units that use R-22 will probably be able to find refrigerant for as long as the air-conditioners last, at least under the current phase-out schedule. That puts a ceiling on production beginning in 1996 and cuts production to 65 percent by 2004, to 35 percent in 2010 and to 10 percent in 2015.

HEADLINE: Shotgun Wedding in Japan

 BODY:
Only a political party system in its death agony could produce a government like the one Japan's Parliament chose yesterday. In a desperation pact that could turn into a kind of slow mutual suicide, Tokyo's two largest traditional parties, the Liberal Democrats and the Socialists, joined forces to elect Tomiichi Murayama as Japan's first Socialist Prime Minister since the 1940's.
Mr. Murayama represents the left wing of a leftist party, but he now must depend on right-wing remnants of Japan's main rightist party, the misleadingly named Liberal Democrats. It is a prescription for domestic and foreign policy paralysis and a serious blow to Clinton Administration hopes for cooperation on Korea and trade. 
 Yet this unnatural alliance will probably accelerate Japan's political realignment, hastening the day of effective, responsive government. Discontented members of both parties are already seeking a new political home. An ineffective coalition could alienate voters and strengthen the forces of reform.
The new partnership reflects the three-way breakdown of Japan's present Parliament, the first since 1955 not dominated by the Liberal Democrats. With neither Liberal Democrats, Socialists nor the reform coalition originally put together by Morihiro Hosokawa holding a majority, exotic coalitions uniting parties with little in common are inevitable.
But it would be hard to imagine a more surrealistic arrangement than this Liberal Democratic-Socialist alliance. The Liberal Democrats' main claim to business and American support had always been their ability to keep the Socialists out of power. The Socialists' only consistent principle for the past 40 years has been to oppose the Liberal Democrats.
Both elements of the new ruling tandem are in decline. The Liberal Democrats have been reduced to a collection of factions whose loyalties shift on a day-to-day basis. Two of those factions bolted ranks before the Murayama vote, strengthening the reform alliance that now becomes the main opposition. And the Socialists lost half their seats in last July's parliamentary elections, a fair verdict on their inability to adjust to changing times.
Both are likely to lose even more ground after more representative electoral districts are drawn later this year. Unsurprisingly, Mr. Murayama has suggested he may dissolve Parliament soon to permit one more race before redistricting.
For now, the old-style politicians most threatened by the past year's political upheavals are enjoying a sweet revenge. Yet unless the Socialists and Liberal Democrats begin responding to an electorate that is fed up with corruption, a stagnant economy and high prices, the future of these uninspiring veterans looks anything but bright.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Essay;
Kneejerks for July

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
For those who base their opinions on principled disagreement with everything that appears in this space, here is a set of kneejerks for July to cope with events to come while I am on vacation.
1. Leon Panacea Presents the New, Organized, Pragmatic, Nonfrenetic, Clinton White House. Do not be suckered: Although this annual summer shuffle will be heralded as the most fundamental shift in governance since the importation of Superpragmatist David Gergen last summer, the sense of shake-up will last only until Leon attempts to wrest power from the real chief of staff. 
 Mack McLarty, who went to kindergarten with Clinton, is taking their old sandbox into the Oval Office's bunker of broken dreams. Meanwhile, over at State:
2. Vorpal Blade Goes Snickersnack. The new Foggy Bottom triumvirate of Talbott, Holbrooke and Gergen will praise Warren Christopher and Tony Lake with such vehemence that the cynical media will assume a coup is in the making. Ignore such hall-of-mirrors analysis; Chris and Tony are as solidly ensconced as Mack was.
3. Whitewater Hearings Begin in the House Banking Committee on July 26. If Chairman Henry Gonzalez, protective Democrat, bangs his gavel on Republican prober Jim Leach's head, denounce the Congressional cover-up. If a judicial panel rubber-stamps the appointment of anti-oversight Robert Fiske as Independent Counsel, castigate the pusillanimous Court of Appeals.
4. Clinton Lays Down Freedom's Marker in the Baltics. The President will speak at Riga, Latvia's Freedom Monument next week, at the spot where the Soviet Union began to come apart. His lofty theme will be the new nature of post-cold war courage. If he makes clear that Eastern European nations are on their way to full NATO membership, hail a historic address; if he fudges to avoid offending Yeltsin, deride his straddle and decry his missed opportunity.
5. Democrats Try to Portray Republicans as Captive of Christian Right's Lunatic Fringe. The Democratic campaign chairman, Vic Fazio, will step up the effort to scare Jews out of the Republican Party by bashing the evangelical right. The G.O.P. should point to Fazio's refusal, for fear of offending the Demo black caucus, to join the House vote condemning Khalid Abdul Muhammad's bigoted "bloodsucker" speech. Beware of hardball-playing pols masquerading as libertarians while pumping up a "radical" villain.
6. Wars Ain't Over Till They're Over. The West will lean on Muslims and Croats to legitimize Serbian conquest of half of Bosnia, and will denounce aggression's victims when they don't. The fairest deal will be struck when forces are equal and tired; get the U.N. out, arms to Muslims in.
7. Double Helix Solves Double Murder. If DNA's "superfingerprints" put O. J. Simpson at the scene of the crime, defense counsel's strategy could shift to attack on ex-wife as maddening slut. Stop treating this merely as a true-life whodunit, but focus on the need not only to find the perpetrator but to avenge the victims. Exhort vast audience to root for justice, not for the dramatic protagonist or to marvel at competing counsel.
8. Yen for a Penny. If the dollar drifts down another 5 percent or so, yawn; but if, after the G-7 meeting in Naples, the dollar keeps slipping without a bottom in sight, inflation and higher interest rates are a-comin' and it would be prudent to indulge in a bit of panic.
9. Apostles of Haiti. July is a customary month for hitting the beach. If Clinton wants to look strong to his liberal constituency, he will send the Marines into Haiti; if he wants to look smart as well as tough to conservatives, he will send in a force led by secretly-trained Haitians. Cautiously salute the former; throw hats in the air at the latter; hoot at continued water-treading.
10. Beltway Wisdom Confounded on Health Insurance. On the Senate floor, Dole's "four no's" will prevail: no price controls, no mandates, no triggers, no taxes. Instead, we'll see more coverage despite pre-existing conditions, which "cannot be taken away," with insurance subsidies for the poor. Credit Clinton for starting it, Dole for stopping it at the right point, and -- on July 4 -- the glorious American system for lurching forward sensibly.

HEADLINE: A New Mythology Grows Up Around Wolves;
Defend Hunters?

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Protecting the Targets of Protest: A Debate Now Shifts to Hunting" (front page, June 11) appears to imply that the right of sport hunters to kill animals for pleasure has as much need for protection as a woman's right to control her own body and reproductive life. 
 This might be defensible if the hunters were native Americans or others who, for cultural or economic reasons, hunt for subsistence. But I got the sense that the proposed hunter protection bill would protect primarily recreational hunting and impose more stringent prohibitions on opponents of hunting than those enacted against anti-abortion activists.
I am not an animal rights activist; I feel that this movement has moved beyond the rational on some subjects. As a child, I accompanied my father when he went hunting, which we did for food. Yet I vividly remember one occasion when we came upon a deer carcass that had been shot and left to rot. Only the head had been taken as a trophy. My father was moved to tears of anger at the sight of such waste.
I agree that the rights of humans supersede those of other species in most cases, and that hunting is a practical solution to animal overpopulation. But silencing dissent seems a drastic solution to the apparent sensitivity of some hunters to criticism.
As an anthropologist I can't help wonder if the key to this extreme legislative response lies in the cultural background of sport hunting. Hunting has traditionally been a male activity. Furthermore, the European societies from which mainstream American culture is derived have depended on domesticated animals for food for millenniums. Hunting in these cultures was traditionally a sport of the upper classes.
Could it be that the people in charge (who are mostly upper-class males) feel the need to impose stricter sanctions protecting their own leisure activities than women's reproductive rights?
 
LINDA M. VAN BLERKOM
Assistant Professor of Anthropology
Drew University
Madison, N.J., June 18, 1994

SECTION: Section A; Page 10; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 1393 words

HEADLINE: TURNOVER IN JAPAN: A Startling Choice;
Socialist's Leap to the Top in Japan Endangers Painfully Won Measures

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, June 29

 BODY:
When the Socialist Party leader, Tomiichi Murayama, was asked by reporters a few days ago about rumors that he was a candidate to become the country's next Prime Minister, the plain-spoken 70-year-old politician replied, "Which country are you talking about?"
 Tonight, the Japanese people might be asking the same question, as the political and business worlds were thrown into disarray with Mr. Murayama's election as the first Socialist Prime Minister in 46 years. 
I can't believe it, I can't believe it," groaned Keiichi Nagamatsu, an official of Keidanren, the most powerful business lobbying organization, who said the election would make a mockery of Japanese politics in the eyes of the world.
 "We are ashamed," he said.
 
A Compromise Choice
 Amid the political upheaval, there were fears that Mr. Murayama's government could slow or reverse the opening of Japan's markets, the deregulation of its economy, and moves toward reform of the corruption-ridden electoral system. Trade talks with the United States also look likely to stall.
 In addition, income tax cuts to stimulate the economy, long supported by the United States on the theory that economic growth would draw in more American imports, might be delayed. And there are questions about whether Japan will cooperate with the United States in imposing sanctions on North Korea over its nuclear weapon program.
 It is not that Mr. Murayama, a fisherman's son and onetime labor union officer, is disliked. He is regarded as an amiable, honest and down-to-earth grandfatherly figure.
 But he was virtually unknown to the public as recently as a year ago, and even now is more famous for his distinctive long gray eyebrows than for his policies. He is an undistinguished legislator who floated to the top of the political world not by being a dynamic leader, but by being everyone's compromise choice -- first when he was elected chairman of the Socialist Party last September and then when he was chosen Prime Minister tonight.
 He has no experience as a Cabinet member at a time when Japan is facing a host of difficult problems, including a slumping economy, a soaring yen and the need to conclude crucial trade talks with the United States.
 What is even more appalling to some is that Mr. Murayama was elected by a marriage of convenience between the Socialists and their archrivals, the conservative Liberal Democrats, whose policies are often diametrically opposed. It is a marriage based solely on the desire for power.
 "It is without any kind of philosophy or rationality," said Ikuo Kabashima, a professor of political science at the University of Tsukuba. Haruo Shimada, an economist at Keio University, agreed. "If you check 10 people on the street, 8 of the 10 are angry, disappointed."
 The wide differences between the Socialists and Liberal Democrats mean that the ruling coalition is likely to be fractious and fragile and probably short-lived, with paralysis in the meantime. And business leaders here worry it will present a poor picture of Japan to the world.
 Mr. Murayama, who has virtually no experience in foreign policy, will appear on the world stage for the first time when he represents Japan at the summit meeting of industrialized nations at the end of next week in Naples and meets President Clinton in a separate meeting while he is there.
 The election of the first Socialist Prime Minister in decades could slow or reverse the movement Japan has been making in reforming its electoral system, opening its markets to foreign products and removing onerous economic regulations. Trade Talks Endangered
 Trade negotiations with the United States could also be slowed, either because of policy paralysis or because the Socialists have little experience in such matters. Agreements to open Japan's markets to foreign insurance policies, telecommunications and medical equipment and automobiles by the Naples meeting are now virtually out of the question
 The Socialists are likely to oppose any sanctions against North Korea, making it harder for the United States to maneuver.
 The new government might also delay income tax cuts that are sought by businesses here and by the United States as a way of stimulating Japan's economy. The Socialists oppose increasing the sales tax to compensate for lost revenue from the income tax cut. But the powerful Finance Ministry is insisting on a sales tax increase.
 The business community is fearful of the Socialists. The Japanese stock market is expected to sink and the yen to rise to new highs against the dollar, as prospects for trade agreements with the United States grow dimmer. Already in New York trading today the dollar dropped below 99 yen for the first time since the modern trading system began after World War II.
 But predicting the policies of the new government is difficult, in part because Mr. Murayama's views are not well known and because of the nature of the coalition. Some experts say the Socialists will have to subordinate their policies to those of the Liberal Democrats, who outnumber them.
 "This is a puppet government of the Liberal Democratic Party," said Michitoshi Takabatake, professor of political science at Rikkyo University. "Murayama-san is only a figurehead."
 The Liberal Democratic Party is friendly to the United States, and would likely cooperate in sanctions on North Korea if need be. But they, like the Socialists, would also have an interest in slowing down political reform and deregulation. Both the Socialists and the Liberal Democrats draw much of their support from farmers, who suffer from freer imports.
 Mr. Murayama was born on March 3, 1924, in Oita City on Japan's southern island of Kyushu, the seventh of 11 children. He entered the Socialist Party after graduating with a degree in politics and economics from Meiji University in Tokyo in 1946. He began working for the Oita Prefecture government and also became active in the union of prefectural workers.
 Mr. Murayama's political rise was slow and inconspicuous. In 1955 he won a seat on the Oita City Assembly. In 1963 he was elected to the prefectural assembly.
 
Party Picks Him as Chief
 In 1972 he was elected for the first time to the lower house of Parliament. He did not stand out as a legislator, although he had a reputation as a specialist in social welfare and health care issues.
 His sudden propulsion into the limelight came after the Socialist Party had performed poorly in parliamentary elections last July. Sadao Yamahana, the leader of the party, was forced to resign.
 The party is split between left wing the right wing. Mr. Murayama is on the left side of the party but not the extreme left, and he emerged as a compromise choice, becoming chairman last September.
 Married with two daughters, Mr. Murayama is portrayed as being untainted by corruption and unassuming in style.
 He is proud of the trademark long eyebrows that are so long that they can be combed. On the wall of his office is a sign he received from another politician reading, "Firm eyebrows and decision-making."
 But his policies are more of a mystery and some critics say he changes them to mollify different party factions. His loyalty seems first and foremost to the party, his aim to keep the party from splintering.
 Mr. Murayama had to be flexible in the last year. The Socialist Party entered the coalition of Prime Minister Morihiro Hosokawa last summer but disagreed with the other coalition members on major issues and had to swallow its principles to keep its share of power.
 The Socialists went along with opening Japan's rice market and with political reform, even though they opposed both. They revolted against Mr. Hosokawa's plan to cut income taxes and raise sales taxes, forcing the Prime Minister to withdraw his proposal one day after making it.
 After Mr. Hosokawa resigned because of questions about his personal finances and Tsutomu Hata became Prime Minister, some conservative parties in the coalition formed a parliamentary bloc.
 Sensing a plot to isolate the Socialists, Mr. Murayama and his party bolted from the coalition. The coalition became a minority government, setting the stage for Mr. Hata's ouster and the elevation of an obscure fisherman's son to assume the most powerful seat in the world's second largest economy.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Tomiichi Murayama, chairman of the Social Democratic Party, acknowledging applause yesterday as he was formally elected Prime Minister of Japan in a lower house runoff election. The conservative Liberal Democrats and the leftist Socialists united in a coalition, ending weeks of political upheaval. (Associated Press)
